Part One
I
When the bohemian Crozat was lifted out of poverty by a good marriage which made him a bourgeois in the rue de Vaugirard, he did not break with his old comrades; instead of shunning them or keeping them at a distance, he took pleasure in gathering them around him, very happy to open his house to them, whose comfort threw him far from the garret in the rue Ganneron which he had lived in for so long, and flattered him pleasantly.
Every Wednesday, from four to seven o'clock, there was a meeting at his house at the Hôtel des Médicis, and it was a sacred day for which people reserved themselves: when a new idea germinated in the mind of one of the regulars, it was caressed, matured and studied in silence, in order to be presented in its full flower to the cenacle. "I'll talk about it at Crozat's"; the lips would turn into a hopeful smile, and people would fall asleep quietly, already listening to the noise that would be made in the small, low room of the hotel where Crozat, his hands outstretched, his face open, was receiving his friends.
It was an amiable reception, as simple as a man's, cordial on the part of both husband and wife, who, having been an actress, had kept the religion of camaraderie. On a table were jugs of beer and mugs; at arm's length was an old Beauvais stoneware pot full of tobacco. The beer was good, the tobacco dry; the mugs were never empty; you could put your dirty feet on the bars of the chairs while chatting freely between men, and spit without embarrassment around you.
And there was no nonsense or trivia to be discussed, no worldly gossip, no gossip about absent friends, no clique gossip, but the great philosophical, political, social and religious questions that govern humanity.
Initially made up of friends, or at least comrades who had worked and endured poverty together, the circle of these meetings had gradually widened, so much so that one day the room in the Hôtel des Médicis had become a 'parlotte' where preachers of new ideas and religions, thinkers, reformers, apostles, politicians, aestheticians and even simply chatterboxes in search of more or less complaisant ears met; Anyone who wanted to came along, and if you didn't go in quite like a brewery, all you had to do was to be brought in by a regular to be entitled to a pipe, a beer and a word. 
But, although a certain amount of freedom governed the agenda, it was not always possible to deliver the prepared speech for which one had come; for Crozat, who, in his own words, "pursued the conciliation of modern science with religions, whatever they were", used and even abused his position as master of the house not to allow the discussions to stray from the subjects that fascinated him.
What's more, had he weakened by giving in to considerations of benevolence, politeness or even weakness, which were quite in his character, the most assiduous of his regulars, Father Brigard, would have stood firm for him.
Brigard was a kind of apostle, who had made a name for himself by putting into practice in his life the ideas he professed and preached: As a law tutor, he could easily have earned a thousand or twelve hundred francs a month, but he had arranged the number and price of his lessons so that he earned only ten francs a day, so as not to be a slave to money; -- living with a woman he loved, he had always insisted, even though he had two daughters, on remaining with her in a 'free union' and not recognising her children, because the law would have weakened the ties that bound him to them and lessened his duties; it was conscience that sanctioned these duties; and nature and conscience made him the most faithful of husbands, the best, the most affectionate and the most tender of fathers. Tall, proud, with the native elegance of his race in his person and manners, he dressed like the local commissionaire, only replacing blue velvet with brown velvet, a less frivolous colour. Having lived in Clamart for twenty years, he had never come to Paris except on foot, and the only concessions he made to the superfluous or to well-being consisted, in winter, in walking in clogs and, in summer, in carrying his jacket on his arm.
Thus organised, he needed followers, and he looked for them everywhere, in the streets, where he held by the button the people he was able to grab under the trees of the Luxembourg, and on Wednesdays at the home of his friend, his old comrade Crozat. How many had he not had! Unfortunately, most of them had turned out badly; some had become ministers; others had allowed themselves to be buried in the high places of the irremovable magistracy; some were making millions; two were in Nouméa; one was preaching in the pulpit of Notre-Dame.
One afternoon in October, the small room was full; the end of the holidays had brought back the regulars and for the first time there were more or less enough people to start a useful discussion. Crozat, standing by the door, was smiling at the newcomers and shaking hands 'back from holiday'; and Brigard, his soft felt hat on his head, was presiding, assisted by his two favourite disciples at the moment, the lawyer Nougarède and the poet Glady, who, he was sure, would not turn out badly.
To tell the truth, for those who knew how to look and see, Nougarède's pale face, thin lips, worried eyes and austerity of bearing and manners that clashed with his twenty-six years, made one think of an ambitious man rather than an apostle. Similarly, when you knew that Glady owned a beautiful house in Paris and buildings in the provinces which brought him an income of a hundred thousand francs, it was hard to imagine that he was continuing Father Brigard's work.
But seeing was not Brigard's dominant faculty, it was reasoning, and reasoning told him that ambition would soon make Nougarède a deputy, just as fortune would one day make Glady an academician, and then, although he hated assemblies as much as academies, they would have two high platforms from which the good word would fall on the crowd with greater weight. You could count on them. When Nougarède had started coming to the Wednesday meetings, he was as hollow as a drum, and, if he spoke brilliantly on any subject with unflappable faconde, it was to say nothing. In the first volume of Glady, there were only words cleverly arranged for the pleasure of the ears and eyes. Now, the lawyer's speeches were underpinned by ideas, just as the poet's verses were saying something -- and these ideas were his own; this something was the fragrance of his teaching.
For half an hour after the pipes had been burning with a forced draught, the smoke rose only heavily to the ceiling, and it was in a cloud that Brigard could be seen, like a bearded god, proclaiming his law, with his hat on his head, for, although it was his rule never to take it off, he manoeuvred it continually while he was speaking, putting it sometimes forward, sometimes back, to the right, to the left, raising it, flattening it according to the needs of his argument.
There's no denying it," he said, "that we scatter our great strength when we should be concentrating it.
He pushed down his hat.
- In fact," he raised his hat, "the time has come for us to assert ourselves as a group, and it is our duty, since it is a need for humanity...
But Crozat, who was sitting near the entrance, stopped him as he passed and shook his hand:
- Well, Saniel! Hello, doctor.
- Good evening, dear sir.
- Come closer to the table: the beer is good today.
- Thank you, I'll be very happy here.
Without taking the chair that Crozat pointed out to him, he leaned against the wall: He was a tall, solid lad of about thirty, with tawny hair falling over the collar of his frock coat, a long, curling beard, an energetic but tormented and ravaged face, with pale blue eyes that gave him a hard expression that was further emphasised by his bony jaw and decisive bearing: in all, a Gaul, a true Gaul of times gone by, strong, skull-faced and resolute. 
Brigard continued:
- It is indisputable -- that was his formula, for everything he said was indisputable for him, by the very fact that he said it -- it is indisputable that, in the disarray in which humanity is struggling, it is important to establish the dogma of conscience, with duty done and inner satisfaction as its sole sanction...
- Duty done to whom?" interrupted Saniel, breaking away from the wall to take a step forward.
- To ourselves.
- So start by establishing what our duties are, and to do that codify what is good and what is bad.
- That's easy," says a voice.
- Easy if you accept a sort of innate respect for human life, property and the family. But you will recognise that not all men have this respect. How many men do not believe that it is wrong to take their friend's wife, a crime to appropriate something they need, or to kill an enemy! Where then are the duties of those who reason and feel in this way? What is their inner satisfaction worth? That's why I don't accept that conscience is a precision instrument capable of qualifying or weighing our actions.
Brigard suppressed a few exclamations.
- What rule will mankind obey, I ask you?
- To that of force, which is the last word in the philosophy of life...
- ... Which leads to gradual and skilful extermination. Is that what you want?
- Why not? I do not shrink from an extermination that relieves mankind of the non-values that it drags along without being able to move forward and free itself, succumbing to the pain. Wouldn't it be to humanity's advantage to get rid of these non-values that obstruct its path?
- It's at least a strange idea for a doctor," Crozat interrupted, "since it does away with hospitals.
- But not at all: I'm keeping them for the study of monsters.
- By placing society on this footing of acute antagonism," said Brigard, "you are suppressing society itself, which is based on reciprocity and solidarity, and you are thus creating a state of mistrust in your strongest members that paralyses them. Carthage and Venice practised this selection by force, and they collapsed.
-- You speak of force, my dear Saniel," interrupted a voice; "where do you get that, the force of things, fatum; there is no initiative, no will; it's events that decide for us, the climate, the temperament, the environment.
- So," replied Saniel, "there is no responsibility, and this instrument, conscience, which should weigh everything up, is useless. Not to mention the fact that the consequences of events, that success or defeat, also distort it, because a certain act that you thought reprehensible when you carried it out may serve the species, while another that you thought beneficial may harm it; from which it follows that only intentions should be judged and that only God can fathom hearts.
He laughed:
- Do you want that? Is that your conclusion?
A waiter from the hotel came in carrying jugs of beer on a tray, and the discussion was inevitably interrupted, with everyone surrounding the table where Crozat was filling mugs.
Then private conversations began, with those who had been on holiday telling those who had stayed in Paris what they had done.
Saniel had come to shake hands with Brigard, who had greeted him rather coldly; then he had approached Glady with the obvious intention of trying to corner him; but the latter had announced that he was obliged to leave, and Saniel had then said that he could not stay either and that he had only come in passing.
When they had both left, Brigard, addressing Crozat and Nougarède, who were at that moment near him, said that Saniel worried him:
- He's a boy who thinks he's stronger than life," he said, "because he's strong and intelligent; let him be careful it doesn't crush him!
II
When Saniel and Glady stood on the pavement of the rue de Vaugirard, the rain that had been falling since morning, whipped up by gusts from the west, had just stopped, and the asphalt shone clean and shiny like a mirror.
- It's nice to walk on," said Saniel.
- It's going to start raining again," replied Glady, looking up at the sky, which was full of big black clouds blowing across the face of the moon.
- I don't think so.
It was obvious that Glady only wanted to take a carriage, but as none was passing by at the moment, he had to walk alongside Saniel.
- Do you know," he said, "that you have injured Brigard?
- Sincerely, I regret it; but our friend Crozat's room is not quite a church yet, and I had no idea that discussion was forbidden there.
- To deny is not to discuss.
- You say that as if you were angry with me.
- Don't believe it; I'm angry that you hurt Brigard, that and nothing more!
- That's too much, because I have a sincere regard for you and, if you'll allow me to say it, a real friendship.
But Glady did not seem to want the conversation to take this turn.
- I think there's an empty carriage here," he said, spotting a hansom coming towards them.
- No," replied Saniel, "I can see the glow of a cigar behind the window.
Glady made a gesture of impatience which he did not abandon, but which Saniel, who was watching him, must have noticed all the more because he was watching him.
Glady lived in fear of borrowers. It was enough for someone to appear to want to speak to him in private for him to immediately believe that someone was going to ask him for fifty louis or twenty francs, so that any friend or comrade was an enemy against whom he had to defend his purse. At a meeting, if he felt that someone was looking at him, he immediately became suspicious. In the street, if someone came up to him, he was immediately on his guard. People smiled at him: he was afraid, and even more afraid when someone held out their hand to him, never knowing whether it was to shake his or to put something in. And to make sure he didn't put anything in, he was on the lookout as if someone was going to jump out at him, his eyes open, his ears tense, both hands on his pockets. Hence his attitude towards Saniel, in whom he sensed a demand for money, and his attempt to escape by taking a car. The guignon didn't want him to find any, so he tried to defend himself in another way:
- Don't be surprised," he said volubly, like a man who speaks so that no one can get a word in, "that I was sorry to see Brigard take to heart an outburst which was obviously not directed against him.
- Neither against him nor his ideas.
- I admit it; you don't have to defend yourself; but I have so much friendship, so much esteem, so much respect for Brigard that everything that touches him resonates with me. And how could it be otherwise, when you know what he's worth and what a man he is? Isn't it admirable, this life of mediocrity that he has voluntarily made for himself to ensure his freedom? What better example!
- Not everyone can follow it.
- You think we can't make do with ten francs a day.
- I mean, not everyone is lucky enough to earn ten francs a day.
Glady's vague fears, which had only been based on a premonition, were clarified by this word. After walking down the rue Férou, they arrived at the Place Saint-Sulpice.
- I think I'm going to find a carriage at last," he said hastily.
But this hope was not fulfilled: there was not a single carriage at the station; as a result, his impatience grew; he was caught and forced to endure Saniel's assault without being able to escape.
This is what Saniel said:
- You are now obliged to travel with me, and frankly, I'm delighted, because I have to talk to you about something... serious... on which my future depends.
- This is no place for a serious conversation.
- I can't think of anything.
- We could make an appointment.
- What's the point, when chance gives it to us?
We had to resign ourselves and at least, in the meantime, show some good grace.
- I'm all yours," he said, in a gracious tone that contrasted with his initial resistance.
Saniel, who had been so urgent a few moments earlier, remained silent for a moment, walking beside Glady, who was looking at the shiny asphalt:
- I told you that my future depended on the matter I wished to discuss with you; here it is in a nutshell: if I don't manage to get 3000 francs within two days, I shall be obliged to leave Paris, to give up my studies and my work on the train, to go and live in my native country and become a country doctor.
Glady didn't flinch, for if he hadn't foreseen the figure, he was expecting the request: he continued to look at the tips of his toes.
- You know," continued Saniel, "that I'm the son of peasants: my father was a blacksmith, a very small blacksmith in a poor village in the Auvergne. At school I showed a certain aptitude for work that my classmates didn't have to the same degree. Our parish priest took a liking to me and wanted to teach me what he knew, which didn't take very long. So he sent me to the minor seminary. But I didn't have the docility of mind and the submissiveness of character that such an education requires, and after a few years of tug-of-war, if I wasn't sent away, I was made to understand that they would be very happy to see me leave. I then joined a small boarding house as a tutor, without any salary, of course, for board and lodging. I passed my exams well and was preparing for my bachelor's degree when, following a discussion, I left the boarding house. I had earned some money giving private lessons and had about eighty francs to my name. I left for Paris, where I arrived at five o'clock one morning in June, not knowing a soul. I had a small case with a few shirts in it, which meant I had to take a carriage. I told the coachman to take me to a hotel in the Latin Quarter. What hotel?" said the coachman. -- I don't care. -- Do you want the Hôtel Racine? -- Go for the Hotel Racine: I like the name. We had been driving long enough when the coachman stopped his horse and wanted to turn back. "What's the matter? -- I have passed the Hotel Racine. -- Go on. I don't care for the Hotel Racine any more than any other. -- Do you want the Hotel du Sénat? -- The name suits me even better; perhaps it's an omen. He took me to the Hôtel du Sénat, where, with what was left of my eighty francs, I paid a month's rent in advance. I stayed there for eight years.
- It's a funny thing.
- What could I do? I knew Latin and Greek as well as any man in France, but as for the rest I was as ignorant as a spoon. That very morning, I tried to make the most of what I knew, and went to see a publisher of classical books whom my Greek literature teacher had told me about. After questioning me, he gave me a Pindar to prepare, with notes in Latin, and advanced me thirty francs, which kept me going for a month. What had brought me to Paris was the desire to work, but I hadn't told myself in advance what I would be working on; I went wherever courses were open: to the Sorbonne, the Collège de France, the École de droit, the École de médecine, and it was only after a month that I made up my mind: the subtleties of law had displeased me; on the contrary, the teaching of medicine based on the observation of facts attracted me: I would be a doctor.
- Quite a marriage of reason, come on.
- No, a marriage of love; for reason, if I had consulted it, would have told me that to marry medicine when you have nothing, no family to support you, no connections to push you, is to condemn yourself to a life of hardship, struggle and misery, in which the best-tempered leave shred after shred of physical health as well as moral health, their strength as well as their dignity. My time at school was a happy one; I worked and, with the few Latin lessons I gave, I had enough to eat. When I was paid six hundred francs, eight hundred francs, nine hundred francs as a boarder, I thought it was a fortune, and I would have stayed a boarder all my life if I could. When I became a doctor, I had to leave the hospital. Rich with a few thousand francs, I would have strictly followed the path my ambition had dreamed of, that of the competitive examination. But I had no money to wait. While looking after the mistress of one of my comrades, I met an upholsterer who offered to furnish a flat that I would pay for later...
- Like a casserole dish.
- Precisely. I was tempted. Don't forget that I had spent eight years at the Hôtel du Sénat and that I knew nothing of Parisian life; at home! in my own furniture! with a servant in my anteroom, I was going to be somebody. My upholsterer could have set me up in his district and perhaps he would have found me some patients among the clientele of the haute noce; but he didn't have the idea, no doubt judging that with my clumsy appearance I wasn't made to succeed in that world: when you arrive, it's an originality to be a peasant, people think you're strong; on the road, it's a disgrace. It was in the rue Louis-le-Grand, in a serious-looking house, that he chose the flat he furnished for me: a masterly salon with six armchairs and two Louis XIV sofas in the grand style, a study that was both austere and comfortable, nothing in the dining room, a small iron bed and a straw chair in the bedroom. So here I was, ready to go down fighting with ten thousand francs in debt behind me, interest on that sum, very high interest, rent of two thousand four hundred francs, not a penny in my pocket, not a relation...
- It was bravery.
-- I didn't know that in Paris everything is done through relationships, and I imagined that strong arms were enough for an intelligent man to open up an opening. Experience was to teach me. When a new doctor arrives somewhere, it's not usually with sympathy that his colleagues welcome him: "What does this intruder want? Weren't there enough of us already! They keep an eye on him and, the first time he loses a patient, they take advantage of his ignorance or carelessness to make his job difficult. I was made to feel the distance that separates an honourable businessman from a starving man, and I had to understand that they would only protect me if I ordered the specialities they were exploiting, the iron of this one, the tar of that one. So when I started out, my only customers were the locals, whose policy was not to pay their doctor, waiting for the arrival of a new one before leaving the old one - and there are a lot of them everywhere. As chance would have it, my concierge was an Auvergnat like me, and he saw it as his duty to give me free treatment for all our countries, which he canvassed in the neighbourhood and everywhere else, so that I had the patriotic satisfaction of seeing all the charcoal burners from the Auvergne fit into my beautiful armchairs. In the end, by staying religiously at home on summer Sundays, while my colleagues were out in the fields; by getting up quickly at night every time my doorbell rang, I ended up hooking a few less fanciful customers. I won a prize at the Académie. At the same time I was giving anatomy lessons on the cheap in boarding houses in the suburbs; I gave lessons and undertook all the anonymous bookshop and journalistic work I could get my hands on. I slept five hours a day, and in four years I managed to reduce my debt by seven thousand francs. My upholsterer would have liked to be paid: I would have managed, but that was not his intention: what he wants is to take back his furniture, which is not worn out, and keep what he has received. If I don't pay these three thousand francs in a few days, I'll be out on the street. The truth is that I have a thousand francs to collect, but the customers who owe me are not in Paris or won't pay until January. That's my situation: desperate, because I have no-one to turn to; the people I've turned to haven't listened to me; I told you I had no contacts, and I have no friends either... perhaps because I'm not likeable. That's when I thought of you. You know how I am. You know that people think I have a future: in three months' time I'll be a hospital doctor; my competitors agree that I won't fail the agrégation; I'm working on experiments that might make me a name; would you like to offer me your hand?
Glady held out his hand.
- I thank you for coming to me, it's a proof of confidence that touches me," he shook the hand he had taken warmly, "I can see that you have guessed the feelings of esteem that you inspire in me.
Saniel drew a breath.
- Unfortunately," continued Glady, "I could only do what you want me to do by contradicting my line of conduct. When I started out in life, I obliged everyone who came to me, and when I didn't lose my friends, I lost my money. So I swore to myself that I would refuse all loans. It's an oath I can't break. What would my old friends say if they knew that I had done for a young man what I had refused them?
- Who would know?
- My conscience.
They reached the Quai Voltaire, where a number of carriages were parked.
- Here are some carriages at last," said Glady, "forgive me for leaving you, I'm in a hurry.
III
Glady had got into the carriage so quickly that Saniel stood on the pavement, dumbfounded; it was only when the door closed that he understood:
- His conscience!" he murmured; "there they are! Tartufes!
After a moment's hesitation, he continued on his way and took the Pont des Saints-Pères; but he walked with hesitant steps, like a man who doesn't know where he's going. Soon he stopped and, leaning both arms on the parapet, watched the Seine flow swiftly, darkly, with small waves that were fringed with white foam at the circumference of the eddies. The rain had stopped, but the wind was still gusting, lifting the river and swinging the red and green lights of the omnibus boats in the darkness. Passers-by came and went, and more than one looked at him out of the corner of their eye, wondering what that big body was doing there and whether he was going to jump into the water.
And why not? What better thing to do?
In fact, that's what Saniel was telling himself as he watched the water flow: take a plunge and put an end to the crushing struggle he had been waging madly for four years and which, in the end, was panicking his spirit.
It wasn't the first time he had been tempted by the idea of putting an end to it, and he had only put it aside by constantly inventing new combinations which, it seemed to him at the very moment they came to mind, could save him. Why give up before you've tried and exhausted everything? That was how he had come to Glady. He knew him, however, and knew that his reputation for avarice, which everyone joked about, was based on certain facts; but he told himself that, if the owner stubbornly refused any friendly loan, which would only serve to pay youthful debts, the poet might very well want to fulfil the role of Providence and save from shipwreck, without risking anything, a man of the future who, later on, would return the service he had received. And it was under these conditions that he had risked his request. The owner had replied; the poet had remained silent. Now he could expect nothing from anyone. This was the last one.
By explaining his situation to Glady, he had lessened rather than exaggerated his misery. It wasn't just his upholsterer that he owed, it was also his tailor, his bootmaker, his coal merchant, his concierge, everyone with whom he was in contact. In reality, his creditors had not harassed him too much up to now, because they expected to be paid, but that was no longer going to be the case when they saw him being sued: they too would set the bailiffs in motion; so how would he defend himself? How would he live? He would have no choice but to return to the Hôtel du Sénat, where they would not leave him alone, or to go back to his native country and become a country doctor. In either case, he would have to give up all his ambitions. Wasn't death better?
What good was life if it gave him nothing of what he had dreamed and wanted?
Like many people who come into regular contact with death, life itself meant little to him, his own as well as that of others. Like Hamlet, he would say: "To die... to sleep, nothing more", but without adding: "To die... to sleep, to dream perhaps", as he was quite sure that the dead did not dream; and what could be better than to sleep for those whose road has been hard?
He remained thus absorbed in his thoughts, when a body, coming between him and the flickering gas burner, cast a shadow over his head which mechanically made him sit up straight. Who was there? Just a town sergeant who had come to lean against the parapet on which he himself was leaning, he realised: his attitude was certainly that of a man about to throw himself into the river and the town sergeant was standing there to prevent him from doing so.
- Thank you!" he said to the astonished sergeant.
And he set off again, walking quickly, but clearly hearing the policeman who was following in his footsteps, taking him for a madman who needed to be watched.
When he left the Pont des Saints-Pères for the Place du Carrousel, this surveillance ceased and he was able to return to his thoughts freely, at least as freely as his confusion and despondency allowed:
- It is the weak who kill themselves; the strong fight to their last breath.
And however low he had sunk, he had not yet drawn his last breath.
When he had decided to go to Glady, he had hesitated between the latter and a loan shark called Caffié whom he did not know personally, but of whom he had often heard as a real rascal who dealt in all sorts of affairs, bad rather than good, successions, marriages, prohibitions, blackmail ; And if it hadn't been his, it was as much for fear of being refused as for fear of putting himself in such hands, should they wish to accept him. But these scruples and fears were no longer in season: since he missed Glady, whatever the cost and whatever the outcome, he had to turn to the rascal.
He knew that Caffié lived in the rue Sainte-Anne, but he didn't know his number: all he had to do was go to one of his customers, a wine merchant in the rue Thérèse, to find him by consulting the Bottin. It was only a stone's throw away, and he immediately decided to risk the adventure; the business was urgent. Discouraged by all the approaches he had tried up to that point, put off by betrayed hopes, irritated by the rebuffs he had received, he did not delude himself about the chances of this last attempt, but in the end he had to make it, no matter how flimsy those chances were.
Caffié lived in an old house on the Butte des Moulins, which must once have been a private mansion: it consisted of two buildings, one on the street, the other on an inner courtyard. A carriage entrance gave access to this courtyard, and under its vault, after a staircase, was the caretaker's lodge. He had to wait a few moments and, in his nervous impatience, began to walk up and down the courtyard. Finally, a broken, stooped old woman appeared, a rat-de-cave in her hand, and excused herself: alone, she couldn't be everywhere at once, guarding her dressing room and lighting the landlady's staircase. Caffié lived on the first floor of the building on the street.
Saniel went up to the first floor and rang the bell; a fairly long time passed, or at least it seemed very long to his concern, before he was answered; finally, he heard a slow, shuffling step on the pavement, and the door opened, but held open by his hand and foot:
- Who are you asking for?
- M. Caffié.
- I'm here. And who are you?
- Doctor Saniel.
- I haven't called a doctor.
- I'm not here as a doctor, but as a customer.
- This is not the time to consult me.
- Since you're at home.
- By the way!
And Caffié, deciding to open the door, gave way to Saniel, then closed it again.
- Come into my study.
They were in a very small room cluttered with files, furnished only with an old desk and three chairs; it communicated directly with the businessman's study, which was larger but furnished with the same simplicity and cluttered with paperwork that smelt of mould.
- My clerk is ill at the moment," said Caffié, "and when I'm alone I don't like to open the door.
With this excuse, he showed Saniel to a chair and, sitting himself down in front of his desk, lit by a lamp whose shade he had removed, he said:
- Doctor, I'm listening.
He put the shade back on the lamp.
He owed three thousand francs to the upholsterer who had supplied his furniture and, as he was unable to pay at the moment, he was facing imminent legal proceedings.
- Who is this upholsterer?" asked Caffié, holding his left cheek in his right hand.
- Jardine, boulevard Haussmann.
- Well-known. It's his business to take back furniture he's sold when it's three quarters paid for, and it's made him rich. How much have you already paid him for this ten thousand francs' worth of furniture?
- With deposits and interest, nearly twelve thousand.
- And you're back to three thousand?
- Yes, I will.
- That's very kind.
Caffié seemed full of admiration for this way of proceeding.
- What guarantees do you have to offer for this loan of three thousand francs?
- None other than my present position, I admit, and above all my future.
At a sign from Caffié, he explained what that future was, while the businessman, his cheek in his hand, listened with an occasional muffled sigh, a sort of complaint.
- Hum! hum!" said Caffié when Saniel had reached the end of his explanation; "you know, my dear sir, you know :
Ma foi, sur l'avenir bien fou qui se fiera :
Those who laugh on Friday will cry on Sunday.
You have reached Sunday, my dear sir.
- But I am neither at the end of my life, nor at the end of my energy, and I assure you that this energy makes me capable of many things.
- I don't doubt it; I know what energy can do: tell a starving Greek to go to heaven, and he will:
Greculus esuriens in caelum, jusseris, ibit.
But I don't see that you've gone to heaven.
Caffié smiled evilly and grimaced: before becoming the usurer in the rue Sainte-Anne whom everyone spoke of as a rascal, he had been a solicitor in the provinces, a deputy judge, and although undeserved misfortune had forced him to resign and come to Paris to hide his unpleasantness, he never lost the opportunity to show that he was, by virtue of his education, above his present position: Finding in this new client a scholar, he was quite happy to place a few quotations which should earn him some consideration. 
- Perhaps it's because I'm not Greek," replied Saniel; "but I'm from Auvergne, and people from my country have strong backs.
Caffié shook his head:
- My dear sir, I have to tell you frankly that I don't think it's possible: I would do it myself, because the intelligence I can see in your face and the resolution that is evident throughout your person inspire my confidence; but I have no funds to put into these kinds of operations; as always, I can only act as an intermediary, in other words, I can only offer this loan to one of my clients, and I don't see who will be satisfied with guarantees based only on a more or less problematic future; there are so many doctors in Paris who are in your position! 
Saniel rose to his feet.
- You're leaving!" cried Caffié.
- But...
- Do sit down, my dear sir. You mustn't throw the handle after the axe. You make me a proposal, I'll show you the difficulties that I think it will encounter, but I'm not saying that there isn't a way to get you out of your predicament; it's up to you to look for it. I've only known you for a few minutes, but it doesn't take long to appreciate people like you, and frankly I'm very interested in you.
What was his point? Saniel was not a naïve man who was taken in at the drop of a hat, nor was he a fat man who accepts compliments with his mouth wide open. Why did this rascal, who had a reputation for taking the harshness of businessmen to the point of ferocity, take such a sudden interest in him? That remained to be seen. In the meantime, he had to be on his guard.
- I am very touched by your sympathy," he said.
- I want to prove to you that it is real and that it can be effective. You come to me because you need three thousand francs. If I find them for you - and I promise to look for them, even though it seems difficult, very difficult - they will ensure your present rest; but will they ensure your future, that is to say, will they allow you to continue the important work you have just told me about and on which your ambition is counting? No. The struggles in which you are struggling and wearing yourself out will soon begin again. And it is these struggles that you need to get rid of if you are to secure the freedom to work that is essential if you want to walk straight and fast. For this, I see only one way: -- get married.
IV
Saniel, who was on his guard and expecting some sort of cunning on the part of the business agent, had not at all foreseen that these expressions of interest would lead to a proposal of marriage; an exclamation of surprise escaped him. But it was lost in the ringing of the doorbell.
Caffié stood up:
- What a nuisance not to have a clerk!
He went to open the door with an alacrity he had not had for Saniel, and which proved that, not being alone, he no longer had the same fears of introducing someone into his home.
It was a banker who entered.
- If you don't mind," said Caffié, returning to his study and addressing Saniel, "it'll only take a moment.
Under the lamp, the banker was looking in his wallet; he drew out a draft which he presented to Caffié.
- The funds are ready", said the latter.
- With you, Monsieur Caffié, the money is always ready.
Caffié had taken a key from his waistcoat pocket and used it to open the iron box behind his desk, and turning his back to Saniel and the banker, he counted out the notes, the sound of which they could hear. He soon straightened up and, pushing back the door of his box, placed the bundles he had just counted under the lamp. The waiter in turn counted them and, having placed them in his wallet, saluted.
- Pull the door shut on your way out," said Caffié, who had already returned to his chair.
- Don't worry.
As the banker left, Caffié apologised for the interruption.
-- Let's resume our conversation, if you don't mind, my dear sir. I was telling you that there was only one way for you to get out of your predicament for ever, and that you would find it in a good marriage which would put a reasonable sum at your disposal here and now.
- But it would be madness for me to marry at the moment, when I have no position to offer my wife.
- And your future, which you spoke of earlier with such assurance, do you not have faith in it?
- Absolute faith, as firm today as when I entered the fight, but more enlightened. However, as others don't have the same reasons as I do for hoping and believing what I hope and believe, I find it quite natural to doubt this future: which is what you did yourself, just now, when you didn't think it was good enough to guarantee a simple loan of three thousand francs.
- Loans and marriage are not the same thing: a loan only gets you out of trouble momentarily, leaving you with many chances of being obliged to take out several others successively: which, you will agree, singularly attenuates the guarantees you can offer; whereas a marriage immediately opens up the road your ambitious dream has promised to travel.
- I've never thought about marriage.
- What if you did?
- For that you would first need a wife.
- If I offered you one, what would you say?
- Well...
- You're surprised, aren't you?
- I admit I am.
- My dear sir, I am a friend to my clients and to many of them, - dare I say it - a father. This is why, having great affection for a young lady -- and the daughter of a friend of mine, I thought, seeing you and listening to you, that one or the other could be the woman you need; both have wealth, they are intelligent and have physical advantages that a man, a handsome man like you, has the right to demand. Besides, I have their photographs precisely, and you can see for yourselves what they are like.
He opened a drawer in his desk and pulled out a packet of photographs, which he began to search through. Saniel, who was following him with his eyes, noticed that all the photographs were portraits of women; finally he made his choice and presented Saniel with two cards.
One was of a woman aged between thirty-eight and forty, of strong build and robust appearance, all covered in the horrible jewellery she had worn to have her portrait taken; the other was of a young woman in her twenties, quite pretty, dressed simply and elegantly, with a distinguished and discreet face that contrasted with that of the first portrait.
While Saniel was looking at these portraits, Caffié was examining him, trying to guess the effect his two subjects were producing.
- Now that you've seen them," he said, "let's talk about them a little. If you knew me better, my dear sir, you would know that I am frankness itself and that in business I make it a rule to say everything: the good and the bad, so that my clients alone are responsible for the decisions they make. In reality there is nothing bad about these two people, because if there were, I wouldn't suggest them to you; but in the end there are sides that my delicacy obliges me to point out to you, which I do without worrying, since I am sure that a man like you is not a slave to narrow prejudices.
He made a painful grimace and once again grabbed his jaw with both hands.
- Are you in pain?" asked Saniel.
- Yes, my teeth, cruelly, forgive me for saying so; I know for myself that nothing is more irritating than the sight of someone else's pain.
- Not for doctors, anyway.
- Anyway, let's leave that and get back to my customers. This one," he presented the portrait of the woman with the jewels, "is, as you may have guessed, a widow, a very amiable widow. She may be a few years older than you, but I don't think that's a serious grievance for you to raise, since your life experience has surely taught you that a man who wants to be loved, tenderly loved, pampered, caressed, spoiled, must take a woman who is older than he is, who will treat him like a husband and a son. Her first husband was a skilful merchant who, had he lived, would have made a handsome fortune in the butcher's trade, - this was chewed rather than clearly pronounced, - but who, although he died just as his business was expanding, left his wife twenty thousand pounds in annuities. As I say the good, I must also say the regrettable. Carried away by the company his business required, this highly intelligent man had acquired unfortunate habits of intemperance which, from outside, he had brought into his home and which, in a way, he had imposed on his wife. I have every reason to believe that she has corrected herself; but, if it were otherwise, you, the doctor, could easily cure her of it...
- Do you think so?
- I suppose so. However, as the opposite is possible, you would only have to abandon her to her vice, which would take her over in a fairly short time, and, as the contract would be settled by me with this eventuality in mind, you would find yourself invested with the fortune and rid of the wife.
- Shall we move on to the other?" said Saniel, who had listened uninterrupted to this curious presentation of the situation by Caffié with the utmost bonhomie; however serious the circumstances, he could not help but be amused by this ready-made diplomacy.
- I was waiting for your request," replied the businessman with a grim smile, "and if I told you about this amiable widow, it was more out of a clear conscience than in the hope of succeeding: however free of prejudice one may be, one always retains a few. I understand yours, and what's more, I share them. Fortunately the one I have to talk to you about now does not give rise to grievances of this kind. Take her photograph, my dear sir, and look at it while I speak. Charming physiognomy, isn't it true? Superior education in a fashionable convent. In a word, a pearl to adorn yourself with. Now I'm going to the straw, because there's one. Who doesn't? Daughter of an actress, one of our most graceful genre actresses. When she left the convent, the young girl lived with her mother. It was there, in that heady environment, if you please, that she had an accident. In short, a child, a delicious little boy, whom the father would surely have recognised, such was his esteem for the mother, if he himself had not been married. At least he has secured his fate with a gift of 200,000 francs, so that whoever marries the mother and legitimises the child by subsequent marriage will have the legal right to these two hundred thousand francs until the boy comes of age... if he makes it that far: these little creatures are so fragile! You, as a doctor, know this better than anyone. In the event of misfortune, the father would inherit half of his son's fortune; and while it is cruel for a real father to inherit from a real son, the situation changes completely when it is from a stranger that one receives a fortune. That is the case, my dear sir, clear and frank, and I do you no injustice in supposing that you do not see the advantages of it without needing to insist. If I have not explained myself more clearly...
- But nothing could be clearer.
- ... The fault lies with this fluxion that paralyses me.
He grabbed his jaw and whimpered.
- Do you have a tooth that's causing you pain?" asked Saniel in the tone of a doctor questioning a patient.
- All teeth cause me pain. In fact, they've given up on me.
- Have you consulted a doctor?
- Neither a doctor nor a dentist. Of course I believe in medicine, but when I've gone to doctors, which has rarely happened to me, I've noticed that they think much more about their own business than about what I tell them, and that's what's kept me away from them. But, my dear sir, when a customer comes to see me, I put myself in his place and I get under his skin.
As he spoke, Saniel examined him, which he had not done until that moment, and saw in him signs of rapid emaciation that were quite characteristic; he was floating in his clothes, which were made for a man half as fat as he was now; his face was red and shiny as if it had been covered with a layer of cherry sugar.
- Would you like to show me your teeth?" asked Saniel; "it might be possible to ease your pain.
- Do you think...
His examination didn't take long.
- You often have a dry mouth, don't you?
- He asked.
- Are you very thirsty?
- Really annoying.
- Do you sleep well?
- No, I haven't.
- Do you have trouble seeing?
- Yes, I have.
- Didn't you notice that you were putting sticky stains on your laundry?
- No doubt, but I didn't think it was important.
- Are you eating well?
- I'm devouring; and the more I eat, the thinner I get; I'm turning into a skeleton.
- I see you still have boil scars on the back of your neck.
- They made me suffer enough, the rascals; but they left as they came. You're not young any more at seventy-one, you have your little troubles, because they're just troubles, aren't they?
- Certainly; with a few precautions and a diet that I'll show you, if you don't mind, you'll get rid of them easily. I'm still going to write you a prescription to soothe your toothache.
- We'll talk about the rest again, because we'll have the opportunity to meet again if, as I presume, you appreciate the advantages of the proposal I've made.
- I'd like to think about it.
- Nothing could be fairer; besides, there's no hurry.
- If there is an emergency, it's with me, because if I don't pay Jardine, I find myself on the street, which is not a position to offer a woman.
- In the street, in the street! Things won't go as quickly as that. How are the proceedings going?
- They're about to start; Jardine threatened me with them.
- They're going to start; they haven't started yet. If, as I presume, he proceeds with a seizure-revendication, we'll have time before the judgment. Do you owe anything to your landlord?
- The term expires on the 15th.
- Don't pay him.
- That's easy; it's the only thing that's easy for me.
- It's an obstacle in your Jardine's path that could stop him for a moment. That way we can manoeuvre more easily. The main thing is to let me know as soon as the fire starts. Goodbye then, dear sir.
V
Although Saniel had no experience of business, he was not naive enough not to understand that Caffié, by refusing him this loan, wanted to keep him in close dependence.
- The calculation is simple," he said to himself as he descended the stairs. "He is taking on my defence and conducting it in such a way that one fine day, which is not far off, I can only save myself by holding out my hand to the charming young girl. What a scoundrel!
However, such was the situation, that he should be happy to obtain the help of this scoundrel: at least, it was time saved, and Jardine, seeing that he no longer had a sheep before him willing to let his throat be slit, would perhaps agree to a reasonable arrangement; the only thing was to manoeuvre in such a way that Caffié did not prevent this arrangement.
Unfortunately, he felt unsuited to this manoeuvre, having always been straight ahead, with his eye fixed on his goal, thinking only of the work that would make him achieve it; -- and now he had to improvise himself as a diplomat, bending to finesse, to tricks that were not at all in his brutal nature: He had begun by not immediately telling Caffié what he thought of his proposals; but it is more difficult to act than to contain oneself, to speak than to remain silent. 
What would he say, what would he do, when the moment for action came?
He arrived home without having found anything, and as he passed the concierge's lodge, absorbed in his preoccupation, he heard someone calling him:
- Doctor, would you come in for a moment, please?
He thought it was some consultation they wanted to ask him about, a country awaiting his return as so often happened, and although he was not in a position to listen patiently to idiotic chatter, he retraced his steps and entered the dressing room.
- Cha has been brought in," said the concierge, handing him a sheet of stamped paper covered in handwriting.
Cha was the beginning of the fire that Caffié had mentioned. Without reading it through, Saniel put it in his pocket and prepared to leave, but the concierge stopped him.
- I'd like to say a few words to the doctor about this paper.
- Have you read it?
- Of course not, but I spoke to the bailiff's clerk who gave it to me 'talking to my perch' and he explained the situation to me. That's a shame, doctor!
All Saniel needed was to be pitied by his concierge.
- She's not what we told you," he replied haughtily.
- Well, so much the better! I'm very happy, for you and for me. You can pay me my little bill.
- You can give it to me.
- I've already given it to you twice, but I've done it again; here it is.
Saniel was either paralysed by a creditor's complaint, his mouth agape with humiliation, or he could only respond with clumsiness. Taking the note the concierge handed him, he put it in his pocket and stammered a few words.
-- You see, doctor, I have to tell you what has been in my heart for a long time. You are my country and I think too highly of you not to speak out. By taking your flat, by getting involved with your upholsterer, you've done more than you bargained for: you're wearing yourself out; leave this flat, take the one opposite which costs half as much, and you'll be fine. You won't be forced to abandon the neighbourhood. What would happen to the country if you left us? You're a good doctor, everyone recognises that and says so, including the local people. Now, for my little note, it's agreed that I'll go first, isn't that right?
- As soon as I have the money, I'll pay you.
- Is that agreed?
- You have my word.
- Thank you very much. I'm not rich, you know, and yet I've always paid for the gas for your experiments.
With his stamped paper in his pocket, Saniel returned to Caffié, whom he met under the carriage entrance, where he handed him the bailiff's writ.
- I'll see about it this evening," said the businessman; "for the moment, I'm going to dinner. But don't worry, I'll take care of everything tomorrow morning. Good evening; I'm starving.
If Saniel wasn't starving, he too would have gladly eaten dinner, but three days earlier he had bled himself dry to pay his upholsterer as much as he could on account, keeping only five francs for himself, and it wasn't with the few pennies he had left that he could get into a restaurant or even a gargote, however miserable it was. All he had to do was buy a loaf of bread and eat it while he worked, as he had so often done.
But when he got home, he couldn't get down to the article he had to write and deliver that evening. Among the tasks he had taken on was one, and not the least tedious, which consisted of giving advice by mail to the subscribers of a fashion journal, or, more correctly, recommending it, in the form of medical advice, all cosmetics -- depilatory pastes, elixirs, aromatic waters, tinctures, essences, oils, vinegars, milks, creams, soaps, opiates, ointments, glycerines, vaselines, sachets, pastilles, toothpastes, eye shadow ; and also all the pharmaceutical specialities -- fortifying wines, regenerative pills, pectoral pastes, tars, irons, syrups, purgatives, to which their inventors wanted to give an authority that the public, which thinks it is clever, refuses to give to the simple announcement on the last page. With the ambition he had and the career he wanted to follow, he would never have agreed to write this correspondence under his own name; so for this work he was only the secretary of one of his colleagues who, as a simple local doctor, did not have the same reservations and bravely signed these consultations, finding that clients and money were always good to take, wherever they came from. To make up for his trouble, Saniel replaced his colleague on Sundays in the summer, and from time to time received a free case of perfume or pharmaceutical products, which he sold at a discount when the opportunity arose.
Every week, he was given a list of the cosmetics and specialities that were to feature in his correspondence, and it didn't matter how he recommended them, either by replying to letters that were actually addressed to him, or by inventing questions that would allow him to introduce them more or less appropriately.
He was beginning to consult this list and the bundle of letters from subscribers that the newspaper had sent him, when the doorbell rang; it might have been a sick man, the good sick man he had been waiting for in vain for four years: he left his desk to go and open the door.
It was his coalman, coming to pick up his bill.
- I'll come and see you one of these days," said Saniel; "tonight I'm in a hurry.
- Well, I'm in a hurry too: I've got a deadline tomorrow and I've counted on the doctor.
- I don't have any money here.
- I just want the doctor to give me a deposit.
- I'm telling you I don't have any money here.
- So it's true what they're saying about the doctor being sued by the bailiffs, that he's going to be sold or have his furniture repossessed. He wouldn't want me to lose my money; I'm a family man.
Saniel knew only too well that he was a father, having had to look after this family for four years, made up of a mother and three children who were constantly ill, without there ever being any question of paying him for his visits.
Somehow, after an interminable discussion, he managed to dismiss the coalman, and went back to his office to work on his article.
The first letter he picked up, signed "Parfum de cyclamen", asked for advice on teeth:
"Cyclamen scent. -- Give up your toothpaste, which is dangerous and will make you lose all your teeth before you are five, and switch to the Durand pharmacy, 215, rue Richelieu, whose good effects I guarantee...
"A pale young woman. -- The operation is radical, without danger to the skin or health; but it must be performed by a hand skilled in handling electricity. Come and see me, 117, Chaussée-d'Antin, from two to four o'clock; I'll be delighted to see you.
It wasn't him, Saniel, but his colleague, the one who signed this correspondence and who, by using these baits, was attracting a few customers.
He was about to move on to the third letter, signed: "Une affligée de vingt ans", when the doorbell rang again. This time he wouldn't open the door: another creditor, no doubt. And he wrote down his advice.
And yet? For the last four years, he had been waiting for a lucky ticket in life's lottery: a rich patient, suffering from a cyst or tumour, whom he took to a fashionable surgeon, who would share the ten or fifteen thousand francs, the price of the operation, with him. Then he was saved.
He ran to his door. The patient with the cyst appeared in the form of a little bearded man with a flaming face, wearing over his jacket the thick black canvas apron worn by wine merchants. It was, in fact, the local wine merchant who, having also learned the truth from the bailiff, had come to collect his small bill for wine and portions supplied over the last three months for the doctor's lunches.
The scene that had taken place with the coal merchant began again, more lively, more violent, and Saniel had to get angry and threaten to throw out the wine merchant, who only left promising to come back the next day with his bailiff.
Saniel resumed his article:
"A Parisienne in perspective. -- Since you will soon be coming to Paris, I will postpone my prescription until you arrive: not all explanations are worth a glance. Let your first visit be to 117 de la Chaussée-d'Antin: you're sure to find me between two and four.
"Between parakeet and marmoset. -- Make use of the sachets of toiletries from the Magnolia perfumery, they will delay your wrinkles, which you are certainly exaggerating, your style says so."
His pen was running over the paper when the sound of footsteps made him look up: either he had locked his door on the wine merchant's back, or it was his servant who had just come in with his key... So what did he want? He didn't use it all day, but only at the time of his consultation, for housework and to open the door for customers when they came in.
As he was about to get up to see who it was, a knock came at the door: it was indeed his servant, looking sheepish and embarrassed.
- What is it, Joseph?
- I thought I'd find the gentleman, so I came.
- Why did you come?
Joseph hesitated; then, taking courage, he said volubly, keeping his eyes lowered:
- I've come to ask the gentleman to pay me my month's rent, which is due on the 15th, because the house needs money right away; if we didn't need money, I wouldn't have come.
Saniel looked at him.
- Don't you know that a bailiff left some stamped paper with the concierge?
- Who could have told him that?
- Do you know or don't you?
- Well, it's true; so, as when the bailiffs are somewhere they raid everything, I thought that monsieur, who is so fair, wouldn't want me to lose my poor money that I've had so much trouble earning. So I came, and here I am.
- Well, I don't have any money; if I had, I would have paid the bailiff.
- So I have to lose my wages?
- I'll pay you later.
- When will that be?
- As soon as I can.
- Will the bailiffs let you do that? They're going to sell everything here. If the gentleman would like, I'll hold him harmless...
- How would you do that?
- I'd take the frock coat that the gentleman had made for me two months ago; of course it's not worth what I'm owed, but it would still be something.
- Take the frock-coat with you.
Joseph quickly took his frock coat from the hall cupboard where it was hanging, and rolled it up in a newspaper.
- So Monsieur won't be counting on me tomorrow," he said, putting his key in a chest; "I'll have to look for a job.
- That's fine, I won't count on you.
- Good evening, sir.
And Joseph left as quickly as possible.
Left alone, Saniel did not go back to work straight away; but, falling back into his armchair, he cast a melancholy gaze around his study and into the living room, whose door had been left open: in the dim light of his candle, he could see his big armchairs methodically lined up on either side of the fireplace, the draperies of the windows drowned in shadow and all the furniture that had cost him so much effort over the last four years. It was this junk Louis XIV that he had been a prisoner of for so long, and by whom he was now going to be executed. What a clever and skilful affair! All that had served only poor Auvergnats, and he himself had not enjoyed it, not having the bourgeois taste for knick-knacks, nor the need for well-being. A movement of anger and revolt against himself made him slam his fist down on his desk: how naive he had been!
The doorbell rang again. This time he wouldn't hear it, as he was no longer counting on the rich client.
After a brief moment, the doorbell was gently tapped. He got up quickly and ran to open the door.
A woman threw herself around his neck:
- Ah, my darling, how happy I am to find you home.
VI
She had put an arm round his waist and, snuggling up to him, they had entered the study.
- I'm so glad," she repeated, "what a good idea I've had!
And with a sudden movement she got rid of the long grey frock coat that wrapped her up to her feet.
- And you, are you happy?" she said, standing in front of him to get a better look.
- Can you ask?
- Just to hear you say it.
- Aren't you my only joy, the soft light that illuminates me at the bottom of the pit where I dig day and night!
- Dear Victor!
She was a tall, slender young woman with chestnut-coloured hair in thick curls that reached all the way to her eyebrows. Beautiful dark eyes, a short nose, superb teeth and gums the colour of strawberries gave her the air of a pretty dog; she had the gaiety, the vivacity, the graceful effrontery, the passionate caress of the gaze. Dressed to the nines, like a penniless Parisian who adorns everything she wears, she had a casualness and a natural elegance that charmed: she had a good-natured tone, a joyful laugh and an expression of sensitivity spread across her fresh face.
- I'll come and have dinner with you," she said cheerfully, "and I'm hungry!
He let out a movement that she caught.
- Am I embarrassing you?
- Not at all.
- Do you have to go out?
- No, I don't have to go out.
- Then why did you make a movement that betrayed boredom or at least embarrassment?
- You're wrong, my little Philis.
- With anyone else I might be wrong, but with you, is it possible? You know very well that between us there is no need for words, that I can read in your eyes what you are going to say, in your face what you are thinking and feeling. Isn't it always like that when we love... as I love you?
He took her in his arms and kissed her for a long time; then, going to an armchair on which he had thrown his overcoat when he came in, he took out of a pocket the bread he had bought.
- Here's my dinner," he said, pointing to the bread.
- Oh, I must scold you: work is making you lose your head. Can't you take time to eat?
He smiled sadly:
- I've had plenty of time.
He reached into his pocket and pulled out three large pennies he had left:
- You can't eat out on sixpence.
She threw herself at him:
- Oh, darling, forgive me," she cried. Poor dear martyr, dear great man, it is I who accuse you, when I should be kissing your knees. And you don't scold me; a sad smile is your answer. So there you are: not even enough to eat!
- You can eat very well with bread; I'm sure I'll always have some!
- Well, today I want you to have more and better. This morning, when I saw the bad weather, I had an idea that involved you: it's quite natural, since you're never out of my heart or out of my mind: I told Mum that, if the gale continued, I would sleep at the boarding house. You can imagine the emotion with which I listened to the wind all day, watching the rain fall mixed with the leaves and dead branches that whirled past. Thank God the weather was bad enough for Mum to think I'd be safe at the boarding house, and here I am, yours until tomorrow morning. But as we won't be able to stay sober until then on just your bread, I'm going to go and buy some dinner; we'll have dinner by the fire, which will be much more fun than going to the restaurant.
She quickly put on her frock coat.
- Set the table while I do my shopping.
- I've got my article to finish, which we're going to collect at eight o'clock; don't forget that I've still got three tonic wines to recommend, five iron preparations, a henna tincture, a breast milk, two hair lotions, an opiate, I don't know how many soaps and rice powders, and they've got to be included in my article, whether by force or by choice. What a job!
- Well, don't worry about the table; we'll set it together when you've finished, which will only be more fun.
- You take everything in stride!
- Is it better to look at it from the wrong side? See you later!
She was about to pull the door open.
- Don't do anything foolish," he said.
- There's no danger," she replied, tapping her pocket.
Then, coming back to him, she kissed him passionately:
- Work.
And she ran off.
They had been in love for two years. At that time, Saniel went every week to a boarding house near Paris to give an anatomy course for girls preparing for the Hôtel de Ville exams, and every time he met a young woman he couldn't help noticing: She left and returned at the same times as he did, and gave lessons in the boarding house rival to the one where he taught: as she often carried under her arm a large cardboard box or sometimes a plaster cast, he had conjectured, without needing to make an effort, that it was drawing that she was teaching. At first he hadn't paid any attention to her: he didn't care about this drawing teacher; he had other things on his mind than women. But little by little, precisely because she was discreet and reserved, he had been struck by the vivacity and cheerfulness of her face: it was a real pleasure to look at this pretty and above all pleasant young woman. However, he hadn't let on what he thought of her: if their eyes smiled at each other when they met, that was all; they didn't know each other at all. When he took the left-hand pavement, he was sure she would take the right, and vice versa. Things went on like this for several months without a word being exchanged between them: only by force of circumstance had they both learnt who they were: She was the daughter of a painter who had been dead for seven or eight years and who had enjoyed a certain reputation. He was a doctor with a promising future, a very strong man who would one day be seen at work. These reasons had arisen by chance: One summer day when the weather had suddenly turned stormy at the hour when they usually took the train back, Saniel, returning to the railway, had joined Miss Philis Cormier on the way, whom he saw hurrying ahead of him; they still had five or six hundred metres to cover across a plain without houses before arriving at the station, i.e. more than enough time to be flooded if the black clouds rolled by the wind decided to burst: He had an umbrella that had just been lent to him when he left the boarding house; she had none. For the first time, he decided to speak to her:
- You don't have an umbrella: would you allow me to walk beside you? I'll shelter you with the one they've just lent me.
She replied with a smile, and they walked side by side until the rain came down; then she came closer to him, and they entered the station chatting happily:
- Your umbrella is better than Virginie's skirt," she said.
- What is Virginie's skirt?
-- Haven't you read Paul and Virginie?
- No, I haven't.
She had looked at him with a slightly mocking smile, wondering what very strong people could read.
Not only had Saniel never read Bernardin de Saint-Pierre's novel, no more that one than any other for that matter, but he had never even liked it, since the things of the heart were no more his doing than those of the imagination. Leisure is needed for pleasure reading, and even more so for love, just as it needs a freedom of spirit and an independence of life that he did not have. Where would he have found the time to read novels? When and how would he have cared for a woman? The women he had had since his arrival in Paris had never had the slightest influence on him, and he had not kept a distinct memory of any of them. On the contrary, thinking of that walk in the rain, he had rediscovered that young girl with a sureness of memory that was quite extraordinary for him; the impression had therefore been so strong that it continued: he saw Philis again with her smile that uncovered her dazzling teeth, he heard the music of her voice, and that monotonous plain, which he had crossed so often without ever seeing her, appeared to him as the most beautiful landscape in the world. For the first time, he had realised that the hollow, muscular conoid organ known as the heart could serve a purpose other than the circulation of blood.
What a surprise and disappointment! Was he going to be naive enough to love this young girl and entangle a woman in a life that was already so difficult and so full? Quite an affair, really, and how nature had built him to play the lover! It's true that only those who want it become lovers, and from experience he knew the strength of will.
But this self-confidence soon had to give way: away from Philis, he could do as he pleased; near her, it was she who wanted; with one look she was master of him; he arrived furious at the influence she had taken over him, and against which he had struggled since they had last seen each other; he left her delighted to feel how deeply he loved her.
For a man whose life had hitherto been governed by reason and logic, these contradictions were exasperating, and he could only forgive himself for enduring them by telling himself that they could in no way alter the course of action he had set himself, nor make him deviate from the path he was following.
Rich, or simply with a little fortune, he could have - when he was close to her and in her power - let himself be dragged along; but it was not when he was starving that he was going to do the folly of taking a wife; what would he have to give her? his misery, nothing but his misery; and shame, for want of any other reason, would prevent him from offering her anything ever again.
Since they had known each other, she herself had quite naturally, in conversation, added to the information he had first had: she was indeed, as he had been told, the daughter of a painter; her father, who had had a difficult start, had died just as, after years of hard struggle, he was coming into his own; ten more years of work and he would have left his family, if not wealth, at least a great deal of ease. In reality, all he had left them was ruin; the hotel he had built had been sold and their debts paid off, leaving them with nothing but a few pieces of furniture. They had had to work, and there were three of them, a mother, a son and a daughter; the mother, who had no trade, had taken up linen work; the son had left school to become a clerk for a businessman called Caffié; the daughter, who fortunately for her had learnt to draw and paint under her father's guidance, had sought lessons, and, to add to the little she earned, she designed dinner menus for paper manufacturers and painted boxes and fans on silk: The brother, tired of his sad existence and the toil imposed on him by his businessman, had just left them to try his luck in America. If Saniel ever married, which he doubted, he would certainly not marry a woman in these circumstances.
This thought reassured him, and he gave himself over to her a little more; why shouldn't he enjoy the sweet pleasure of seeing and hearing her? He felt perfectly sure of himself and, as he knew her now, he was just as sure of her: a good, honest girl; besides, how could she have guessed that he loved her?
So they continued to see each other with a pleasure that seemed equal on both sides, going to meet each other as soon as they saw each other at the station, waiting for each other, getting into the same carriage, always arranging to travel together on the outward and return journeys, and chatting freely and cheerfully, forgetting the weather so well that it was rare for their arrival not to surprise them.
Things had gone on like this until the holidays approached, meaning a momentary separation, and a little before then they had decided that after their last lesson, instead of taking the train to the station as usual, they would go a league away to return to Paris by tram, which would give them a good hour's walk through the woods.
The sun was hot that day: halfway along, Philis asked to rest for a moment; they sat down in a copse, and soon found themselves in each other's arms.
Since then, Saniel had never spoken of marriage and Philis had never said anything of her own.
They loved each other.
VII
Saniel was still at work when Philis came in.
- Haven't you finished yet, poor dear?
- Time enough to treat by correspondence an illness for which the careful examination of ten doctors might not be enough, and I'm all yours.
In three lines the deal was done; he left his office:
- Here I am: what do you want me to do?
- Help me take out what's in my pockets.
She had already got rid of a bottle wrapped in a sheet of paper and placed it on the desk.
- You're so heavy!
- Just the right amount.
She had parcels under her arms, and the pockets of her frock coat and dress seemed full: it was a job to empty them.
- Don't squeeze too hard," she said to each packet he took from her.
In the end, the pockets were empty.
- Where shall we dine?
- Here, because the dining room has been turned into a laboratory.
- Well, let's start with the fire; I got my feet wet wading through the road to the station.
- I don't know if there's any wood.
- Let's have a look.
She took the candle and they went into the kitchen, which, just as the dining room was a laboratory, was also a stable where Saniel kept guinea pigs and rabbits in cages for his experiments, and where Joseph piled up everything that bothered him, without having to worry about the stove or the grill, which had never been lit. There were old brooms, shoe polish brushes, cabbages for the rabbits, carrots for the guinea pigs, piles of newspapers, crates and boxes.
- Do you care about these boxes?" she asked, stroking a little pig she had held in her arms.
- Not at all; they were used to pack perfume and pharmaceutical specialities; they're useless now.
- Well, you can make a lovely fire with these boards; broken up, they'll make a nice, bright, blazing fire.
Saniel took it and quickly split enough boxes to have a good supply of wood.
- What it's like to be from Auvergne!" she said, laughing; "it's as if you're all born with the genius for charcoal-burning.
- So you're making fun of me?
- No, but you chop your wood seriously, gloomily, as if you were skinning a sick man, and I'd like to make you laugh a little, by laughing at you myself, at me, at anyone, as long as you lighten up.
They returned to the study, Saniel carrying the supply of wood.
- Now let's set the table," she said cheerfully.
A small folding pedestal table was placed in front of the window, and Saniel often used it for lunch, with the matching plate that Joseph would fetch for her from the butcher's, or with the portion provided by the wine merchant who, a few moments earlier, had come to make a scene with her; she took it and brought it in front of the fireplace where she opened it.
- Where's the washing?
- I'm not rich in linen, but in this cupboard I have towels that I spread over the chests and shoulders of the people I examine; let's see if there are any clean ones.
There were four left, which was one more than I needed.
- Do you have plates, knives, forks and glasses?
- Yes, in a cupboard in the dining room.
- Let's go and get them.
This dining room, where we had never eaten, was the most curiously furnished room in the flat. No table, no chairs, no sideboard; but along the wall, white wooden boards forming shelves, and on these boards, matras, balloons, horizontal or vertical effilure flasks, culture tubes, filters, a gas oven, a microscope, slices of bread, pieces of potato, here and there jars, vials, and also a few books, in short the equipment of a small bacteriological research laboratory: This was the room where Saniel worked more often and for longer than in his consulting room. 
The five or six plates, three knives and glasses that made up Saniel's crockery and glassware were kept in a cupboard.
- Are you sure there aren't any germs on the plates?" asked Philis as she took what she needed to serve the table.
- I hope not.
- Well, if you wipe them clean.
Philis promptly set the table, coming and going, circling the table with a graceful lightness that Saniel admired.
- So you do nothing," she said.
- I look at you and think.
- And the result of these reflections, can we ask?
- It's that in you there's a fund of good humour and gaiety, an exuberance of life that would cheer up a man on death row.
- And what would have become of us, I beg you, if I had been a melancholic and despondent person when we lost my poor dad? He was a joy to be around, singing all day long, waking up with a song on his lips and, while working, laughing and joking, never for a minute in a bad mood. I was brought up by him and close to him, and I look like him. When, in the space of a few days, he was taken away from us, you can imagine how we were devastated, falling from this happy life into distress and grief; Mum, as you know, is melancholic and restless, shy and inclined to see everything in black; my brother Florentin is like her. It was a time of dreary despair: Mum kept saying from morning till night that all we had to do was die of hunger; my brother wanted to join the army; I didn't give up, and although I couldn't laugh or sing, I did stir myself up enough to shake off the numbness of despair: I got Florentin a job, I found work for Mum, and I found some for myself too.
She looked at him with a smile that said: "Will you let me do for you what I did for them?
But she did not say these precise words; on the contrary, she immediately tried to erase their impression if, as she believed, he had guessed them.
- Go and fetch some water," she said; "in the meantime, I'll light the fire; now's the time.
When he returned with a full decanter, the fire was blazing, casting golden fizzes that lit up the study. Sitting at the desk, Philis was writing.
- What are you doing here?" he asked in surprise.
- I'm writing our menu, because you can imagine that we're not going to sit down to a bourgeois dinner like that. Here it is: what do you think.
She read out loud:
- Sardines from Nantes. -- Roast turkey leg. -- Terrine of foie gras pâté with Périgord truffles". But this is a feast, your dinner!
- Did you think I was going to offer you a portion of fricandeau au jus?
She continued:
- Brie cheese. -- Vanilla cream puffs. -- Normandy apple. -- Wine..."
- Ah! voilà. What wine? I wouldn't want to deceive you. Let's say: "Wine from the local wine merchant." And now, to the table.
As he was about to sit down, she stopped him:
- Aren't you going to take my arm and lead me to my seat? If we don't do things seriously, methodically, we won't believe it, and the Périgord truffles might turn into little black pieces of anything.
When they were sitting opposite each other with their napkins unfolded, she continued her joke:
-- Are you going to the performance of Don Juan on Monday, my dear doctor?
And Saniel, who in spite of everything had kept a sombre expression on his face, began to laugh frankly.
