- Come on!" she exclaimed, clapping her hands together. No more worrying, no more worrying! Put your eyes in mine, dear Victor, and let's think only of the present moment, of the joy of being together, of our love. Is that all?
She held out her hand to him over the table.
He took it and shook it:
- It's said.
The dinner continued merrily, Saniel responding to the smiles and gaiety of Philis, who led the conversation without letting it languish: she served him, poured him a drink, and there was a burst of voices and laughter the like of which this cabinet had never heard; from time to time, she left her chair and threw a handful of wood into the fire, which, half extinguished, resumed its fizzing.
However, she noticed that little by little Saniel's face, relaxed for a moment, darkened and took on the expression of concern and bitterness that she had found so hard to banish.
- Doesn't this charming dinner party give you the idea of starting up again soon?
- Start again! But how? Where could I?
- But if I was able to come this evening without Mum worrying, I'll find some way, some excuse, to start again next week.
He shook his head.
- You won't be free next week?" she asked, worried.
- Where will I be next week, tomorrow, in a few days' time?
- You're scaring me! Please explain. Oh, Victor, have pity on me, don't leave me in anguish.
- You're right; I must tell you everything and not let your tenderness seek explanations for my worries, which can only torment you.
- If you have worries, don't you value me enough to share them with me? You know very well that I am yours, all yours, today, tomorrow and forever!
Without letting her ignore the difficulties of his situation, he had never gone into specific details, preferring to talk about his hopes rather than his present misery.
He repeated the story he had already told Glady and Caffié, adding what had just happened with the concierge, the wine merchant, the coalman and Joseph.
She listened in shock.
- He's taken the frock-coat!" she murmured.
- That's the only reason he came.
- And what about tomorrow?
- Ah, tomorrow... tomorrow!
- With so much work, how could you have got there?
- Like you, I believed in the virtue of work, and here I am! Because I felt in myself a will that nothing would weaken, a strength that nothing would weary, a courage that nothing would discourage, I imagined that I was armed for the fight, so that I couldn't be defeated, and I know it, as much through the fault of circumstances as through my own...
- And what are you guilty of, poor dear?
- Ignorance of life, clumsiness, presumption, blindness. If I had been less naive, would I have let myself be taken in by Jardine's proposals? Would I have accepted this furniture, this flat? He told me that the commitments he had me sign were mere formalities, that in reality I would pay him when I could, that he would be satisfied with an honest interest: this seemed plausible to me; I didn't look any further and I accepted, happy, glorious to be settling down... certain that I was strong enough to carry this burden. Self-confidence is a strength, but it's also a weakness. Because you love me, you don't know me, you don't see me. In reality, I'm not very sociable, and I'm absolutely lacking in flexibility, finesse and politeness, both in character and in manners: how, with that, do you expect to do business and be successful if you don't make a splash? I'm counting on it, but it's not time yet. Because I lack flexibility, I have not been able to win the sympathy or the interest of my masters; they only saw my stiffness, and, as I did not go to them, even more out of shyness than pride, they did not come to me, -- which is quite natural, I agree; moreover, as I did not bow my ideas to the authority of some, they took a dislike to me, which is even more natural. Because I lack politeness and have remained for many things the heavy and awkward Auvergnat that nature made me, the people of the world have disdained me, sticking to the bark they saw and which displeased them. If I had been wiser, smarter and more experienced, I would at least have been able to rely on comradeship; but I didn't care. What was the point? I didn't need it: my strength was enough for me; I thought it was better to be feared than to be loved. Thus built, I had only two choices: either to stay in my poor room at the Hôtel du Sénat, living on lessons and bookshop jobs until the day of the competitive examination for the central office and the agrégation; or to set up in an eccentric district, in Belleville, Montrouge or elsewhere, and there to build up my clientele with the strength of my hocks with people who would not ask me to be polite or well-mannered. As these parties were reasonable, I took neither one nor the other: -- Belleville because I wanted to work only with my legs, like one of my comrades whom I saw working at La Villette: "Your tongue. -- Good. -- Your arm. -- Good! And while he was supposed to be feeling his patient's pulse, with his other hand he wrote out his prescription: "Vomiting, purgative... -- It's forty sous. -- And off he goes, never wasting five minutes on his diagnosis: he hasn't got the time; -- the Hôtel du Sénat, because I'd had enough, and Jardine's proposals were tempting me. That's where he took me.
- And now what?
VIII
At that moment, the candle that lit the table went out in the torch, without its flickering light, which had been flickering for a few moments, having warned them that it was going to die. Philis stood up:
- Where are the candles?" she asked.
- There are none left; this was the last one.
- Well, all we have to do is light the fire.
She threw a small handful of wood into the hearth; then, instead of taking her chair, she went and found a cushion on the sofa and, placing it in front of the fireplace, she sat down on it, leaning on Saniel's knee.
- And now," she repeated, looking up at him.
- Now, I suppose all I have to do is run off to the Auvergne and become a country doctor.
- My God, is that possible?" she murmured in a tone that surprised Saniel; for, if there was pain in her cry, there was also another feeling that he did not understand.
- When I left the École, I could continue to live at the Hôtel du Sénat and, by giving lessons for a living, prepare for my competitive examinations; now, after having occupied a position of some prominence, can I return to this existence of a beggar student? My creditors, who have already fallen upon me here, will harass me and my competitors in the competition will exploit my misery... which has no other cause than my vices; they will find that I would dishonour the Faculty and I will be rejected. Neither a hospital doctor nor an agrégé, I'd be reduced to being a local doctor; what's the point? The test was done here; you see how well it went.
- So you'd leave?
- Not without heartbreak, not without despair, because that would mean our separation and the renunciation of the hopes on which I have lived for ten years, the abandonment of my work, death; you see now why, despite your cheerfulness, I didn't have the strength to hide my concern from you: the more charming you were, the more I felt how dear you were to me, the more I despaired of this separation.
- Why separate us?
- What is it you want?
She turned to him:
- To go with you. I want you to bear witness to the fact that up until now I have never spoken to you about marriage and never even hinted that you might one day make me your wife. In the position you found yourself in, in the struggle you were waging, a wife would have been a burden that would have paralysed you, especially when that wife was just a poor miserable creature like me, who brought only her misery and that of her family as a dowry. But conditions are no longer the same: here you are, just as miserable and even more desperate; in your country, where all you have are distant relatives who are nothing to you, since they have neither your education, nor your ideas, nor your needs, nor your habits, what are you going to become all alone with your disappointments and your regrets? If you accept me, I'll go with you; when there are two of us and we love each other, we'll never be unhappy. When you come home tired, you'll find me smiling; when you stay at home, you'll involve me in your thoughts, in your work, and I'll try to understand you. I'm not afraid of poverty, you know, and I'm not afraid of solitude either; wherever we are together, I'll be fine. All I ask is that you take my mother with us, because you know I can't abandon her; by looking after her, you've got to know her well enough to know that she's neither troublesome nor difficult; as for Florentin, he'll stay in Paris where he'll find work: his trip to America has mellowed him and his ambitions are now easy to satisfy: making a modest living is all he asks. No doubt we'll be a burden on you, but not as heavy as you might think at first sight: a woman, when she wants to, brings order and economy to a house, and I promise you that I'll be that woman. And then I'll work: I'm sure that my stationer will give me menus just as well when I'm in Auvergne as he does in Paris. I'll probably also be able to get some other work; it's a hundred francs a month, maybe a hundred and fifty, maybe even two hundred. Until you've built up a clientele, we'll live on the money; life in the Auvergne must be cheap.
She had taken both his hands and was anxiously watching the effect of his words on his face, lit by the capricious flame of the fireplace: it was their lives that were about to be decided, and the emotion that gripped her heart made her voice tremble. What was he going to say? She could see his face flustered, but she couldn't read any further.
As she remained silent, he freed both his hands and, taking her head, looked at her in silence for a few moments:
- How you love me!
- Give me some way of proving it other than in words.
- It would be cowardice to associate you with my misery.
- It would be esteeming me enough to be sure that I would be happy.
- But what about me?
- Won't love in your heart win out over pride? Don't you feel that since I've been in love with you my love has taken over my whole life, and that nothing in the world but what is him, what is you, exists in the present or in the future! Because I see you for a few hours now and then in Paris, I am happy; whatever the difficulties that await us, I will be even happier in the Auvergne, simply because we will always see each other.
He remained silent for a long time:
- Could you love me there?" he murmured.
This question, which summed up his thoughts, was obviously addressed more to him than to her.
- Oh, dear Victor!" she cried, "why do you doubt me? Have I deserved it? Don't the past and the present answer for the future?
He shook his head:
- The man you have loved, whom you love, has never shown himself to you as he really is. Despite the difficulties and sadness of his life, he was able to smile at you because, however cruel that life may have been, he was sustained by hope and confidence; in Auvergne there will be no more hope or confidence, only the rage of a shattered existence and the overwhelm of powerlessness. What kind of man would I be? Could you love him?
- A thousand times more, because he would be unhappy and I would have to support him.
- Would you have the strength? In the long run, you would grow weary, because the weight would be too heavy, no matter how great your devotion, no matter how deep your tenderness. Look at my situation, look at my hopes and, going down into the future, look at my crushing. You know I'm ambitious, but only vaguely, don't you? Without ever having measured the scope of that ambition and the hopes, the dreams, if you like, on which it rests. Understand that these dreams are about to come true: in two more months, in December or January, I will take the competitive examination for the central office, which will make me a hospital doctor, and at the same time the competitive examination for the agrégation, which will open the Faculty of Medicine to me. Without any proud illusions, I think I'm in a position to succeed -- what sports people call in condition. So when all I had left to do was wait a few days, I was defeated forever.
- Why forever?
- You come from your village to Paris to make your mark, you don't come back when bad luck or impotence have sent you back. Besides, it's only every four years that a competitive examination for the agrégation is held. In four years' time, what would my moral or intellectual condition have been like; how would I have endured this four-year exile; can you imagine what four years of isolation deep in the mountains can produce? But that's not all. In addition to this ostensible goal that I have been pursuing since I left my village, I have my work in progress, which absolutely requires Paris. Without my ever having knocked you on the head with medicine, you know, don't you? that it is on the eve of undergoing a revolution that will transform it. Until now, it has been officially taught in pathology that the human organism carries within itself the germ of a large number of infectious diseases which develop spontaneously under certain conditions: thus, tuberculosis is the result of fatigue, deprivation and physiological misery. Well, for some time now, revolutionaries have been admitting that these diseases have a parasitic origin, and there is a whole army in France, Germany and Europe looking for these parasites. I am a soldier in this army, and it is for this research that I use this laboratory set up in the dining room. It's the parasites of tuberculosis and cancer that I've been working on, and in the case of cancer, for seven years already, which, when I was an intern, led my comrades to call me "the cancer topical". As for tuberculosis, I've managed to discover its parasite, but not yet to rid it of all its impurities using cultivation techniques. That's where I'm at. In other words, I'm on fire, and perhaps tomorrow, in a few days' time, I'll have made a revolutionary discovery that will bring glory to the man who made it. In the same way, I've found the micro-organism that causes cancer. But that's not the whole story. And that's what I have to give up when I leave Paris.
- Why give up? Can't you continue your research in Auvergne?
- It's impossible for all sorts of reasons too long to explain, but one will suffice. These parasites can only be cultivated at certain temperatures that are rigorously maintained at the required level, and these temperatures can only be obtained in ovens like the one in my laboratory, fed by gas whose inlet is automatically regulated by the amount of heat in the water. How do you expect this oven to work in a country where there is no gas? No, no, if I leave Paris, it's all over: my position as well as my work; I become a village doctor and nothing but a village doctor. If the bailiffs throw me out tomorrow, everything I've accumulated over the last four years in this laboratory, all my work in progress, everything that's finished and everything that needs just a few more days, just a few more hours, will go to the junk shop or be thrown out on the street. So much effort, so many nights spent, so much hardship, so many hopes, all that remains is a memory... for me. And even if it didn't, perhaps I'd be less exasperated and accept with a less ulcerated heart the life I'll never resign myself to. You know very well that I am a rebel, not a resigner.
She stood up and took his hand, squeezing it tightly:
- You must stay in Paris," she said. Forgive me for insisting earlier on proving to you that you could live in your village. I was thinking of myself more than you, of our love, of our marriage; it was a selfish thought, a bad thought. You have to look for a way, any way, whatever it may cost, not to give up your work.
- We must! But how? Do you think I haven't exhausted everything?
He recounted his approaches to Jardine, his requests, his prayers and also his request for a loan from Glady, and finally his visit to Caffié.
- Caffié!" she exclaimed, "how did you get the idea of approaching Caffié?
- Because you'd often spoken to me about him.
- But I spoke of him as the hardest and meanest of men, capable of anything except what is good and what is right.
- And also because I knew from one of my clients that he lent money to those he could exploit.
- And he told you?
- That he would probably not find anyone to grant the loan I wanted; however, he promised to look into it, and he should give me an answer tomorrow evening; he also promised to defend me against Jardine.
- You've put yourself in his hands!
- What do you want? In my position, I don't have the freedom to turn to anyone who I want and who inspires confidence in me because he's honourable. If I go to a notary or a banker, they won't listen to me, because at the first word I'll be obliged to tell them that I have no pledge or guarantee to offer. That's why unfortunate people fall prey to scoundrels; at least those scoundrels listen to them and give them something, no matter how small.
- What did he give you?
- His advice.
- And did you accept it?
- That's always time saved. Maybe tomorrow I'll be out on the street: Caffié will get me some respite.
- And what price will you pay for this defence?
- Only those who have something worry about the price.
- You have your name, your rest, your dignity, your honour, and once you're in Caffié's hands, who knows what he'll demand of you, what he'll force you to do that you can't resist!
- So you want me to leave Paris?
- No, of course not; but I do want you to be on your guard against Caffié, whom you don't know and whom I know from everything Florentin told us when he was at home. No matter how secretive he is, a businessman can't hide from his clerk: Caffié is guilty not only of dirty tricks, but also of real crimes; I can assure you that he deserves to be killed ten times over. To earn a hundred francs he's capable of anything: he has to win, he has to amass, just for the pleasure of amassing, since he has no children, no parents, no heirs.
- Well, I promise to be on my guard, as you advise me; but, however rascal Caffié may be, I think I must accept the help he has offered me. Who knows what might happen during the time he will buy me? Because I don't have to tell you, do I, that I know in advance his answer to the loan I asked him for: he won't have found anyone.
- I'll still come tomorrow evening to find out his answer.
IX
Although Saniel had no illusions about Caffié's reply, he went the next day, at the same time as the day before, to ring the businessman's doorbell.
As on the previous day, he had to wait quite a long time for the door to open; finally he heard a shuffling step on the pavement.
- Who's there?" asked Caffié.
As soon as Saniel answered, the bolt was pulled.
- As I don't like to be disturbed in the evening by intruders," said Caffié, "I don't always open; but I have a signal for my customers that allows me to recognise them: after ringing the bell, you strike your finger against the wood of the door with three equally-spaced knocks.
During this explanation, Saniel had entered the businessman's office.
- Have you taken care of my request?" he said after a moment's delay, as Caffié seemed determined not to initiate the discussion first.
- Yes, my dear sir, I've been running all morning on your behalf; I never neglect my clients, their business is my business.
He paused for a moment.
- Well?" asked Saniel.
Caffié gave his face a desolate expression.
- What did I tell you, my dear sir, please remember? An experience like mine does not speak lightly, do me the honour of believing that. Well, what I predicted has come true: everywhere the same response: the risk is too great; nobody wants to take the chance.
- Even for a large stake?
- Even for a big interest: there is so much competition in your profession. I believe in your future and I proved it to you with my proposal; but, unfortunately, I am only the intermediary and not the provider of funds.
Caffié insisted on the word 'my proposal' and emphasised it with his eyes; but Saniel did not seem to understand.
- What about the upholsterer's summons?
- Your landlord, who is owed a term, will intervene, and your creditor will have to pay him off before going any further. Will he resign himself to this? That remains to be seen. If so, we'll defend ourselves on another ground. I'm not saying victoriously, but in order to gain time.
- How much time?
- That, my dear sir, I cannot know: it depends on our adversary and his advice. And what do you mean by 'how much time'?
- I mean until April.
- So it is eternity. So do you think you'll be able to free yourself in April? If you have that hope - based on guarantees - you have to say so, my dear sir.
This question was asked in a very benevolent tone, which Saniel accepted.
- I have no such guarantees," he said; "but, on the other hand, it would be of the utmost importance to me if the matter were to drag on any longer. As I explained to you, I'm on the eve of taking two competitive exams; they last three months; and in March, or April at the latest, I can be a hospital doctor and an associate of the Faculty. If that's the case, I'll then offer a surface to the lenders, which will no doubt enable you to find me the sum I need to pay Jardine and the expenses that will have been incurred, including your fees.
As he spoke, Saniel realised that he was wrong to give himself up in this way, but he went through with it.
- I would be unworthy of your trust, my dear sir," replied Caffié, "if I kept you thinking that we could win this time. Whatever it costs me -- and it costs me a lot, I assure you -- I must tell you that it is impossible, radically impossible: a few days, yes, perhaps a few weeks, but that's all.
- Well, get me those few weeks," said Saniel, rising to his feet, "that will always be something.
- And after that?
- Until then, we'll see.
- My dear sir, don't go; you wouldn't believe how deeply your position touches me; I'm only thinking of you. When I saw that I really couldn't find the money you needed, I went to pay a friendly visit to my young client, the one I told you about...
- Who has received a higher education in a fashionable convent?
- Precisely; and I asked her what she would think of a young doctor with a promising future, a future professor at the Faculty, already considered to be a first-rate scientist, handsome -- for you are handsome, my dear sir, it is not flattery to say so -- in good health, a peasant by birth, who would present himself as her husband. She seemed flattered, I can tell you frankly. But she immediately said to me: "And the little one? To which I replied that I knew you to be too great, too noble, too generous not to have that indulgence of superior men which makes them accept with serenity an involuntary fault. Have I gone too far?
He didn't wait for an answer:
- No, wasn't it? In fact, the little one was there, because the mother watches over him with a solicitude full of promise for the future, and I was able to examine him at my ease. Very fragile, my dear sir; he takes after his father, the poor baby, and I doubt that in spite of all your knowledge as a doctor you will be able to make him live: if by misfortune his death should occur, as is certainly to be feared, it will not harm your reputation: you give care, don't you, not life!
- Speaking of care," interrupted Saniel, who didn't want to answer, "have you done what I advised you to do?
- No, I haven't yet. The pharmacists in this district are notorious cut-throats; but this evening I'm going to see one of my clients, a pharmacist in Les Batignolles, who will treat me as a friend.
- I'll see you then.
- Whenever you like, my dear sir, when you've thought it over; now you have the password.
Before leaving home, Saniel had left his key with his concierge so that Philis wouldn't be waiting for him in the street if she came in his absence; when he returned, the concierge told him that 'madame' had already been upstairs long enough, and when he rang the bell, it was she who quickly opened the door.
- Well?" she asked in a trembling voice before he had even entered.
- What I told you yesterday: he hasn't found anyone.
She squeezed him in a long, passionate embrace.
- What about the upholsterer?
- He promised to save time.
As they spoke, they entered the study: the fire was burning in the fireplace, and it wasn't pieces of plank that were blazing, as the day before, but logs of hornbeam; on the table, lit by two candles, was a beautiful roast chicken, surrounded by watercress, and a bottle of red wine stood opposite the carafe of water.
He looked at her surprised.
- I've set the table," she said, "you see, I'm dining with you.
And throwing herself into his arms:
- Knowing Caffié better than you, I guessed his answer, and I didn't want you to be alone when you came back here: I've found another excuse not to have dinner with Mum.
- But what about the chicken?
- We needed a main course.
- This wood, these candles?
- That's the end of my savings; I would have been so happy if they had been less miserable and could have been used for something useful.
As on the previous evening, they sat down in front of the fire, and she immediately began to talk about various things to occupy and distract him: but what their lips did not say, their eyes, when they met, expressed with more intensity than words; nevertheless, until the end of dinner, they were both able to say nothing decisive.
It was he who, at one point, betrayed his preoccupation.
- Your brother had taken a good look at Caffié," he said, as if talking to himself.
- Didn't he?
- He's certainly the most perfect rascal I've ever met.
- I'm sure he's offered you some sort of infamy?
- He proposed marriage.
- I had a premonition of it.
- And that's why he refused me the loan I asked for. I had the simplicity to explain my situation to him frankly; at the same time, I told him how important it was for me to win the month of April, and he hopes that, under the weight of the lawsuits, when I see that I'm going to be put out on the street, I'll accept one of the two women he's offering me: with the knife to my throat, I'll have to give in; it's to keep him hanging on that he promised to delay the Jardine lawsuits and drag them out.
- And those women?" she asked, not daring to look him in the face.
- Don't worry, you have nothing to fear from them: one is a drunken butcher, the other is a young girl with a child.
- And these are the women he dares to propose to a man like you!
- His proposals are not as naked as I present them to you; they are cooked up in a sauce that, according to his feelings, should make them pass. If I don't cure the butcheress of her drunkenness, all I have to do is abandon her to her vice, which will take her in a short time, and, as the contract will be settled with this eventuality in mind, I will find myself the heir to her twenty thousand pounds of income. For the virgin with the child, the combination is different: this child has been endowed by his real father with two hundred thousand francs, and whoever legitimises him by marrying the mother will have the right to the income from these two hundred thousand francs until the child comes of age... if, however, he comes of age, because he is very fragile, so fragile even that, if his death were to occur, it would in no way harm my reputation as a doctor.
- You see what a monster he is!
- While he was explaining his schemes to me, offering me the death of others, I was thinking about his own, and telling myself that if he were killed, he would really only get what he deserved.
- That's the truth.
- For me, there was a time when nothing could have been easier. As he had a toothache, he showed me his jaw: all I had to do was strangle him; we were alone: a miserable diabetic like him who, I'm sure, doesn't have six months to live, wouldn't have resisted a grip like that. I took the keys out of his waistcoat, opened his cash register and took the thirty, forty, sixty thousand francs I saw piled up there. The devil if the law ever found out: a doctor doesn't strangle his clients, he poisons them, he kills them scientifically, not brutally.
- That's the trouble, it's that these ways of sorting things out are only available to people who have no conscience, and that they don't exist for us.
- I can assure you that it wasn't conscience that held me back.
- Fear of remorse, if I use the wrong word.
- But intelligent people have no remorse, my dear child, because with them reasoning precedes action and does not follow it: before acting, they weigh up the pros and cons, and know what the consequences of their actions will be for others as well as for themselves; if this preliminary examination proves to them that for whatever reason they can act, they will be at peace forever, assured of not being exposed to remorse, which is only the reproach of conscience.
- Undoubtedly, what you are saying is right, and yet it is impossible for me to accept it. Although I have not committed any crimes in my life, I have nevertheless done foolish things, even made mistakes, and some of them were deliberate, after the preliminary examination of which you speak: I should therefore have been perfectly at peace and sheltered from the reproaches of my conscience; nevertheless, the next morning I woke up unhappy, tormented, sometimes upset, without being able to stifle the mysterious voice that was accusing me.
- And in whose name was it speaking, this voice that was even more vague than mysterious?
- In the name of my conscience, obviously.
- Obviously' is too much, and you would be very embarrassed to show me the obvious, since nothing is more uncertain and elusive than what we call conscience, which is really just a matter of environment and upbringing.
- I don't understand.
- Does your conscience punish you for loving me?
- No, of course not.
- You see, then, that you have a personal way of understanding what is right and what is wrong that is not the one followed in our country, where it is accepted, both religiously and socially, that a young girl is guilty when she has a lover. Consequently, you can also see that conscience is a poor weighing instrument, since everyone uses weights of their own making to make it work.
- In any case, you did well not to strangle Caffié...
- You yourself, however, condemned him to death!
- By the hand of providential or human justice, but not by yours, any more than by Florentin's or mine, even though we know better than anyone that he deserves no mercy.
- You can see that I anticipated your objections, since I didn't tighten his tie.
- Fortunately.
- Is 'fortunately' the right word?
X
That evening Philis had to go home early, so dinner didn't go on as late as the previous evening; however, before leaving, she wanted to clear the table and put everything back in order.
- You'll be able to have your lunch tomorrow with the rest of the chicken," she said, shoving it into the larder, where it joined the tin of sardines and the terrine of foie gras.
And as he accompanied her, a torch in his hand to light her way, he could see that it wasn't just her lunch for the next day and the days to come that she had in mind: in the kitchen, a supply of wood occupied one corner; on a shelf were two packs of candles, and on the rabbits' chests was piled a supply of carrots sufficient to feed them and the guinea pigs for several days.
- What a brave little heart you have!
- Because I'm thinking about rabbits?
- For your tenderness and discretion.
- I wish I could be good for something!
When she had left him, he immediately sat down at his desk and started work, in a hurry to regain the time he had just given to sentiment. The fact that his work was to be for nothing, and that his experiments were to be abruptly interrupted the next day or a few days later, did not stop him: he had to work, and he worked as if he had the certainty of passing his competitions, and also the certainty that the experiments he had been carrying out for several years would be completed without anyone being able to disturb them.
In the street, he could think of Philis, be hungry, feel the weight of sleep; at his desk, there was no Philis, no hunger, no sleep, no worries, no memories, just his work, which took up all his time.
It was his strength and also his pride, the only superiority he could boast; for, although he recognised others, he never spoke of them, while he readily said to his comrades:
- Me, I work when I want and as long as I want; my will applied to work has never failed; what they say about Alexander the Great who, to stay awake at night, held a metal ball in his hand over a bronze basin, simply proves that the Macedonian was a softy.
That evening, for an hour, it was more or less as it always was: neither the bailiffs, nor Jardine, nor Caffié disturbed him; however, having had some research to do, he noticed that his memory did not obey him as usual: it hesitated, got confused, above all it had truly astonishing distractions.
He was definitely not in a normal state and his will was obeying instead of commanding.
There was a name and a phrase that he repeated to himself from time to time mechanically; the name was Caffié's; the phrase was "Nothing could be easier".
Why did this hypothesis of strangling Caffié, which he had only mentioned in passing and without attaching any importance to it at the time he had uttered it, come back to him like a sort of obsession?
Wasn't that strange?
Until now he had never thought he could strangle a man, no matter how rascal he was, and now, while chatting, he had come up with reasons that made the death of this rascal quite natural and even legitimate.
Hadn't Philis herself condemned him?
To be sure, she had added that Providence or Justice should carry out the execution, but that was the scruple of a naive conscience formed in an environment not influenced by him.
Did he have such scruples, the old businessman who, coldly, in the sole interest of a percent on a dowry, advised killing a woman through drunkenness, and a child anyhow?
With him, the game was really played by two and by the strongest of the two.
As he came to this conclusion, he paused, wondering if he was mad to follow such an idea; then immediately, to chase it away, he went back to work, which absorbed him for some time, though not as long as the first time.
Then, once again, his will escaping him, he began to think about Caffié again.
It was only too obvious that, if he had realised the idea of strangling Caffié, all the difficulties he was struggling against and which were going to crush him, if not the next day, at least in a few days' time, would have been immediately ironed out.
No more bailiffs, no more creditors! What a relief!
Rest, the possibility of taking his competitive exams with a calm mind that would not be troubled by the fever of material worries: under these conditions, his success was assured, he felt.
And his experiments! He no longer ran the risk of seeing them suddenly interrupted, his preparations were not thrown into the street, his culture tubes were not broken, his matras and balloons did not go to the junk shop; he continued his research, it led to the results he was after: for him, glory; for humanity, the cure of one of the most terrible diseases that mow it down, and perhaps of two!
So the question was quite simple:
On the one hand, Caffié;
On the other, humanity and science;
An old rascal, who had deserved to die twenty times over, and who was, moreover, going to die naturally in the near future;
And humanity and science, which would benefit from a discovery he had made.
He noticed that sweat was beading on his hands and trickling down his neck.
Why did he faint? Was it out of horror at the crime he was admitting was possible? Or out of fear of seeing his experiments destroyed?
He needed to think, to realise, to observe himself.
He had told Philis that intelligent people, before committing themselves to an action, weigh up the pros and cons.
He could see nothing against Caffié's death.
On the contrary, everything came together.
If he had had Philis' scruples or Brigard's beliefs, he would only have had to stop.
But, not having them, wouldn't he be naive to back down?
What would he back down from? Why would he stop?
Remorse? But he was convinced that intelligent people don't feel remorse when they've made up their minds with full knowledge of the facts: they feel remorse before they've made up their minds, not afterwards.
Fear of getting caught? But intelligent people don't get caught. The ones who get lost are either the brutes who go straight ahead, or the half-intelligent who put all their skill and finesse into combining a complicated or romantic action in which their hand is found. He was a man of science and precision, and he would compromise himself neither by the act nor by sentiment: nothing to fear during, nothing to fear after. If Caffié were strangled, suspicion would fall not on a doctor, but on a brute; when doctors want to kill someone, they do it skilfully, using poison or any other scientific method; brutes do it brutally; murder tells you the murderer's profession.
A few moments earlier, he had broken out in a cold sweat.
He rose nervously to his feet and began to walk jerkily around his study. The fire had been out for a long time already; outside the noise of the street had ceased, and in his brain resounded the word he was uttering: murderer!
Was he a man to let himself be influenced and stopped by a word? Where are the rich, the upwardly mobile, the upwardly mobile who have not left corpses behind them along the way? Success carries them along, and they were only successful because they had the strength.
Certainly violence was not a recreation, and it would be more pleasant to make one's way quietly, by the sheer power of intelligence and hard work, than to open it up with fists; but one does not choose this path, one is thrown into it by circumstances, by the fatalities of life, and whoever wants to reach the end has no choice of means; if one has to walk in the mud, what does it matter, when one knows that one will not soil oneself?
If Caffié had had heirs, poor people saved from poverty by this fortune on which they were counting, he would no doubt have allowed himself to be touched by this consideration: thief, the word was even nastier than that of murderer; but who would miss the few banknotes he took from this cash box? To steal is to wrong someone. Who would he harm? He couldn't see. But he could clearly see the army of afflicted people he would be helping.
A timid ring of the doorbell startled him, and he felt angry at feeling so nervous, he who was usually in control of his mind as well as his body.
He went to open the door: a man dressed as a workman greeted him humbly.
- I beg your pardon for disturbing you, doctor.
- What's on your mind?
- It's about my wife. I'll come and get you if you'd like to come.
- What about her?
- The midwife is no longer there; she wants a doctor.
- Did the midwife advise you to come and get me?
- No, doctor; she sent me to Mr Legrand.
- Did she?
- His wife told me that he couldn't get out of bed because of his bronchitis. So the pharmacist gave me your address.
- That's fine.
- I'm going to tell you, Mr Doctor, I'm an honest man; we're not rich, we won't be able to pay you... straight away.
- I'll be off. Wait for me.
Saniel picked up his tools and followed the workman, who explained to him what his wife was going through on the way.
- Where are we going?" asked Saniel, interrupting these explanations.
- Rue de la Corderie.
It was behind the marché Saint-Honoré, on the sixth floor, under the roofs, in a room that was clean despite its poverty. When Saniel entered, the midwife came to meet him, and stopping him, she said in a low voice, sententiously:
- This is a case of dystocia due to a malformation of the pelvis.
- Is the child alive?
- Yes, it's alive.
- All right, we'll see.
He approached the bed and took a long look at the patient, who kept repeating:
- I'm going to die! Save me, doctor.
- We'll certainly save you," he said softly, "I promise.
He stood up and took off his frock coat, then his waistcoat.
- Give me an apron," he said to the midwife, rolling up the sleeves of his shirt.
She brought him the apron and, as he tied it under his arms :
- Well?" she asked.
- All we have to do is kill the child to save the mother; we've waited too long.
- You're going to perform an embryotomy!
- I'm not going to bother.
The operation was long, difficult and painful, and after it was over, Saniel stayed with the patient for a long time; when he went down to the street, five o'clock was striking on a clock, and the market square was already buzzing with activity.
But in the streets, returning to the silence and solitude of the night, he began to reflect: he had not hesitated to kill this child, who had perhaps sixty or eighty years of happy life ahead of him, and he stopped at the death of Caffié, who had only a miserable existence of a few days. The interests of a poor, debilitated, stunted woman had made up his mind; his own interests and those of humanity left him perplexed, irresolute, weak and a coward. What a contradiction!
He was walking with his eyes downcast; just then, on the pavement in front of him, he saw an object shining under the flickering gas; he approached it. It was a butcher's knife, which a boy going to the abattoir or coming to the market had lost.
He hesitated for a moment whether to pick it up or leave it there: then, looking around and seeing no one in the deserted street, hearing no sound of footsteps in the silence, he bent down quickly and picked it up.
Caffié's fate was decided.
XI
When, after two hours' sleep, Saniel awoke, he did not at first think of the knife: he was weary and his confused thoughts remained numb; he moved about the room mechanically, without realising what he was doing, as if he were sleepwalking; and this surprised him, for he never felt tired in mind or body, no matter how little he slept.
But all of a sudden, when his eyes met the knife that he had placed on the marble mantelpiece on his way home, he felt a shock that shook off his numbness and fatigue: it was like a bolt of lightning that dazzled him.
He picked it up and, approaching the window, examined it in the pale light of the new day; it was a solid instrument which, in a firm hand, would be a terrible weapon: newly sharpened, it had the edge of a razor.
Then the idea, the vision he had had two hours before, came back to him clear and complete as it had been: At dusk, in other words when the concierge was in the second block of the building, he would go up to Caffié's, without being seen, and, with this knife, he would cut his throat; it was as simple as it was easy, and this knife left next to the corpse, as well as the nature of the wound, told the police that they should look for a butcher or, at least, a man used to using a knife of this kind. 
When he had discussed Caffié's death the day before, both the time and the manner of the execution had remained vague; but now the day and the means had been specified: it would be with this knife and this very evening.
This shook him out of his torpor and sent a shiver down his spine.
But he was angry at this weakness: did he or did he not know what he wanted? Irresolute or cowardly?
Then, jumping from one idea to another, he thought of an observation he had made which seemed to prove that in many subjects there was less firmness in the morning than in the evening. Was this a result of the dualism of the nervous centres, and was the human personality as dual as the brain? Were there hours when the right hemisphere was the master of our wills; were there others when it was the left; did one of these hemispheres possess special qualities that the other did not, and according to whether it was this one or that one that came into activity, did we have this character or that temperament? That would be curious and would be like saying that you can be a sheep in the morning and a tiger in the evening. In his case, it was a sheep that woke up and a tiger that devoured it during the day. To which hemisphere did either of these personalities belong?
It was Caffié he had to deal with and the plan which, in the street, before he had decided to pick up the knife, had instantly taken shape in his mind.
Obviously, things were neither as simple nor as easy as he had first seen them, and for his plan to succeed, a whole series of circumstances would have to come together, which might very well not.
Would the concierge not see him pass? Would anyone go up or down the stairs? Would he be alone? Would he open the door? Wouldn't the doorbell ring when they were locked in together?
There was a whole series of questions which had not at first presented themselves to his mind, but which now imposed themselves.
He had to examine them, weigh them up, and not throw himself headlong into an adventure that could present such hazards.
Fortunately, he had the whole day to himself and, since in his agitated state there was no time to think about work, he would give it over to this examination: the stakes were worth it: his honour and his life.
As soon as he was dressed, he went out and headed straight ahead through the streets whose pavements were already clogged with the morning's hustle and bustle.
It was only when he had left the centre of Paris that he could think as he wished, that is, without being disturbed at every moment by people in a hurry whom he had to avoid or by newspaper readers who, not looking ahead, pounced on him.
Obviously the risks were more serious than he had imagined, and as he saw them taking shape, he wondered whether he should go any further. Removing Caffié was fine; getting caught was not.
Then he was surprised to find that he felt no disappointment; on the contrary, a sort of appeasement was building up inside him.
- If it's impossible...
He wasn't the sort of man to go madly after the impossible: he would have had a dream... a bad dream, and that would have been that.
He stopped and, after a moment's hesitation, turned on his heels and turned back: what was the point of going any further? He didn't have to think about it any more, or weigh up the pros and cons; he had to abandon this plan, which was decidedly too dangerous.
But he had hardly taken a few steps to get back to his house when he wondered whether the dangers were really as he had just glimpsed them, and whether he was really faced with a radically demonstrated impossibility.
Without doubt, the concierge might notice him when he passed her dressing room, either on his way up or on his way down; but she might also not notice him at all: that, in reality, depended a great deal on him, and on the way he proceeded.
Every evening, this old caretaker with ankylosis in her back had to turn on the gas in two buildings, the one on the street and the one in the courtyard. She started with the one on the street and, with the discomfort she felt walking, it took her a long time to climb up the five flights of stairs and back down again. Let them watch from the street for the moment when, at dusk, she came out of her dressing room, her rat-de-cave lit in her hand; let them immediately climb the stairs behind her, but a little way behind and without joining her, so as to be on the landing of the first floor when she herself arrived on that of the second, and they would have plenty of time, the matter done, to get back to the street before she returned to her dressing room after lighting the gas on her two staircases. It was a question of proceeding regularly, methodically, without hurrying, but also without lingering.
Was this impossible?
This was precisely the delicate point, the one that had to be examined with composure, without allowing himself to be influenced by any consideration other than that which derived from the fact itself.
He had therefore been wrong not to continue on his way, and the best thing was surely to get out of Paris: in the countryside, in the fields or woods, he would find the calm that was indispensable to his overexcited brain, in which ideas were crashing like the waves of a stormy sea.
At the moment he was in the middle of the Faubourg Saint-Honoré: he took a street that would lead him to the Champs-Élysées, which at this early hour were deserted.
For a long time he considered all the possibilities that might arise, and he came to the conviction that what had seemed impossible to him was in no way so: if he kept calm, if he did not lose track of the time that had passed, he could very well escape from the concierge, -- which was the most important point.
In truth, once the danger of the concierge had been averted, all was not said; there remained the danger of being met on the staircase by one of the tenants of the house; there also remained the bad luck of not finding Caffié at home, or that he was not alone, or that a ringing bell stopped his hand at the decisive moment; but, by the very fact that they depended solely on chance, these circumstances could not be decided in advance: If one of them came true, he would have to wait until the next day, which would be another day of agitation. 
But one question that had to be decided in advance, because it was bound to present itself with serious dangers, was how he would justify the arrival in his hands of a sum of money which, providentially and at the right moment, would relieve him of the embarrassments he was struggling with: "You've paid your debts, that's perfect; with what? You were destitute, desperate, drowning; where did you find the money that miraculously saved you?
He had reached the Bois de Boulogne; he continued on ahead; but as he passed a fountain, the splashing of which attracted his attention, he stopped: although the weather was damp and cold under the influence of a strong west wind laden with rain, his tongue was parched: he drank two goblets of water, then resumed his walk, without worrying about where it would take him.
He came up with a scheme that seemed ingenious at the time: he had gone to Caffié's to borrow three thousand francs; why shouldn't Caffié have lent them to him, if not on the first day, at least on the second? It would have been with this loan that he would have paid his debts, if he were ever questioned on this point; to prove this loan, all he had to do was sign a note which he would place in the cash register and which would be found there. Isn't the first duty of anyone who has signed an undertaking of this kind to take it back when the opportunity arises? If he didn't seize the opportunity and make the note disappear, that would be proof that he hadn't opened the box.
Among other advantages, this arrangement eliminated theft: it was now just a loan; later he would return the three thousand francs to Caffié's heirs; too bad for Caffié if it was a forced loan!
On his way back to Paris, he would buy a sheet of stamped paper, and as he had paid for a visit the day before, this expenditure was possible.
On arriving at Saint-Cloud, he went into a cabaret and had a piece of bread served with cheese and wine; but although he drank a lot, he could only eat a little, as his tight throat refused to swallow the bread.
He resumed his route and took to the clay paths that run along the slopes of Mont Valérien; but he was impervious to the discomfort and fatigue of slipping on soggy ground, and he always went at random, having no other concern than not to stray too far from the Seine, so as to be able to return to Paris before nightfall.
Ever since he'd come up with the ticket, he'd enjoyed it; but after examining it and turning it over, he realised that it wasn't as ingenious as he'd thought, and that it could even be his undoing. Don't stamp-paper dealers often number their paper? With this number, it would be possible to trace the sale of the sheet on which the note was written back to the person who had bought it. And then, was it likely that a meticulous businessman like Caffié would not write down the loans he granted in a notebook or a book, and would the absence of this entry, when a note was found, not be enough of a clue to arouse suspicion and lead people to it?
Decidedly, he was escaping one danger only to fall into another: there were pitfalls everywhere.
He was discouraged, but not to the point of giving up. His mistake had been to imagine that carrying out the idea that had occurred to him when he picked up the knife was as simple as it was easy: but, complicated and perilous as it was, it was not impossible: who doesn't have his dangers?
Ultimately, the question was whether he had the strength within him to face up to these dangers, and there was no room for hesitation on this point: wanting to foresee everything in advance was madness; what he hadn't thought of would happen.
He returned to Paris and, via the Suresnes bridge, entered the Bois de Boulogne; as it was not yet three o'clock, he had plenty of time to reach the Rue Sainte-Anne before dark; but, on the way, a shower forced him to take shelter under a mushroom tree: and he stayed there for quite a long time watching the rain fall, wondering if this chance he hadn't foreseen was going to disrupt his plan, at least for this very evening: a man who had received this downpour couldn't walk down the street in front of Caffié's door without attracting the attention of passers-by, and the important thing was that he didn't attract anyone's attention. 
At last the rain stopped, and at four forty he arrived home: he had fifteen or twenty minutes of daylight left, which was more than he needed.
He stuck the tip of the knife into a cork and, having placed it between the folded leaves of a newspaper in the inside pocket of the left side of his frock coat, he left.
XII
When he arrived at Caffié's door, it was not yet completely dark, and although the gas in the shops was already blazing, the gas in the street lanterns had not yet been lit.
The best and safest thing for him to do would have been to park in front of the carriage entrance on the opposite side; from there he could watch out for the concierge, who could not have left her lodge without him seeing her. But even though there weren't many passers-by at the time, they might have noticed him: just opposite the carriage entrance was a small café, whose gas-bright front would have put him in too much light. So he continued on his way, but slowly and almost immediately retraced his steps.
All irresolution and hesitation had disappeared, and the only point on which he still wondered was the state in which he found himself at that very moment: he felt firm, and his pulse, he was sure, was beating regularly: he was as he had imagined he would be; experience confirmed his predictions: his hand would not tremble any more than his will.
As he passed in front of the house again, he saw the concierge slowly leave her dressing room and carefully lock her door, putting the key in her pocket; in her left hand she was holding something whitish that in the darkness he could hardly make out, but which must have been a rat-de-cave, which she had not lit for fear, no doubt, that the wind rushing through the arch of the carriage entrance would extinguish it.
This was a favourable circumstance which gave him one or two minutes more than he had counted on, since on the stairs she would be obliged to strike matches to light her rat-de-cave; and, in the execution of his plan, two minutes, even one minute, could be of decisive importance.
With shuffling steps and a hunched back, she disappeared through the staircase vestibule; he then continued on his way as a mere passer-by, so that she would have time to ascend to the first floor; then, turning on himself, he came back to the porte-cochère and passed through it briskly: In the light of the gas burner in the vestibule, he saw on his watch, which he was holding in his hand, that it was fourteen minutes past five; if his calculations were correct, he would have to pass the dressing room at twenty-four or twenty-five minutes past five, which must have been empty at the time. 
Above him he heard the heavy footsteps of the concierge on the staircase; she had just lit the first-floor burner and was continuing her ascent slowly. With quick but light steps, he climbed up behind her and, on reaching Caffié's door, he rang the bell, taking care that it was neither too rudely nor too timidly; then he knocked with equally spaced knocks, as he had been instructed.
Was Caffié alone?
Up to this point, everything had gone smoothly: no one in the hallway, no one on the stairs; luck was on his side; would it accompany him to the end?
While he waited at the door, asking himself this question, an idea crossed his mind: he would make one last attempt; if Caffié agreed to the loan, he would save himself; if he refused, he would condemn himself.
After a few seconds, which seemed as long as hours, his alert ear picked up noises which announced that Caffié was at home: a scrape of wood on the floor said that a seat had been pushed back; heavy footsteps trailed, then the bolt creaked and the door opened cautiously.
- Ah! it's you, my dear sir!" said Caffié in surprise.
Saniel had entered quickly and had closed the door himself, pressing it firmly.
- Have we got any news?" asked Caffié, passing from the entrance into his study.
- No," replied Saniel.
- Well, then?" asked Caffié, taking a seat in his armchair in front of his desk, which was lit by a lamp. "So you've come for my young person; this eagerness is a good omen.
- No, it's not about this young person that I want to talk to you...
- I'm sorry.
Saniel had pulled out his watch as he sat down opposite Caffié; two minutes had passed since he had left the vestibule; he had to hurry... For fear of not realising how much time had passed, he kept his watch in his hand.
- Are you in a hurry?
- Yes, and I'll come straight to the point: it's about me, my position, and I want to make a final appeal to you. We have to put our cards on the table. You think, no doubt, that driven by my distress and seeing that I am going to be lost forever, I will decide to accept this marriage that would save me?
- Can you imagine that, my dear sir?" exclaimed Caffié.
But Saniel stopped him:
- The calculation is too natural for you not to have made it. Well, I have to tell you that it's wrong: I would never lend myself to such a deal. I absolutely need these three thousand francs, and I'll pay whatever price you decide.
- I couldn't find a landlord, my dear sir; I was very sorry about that, I can assure you; but what do you want?
- That you make an effort yourself.
- Me! My dear sir?
- I'm talking to you.
- But I don't have any cash!
- I'm making a desperate appeal. I understand that long experience of business has made you insensitive to the misery you see every day...
- Insensitive! Please tell me that they are heartbreaking, my dear sir.
- ... But won't you let yourself be touched by the plight of a young, intelligent, courageous man, who is going to drown for want of a hand stretched out to him? For you, this help that I am asking you for so insistently is nothing...
- Three thousand francs is nothing! My, how you go on!
- For me, if you refuse, it's death.
Saniel had begun speaking with his eyes fixed on the hands of his watch, but soon, carried away by the fever of the situation, he had raised them to look at Caffié and see the effect he was having on him.
Caffié's study was a room longer than it was wide, with a high window overlooking the courtyard; having only been in the study at night, he had not noticed that the window was not closed with shutters or curtains, either muslin or drapery: the glazing was quite simple. In truth, two large woollen damask curtains hung on either side of the window, but they were not drawn. Talking to Caffié, who was standing between him and the window, Saniel had suddenly realised that on the other side of the courtyard, in the second part of the building, on the second floor, there were two lighted windows facing the study, and that from there you could dive into the study and see everything that was going on. How could he carry out his plan in front of the people he saw coming and going in this room? It would surely mean getting lost. In any case, he was risking an adventure so risky that he would have had to be mad to attempt it, and he was not mad; he had never even felt so in control of his mind and his nerves.
So it was no longer to save Caffié's life that he was pleading, it was to save himself by snatching this loan.
- With great regret, I can only repeat what I have already told you, my dear sir: no cash!
And he clutched his jaw, whimpering as if this refusal was reviving his toothache.
Saniel had got up: obviously all he had to do was leave: it was over and, instead of despairing, he felt a kind of relief.
But as he was about to head for the entrance, a flash of lightning struck him.
He looked quickly at his watch, which he had not consulted for some time; it read twenty past five: he therefore had four minutes left, five at the most.
- Why don't you close those big curtains?" he said; "I'm sure that a lot of your pain is caused by the wind blowing in through that badly closed window.
- Do you think so?
- I'm sure of it; you need warmth for your head and you must avoid draughts.
Passing behind Caffié's back, he went to the window to draw the curtains, but the cords resisted.
- It's been years since they were closed," said Caffié; "no doubt the cords are tangled. I'll give you some light.
And, taking the lamp, he came to the window, holding it high to illuminate the cords.
In the twinkling of an eye, Saniel had untangled the cords, and the curtains he drew slid back on their rods, just about closing the window.
- It's true that there was a lot of wind coming through this window," said Caffié; "thank you, my dear doctor.
All this had been done with a feverish rapidity that astonished Caffié.
- You're definitely in a hurry? he said.
- Yes, I'm in a great hurry.
He looked at his watch.
- But I still have time to give you a consultation if you wish.
- I wouldn't want to take advantage...
- You're not taking advantage.
- Yes, I am!
Sit down in your armchair and show me your mouth.
While Caffié sat down, Saniel continued in a vibrant voice:
- You see, I do good for evil.
- What do you mean, my dear sir?
- You refuse me a service that could have saved my life, and I give you a consultation; it's true that it's the last one.
- And why the last, my dear sir?
- Because death is between us.
- Death!
- Can't you see it?
- No, I can't.
- I see it.
- You mustn't have such ideas, my dear sir; you don't die because you can't pay three thousand francs.
The armchair in which Caffié had taken his seat was an old voltaire with a sloping back, and he was standing in it upside down: as he was wearing shirt collars that were too wide since he lost weight, and narrow ties that were barely knotted, he was stretching his throat as much as his jaw.
Saniel, behind the armchair, had drawn the knife with his right hand, while with his left he pressed firmly on Caffié's forehead, and with one powerful stroke, as quick as lightning, he had cut the larynx below the glottis, as well as the two carotid arteries with the jugular veins; From this terrible wound, a large jet of blood had escaped and crashed through the cabinet against the front door; not a cry had been able to escape from the trachea, which had been cut clean through, and in his armchair Caffié was shaken by general convulsions that shook his whole body, legs and arms. 
Coming out from behind the armchair, Saniel, who had thrown the knife on the floor, was watching him with his watch in his hand, counting the beats of the seconds hand and writing them down half aloud: one, two, three...
At ninety, the convulsions stopped. It was twenty-three minutes past five: now it was important to hurry and not waste a second.
The blood, having gushed out in a thick stream, had run down the body and wetted the pocket of the waistcoat in which the key to the cash register must have been; but blood does not have the same effect on a doctor or a butcher as it does on those who are not used to the sight, smell and feel of it: Despite the tepid pool in which it bathed, Saniel took the key, and after wiping his hand on one of the flaps of Caffié's frock coat, he inserted it in the lock. 
Would the bolt work freely, or was the mechanism locked by a combination? It was a poignant question... The key turned and the door opened. On a shelf and in a begging bowl were the bundles of banknotes and rolls of gold that he had seen the evening the cashier had come to collect a draft: suddenly, without counting, at random, he stuffed them into his pockets and, without closing the till, he ran to the front door, taking care not to step in the trickles of blood that had run down the sloping pavement towards this door. The clock was ticking.
Now was the moment of greatest danger, the moment of an encounter behind this door or on the stairs: he listened, no noise; he went out: nobody. Without running, but hurrying, he descended the stairs. Would he look into the dressing room or turn his head away? He looked and did not see the concierge.
A second later he was in the street, mingling with the passers-by, breathing.
XIII
He no longer had to observe himself, to listen, to tense his nerves, to hold back his heart; he could walk freely and think.
His first thought was to try and realise what he was feeling, and he found that it was an immense relief, something analogous no doubt to the state of the asphyxiated person who comes back to life: physically, he had regained his composure; morally, he felt no turmoil, no remorse: he had therefore not been mistaken in his theory when he had explained to Philis that in the intelligent man remorse precedes action and does not follow it.
But where he had gone wrong was in imagining that he would bring to his act a composure and strength that in reality he had completely lacked.
Moving from one idea to another, tossed about by irresolution, he had in no way been the strong man he had believed himself to be: the man who marches towards his goal without being moved, ready to face any attack from wherever it comes, master of his nerves as well as his will, in full possession of all his means. On the contrary, he had been the victim of agitation and weakness. If a serious danger had stood in his way, he would not have known which way to approach it, fear would have paralysed him and he would undoubtedly have lost himself.
In truth, his hand had been firm, but his head had been panic-stricken.
There was something humiliating about this that he had to admit to himself and, what was more serious, something worrying; for, because everything had worked out so well up to now, it was not all over and nothing had even begun.
If the investigations that the law was going to undertake reached him, how would he defend himself?
He thought he was quite sure that he had not been seen in Caffié's house at the time the crime was committed; but can you ever tell whether you have been seen or not?
Similarly, the source of the money he was going to use to pay his debts could become an accusation against which it would be difficult for him to defend himself. "You were destitute yesterday, in dire straits; how, from one day to the next, did you procure the considerable sums for yourself, with which you paid off your creditors?" -- He had heard this question and could not find an answer to it any more now than when he had first considered it; and it was no longer for an indeterminate day that it had to be resolved, it was for tomorrow; in any case, he had to be ready as if it had certainly been for the following day. And what could complicate matters was the fact that Caffié, being the cautious man that he was, had taken care to note down the numbers of his banknotes in a book that could be found.
On leaving the rue Sainte-Anne he had taken the rue Neuve-des-Petits-Champs to go home to deposit his banknotes, to remove the bloodstains that must have splattered him and to wash his hands, especially his right hand, which was still red; but suddenly the idea crossed his mind that he could be followed and that it would be foolish to say where he lived. So, quickening his pace enough to force whoever was following him to run, he set off ahead of him, having no other concern than to take the poorly lit streets where there was little chance of anyone seeing the stains, if they showed on his clothes, his linen or his socks. He walked like this for about half an hour, turning and turning on his trail, blurring it, and after crossing the Place Vendôme twice, where he could see far behind him, he had decided to return, not knowing whether to be satisfied at having diverted the search in this way or whether to be angry at having given in to a kind of panic.
As he passed the lodge without stopping, his concierge called to him and, coming out at once, handed him a letter with unusual alacrity; Saniel, who wanted to avoid any examination, took it quickly and stuffed it in his pocket.
- It's an important letter," said the concierge; "the servant who gave it to me told me it contained money.
It needed this recommendation for Saniel to think of opening it at such a moment, which he did on entering his house.
"My dear doctor, I don't want to leave Paris for Monaco, where I'll be spending two or three months, without thanking you.
"Yours sincerely
" C. DUPHOT.
These thanks were represented by two hundred-franc notes, more than sufficient payment for the care Saniel had given, a few months previously, to the mistress of this former comrade. He threw them on his desk and immediately, after lighting two candles, inspected his clothes and linen.
Only the hand holding the knife and the cuff of the shirt had been splashed, but that was of no consequence: a doctor has every right to have blood on his sleeves, and this shirt would join the one with which he had rescued the woman in the Rue de la Corderie the previous night.
He emptied them onto his desk, where he counted everything: five rolls of gold worth a thousand francs and three bundles of ten thousand francs each in bank notes.
How could he get rid of this considerable sum immediately, and how could he later justify its provenance when the time came... if it came?
The question was complex and, unfortunately for him, he was in no position to examine it coldly.
As for the gold, all he had to do was burn the papers on the rolls straight away; the louis had no numbers or particular marks, but the notes did: where could he hide them until he found out from the newspapers whether Caffié had or had not noted down the numbers?
While burning the papers on which Caffié had written "1000 francs" in his own handwriting, he looked for the hiding place he needed: behind a mirror, under the mantelpiece which he would lift, under a sheet of parquet flooring, in his books: but all these means did not seem secure enough to him to ensure that, in a properly conducted search, the hiding place would not be discovered, which would be his undoing.
As he looked around him, asking things for an inspiration that his brain did not provide, they came across the letter he had just received, and it was this letter that suggested something less banal: Duphot was in Monaco to play; why shouldn't he go too and not play?
Since he had neither relatives nor friends from whom he could obtain a certain sum, his only resource was to gamble for it, and in the desperate position he was in, in full view of everyone, nothing was more natural than this attempt: he had just received two hundred francs which could not save him from his creditors; he was risking them at the roulette wheel in Monaco. It didn't matter whether he won or lost; he would have played. That was enough. He would have been seen gambling. Who would know if he had won or lost? He would say he had won; no one would contradict him. From Monaco, he would have Jardine pay him by telegraphic order, out of the five thousand francs in gold, which would be more than enough for that; and, when he returned to Paris, he would get rid of his other creditors with what he had left.
The matter of the provenance had been settled, and it seemed to him that it had been settled intelligently, but the matter of the banknotes which, spread out before his eyes, were in the way had not been resolved. What should he do with them? It would really have been wiser for him not to take them from the cash register. No matter how light those three bundles were, they crushed him, and under their weight he felt paralysed. For a moment, he thought of lighting a fire and throwing them in. But reflection held him back: wouldn't it be madness to wipe out this fortune; in any case, wouldn't it be the mark of a narrow-minded person with few resources? He had to look; he had to find; and if he looked hard enough, a way would certainly present itself.
He searched and searched, but no matter how deeply absorbed he wished to be, he could not banish a thought that was imposing itself on his mind: What was happening now, in the Rue Sainte-Anne? Had death been discovered?
That was where he should have been to see, instead of politely shutting himself up in the study where he was devouring himself.
For a few moments, he tried to resist this obsession, but it was stronger than his will and his reasoning: as long as he was under its influence, he would find nothing.
Whether he liked it or not, whether it made sense or not, he had to go.
He washed his hands, changed his shirt and, after throwing the notes and the gold in a drawer, he left.
He realised very well that there was a certain danger in leaving the evidence of the crime at his place, which, if found in a search, would overwhelm him without his being able to defend himself; but he told himself that this immediate search was improbable and that, if he did not include the probable and the improbable in his calculations, it would be better for him not to reason: it was a chance he was running, but how many good chances did he not have on his side!
He had walked along the Rue Neuve-des-Petits-Champs in a hurry, but as he approached the Rue Sainte-Anne, he slowed his pace, looking ahead, looking around, listening: nothing unusual struck him; similarly, when he turned into the Rue Sainte-Anne, he found it to be his usual self: few passers-by, no curious onlookers, no groups on the pavements, no shopkeepers on their doorsteps; nothing but what he saw every day.
No doubt nothing had been discovered yet. He had already passed Caffié's door too many times, and when you were built like he was, with an above-average height and a look and bearing that were not those of everyone else, you had to avoid attracting attention.
For a few minutes he strolled slowly back and forth from the rue Neuve-des-Petits-Champs to the rue du Hasard; from there he could see all the way to Caffié's house, but he was far enough away from it that no one would think he was standing guard in front of it.
It seemed to him that someone was watching him, and when two passers-by stopped to chat, he wondered if it wasn't him: why didn't they continue on their way? Why did they occasionally turn their heads his way? He was even more worried about the clerks who were returning a display to their shop: they were in no hurry to finish their work and, every time they returned to the pavement, they followed him with their curious glances; later, they could be dangerous witnesses.
He left the square and, as he couldn't bring himself to leave "the house", he found it ingenious to go and take a seat in the little café opposite.
As he entered, he sat down by the door at a table leaning against the shop window, which he thought would be an excellent vantage point from which to watch the street.
- Shall we serve the gentleman?" asked the waiter.
- Coffee.
He made this reply mechanically, without knowing what he was saying, and only thought about it after he had let it go, wondering if it was natural to have coffee at this hour: the people seated in the room were drinking aperitifs or beer; wasn't it a clumsiness?
But everything seemed clumsy to him, just as everything seemed dangerous; couldn't he regain his composure and his sanity? He drank his coffee slowly, in small gulps; then he had a newspaper given to him, to gain composure. The street was still quiet, and people came out of the café one after the other; dinner was served for the café staff at a table at the back.
And he, behind his newspaper, was thinking: it was his feverish curiosity that had made him assume that Caffié's death would be discovered that evening; in reality, it could very well not be discovered until the next day: there were as many reasons for one hypothesis as for the other, and he could not stay in that café until the next day, or even until midnight; perhaps he had already been there too long.
However, he didn't want to leave yet, and as he couldn't, he thought, read indefinitely, he asked what he needed to write and paid the waiter, so that he could get out as quickly as possible if anything happened.
What to write? Just writing on paper. He wanted to force himself to put in order a piece of work that was ready but for which he had run out of time: it would be a test that would tell him what he was capable of. Strangely enough, while writing he was able to follow his ideas and find the right word, but when he reread, his will escaped him: he was in the street.
Suddenly, there was a movement under the carriage entrance of "the house", and a man ran across the street; three or four people stopped and grouped together.
- He got out without hurrying too much and, in a voice that he strengthened, he asked what was going on.
- A business agent has been murdered in his home: the police have been sent to the office in the Rue du Hasard.
XIV
Saniel had come there to see and know, without having decided, during his long wait, what he should do. Instantly, with a decisiveness he had so often lacked since the day before, he resolved to go up to Caffié's house with the police: wasn't he a doctor, and, what's more, the victim's doctor?
- A businessman!" he said; "is that Mr Caffié?
- Precisely.
- But I'm his doctor!
- A doctor! Here's a doctor!
They moved aside and Saniel entered the carriage entrance, where the concierge, half fainting, was sitting on a chair, surrounded by all the maids of the house and a few neighbours to whom she was telling the story.
Elbowing his way over, he managed to approach her.
- Who said Mr Caffié was dead?" he asked authoritatively.
- Nobody said he was dead; not me at least.
- Well, did he?
- Well, there's a spot of blood that's dripped from his study onto the landing, even though it looks like a cat's waste, without resembling it, and since he's at home, since from the courtyard you can faintly see the light from his lamp, which he never lets burn when he goes to dinner... we think there's something wrong; and then why are his curtains closed? He always left them open.
At that moment, two sergeants entered the door, preceded by a locksmith armed with a bunch of hooks, and a small man with glasses, a fine, smart face, wearing a mechanical hat with curling blond hair - probably the police commissioner.
- Which floor?" he asked the concierge.
- On the first floor.
- Come with us.
