He started up the stairs, accompanied by the concierge, the locksmith and an officer; Saniel wanted to follow them, but the second officer blocked his way.
- Excuse me, Superintendent," said Saniel.
- What do you want, sir?
- I'm Mr Caffié's doctor.
- Sir?
- Doctor Saniel.
- Let the doctor through," said the superintendent, "but alone; get everyone out and close the carriage entrance!
As he reached the landing, the superintendent stopped to look at the brown stain that had run under the door and spread over the floor, as Caffié had never had a doormat.
- It's a bloodstain," said Saniel, who had bent down to examine it and dipped his finger in it.
- Open the door," ordered the superintendent to the locksmith; "it should only be locked at half a turn.
The locksmith examined the entrance, looked through his hooks, chose one and on the first try the door opened.
- Don't let anyone in! said the superintendent. Doctor, please follow me.
And, going through the first door, he entered the first study, that of the clerk, followed by Saniel. Two small rivulets of blood, already thickened, starting from Caffié's armchair and running down the slope of the window that sloped towards the staircase, had joined together to form the stain that had uncovered the crime; the superintendent and Saniel were careful not to step in it.
- The unfortunate man had his neck cut," said Saniel. He's been dead for two or three hours; there's nothing we can do.
- For you, Doctor, but not for me.
And, bending down, he picked up the knife from the armchair.
- Isn't that a butcher's knife?" asked Saniel, who had come here only to throw out the word.
- It looks like one.
He had lifted Caffié's head and was examining the wound:
- You can see," he said, "that the victim's throat has been slit; the blow was struck from left to right by a steady hand that must have been used to wielding this knife; but it wasn't just a strong, practised hand that killed, it was also an intelligence that knew how to proceed so that death was swift, almost lightning-quick and at the same time silent.
- You think it was a butcher?
- A professional killer: the larynx has been cut below the glottis, and at the same time the two carotid arteries with the jugular veins. As the killer had to raise his head, the victim was unable to utter a single scream; there was a considerable flow of blood, and death must have occurred in one or two minutes.
- The scene seems to me to have been very well reconstructed," said the superintendent.
- The blood must have gushed out in this direction," continued Saniel, pointing to the entrance; but as the door to this entrance was open, nothing was seen.
While Saniel was speaking, the superintendent cast a circular gaze around him, the gaze of the policeman who sees everything and picks it all up.
- The cash register is open," he said; "the case is clear: murder followed by robbery.
There was a door opposite the entrance, and the superintendent opened it: it was the door to Caffié's bedroom.
- I'm going to give you a man to help you carry the body to this room, where you can continue your examination more easily, while I'll also be able to carry out my investigations more easily in this study.
Saniel would have liked to stay in the study to witness these investigations, but it was impossible to raise any objection. The armchair was rolled into the bedroom, where the candles in the fireplace had been lit, and when the corpse had been laid on the bed, the commissioner returned to the study.
Saniel made his examination last as long as he could, so as not to leave the house, but he could not prolong it beyond certain limits; when they were reached, he returned to the clerk's chambers, where the superintendent had taken his seat and was receiving the concierge's statement.
- So," he said, "from five to seven o'clock, nobody asked you for Mr Caffié?
- Nobody; but I left my dressing room at a quarter past five to turn on the gas for my stairs; it took me a good twenty minutes, because I'm no longer flexible, and during that time they were able to go up and down the stairs without me seeing anyone passing in front of the dressing room.
- Well," the superintendent asked Saniel, "did you find anything characteristic?
- No; there's no wound other than the one on the neck.
- Would you like to write your forensic report while I continue my investigation?
- Of course.
And, without waiting, he sat down at the clerk's desk, facing the secretary of the Commissaire, who had arrived a few moments earlier.
- I'm going to swear you in," said the commissioner.
When this formality had been completed, Saniel began his report:
"We, the undersigned, Victor Saniel, Doctor of Medicine of the Faculty of Paris, residing in Paris, rue Louis-le-Grand, having taken an oath to fulfil in honour and conscience the mission entrusted to us..."
As he wrote, he paid close attention to what was being said around him and did not miss a word of the concierge's statement.
- I'm certain," she said, "that from half past five to the present time, only people from the house passed through the staircase.
- But before half past five?
- I told you that, from a quarter past five to half past five, I was not in my dressing room.
- And before a quarter past five?
- Many people I don't know passed by.
- Did any of these people ask for Monsieur Caffié?
- No; that is, yes. There was someone who asked me if M. Caffié was at home; but I know him well; that's why I said no. And who was this someone?
- And who was this someone?
- A former clerk of M. Caffié.
- What's his name?
- Mr Florentin... Mr Florentin Cormier.
Saniel's hand stopped, but he had the strength not to raise his head.
- What time did he come?" asked the superintendent.
- Around three o'clock, more before than after.
- Did you see him leave?
- Of course; he even spoke to me.
- What time was it?
- Half past three.
- Do you think death could have happened at that time?" asked the commissaire, addressing Saniel.
- No; I think it was between five and six o'clock.
- The superintendent must not suspect M. Florentin," cried the concierge; "he's a good young man, incapable of harming a flea. And then, there's a good reason why the death didn't happen at three or three-thirty: M. Caffié's lamp was on, and you know, the poor man, he wasn't a man to light his lamp in broad daylight; looking as he did... as it should be.
Suddenly, she stopped and punched herself in the forehead.
- It's all coming back to me and you'll see that Mr Florentin had nothing to do with it. As I was going up the stairs at a quarter past five to light my gas, someone came up behind me and rang Mr Caffié's doorbell, knocking three or four times, which was the signal for the door to be opened.
Saniel's pen stopped again, and he had to rest his hand on the table to stop it shaking.
- Who was this someone?
Saniel didn't have the strength not to look at the concierge.
- I don't know," she replied; "I didn't see it, but I heard it, a man's step. It was the rascal who did it, you can be sure of that.
It was indeed likely.
- He left while I was on the other staircase; he knew the habits of the house well.
Saniel had resumed writing his report.
After turning the concierge round and round without being able to get any more out of her, the superintendent dismissed her, and leaving Saniel to his task, he went into Caffié's study, where he stayed for quite a long time.
When he returned, he had brought a small notebook which he consulted: no doubt it was Caffié's cash book, simple and primitive like everything that related to the habits of the old businessman, governed by the strictest economy, both in spending and in work.
- From this notebook," said the superintendent to his secretary, "it would appear that 35 or 36,000 francs were taken from the till; but a considerable amount of securities and valuables were left behind.
Saniel, who had finished his report, did not take his eyes off the notebook, and what he could see reassured him. Obviously, this bookkeeping was reduced to a minimum: a date, a name, a sum, and after this sum a capital P which, no doubt, meant paid, or some other hieroglyphic sign, and that was all; it therefore seemed unlikely that with such a system, Caffié would ever have taken the trouble to write down the numbers of the notes that passed through his hands; in any case, if he did, it was not in this notebook. Would we find another one?
- My report is finished," he said, "here it is.
- Since I've got you, can you give me some information about the victim's habits and the people he entertained?
- Not at all, I'd only known him for a short time, and he was only my client as I was his, by chance: he was handling a case for me, and I had just given him some advice; he was extremely diabetic; the murderer only brought forward his death by a very short time, by a few days.
- It doesn't matter, he brought it forward.
- Oh, perfectly. Besides, if he's good at cutting people's necks, perhaps he's not so good at diagnosing their illnesses.
- It's probable," replied the commissaire, smiling.
- You think he's a butcher?
- There are certain presumptions.
- The knife?
- It may have been stolen or found.
- But the way it was used?
- That, it seems to me, is the point from which we must start.
Saniel couldn't stay any longer, so he got up to leave.
- You know my address," he said; "but I must warn you that, if you need me, I'm leaving tomorrow for Nice; besides, I'll only be away long enough to get there and back.
- If we need you, it will probably not be for several days; we are not going to walk very fast in the unknown where we are.
XV
On his way home along the rue des Petits-Champs, Saniel walked briskly. If, more than once, his emotions had been poignant during this long session, on the whole he could only be satisfied with his results: the concierge had not seen him, that was now established; the hypothesis of the butcher's knife had been put forward in such a way as to make its way; finally, it seemed likely that Caffié had not taken the numbers of his tickets.
But had they been written down, and had the notebook containing them been discovered later, the danger was not immediate. In fact, while he was writing his report and listening to the concierge's deposition, his mind, instead of focusing on these two things, had, by a strange anomaly, run to a third: then, as if by a sort of inspiration, he had found the means which had always eluded him when he was searching for it with all the strength of his application, -- and which consisted in getting rid of the banknotes that very evening, without destroying them; to do this he had only to divide them into small packets, put them in envelopes, and entrust them, under various initials, to the poste restante, which would keep them faithfully until the day when he could ask for them again without compromising himself. 
In the concierge's statement, in the trail indicated by the knife, in the invention of the poste restante, there were therefore just grounds for satisfaction that could make his breath free; decidedly luck seemed to be with him, and he could have told himself that all was for the best, had he not committed the truly mad imprudence of entering this café. What did he need to establish himself there and stay long enough to attract attention? To avoid the attention of passers-by, he had exposed himself to the curiosity of the café staff; quite an affair really, and worthy of a fool who has lost his head! Had he been told this by an intelligent man, he would have refused to believe it, but for him it was true. What would be the consequences of this blunder? That was impossible to predict. None, perhaps. And perhaps very serious ones. In these conditions of uncertainty, the best thing to do was to pretend it hadn't happened, and try to forget about it; what was urgent for the moment was the banknotes, and he had only them to think about.
When he got home and his door was closed, he immediately put his idea into practice: from the three bundles of notes he made ten packets, so as to form a small volume, folded each one in a sheet of strong paper, placed it in an envelope simply gummed, and on this envelope he wrote two letters of the alphabet following each other, starting with A, and two numbers starting with 1 and also following each other: A. B. 12, -- C. D. 34, -- E. F. 56; -- then he addressed them poste restante to the first ten Paris offices listed on his almanac. This logical and easy-to-remember order enabled him not to keep a note of this combination, and thus to defy any search if one were ever made. It was not for thirty thousand francs that he had killed Caffié, it was simply for three thousand; and since he had thought of burning these notes, he could now, without worry, expose himself to losing some of them.
When this idea of the poste restante had occurred to him, he had told himself that he would throw his envelopes into the box nearest his home, which would finish everything; But as he was about to leave, he thought that these ten letters with more or less the same description, found in a heap in the same box, might provoke curiosity, and he resolved to divide them into five or six offices, where he would carry them himself, on foot, without taking a carriage, although he now had enough to pay for it, because the coachman who would have driven him might one day become a formidable witness. 
After the day's mad dash through the woods and fields, after the emotions of the evening, he felt broken and weary from a weariness he knew nothing about, but he understood that he was not at liberty to listen to this weariness. A new situation had been created for him which had the peculiarity that he ceased to belong to himself and would henceforth be, and remain for the rest of his life, the prisoner of his crime; it would be this crime which, from this evening onwards, would be in charge and which he would have to obey.
How could he not have foreseen this situation when, weighing at such length the pros and cons, as an intelligent man who can scrutinise the future from every angle, he had considered what should happen? But however surprising it was, the discovery was nonetheless an incontrovertible certainty, and the unfortunate and disturbing proof that emerged was that, no matter how intelligent you are or how intelligent you think you are, you can always learn from experience.
What else would he learn? He had to admit to himself that he was facing the unknown, and all he could hope for was that this lesson he was receiving from the facts would be the hardest; as for imagining that it was the last, that would have been madness: one would see.
For the moment, it was just a question of the letters he had prepared, and whether he was tired or not, he had to get rid of them as quickly as possible: he took them and set off straight away, going through the streets that were becoming deserted and dark, from the office in the rue Cambon to the one in the place Ventedour, from the rue de Choiseul to the place de la Bourse, and so on until he had finished.
By the time he got home, it had been quite a long time since one o'clock in the morning; he went straight to bed and slept a heavy, dreamless sleep.
It was broad daylight when he opened his eyes the next morning; surprised at having slept so late, he jumped out of bed: his watch showed eight o'clock; but as he wasn't due to leave until eleven fifteen, he had plenty of time.
What could he do with it?
It was the first time in years that he had asked himself such a question, as every morning he always found that he was three or four hours short of his schedule.
He dressed slowly, which was slow for a man who usually took ten minutes to get dressed, but it was still only eight twenty.
Then he thought of writing to Philis to let her know about his trip, but immediately changed his mind and decided to go and tell her himself.
The previous year, he had treated Madame Cormier for an attack of paralysis, and he was able, on condition that he did not repeat his visits too often, to go to her house without appearing to come to see Philis: it was in passing, to get news of a patient in whom he was interested by the very fact that he had cured her, and whose recovery he wanted to follow.
At the time he was treating her, Madame Cormier was living in the rue des Moines in Les Batignolles, on a small ground floor at the end of a garden, which he had moved her from because it was too damp, to a fifth floor where she could find air and light. At nine o'clock he knocked on her door.
- Come in," answered a man's voice.
He was surprised, because in the time he had been coming here almost every day, he had never met a man. Who was that answering as if he were at home? He turned the key in the lock and found himself in a dark hallway from which the voice had obviously come, but where he saw no one, the person who had answered being hidden by a curtain that divided the hallway in two. Increasingly surprised, for he had not yet seen the curtain, he knocked on the dining room door; this time it was Philis' clear voice that answered:
- Come in.
He opened the door and, in front of a large table set against the window, he saw Philis, dressed in a grey smock, at work. Small cardboard boxes were spread out on the table and she had a watercolour box with a glass whose water was dirty from washing the brushes.
At the sound of footsteps on the parquet floor, she turned her head and was instantly on her feet; but she held back the cry, the name that had risen to her lips:
- Mama," she said, "it's Dr Saniel.
Immediately Madame Cormier left the kitchen and entered the room, walking with difficulty, for although Saniel had put her back on her feet, he had not given her back the suppleness and ease of youth.
After the first pleasantries had been exchanged, Saniel explained that, as he had a visit to make to Batignolles, he had not wanted to come and see his patient without seeing her and asking her how she was.
While Madame Cormier explained, with the prolixity of patients, what she was feeling and also what she was surprised not to be feeling, Philis looked at Saniel, worried to find his face so convulsed. Surely something very serious had happened; her visit said as much. But what was it? Her anxiety was heightened by the fact that he avoided looking at her. Why was he doing that? She had done nothing and could find nothing to reproach herself about.
At that moment the door to the hall opened and a tall young man with a curly blond beard entered the room: the man from the hall, Saniel thought as he examined him.
- My son," said Madame Cormier.
- My brother Florentin, whom we've spoken of so often," said Philis.
Florentin! So he had become a fool for not thinking that this man, hidden from him by the curtain and answering like a master when someone knocked, could only be Philis's brother? Was he so deeply upset that such simple reasoning was impossible? It was definitely important for him to leave as soon as possible; the journey would calm his panic-stricken nervous machine.
- People have written to me," said Florentin, "and since my return they have told me how good you were to my mother. Allow me to thank you for this with a moved and grateful heart; I hope that soon this gratitude will not remain an empty word.
- Let's not talk about that," said Saniel, looking at Philis with a frankness and an open face that reassured her to a certain extent. If the word were not barbarous, I would say that her illness has been good fortune for me.
Then, to divert the conversation, and above all to try to tell Philis that he wanted to talk to her alone for a short while, he approached the table:
- And what charming things are you doing here, mademoiselle?
- Oh, charming! I don't know if, by working conscientiously, I would ever have acquired a talent that would have allowed me to do charming things; but I'm reduced to the trade, and the trade has to be content with a little. Look at these twelve menus: they all have one and the same design, and that's how I have to make something at the end of the day.
Saying this, she dipped her brush into her colour box, established the shade she wanted on the bottom of a broken plate she was using as a palette, and quickly applied this shade to each of these twelve menus.
- It saves time and colour," she said; "but with processes like these, which are a necessity, how can I think of making something charming? I'd like to try, though... if I had the time.
- Life has its difficulties, and for everyone. You have to work only for pleasure...
- Then we wouldn't be doing anything," said Florentin blithely.
- Will you shut up, you lazy bastard!" cried Philis.
Saniel gave some advice to Madame Cormier, then got up to leave.
Philis followed him, and Florentin seemed to want to go with them, but she stopped him:
- I have a question for Mr Saniel," she said.
When they reached the landing, she pulled the vestibule door shut completely.
- What is it?" she asked in a hurried, trembling voice.
- I wanted to warn you that I'm leaving for Monaco at eleven.
- Are you?
- I've received 200 francs from a client and I'm going to gamble it away. Two hundred francs can't pay Jardine or the others; gambling, they can give me a few thousand francs.
- Oh, poor dear, how desperate you must be to have such an idea!
- Am I wrong?
- Never wrong in my eyes, in my heart, in my love! May fortune be with you, my beloved?
- Give me your hand.
She looked around, listening: no one, no noise.
Then, drawing him to her, she put her lips to his:
- All yours, with you!
- I'll be back on Tuesday.
- Tuesday, at five o'clock, I'll arrive.
XVI
Nobody knew as little about gambling as Saniel: he knew they played in Monaco, that's all; and in Paris he had taken his ticket for Monaco, where he got out of the carriage.
On leaving the station, he looked around to get his bearings; seeing nothing resembling a gambling house as he understood it, i.e. the Royat casino, the only establishment of its kind he had ever seen, he asked a passer-by:
- The gaming house, please?
- We don't gamble in Monaco.
- I thought not.
- They play in Monte Carlo.
- And is Monte Carlo far away?
- Over there.
With his hand, the passer-by pointed to a green spot on the slope of the mountain where, amidst the foliage, the roofs and facades of large buildings could be seen.
He thanked him and headed that way, while the passer-by, calling out to another, recounted the request that had just been made of him; and they both laughed and shrugged their shoulders: could anyone be as stupid as those Parisians! Another man about to be fleeced, and who'd come all the way from Paris just for that! Was he funny with his long legs and long arms! Can you play with a face like that?
Without worrying about the laughter he heard behind him, Saniel continued on his way, watching the blue sea shimmer under the oblique rays of the sun, already low on the horizon. Despite the night spent in the carriage, he felt no fatigue; on the contrary, he was at ease in body and mind; the journey had calmed the restlessness of his nerves, and it was with perfect tranquillity that he contemplated what had happened before his departure. In his present state of calm, he no longer had to fear any blunders or fits of madness, and since he had regained his will, everything would be all right: no more looking back, let alone forward, only the present had to occupy him.
The present, at that moment, was the game. What was the game? What was the game? Roulette, he knew; but what roulette was, he didn't know. He would do as his neighbours did. If they laughed at him, it didn't matter; in fact, he must have wanted them to laugh at him: we remember with pleasure those we have laughed at; what he was looking for in this country was precisely to be remembered.
When he entered the gaming rooms, he noticed that a religious silence reigned there: around a large table covered with a green cloth carpet divided by drawings and numbers, people were sitting on the high chairs from which they appeared to be standing; others on lower chairs or simply standing around the table were pushing or picking up louis and banknotes from the green cloth, and a loud voice repeated in a monotone: "Gentlemen, make your bet! The game is up! Then a small ivory ball was thrown into a cylinder where it rolled with a metallic noise. Although he had never seen a roulette wheel, it took no effort on his part to guess that it was one.
And before putting the few louis he already had in his hand on the table, he looked around to see how it was done. But no matter how hard he tried, after the tenth game he had not understood any better than after the first: with rakes the croupiers collected the stakes from certain players; with these same rakes they doubled, tenfolded or even paid out in proportions that he did not realise to certain others, and that was all.
Anyway, it didn't matter; he thought he had seen how they put their money on the table, and that was enough. He had five louis in his hand when the croupier said "Gentlemen, place your bets"; he placed them on number 32, or at least he thought he was placing them on that number. "Rien ne va plus! The ball rolled into the cylinder that the croupier had spun.
- 31!" called the croupier, who added a few other words that Saniel either misheard or did not understand.
However little he knew about roulette, he thought he had lost: he had placed his bet on 32, it was 31 that came up, the bank had won. He was surprised to see the croupier push a pile of gold at him, which must have amounted to a hundred louis, or thereabouts, and accompany this movement with a glance which, without any possible doubt, meant: "Yours, sir!
What was he to do? Since he had lost, he could not pick up the money sent to him by mistake.
He had laid down his bet by leaning over the shoulder of a gentleman with hair and a beard of an unbelievable black, who, without playing, was picking a card with a pin. The gentleman turned to him and, with a most amiable smile, in the most gracious tone of voice:
- To you, sir," he said.
Decidedly, he had been mistaken in thinking that he had lost, and he had to pick up that pile of louis; which he did, but he forgot to pick up his first bet as well.
The roulette wheel was spinning again.
- 32!" called the croupier.
Saniel had just realised that his five louis had been left on 32, and he thought he had won since that was the number that had just come up, and his ignorance did not extend to not knowing that a roulette number is paid thirty-six times its stake: the croupier's rake was therefore going to push one hundred and eighty louis on him.
But, to his great surprise, he didn't push any more than he had on the first shot. This was becoming incomprehensible: they paid him when he lost, and when he won they only gave him half of what he was owed.
His face betrayed his astonishment so well that he saw a mocking smile in the eyes of the black-haired man, who had turned towards him again.
As he was playing to play, not to win or lose, he pocketed what had just been sent to him, as well as his stake.
- Since you are no longer playing," said the kind gentleman as he left his chair, "would you allow me to have a word with you?
Saniel bowed and they moved away from the table; when they were far enough away not to disturb the players' contemplation, the gentleman saluted ceremoniously:
- Allow me to introduce myself: Prince Mazzazoli.
Saniel thought he should reply by giving his name and title.
- Well, doctor," said the prince in a strong Italian accent, "I hope you'll forgive me for a simple observation that my age perhaps allows: you play like a child.
- Like an ignoramus," replied Saniel without getting angry, for, however unusual this observation was, he had already calculated that it might be good for the future to have to invoke the testimony of a prince.
- I'm sure you're still wondering why you were paid eighteen times your stake on the first move you made, and why you weren't paid thirty-six times your stake on the second.
- That's true, isn't it?
- Well, I'll tell you. It's because, instead of placing your money on the number you had chosen, you placed it astride 31 and 32: by a stroke of luck that is enviable, the result was the same for you; but what would that luck not give if, instead of relying on chance, it were illuminated! Roulette is not a game of chance, as some people wrongly believe: it is all about calculation and combination. So, by placing your money on two numbers, you had five chances against you, just as you would have had six and a half on three numbers, or seven on four. That's what you need to know before risking anything, and that's what I'm offering you if you want us to form a partnership until this evening. Your luck combined with my experience will break the bank.
Saniel had not waited for this conclusion to guess more or less what she was going to be: a beggar, this old Italian prince so well dyed.
- My intention is not to gamble any more," he said.
- With your luck, that would be more than a mistake.
- I needed a certain sum, I won it, it's enough for me.
- You wouldn't be foolish enough to refuse the hand that Fortune is holding out to you.
- Are you sure she's holding out her hand to me?" said Saniel, who thought it was the prince.
- Don't doubt it; I'll prove it to you...
- Thank you; I never go back on what I have decided.
At any other time, Saniel would have turned his back on this intruder; but he was a witness who had to be spared.
- I have nothing more to do here," he said politely; "allow me to withdraw after thanking you for your offer, which I appreciate.
-- Well," exclaimed the prince, "since you don't want to run out of luck, let me do it for you; this money can be a fetish, take five louis, just five louis, and entrust them to me; I will play them according to my combinations, which are certain, and this evening I will give you your share of the profits. Where are you staying? I live in the Villa des Palmes.
- Nowhere; I'm on my way.
- So meet me tonight at ten o'clock in this room and we'll wind up our association.
His first instinct was to refuse. What's the point of giving charity to this old monkey? But, after all, it wasn't very much to pay his witness five louis, and he gave it to him.
- A thousand pardons! Tonight at ten o'clock.
As Saniel was about to leave the room, he found himself face to face with his old friend Duphot, who was entering accompanied by a woman - the same woman he had treated.
- You here? Ah! for example, she's very good.
And both the lover and the mistress exclaimed.
Saniel explained why he had come to Monaco and what he had done since his arrival.
- With my money! Ah, that's a good one," exclaimed Duphot.
- And you don't gamble any more? asked the woman.
- I have what I need.
- So you're going to play for me.
He tried to defend himself, but they dragged him over to the roulette table and each put a louis in his hand.
- Play.
- How do you play?
- As inspiration would advise: you're lucky.
But his luck was running out; the two louis they had thrown at random were lost.
He was given two more; this time they won eight.
- You see, dear friend.
He continued with varying degrees of success, winning and losing.
After a quarter of an hour he was allowed to give up the game.
- And what are you going to do now?" asked Duphot.
- Send what I owe my creditor by telegraphic order.
- Do you know where the telegraph is?
- No, I don't know.
- I'll take you there.
It was a second witness whose help Saniel could not refuse.
When he had sent his money order to Jardine, he had nothing more to do in Monte Carlo, and as he could only leave in the evening, at eleven o'clock, he remained idle, not knowing what to do with his time. So he bought a Nice newspaper and went to sit in the garden, under a gas burner, facing the calm, dark sea. Perhaps he would find some telegraph dispatch in the paper that would tell him what had happened in the Rue Sainte-Anne since he left.
He searched long enough; then at the end of the paper, in the Latest News section, he read: "The crime in the Rue Sainte-Anne seems to have entered a new phase; more careful investigations have led to the discovery of a trouser button to which a piece of cloth adheres. This proves that before the crime there was a struggle between the victim and his killer. As the button bears certain letters and marks, it is a valuable clue for the police.
This evidence of a struggle between the victim and his killer made Saniel smile: if the police went down this road, they would go a long way to achieving a result. Who knew how long that button had been in the room, which was not very well swept? Who had it come from?
Suddenly, leaving his bench, he entered a grove and quickly felt around: wasn't it he who had lost the button?
But he was soon ashamed of this unconscious movement: the button that the police were so proud to have discovered did not belong to him; the new path they had just embarked upon would not lead to him.
XVII
On Tuesday, a little before five o'clock, Philis, as she had promised, rang her doorbell and he came out of his laboratory, where he had gone back to work, to open the door.
She jumped at his neck:
- Well?" she asked, her voice trembling.
He told her how he had gambled, how he had won, but without specifying the sum; and he also told her about Prince Mazzazoli's partnership proposals, about meeting Duphot, about sending the telegraphic order to Jardine.
- Oh, what happiness," she said, hugging him, "you're free now.
- No more creditors! Master of me: you see I had a good inspiration.
- It was the right thing to do.
Then, interrupting herself, she said:
- Speaking of justice, you didn't mention Caffié to me the morning you left.
- I was so preoccupied that I didn't have time to think about Caffié.
- It's strange, this coincidence of his death with the conviction we brought against him: doesn't that prove the justice of things?
- If you like.
- As the money you earned in Monaco proves for you: what is just necessarily happens. Caffié was punished for all his misdeeds and crimes; you were rewarded for your suffering.
- Wouldn't it have been fair if Caffié had been punished sooner and I had suffered less?
She remained without answering.
- You see," he said, smiling, "that your philosophy remains short.
- It's not my philosophy I'm thinking about, it's Caffié and us.
- In what way can Caffié be associated with you or yours?
- He is, or rather he could be, if the justice in which I believe, despite your jokes, allowed him to be.
- You will agree that this sounds like an enigma.
- What have you learned about Caffié since you left?
- Nothing or almost nothing.
- You know that the crime is believed to have been committed by a butcher.
- The superintendent picked up the knife in front of me, and it's definitely a butcher's knife; what's more, the blow that cut Caffié's throat was delivered by a hand used to cutting throats; that's what I said in my report.
- Since then, a more careful search has uncovered a trouser button under a piece of furniture...
- I read that in a newspaper, but I don't believe it was a struggle: Caffié's position in the chair where he was hit and where he died indicates that the old rascal was surprised; moreover, if he hadn't been surprised, if he had struggled, he would have shouted, and without doubt he would have made himself heard.
- If you only knew how happy I am to hear you say that," she exclaimed.
- How happy! What do you care?
He looked at her surprised.
- What does it matter to you whether Caffié was killed with or without a struggle? You condemned him, he's dead; that should be enough for you.
- I was quite wrong to pass that sentence, just talking and without attaching any importance to it.
- Do you think it was my mistake that led to his execution?
- I don't have that simplicity, but in the end I'd rather not have condemned him?
- Do you regret it?
- I regret that he's dead.
- Decidedly the enigma continues; but you know that I'm not at all into it; and, if you like, we'll leave it at that; we have better things to do than talk about Caffié.
- On the contrary, allow me to talk about it, because we need your advice.
He looked at her again, trying to read her mind and to guess why she was so keen to talk about Caffié, when he himself would have liked not to. What could be behind this insistence?
- I'm listening to you," he said, "and since you have some advice to ask me on the subject, you should have told me straight away what it was all about.
- You're right, and I would have already done so if I weren't held back by a feeling of embarrassment... and shame that I blame myself for, because with you I should have neither embarrassment nor shame.
- Of course not.
- But first of all I have to tell you, you have to know that my brother Florentin is a good and brave boy; you have to believe it, you have to be convinced.
- I am, because you tell me so; besides, he made the best impression on me in the short time I saw him at your house the other morning.
- Isn't it immediately obvious that he's a good person?
- He certainly is.
- And frank, and upright; weak, it's true, and a bit soft too, that is to say lacking in spring and will, letting himself be carried away... by kindness and tenderness. This weakness caused him to make a mistake before he left for America. I have hidden it from you until now, but you must know it now: loving a woman who dominated him and made him do what she wanted, he allowed himself to be led into... embezzle a sum of forty-five francs that she asked him for, that she demanded the same evening, hoping to be able to hand it over three days later, without his boss noticing.
- Was her boss Caffié?
- No; he had left Caffié three months earlier and gone to work for another businessman whom I never told you about, so now you understand why. He missed the money he had counted on to replace the one he had given to this woman. Instead of coming to us, he looked elsewhere, couldn't find it, lost his head, so much so that his embezzlement was discovered and his boss, who was no less hard-line than Caffié, lodged a complaint against him. It would take too long to explain how we managed to get the complaint withdrawn and prevent the poor boy from going to court. Finally we succeeded, and in despair, dying of shame, he left for America, in spite of us, as much to avoid being ashamed in front of anyone as to make his fortune, for his character is as easy to delude as it is to despair. Now that you've seen Florentin, you'll admit that you can be guilty of the crime he committed without being capable... of becoming a murderer.
He was about to answer, but she closed her lips with a quick gesture:
- You'll see why I'm talking about this, and you'll also understand that I didn't stray from Caffié, or from the button the police are counting on to find the culprit: that button belonged to Florentin.
- Your brother's?
- Yes, Florentin, who was at Caffié's on the very day of the crime.
- It's true; the concierge told the police commissioner that he came around three o'clock.
Philis cried out in despair:
- We know he came, which is even more serious than we could have imagined or feared.
- But, as this visit took place between three and half past three, and the crime was certainly committed between five and half past five, nobody can accuse your brother of being the murderer, as he had left before Caffié lit his lamp. As this lamp could not have lit itself, it follows that he could not have slit the throat of a man who was still alive an hour after the concierge had seen your brother and spoken to him.
- What you tell me is a great relief; if you only knew what a fright we were in!
- You were very quick to be alarmed.
- Too quickly; but when Florentin, reading the newspaper aloud to us, came to the story of the button and exclaimed: "But it's mine, that button!" we all felt a shock that made us lose our heads. We saw the police descending on our house, questioning Florentin, blaming him for the past that would be splashed all over the newspapers, and you must feel our emotion.
- But your brother can explain how he lost that button at Caffié's, can't he?
- Of course, and in the most natural way. As the concierge told us, on the day of the murder he went to Caffié's house to ask for a certificate stating that he had been his clerk for several years. Caffié gave him this certificate, which was to replace the one Florentin had lost; then, as we talked, Caffié told him about a file he could not find and which he needed. It was Florentin who had taken more care of this matter than Caffié himself, and when it was finished, he had put the file, which was bulky and could not fit into the boxes where they are usually filed, on a board at the top of a cupboard. As chance would have it, he remembered; he told Caffié, who replied that he had searched the cupboard without finding anything. You have searched incorrectly," said Florentin, "or the file has been disturbed by my successor. -- I did look," replied Caffié, "and your successor never saw it. -- Well, then," said Florentin, "I'll find it. And fetching a small ladder, he climbed up to the top of the cupboard. His memory had not deceived him: the file was indeed where he thought it was, but a thick layer of black dust had obscured the card on which were written the indications that should make it recognisable. He picked it up to take it down, took a false step and, in a sudden movement to restrain himself, one of the buttons on his trousers was ripped off.
- And he didn't pick it up?
- He didn't even notice at first; it was only later, in the street, when he saw that one leg of his trousers was longer than the other and was dragging on his boots, that he thought of the ladder and realised that a button was missing. He wasn't going to go back to Caffié's to look for it, was he?
- Of course not.
- How could he foresee that Caffié would be murdered; that the crime would be skilfully planned and executed enough to let the culprit escape; that, two days later, the police would find a button on which they would build a whole story; that, from this story, it follows that there was a struggle between the assassin and his victim; that in this struggle a button was torn off, and that the one who lost it was necessarily the one who cut Caffié's neck? Florentin didn't think about all that.
- That's understandable.
- That evening he replaced his button with another, and it was only when he read the newspaper that he realised what a serious matter this seemingly insignificant event could be, and like him, we shared his emotion.
- You didn't tell anyone about this button?
- Of course not; we weren't watching; and I only told you about it because I'm telling you everything, and also because, if we were threatened, we could only expect help from you. Florentin is a good boy, but he's a sheep who only knows how to stretch out his back when it's raining on him; Mum is like him in more ways than one, and I, although I'm more resistant, I confess that in the face of the law and the police I would easily lose my head, like children who start screaming when they're left in the dark; isn't the law the night, when you don't know it, and a disturbing night, full of frights, peopled with ghosts?
- I don't think that you are threatened as you imagined in your first moment of emotion...
- Very natural.
- Very natural, I agree, but which reflection shows to be unfounded. This button does not bear the name of the tailor who supplied it...
- No, but it does bear initials and a trademark: an A and a P with a crown and a cockerel.
- Well, how do you expect the police to find, among the two or three thousand tailors in Paris, those who use buttons marked in this way? And how can these tailors be expected to identify the client to whom the button belongs, this one and not another? It's like looking for a needle in a haystack. Where did your brother get these trousers made? Didn't he bring them back from America?
- The poor boy didn't bring anything from America apart from some poor, well-worn clothes, and we had to start by dressing him from head to toe. We were thrifty, and a little tailor on the Avenue de Clichy, called Valérius, made him this suit.
-- I don't think it's very likely that the police will find this tailor on the Avenue de Clichy, although it would have been better if the trousers had come from La Belle Jardinière or another department stores': but if they did, and if the tailor recognised that the button came from his stock, it wouldn't point to your brother. Finally, if we could get to him, we would still have to prove that there had been a struggle, as is supposed, and that the button had been ripped off in the struggle, and that your brother was in the Rue Sainte-Anne between five and six o'clock, when it would no doubt be easy for him to prove where he was at the time.
- But at home, with Mum!
- So you can see that you can rest easy.
XVIII
Reassured, Philis was in a hurry to return to the rue des Moines to share with her mother and brother the confidence that Saniel had put in her heart: it was with hurried steps that she went up the rue Louis-le-Grand to Les Batignolles and with a feverish hand she pulled the cord of their doorbell.
For the time had gone when, in this quiet house where all the tenants knew each other, the key was left on the door and all you had to do was knock before entering. Since the newspapers carried the story of the button found at Caffié's, the freedom and security that had reigned in this house, where until then there had been nothing to fear and nothing to hide, had disappeared; no more keys on the front door, no more loud conversations, no more laughter: Now, the door always remained closed; we no longer laughed; and when we spoke, when we read the newspapers we bought morning and evening, it was in mid-voice, as if, behind the walls, ears had been on the lookout; the doorbell tinkled and we looked at each other with emotion, wondering with a fearful glance if it was not the announcement of danger. 
When her brother came to open the door, she found the table served: they were waiting for her.
- I was afraid something had happened to you," said Mrs Cormier.
- I've been detained.
She quickly got rid of her hat and frock coat.
- Didn't you learn anything?" asked Madame Cormier as she brought the soup to the table.
- No, I haven't.
- Didn't they tell you anything?" Florentin continued in a low voice.
- That's all I've been told; or that's all I've heard, when I wasn't being spoken to directly.
- What are they saying?
- No one will admit that the police are investigating the button.
- You see, Florentin," interrupted Mrs Cormier, smiling at her son.
But he shook his head.
- But everyone's opinion has value," cried Philis.
- Speak lower," said Florentin.
- It is argued that it is impossible for the police to find, among the two or three thousand tailors in Paris, all those who use buttons marked A. P.; and it is said that, if they were found, they would not be able to name those of their customers to whom they have supplied buttons of this kind; so that it is really like looking for a needle in a haystack.
- When you put your mind to it, you can find a needle in a haystack," says Florentin.
- You ask me what I heard, I'll tell you again. But I didn't stop there. As I was passing near the rue Louis-le-Grand, I went up to M. Saniel's: it was time for his consultation and I was hoping to find him.
- Did you tell him about the situation?" exclaimed Florentin.
In any other circumstances, she would have answered frankly, explaining that Saniel could be trusted; but it wasn't when she saw how agitated her brother was that she was going to exasperate him with this confession, especially as she couldn't give the reasons for her faith in Saniel at the same time; so she had to reassure him first of all.
- No," she said, "but I could talk to M. Saniel about Caffié without him being surprised; since it was he who made the first observations, wasn't it only natural that my curiosity should want to know a little more than what the newspapers say?
- All the same, it may seem a strange thing to do.
- I don't think so; but, in any case, the interest we had in finding out made me overlook this consideration, and I think that, when I have told you M. Saniel's opinion, you will no longer regret my visit.
- And this opinion?" asked Madame Cormier.
- His opinion is that there was no struggle between Caffié and the assassin, since Caffié's position in the chair where he was hit proves that he was surprised; therefore, if there was no struggle, there was no button torn off, and the whole police scaffolding collapses.
Madame Cormier heaved a deep sigh of relief:
- You see!" she said to her son.
- And M. Saniel's opinion is not that of just anyone, it's not even that of just any doctor: it's that of the doctor who witnessed the death and who, more than anyone else, has the authority to say how it happened -- by surprise, without a struggle, without the button being torn off.
- It is not M. Saniel who directs the police investigations, nor who inspires them," replied Florentin; "his opinion does not point to a culprit, whereas the button may point to one, at least for those who believe in a struggle, and between the two the police cannot hesitate: already they are mocked in the papers for not yet having discovered the murderer, who will join all those they have let escape, they must follow the trail they have set out on, and this trail..." He lowered his voice.
He lowered his voice:
- This is where she can take him.
- To do that, she would have to go via the Avenue de Clichy, and that seems unlikely.
- It's the possible that torments me, not the plausible, and you can't fail to recognise that what I fear is possible: I was at Caffié's the day of the crime, I lost a button torn off there with violence, this button picked up by the police proves, according to them, the guilt of the person to whom it belonged; if they find that I'm that person...
- She won't find it.
- ... Let's say she does find him, how can I defend myself?
- By proving that you were not in the Rue Sainte-Anne between five and six o'clock, since you were here.
- And what witnesses will testify to this alibi? I have only one: my mother. What good is a mother's testimony on behalf of her son in such circumstances?
- You'll have the doctor's testimony that there was no struggle, and therefore no button was torn off.
- A doctor's opinion that another doctor's opinion can combat and destroy! And then, to demonstrate that there was no struggle, Mr Saniel points to surprise. Who could have surprised Caffié? It wasn't the first person to come along, was it? Likewise, it wasn't the first person to sneak into the house, go in and out, escaping the concierge's surveillance. This person, of course, knew all about the habits of the house. What's more, he knew that to get Caffié to open the door after the clerk had left, you had to ring the bell first and then knock three times in a certain way. Who knew all that better than me?
Philis defended herself against her brother foot by foot, but little by little the confidence that had sustained her at first was weakening. At Saniel's, close to him, she was valiant; between her brother and her mother, in this room which had already seen their worries, not daring to raise her voice, she became confused and let herself be won over by the anxiety of her family:
- Really," she said, "we seem to be guilty and not innocent!
- And while we're here tormenting ourselves, the culprit is probably in perfect peace, laughing at the police investigations; he hadn't thought of that button, chance has put it in his game; luck is for him, it's against us... once again.
This was the complaint that most often came to Florentin's lips. Although he had never been a gambler, and for good reason, everything for him was decided by luck. Some people are born under a lucky star, others under an unlucky one; there are those who, in the battle of life, take the blows without getting discouraged, because they expect everything from tomorrow, just as there are those who falter, because they expect nothing good and know from experience that tomorrow will be for them what the present day is, what the day before was. He was one of them. What had been good since the battle began? Why had their father died at the very moment when, after a lot of hard work and perseverance, he had managed to get his foot on the ladder? A few more years and his children would have inherited a fortune and a glorious name, while he had left them only what he had always had: poverty. Why hadn't he been able to complete his studies himself, instead of becoming a poor businessman's clerk who was burdened with tedious work from morning till night, and so tired that he didn't have an hour's freedom left to do any useful work? Shouldn't he, like his comrades, have taken examinations that would have given him a position similar to those occupied by these comrades, neither more intelligent nor more courageous than he? Why, instead of finding a good man as a boss, which was not impossible, had he fallen on Caffié who had martyred and dumbed him down? Why had his mother, who had been born in the country, was strong and in good health, suddenly been struck down by paralysis? And why couldn't Philis, a beautiful girl as she was, cheerful in spite of everything, intelligent, endowed with all the qualities that make for a happy life in a household, find a husband free enough from prejudice and narrow-minded calculations to marry her? Why was it that, from morning to night, without rest or weariness, she worked bent over her table, or ran through the streets of Paris like a poor labourer looking for or putting off work?
- Why didn't I stay in America! he said.
- Since you were too unhappy, my poor boy!" said Madame Cormier, whose maternal heart had been stirred by this cry.
- Am I happier here? Will I be tomorrow? What does tomorrow hold for us, this tomorrow full of uncertainty and danger?
- Why do you want him to have only dangers?" said Philis in a conciliatory and caressing tone.
- You're always waiting for the right one.
- At least I hope so, and don't take it for granted that it's impossible. I'm not saying that life is always rosy, but it's not always black either; and I think it's like the seasons: after winter, which is ugly, I grant you, comes spring, summer and autumn.
- Well, if I had the money to travel, I'd go and spend the end of winter in a country where it would be less unpleasant than here, and above all less dangerous for my constitution.
- You're not saying that seriously, I hope?" exclaimed Madame Cormier.
- Quite seriously, on the contrary.
- We've hardly got together, and you're thinking of a new separation," said Madame Cormier sadly.
- Florentin isn't thinking of a separation," cried Philis, "he's thinking of running away.
- And why not?
- Because only guilty people run away.
- It's precisely the opposite; the intelligent guilty ones stay, by virtue of your axiom, and, as they're generally resolute people, they know in advance that they'll be able to face up to the danger; whereas the innocent ones, who are everyone else, timid people like me or the unlucky ones, lose their heads and run away, because they also know in advance that, if danger threatens them, it will crush them and they won't be able to escape. That's why I'd go back to America if I could pay my way: at least I'd be safe there.
There was a moment's silence, and everyone remained with their eyes fixed on their plates, as if they had no other concern than to finish their dinner.
- When I realised that this project wasn't feasible," Florentin soon continued, "I had another idea.
- Why do you have ideas?" asked Philis.
- I'd like you to be in my place, and we'll see if you don't have any.
- I assure you that I am, in your place, and that your concern is mine; it's just that it doesn't translate in the same way. So what's your idea?
- It's to go and see Valerius and tell him everything.
- And who can vouch for Valerius's discretion?" asked Madame Cormier. "Wouldn't that be the most reckless thing you could do? You don't play with a secret of this importance.
- Valérius is a good man.
- It's because he can't work when political, or rather patriotic, affairs are going badly that you're saying that.
- And why not? In a poor wretch who lives so meagrely from his work, isn't this concern and pride for his country the mark of an elevated heart?
- I'll give you that; but that's all the more reason to be careful with him," says Philis. Precisely because he may be what you think, we have to be cautious. You tell him what happened: he accepts it and he accepts your innocence, that's perfect; he won't betray your secret either deliberately or through clumsiness. But he doesn't accept it; he's looking for the other side of the story; he assumes that you wanted to deceive him; he suspects you; so won't he tell everything to the police commissioner in our neighbourhood? As far as I'm concerned, that's a danger he'd be mad to provoke.
- And, in your opinion, what should we do?
- Nothing; that is to say, wait, since the chances are a thousand to one that our worries, which we all exaggerate, will never come true.
- Well, let's wait," he says; "what's more, I like it that way; at least I'm not responsible. Whatever happens will happen.
XIX
For the button found at Caffié's to lead us to the murderer, it had to have come from the hands of a Parisian tailor, or at least a French one, and the trousers had not been sold by a ready-made clothes shop, where neither the names nor the memories of passing buyers are kept.
The police's task was therefore difficult, as were their chances of success.
Had they contacted each of the three thousand tailors in Paris, as Saniel imagined, to find out which of them used Coq and Couronne buttons marked A. P., she would really have been looking for a needle in a haystack.
But that wasn't the way she went about it: instead of going after those who used these buttons, she looked for those who made or sold them, and immediately, without going any further than the Bottin , she found this manufacturer: A. Pélinotte, manufacture de boutons métal pour pantalons, marque de fabrique A. P., Couronne et Coq, faubourg du Temple.
At first, the manufacturer was rather reluctant to answer the questions of the agent who went to his home, but when he began to understand that he could benefit from the case, as a good businessman, young and active as he was, he made his books and his salesmen available to the court. His claim, in fact, was that his buttons, thanks to a copper bar around which the thread was wound instead of passing through holes, never cut the thread and that they were unbreakable: when they did jump, it was with a piece of the fabric. What better justification for his claims, what better claim than a button ripped off with a piece of the murderer's trousers? The case had to go to trial and all the newspapers would be talking about A. P. buttons. He would have paid a good price for this.
All that was asked of him was his help and, after a few days, the search could begin, guided by a list whose accuracy spared unnecessary steps.
One morning, a security guard arrived on the Avenue de Clichy and found the tailor Valérius in his shop, busy reading his newspaper. For it was not only when the country was in danger that Valérius was passionate about reading newspapers, it was every morning and every evening: when political affairs were going badly, he took the worries they caused him so tragically that he needed conversation and, above all, drinks at the café to distract him, and then, of course, he didn't work; On the contrary, when things were going more or less well, no one was more diligent than he, he never left his little shop and, when he had finished reading the morning paper, he only left his scissors to sit down at his sewing machine and, while pressing the pedal, he brooded over what he had read: politics, polemics, news, courts and soaps. 
Nothing published in the newspapers escaped his notice, so when the agent began to speak, he knew at once what was going to be discussed:
- Is it for the Rue Sainte-Anne case that you need this information?
- Frankly, yes.
- Well, frankly too, I don't know whether professional secrecy allows me to answer you.
The agent, who was no fool, realised at once who he was dealing with, and instead of giving in to the urge to laugh provoked by this nobly made reply, by this good man whose long bushy black beard and bald head accentuated his seriousness, he put on a dignified face:
- It's up for discussion," he said.
- So let's talk: a customer who trusts my probity and discretion gives me a pair of trousers to make; he pays me as he should, without cutting any corners and on the day he says; things are done fairly between us; I give him a good pair of trousers honestly made, he pays me in good money. We're square; do I then have the right, through careless words or otherwise, to provide weapons against him? This is a delicate matter.
- Do you put the interests of the individual above those of society?
- When it comes to professional secrecy, yes. Where would we go if lawyers, notaries, doctors, confessors and tailors were to compromise on this point of doctrine? To anarchy, quite simply, and, in the end, it would be the interests of society that would suffer.
The agent, who had no time to lose, was getting impatient.
- I would point out to you," he said, "that a tailor, however important his profession, is not in quite the same position as a doctor or a confessor, who do not keep accounts of what their clients entrust to them. Do you have a book in which you record your customers' orders?
- I certainly do.
- So if, misguided and persevering in a theory pushed to the extreme, you don't want to answer my questions, I would just have to go and get the local superintendent who, by virtue of the powers conferred on him by law, would seize your books...
- That would be violence, and my responsibility would be waived.
- And on the basis of these books, the examining magistrate would see who you had supplied with trousers made from this material; all that would be left to do would be to find out in what interest you had sought to mislead the law.
Saying this, he took a small box from his pocket and, unfolding a piece of tissue paper, pulled out a button to which a piece of navy blue fabric was attached.
Valérius, who was in no way moved by the Commissioner's threat, for he was a man who braved martyrdom, looked curiously at the box; when the agent delicately took out the button, he let out a movement of lively surprise.
- You see," exclaimed the officer, "that you know this fabric!
- Will you allow me to look at it?
- Gladly, but on condition that you don't touch it; it's precious.
Valérius took the box and, approaching the front, looked at the button and the piece of cloth.
- It's a button marked A. P., as you can see, and we know that you use these buttons.
- I won't deny it; they're good buttons, and I only give good buttons to my customers.
Returning the box to the agent, he picked up a large book which he began to leaf through; pieces of cloth were stuck to the pages, and beside them were a few lines of thick writing. When he came to a page with a piece of blue cloth on it, he took the boot and compared it with the button, looking at it in daylight.
- Sir," he cried, "I'm going to tell you some serious things.
- I am listening.
- We have the murderer of the Rue Sainte-Anne, and it is I who will give you the means of discovering him.
- Did you make a pair of trousers from this fabric? I've made three; but there's only one that interests you, that of the murderer. I told you earlier that professional secrecy prevented me from answering your questions; but what I've just seen clears my conscience. As I explained to you, when I have made a good pair of trousers for a customer who has paid me good money for them, I don't think I have the right to reveal that customer's affairs to anyone in the world, including the law.
- I understand," interrupted the impatient agent.
- But this reservation on my part is based on reciprocity: a good customer deserves a good tailor. If the customer is not good, the reciprocity ceases, or rather it continues on another ground, that of war: someone has behaved badly towards me, I return the favour. The trousers to which this fabric belongs," he showed the button, "I made them for a... a private individual I didn't know, who introduced himself to me as an Alsatian, which I accepted all the more readily because he spoke with a strong foreign accent. I don't have to tell you how I looked after these trousers: I'm a patriot, sir. It had been agreed that they would be paid for on delivery. When the delivery took place, the young apprentice who was in charge of it had the weakness not to ask for the money: they were going to bring it to me straight away, in other words, all the tricks of bad debtors. I ran there. I was poorly received and was unable to get anything; it would be for the next day without fail. The next day they threatened to throw me down the stairs. The day after that, they moved out at the drop of a hat. Nobody left. Disappeared; without leaving an address, as you might expect.
- And this customer?
- I'll give you his name without any hesitation: Fritzner, not an Alsatian, as I thought, but definitely a Prussian, who certainly did it; his disappearance the day after the crime is proof of that.
- You say you couldn't get his address?
- But you, who have other means than me, will find it: twenty-seven to thirty years old, average height, blue eyes, blond beard and a blue suit of this fabric.
The agent wrote this description in his notebook as the tailor gave it.
- If he didn't leave Paris with the stolen thirty-five thousand francs, we'll find him," he said, "and it'll be thanks to you.
- I'm happy to be of use to you.
The agent was about to leave; he changed his mind:
- You said you'd made three suits from this fabric.
- Yes, but only the Fritzner suit counts; the other two were made for honest people who are well known in the neighbourhood and who paid me fairly.
- Since they can't be bothered, you shouldn't have any qualms about naming them: I'm not asking you for their names for the sake of justice, but for my own sake; they'll look good in my report and prove that my investigations were thorough.
- One is a shopkeeper in the Rue Truffaut, called M. Blanchet; the other is a young man from America called M. Florentin Cormier.
- Did you say Florentin Cormier?" asked the agent, who remembered that this was the name of a person we had seen at Caffié's on the day of the crime.
- Not exactly; it's the first time I've dressed him; but I know his mother and sister, who have lived in the rue des Moines for five or six years at least; very honest people who work hard and owe nothing to anyone.
The next morning, at about ten o'clock, shortly after Philis had left, Florentin, who was reading the newspaper in the dining room while Madame Cormier was preparing breakfast, heard footsteps, intended to be light, stop on the landing in front of their door. Her ear had become too accustomed to the noises of the house for a few days to be mistaken: there was a hesitation or a precaution in these footsteps that obviously betrayed a stranger; and with the few relations they had, a stranger was surely an enemy -- the one he was expecting.
There was no need to hesitate slowly, and assuming an air of indifference, he went to open the door.
He found before him a man of about forty, dressed in a short jacket, wearing a bowler hat, with a fine, affable face.
- Mr Florentin Cormier?
- Yes, that's right.
He ushered him into the dining room.
- The examining magistrate asks you to come to his chambers.
Madame Cormier came out of the kitchen to hear these words, and if Florentin, who was looking at her, hadn't made an energetic gesture, she would have given herself away; the words on her lips: "You've come to arrest my son!
- And can you tell me what case the examining magistrate has summoned me for?
- The Caffié case.
- And what time must I appear before the examining magistrate?
- At once.
- But my son hasn't had breakfast!" cried Madame Cormier. At least have something to eat before you go, my dear boy.
- There's no need.
He made a sign to her not to insist: his throat was too tight to swallow a piece of bread, and it was important not to betray his emotion in front of this agent.
- I'm all yours," he said.
Turning to his mother, he kissed her, but lightly, without effusiveness, as if he were only going out for a short absence.
- See you soon!
She was distraught, but realising that she was compromising her son if she gave in, she restrained herself.
XX
Since it was a role he was playing, Florentin told himself that he had to play it to the end to the best of his ability, by getting into the skin of the character he wanted to be, and that role was that of witness.
He had been Caffié's clerk; the law wanted to question him about his former boss, nothing could be more natural; it was that alone, and that alone, that he could accept; consequently, he had to take an interest in the police investigations, and be curious to find out where they stood.
This was the question he put to the constable as they walked side by side down the street:
- Are you making any progress on the Caffié case?
- I don't know anything about it," said the agent, who thought it prudent to keep a low profile, "and I don't know any more than what's in the papers.
On leaving his mother's house, Florentin had noticed a man on the opposite pavement who seemed to be in a station; after a few minutes, as they turned a corner, he saw that the man was following them at a certain distance: so it wasn't a simple appearance before the examining magistrate, because you don't take such precautions with a witness.
When they arrived at Place Clichy, the officer asked him if he wanted to get into the car, but he didn't accept. What was the point? It was an unnecessary expense.
Then he saw the policeman raise his hat as if greeting someone, but the greeting was certainly not addressed to anyone; and immediately the man who had been following them drew closer. This hat-trick was a signal: as they entered the crowd from the deserted districts of Batignolles, it was feared that he would try to escape; the character of the arrest became more pronounced.
After the forebodings and fears that had tormented him over the last few days, this was not surprising, but the fact that they were being lenient with him meant that everything had not yet been decided: he therefore had to defend himself as best he could. Panicked before the danger, he felt less weak now that he was in it.
On arriving at the Palais de Justice, he was immediately ushered into the examining magistrate's chambers. But the judge did not concern himself with him immediately: he was in the middle of questioning a woman; he continued, and Florentin was able to examine him surreptitiously: he was an elegant, comfortable-looking man, still young, who looked more like a boulevardier or a sportsman than a magistrate, with nothing solemn or imposing about him.
As he continued his questioning, he too was examining Florentin, but with a quick glance, without insisting, as if by chance, and simply because his eyes met his. In front of a table, a clerk was writing, and near the door two gendarmes were waiting with the bored and empty faces of people who are elsewhere.
Soon the examining magistrate looked up at them:
- You may take the accused away.
Then, turning to Florentin, he asked him for his surname, first names and address.
- You used to be a clerk for the business agent Caffié; why did you leave him?
- Because my work was too hard.
- Are you afraid of work?
- No, when it's not excessive; it was at Mr Caffié's and didn't leave me time to work for myself: I had to arrive at the office at eight in the morning, I had lunch there and didn't leave until seven to go and have dinner with my mother in Batignolles; I had an hour and a half for that; at half past eight I had to be back and I stayed until ten or ten thirty. In accepting this position I had thought that I would be able to complete my education, which had been interrupted by my father's death, go to law school, and become something better than a miserable businessman's clerk; this was not possible with Mr Caffié: I left him, and it was for this reason alone that we parted.
- Where did you go next?
This was the tricky question, the one that Florentin dreaded, because it could arouse prejudices against him that nothing would destroy; however, he could not fail to answer, because what he himself would not say others would reveal: an investigation on this point was too easy.
- At the home of another businessman, Mr Savoureux, in the rue de la Victoire, where I was not supposed to work in the evenings. I stayed there for about three months and then left for America.
- Why did you do that?
- Because, when I wanted to get down to serious work, I realised that my studies had been interrupted for too long for me to be able to resume them: I had forgotten a good deal of what I had learned badly; I would probably fail my baccalauréat, and I wouldn't be able to start my law studies until it was too late. I left France for America, where I hoped to find a good position.
- You came back to Paris.
- Three weeks ago.
- And you went to Caffié?
- Yes, I went.
- What did I do?
- Ask him for a certificate to replace the one he gave me that I lost.
- That was the day of the crime.
- Yes, it was.
- At what time?
- I arrived at his house at about two forty-five and left at about half past three.
- Did he give you the certificate you asked for?
- Yes, here it is.
And, taking it out of his pocket, he presented it to the examining magistrate: it was a certificate stating that, throughout the time that Mr Florentin Cormier had been his clerk, Caffié had had nothing but praise for him, for his work and for his accuracy and probity.
- And you didn't go back to his house that evening? asked the examining magistrate.
- Why would I have gone back? I'd got what I wanted.
- So did you or did you not go back?
- I didn't go back.
- Do you remember what you did when you left Caffié's?
If Florentin had had the slightest illusion about his appearance before the examining magistrate, the way his interrogation was conducted would have taken it away from him: he was not being questioned as a witness, he was being charged; he did not have to enlighten justice, he had to defend himself.
- Perfectly," he said; "it wasn't that long ago. On leaving the rue Sainte-Anne, as I had nothing to do, I went down to the quays and read from the Port Royal to the Institut; but just then a shower came down and I went back up to Les Batignolles where I stayed with my mother.
- What time was it when you arrived at your mother's house?
- Five o'clock and a few minutes.
- Can't you be more precise?
- Quarter past five, give or take a few minutes.
- And you didn't go out again?
- No, I did not.
- Did anyone come to your mother's house after you went in?
- Nobody; my sister came home at seven o'clock, as she always does when she comes back from her lesson.
- Before going up to your house, did you speak to any of the tenants?
- No, I didn't.
There was a pause and Florentin felt the judge's eyes fixed on him with a disturbing persistence: it seemed that this gaze, which enveloped him from head to toe, wanted to undress him.
- Another thing," said the examining magistrate; "you didn't lose a button on your trousers while you were at Caffié's, did you?
Florentin had been waiting for this question; and for a long time he had been considering the answer he would give. Denial was impossible. It would be too easy to convince him that he was lying, because the very fact that he was asked would mean that he had proof that the button belonged to him. So he had to confess the truth, however serious it might be.
- Yes," he said, "and this is how...
He recounted in detail the story of the file on the highest shelf of a locker; his near-fall; the removal of the button, which he had only noticed in the street.
The examining magistrate opened a drawer and took out a small box from which he took a button which he presented to Florentin.
- Is this it?" he asked.
Florentin looked at him.
- It's rather difficult to answer," he said at last; "one button looks like another.
- Not always.
- For that, I'd have to have noticed the shape of the one I lost, and I didn't pay any attention to it: it seems to me that no one knows exactly how and what the buttons on it are made of.
The examining magistrate looked at him again:
- But aren't the trousers you have on today the same as the one from which this button was torn off?
- It's the one I was wearing the day I went to see Mr Caffié.
- So it's quite simple to compare the button I'm showing you with those on your trousers, and your answer becomes easy.
It was impossible to escape this check.
- Unbutton your waistcoat," said the judge, "and make your comparison carefully, as carefully as you see fit: the question is important.
Florentin was only too aware of the importance of this question; but, as it was put to him, he could not but answer frankly.
He unbuttoned his waistcoat and compared the button he found with his own.
- I think this is the button I lost," he said.
Although he had tried hard not to betray his anguish, he felt that his voice was trembling and that it had a hoarse accent; then he wanted to explain this emotion:
- This is a truly terrible chance for me," he said.
The examining magistrate did not reply.
- But just because I lost a button at Mr Caffié's, it doesn't follow that the button was ripped off me in a struggle!
- You have your system, you'll put it forward: this is not the place to do so; I have only one question left to ask you: Which button did you replace the one you lost?
- Any button you could find.
- Who sewed it?
- I did.
- Are you in the habit of sewing on your own buttons?
Although the examining magistrate did not insist on this last question, either in tone or form, Florentin could see the accusation that his answer was going to make.
- Sometimes," he said.
- However, when you came home you found your mother, you told me. Did she have any reason for not being able to sew on the button herself?
- I didn't ask her to do it.
- But when she saw you sewing it on, didn't she take the needle out of your hand?
- She didn't see me.
- Why didn't she see you?
- She was busy preparing our dinner.
- That's enough.
- I was in the hallway of our flat, where they've made a bed for me since my return; my mother was in the kitchen.
- Don't the kitchen and the hall communicate?
- The door was closed.
A whole stream of words rose to his lips to protest against the conclusions that seemed to result from his answers, but he stopped; he saw himself caught in a cog, and all his efforts to free himself from it only embraced him more tightly.
Since he was no longer being questioned, the best thing, it seemed, was to say nothing; and he remained silent for quite a long time, the length of which he only vaguely appreciated: the judge spoke in a low voice, the clerk wrote quickly, and all he heard was a monotonous murmur that was torn apart by the squeaking of a quill on the paper.
- Your examination will be read to you," said the judge.
