He wanted to devote all his attention to this reading, but he soon lost the thread: when the questions moved from one fact to another, he stayed with the one that had just been examined and arrived too late at the one that was being dealt with: the impression he got, however, was that what he had said had been faithfully reproduced or summarised; he signed.
- Now," said the examining magistrate, "my duty obliges me, in the presence of the charges that emerge from your interrogation, to issue a detention order against you.
Florentin received the blow without flinching.
- I know," he said, "that all the protests I would make would have no effect at the moment, so I'll spare you them; but I have a favour to ask of you... a grace: to allow me to announce my arrest to my mother and my sister... who love me dearly. Oh! you will read my letter.
- Do so, sir.
XXI
After the departure of her son and the agent, Madame Cormier had been devastated: her son! her Florentine! the poor child! and she had sunk into despair.
Hadn't they suffered enough! They needed this new ordeal! Why was life so mercilessly cruel to them?
One complaint after another had brought her back year after year to the death of her husband, the starting point of their misfortunes. What had been good since that day? After so many others, this latest blow was the hardest and crushed her. Ah, why hadn't Dr Saniel let her die? At least she wouldn't have seen this final catastrophe, this disgrace: her son accused of murder, in prison, at the assizes!
And she would repeat these complaints aloud, weeping, with the relief of a pain that has been abandoned: she was alone in her deserted home; there was no one to hear her, to look at her, to scold her.
For when she allowed herself to be overcome by grief, Philis always scolded her, tenderly it's true, with gentle words and caresses, but in the end she scolded her: a constant watch, not a minute's freedom when she was at home. If a sigh escaped her, if her lips twitched, if her eyes were clouded with sadness, Philis would immediately notice and, with a glance and a word: "Mummy", she would remind her not to let herself go.
And why shouldn't she? Philis was really unreasonable, wanting people never to complain about life and the unfairness of things. To resist, you need nerves that allow resistance, and she didn't have those strong nerves, poor woman that she was.
Now that she was alone, she could at least cry at her ease, and complain and moan.
She wept and she moaned, but there came a time when, after having gone to the extremes of despair that saw her son condemned as a murderer and executed, she stopped and wondered if she wasn't going too far. It was no longer Philis who was telling her that it was wrong to give up, it was herself.
Being called before the examining magistrate did not mean that Florentin should not return and that he would be lost, as her maternal panic had imagined.
Without knowing much about the ways of the law, she believed that one did not deal with people under arrest in the way that this officer had done: "Monsieur le juge d'instruction vous prie de passer à son cabinet" ("The examining magistrate asks you to come to his chambers"); these were not the manners of a policeman.
He was coming back; she could certainly wait for him.
And she had waited for him without wanting to have breakfast; the poor child would be happy, when he returned, not to sit down to dinner on his own. Besides, she was too deeply upset to be able to eat. She had carefully covered the coal in the stove with ashes to keep her mutton beans warm: it was her favourite dish, along with turnips, and she had found some excellent, tender, fresh turnips in the morning at the market; how hungry he would be!
Time had gone by, minutes, hours, and he hadn't arrived; other coals had had to be lit, covered too, and despite all these precautions the sauce had turned sour: what a bore!
And although Florentin had a lot to say about Caffié, and although no one thought to interrupt him when he was speaking, it was becoming more and more impossible to believe that something extraordinary had not happened. Had he been arrested? But then what would become of him?
She had fallen back into a fit of tears and despair, but this time she didn't feel any relief at being alone; on the contrary, she would have liked Philis to be there: with her one wouldn't lose one's head; she always knew how to get out of trouble; she found something to say; perhaps, after all, things weren't as serious as they seemed.
Fortunately, she wasn't going to be home late that day: all we had to do was wait for her and not despair until she arrived.
She waited, and over the years she had become so accustomed to relying on her daughter for everything, that she was almost reassured to think that she would arrive.
As quickly as she could, Madame Cormier went to open the door and was stunned to see her daughter's convulsed face: Philis had obviously been surprised by the sudden opening of the door.
- So you know everything," cried Madame Cormier.
Philis took her in her arms and led her into the dining room where she sat her down:
- Calm down," she said, "don't worry, we won't keep him.
- Do you have a way?
- We'll find a way; I promise you we won't keep him.
- Are you sure?
- I promise.
- You're giving me my life back. But how did you know?
- He wrote to me: as I was passing by, the concierge gave me his letter, which had just arrived.
- What did it say?
Madame Cormier took the letter Philis held out to her, but the paper shook so badly in her agitated hand that she couldn't read it.
- Read it to me.
Philis took it back and read:
"Dear little sister,
"After hearing me out, the examining magistrate is keeping me on. Soften the pain of this blow for Mum; make her understand that it's impossible not to recognise the falsity of this accusation soon, and on your side, do your utmost to make this falsity obvious, while on mine, I'm going to work to prove my innocence.
"Give my love to your poor mother and find consolation for her in your tenderness, your strength and your kindness; mine will be to think that you are close to her, dear beloved little sister.
"FLORENTIN".
- And it is this brave boy who is accused of murder!" cried Madame Cormier, bursting into tears.
It took Philis several minutes to calm this crisis down a little.
- It's him you have to think about, Mum; let's not give up.
- You're going to do something, aren't you, my little Philis?
- I'm going to find Mr Saniel.
- Mr Saniel is a doctor, not a lawyer.
- It's precisely as a doctor that M. Saniel can save Florentin. He knows that Caffié was killed without a struggle between him and his killer, and therefore without the button being torn off. Let him say so, let him prove it to the examining magistrate and Florentin's innocence will be proven. I'm going to his place.
- Please don't leave me alone for too long.
- I'll be right back.
Philis ran down from Les Batignolles to the rue Louis-le-Grand. When she rang the doorbell, Joseph, who had taken his place in the antechamber, opened it quickly and, as Saniel had no one with her, she went straight into his study.
- What's the matter?" he asked, seeing her agitation.
- My brother's been arrested.
- Ah, the poor boy.
What Saniel had said to Philis to explain that this arrest could not take place was sincere, he believed it, and even did more than believe it, he wanted it. When he had decided to kill Caffié, he had not believed that justice could ever find someone guilty: it would be a crime that would go unpunished, like so many others, and no one would be bothered. But now one had been arrested, and the culprit was the brother of the woman he loved. He was taken aback for a moment.
- How was he arrested?" he asked, as much to find out as to recover.
She told him what she knew and read Florentin's letter.
- Your brother's a good boy," he said, as if talking to himself.
- You're going to save him.
- How can I do that?
It was a cry that escaped her without her understanding its meaning, without her guessing the expression of worried curiosity in the gaze he had fixed on her.
- Who do you want me to talk to if not you? Aren't you everything to me! My support, my guidance, my advice, my God!
She explained what she wanted from him.
Once again, an exclamation escaped Saniel:
- You want me to go to the examining magistrate!
- Who better than you to explain how things happened?
Saniel, who had recovered from his initial surprise, didn't flinch; obviously she was speaking in complete good faith, without suspecting anything, and it would be madness to look for anything other than what she was saying.
- But that's not how you present yourself to an examining magistrate," he replied.
- Why don't you go and meet his summons, since you know things that could enlighten him?
- Is it really wise to anticipate this summons? By going to see him, I am defending your brother...
- That is precisely what I am asking you.
- ... And, by the very fact that I am presenting myself as your defender, I am taking away the weight of my testimony, which would have more authority if it were that of a simple witness.
- But when will you be asked for this statement? Think of Florentin's suffering during this waiting period, of Mum's suffering, of my suffering. He could lose his mind. He could kill himself. His soul isn't strong, and Mum's isn't very strong either. Will she be able to withstand everything the newspapers are going to publish? There's that unfortunate past that we'll be reminded of and that will bring shame on us.
Saniel hesitated for a moment.
- Well, I'll go," he said, "not this evening, it's too late, but tomorrow morning.
- Oh, dear Victor," she cried, hugging him, "I knew you would save him: we owe you his life, as we already owe you our mother's, as I owe you my happiness; am I not right to say that you are my God?
When she had left to return to her mother as soon as possible, he had a moment of self-reflection that made him regret his weakness; for it was indeed a weakness, a silly sentimentality, unworthy of a strong man who would not have allowed himself to be touched and drawn away in this way. What need was there for him to go and provoke danger when he could remain quite calm, without anyone thinking about him? Was this not madness? Justice wanted a culprit; public curiosity needed one: why take away the one they had? If he succeeded, wouldn't they look for another? That was imprudence and, to put it bluntly, insanity. Now that he was no longer under the influence of Philis's beautiful grieving eyes, he was not going to commit this imprudence. Throughout the evening he consolidated this idea, and when he went to bed his mind was made up: he would not go to the examining magistrate.
But when he woke up he was surprised to find that the resolution of the evening was no longer the resolution of the morning, and that this dualism of personality which had already struck him was asserting itself once again: it was at night that he had resolved to kill Caffié and in the evening that he had carried it out; it was in the morning that he had abandoned the idea, just as it was in the morning that he was going back on the decision he had taken the day before not to go to the poor boy's aid. What on earth was the will of man, undulating like the sea and variable like the wind, that he had been foolish enough to believe was so firm at home?
At midday, he arrived at the Palais de Justice and handed the examining magistrate his card, on which he had simply written three words: "For the Caffié case".
Almost immediately he was received, and he briefly explained how, in his opinion, Caffié had been killed in a quick and lightning death, by a firm yet intelligent hand, that of a professional killer.
- That's the conclusion of your report," said the examining magistrate.
- What I couldn't say in my report, because I wasn't aware of the button's discovery and the conclusions it led to, was that there was no struggle, as is supposed, between the killer and his victim.
And he demonstrated medically how this struggle had been impossible.
The examining magistrate listened attentively, without a word, without a gesture of interruption.
- Do you know this young man?
- I've only seen him once, but I know his mother, whom I looked after, and it was at her instigation that I decided to present these observations to you.
- No doubt they have their value, but I would point out that they tend to do nothing less than destroy our hypothesis.
- If it is unfounded!
- I would point out to you that you are negative, Doctor, not suggestive. We have a culprit and you have none. Do you see one?
Saniel thought he realised that the examining magistrate was looking at him with worrying persistence:
- No," he said briskly.
Then, having risen, he added more calmly:
- That's not my role.
All he had to do was withdraw, which he did, and as he followed the long, sonorous hallway he told himself that the magistrate was right: he had a guilty man, he believed; why would he let him go?
As far as he was concerned, he had done what he could.
Part Two
I
Saniel had passed the first tests of his two competitions so brilliantly that the results were not in doubt for either of them. In defending his thesis for the agrégation, he had forced his audience to admire him: by turns aggressive, snarling, ironic and eloquent, he had so effectively reduced his opponent to the brink that the latter, crushed in the end, had been unable to reply. In his hour-long lecture on death in heart disease, he had displayed such clarity of demonstration, such beautiful sobriety of speech, such firm, simple, confident scientific eloquence, that his most unfair opponents had had to recognise that he possessed the qualities of the great teachers: a brute but someone. He had also been brutal in the tests for the hospital competitive examination: as his diagnosis was opposed to that of his judges, he had been unsparing, without compliments, simply for the love of truth, and he had accumulated so much strong evidence in support of his opinion, such tight logic, such compelling argumentation, that the jury had decided to return to the patient's bed, and that after a new examination, in which he had acknowledged his error, the president had said to him: "When I am ill, it is you who will look after me".
What did Caffié's death weigh in the balance? So little, in fact, that it didn't count at all and would have been of no concern to him, had it not been for the fact that it was involved in the prosecution that was going to put Florentin on trial.
With this fact out of the way, the old businessman's death rarely passed through his mind: he had something else in mind, really, than this memory, which was imposed neither by regret nor by remorse, and it was not at the moment when he was nearing his goal that he was going, with Caffié, to embarrass or sadden his triumph.
A little before the two-month period during which the poste restante keeps the letters was about to expire, he had gone to claim those containing the thirty thousand francs from the various offices to which he had addressed them, and, putting the notes back in new envelopes, he had sent them out as he had done the first time, but starting with the offices last listed on his almanac, and then he had thought no more about it.
What did he need with this money which, in reality, was a hindrance to him? Since he had it, his habits had remained the same, except that he no longer fought his creditors. He would have considered himself a coward and a wretch if he had used it to give himself a sense of well-being which poverty had hitherto deprived him of, and the idea had not even occurred to him. As in the days of distress, he continued to have lunch with the portion of plain beef or fricandeau au jus that Joseph would fetch for him from the corner greengrocer's, and the only change to the tradition was that now you paid in cash and by force, because the greengrocer, who demanded money when you didn't offer him any, no longer wanted any since we no longer asked for credit: "We'll put it on the bill. And what he did for the material things in life was repeated for everything; not out of prudence, not so as not to attract attention, but simply because for him there was no desire or need to change anything that had been done in the past: his ambition was elsewhere and higher than in the small satisfactions, very small for him, that money can give.
Had he not had to worry about Florentin, he would certainly have spent whole days, perhaps even series of days, without giving a thought to Caffié; but Florentin and especially Philis reminded him that the tranquillity he now enjoyed was due to Caffié's death, and it was relatively disturbed by it.
He did not believe that the law would now come looking for him: everything came together to confirm his security. What he had so painstakingly arranged would have succeeded, and the only imprudence that a moment of aberration had caused him to commit did not seem to have been noticed; no one had reported his presence at the café opposite Caffié's house, and no one had been surprised by his persistence in staying there at such a characteristic hour.
But it was not enough for him to be safe from these searches; he had to prevent Florentin from being unjustly condemned for a crime of which he was innocent: It was bad enough that the poor boy was imprisoned and that his sister was in despair, his mother sick with grief; but for him to be sent to the scaffold or to prison would be too much; in itself, Caffié's death would be of little consequence: it would become atrocious if it led to such an outcome. 
He could not, he did not want that to happen, and he had to do everything he could, not only to prevent the sentence from being passed, but also to prevent the prison sentence from being prolonged.
It was to this feeling that he had obeyed when he went to explain to the examining magistrate that the charges against Florentin resulting from the discovery of the button were groundless, since there had been no struggle; but the way in which his intervention had been received, by showing him that justice was not prepared to allow his hypothesis to be disturbed by a simple medical demonstration, had thrown him into disquiet and perplexity.
Undoubtedly, someone else in his place would have let things continue as they were and, since justice had a culprit with whom it was satisfied, would have done nothing to take him away; while it followed its hypothesis to prove the guilt of the one it had, it did not look elsewhere; when it had him convicted, all would be over; the Caffié affair was buried, just as Caffié himself was buried; silence came with oblivion and security for him. The crime had been punished, the satisfied public conscience demanded nothing more, not even to know if the debt had been paid by the person who really owed it. But he was not that other person, and if he thought the old rascal's death was legitimate, it was on condition that Florentin, who had benefited nothing from it, was not made to pay.
Florentin therefore had to be released as soon as possible, and it was his duty to do so, his imperative duty, -- not only to Philis, but also to himself.
As the case had developed after the approach to the examining magistrate, he had understood and had made Philis understand, distraught, that until the day Florentin appeared before the jury, he could do little or nothing directly. On that day, it's true, he would regain his authority and, speaking in the name of science, he would prove to the jurors that the story of the button was an invention of desperate policemen that couldn't stand up to scrutiny: but until then, the poor boy remained at Mazas, and, however sure we might be of an acquittal at that moment, it would be better to have the case dismissed immediately, if we could obtain it.
For that, the intervention and direction of a doctor were of little use; what was needed was that of a lawyer.
But which one? Philis would have liked them to go to the most illustrious, the one who, by his talent, authority and success, was bound to win all her cases. But he had told her that these miracle-workers probably didn't exist at the bar any more than in medicine, where you couldn't call a doctor who didn't lose patients, and that, if he did exist, neither he nor she had the large sum of money to pay him. In truth, he would have gladly given up the thirty thousand francs that the post office was keeping, or a large part of that sum, so that Florentin could be set free; but, apart from the fact that it would have been imprudent to take the notes out of their hiding place at this moment, he could not admit that he had thirty thousand francs or even ten thousand: how would he have obtained them? From the most illustrious of lawyers, they had therefore had to go down to a modest one, and as Saniel did not recognise any competence in making this choice, he had decided with Philis to consult Brigard, who, better than anyone else, having made a whole army of lawyers over the last thirty years, was qualified to find and indicate a good one.
As usual, he had been received affectionately by Crozat, who had scolded him for making himself so scarce, and, as usual too, so as not to disturb the discussion by making a noisy entrance, he had remained standing by the door: when the meeting was over, he would talk to Brigard alone.
That evening, it was a phrase from Chateaubriand that served as the theme for the speeches: "The tiger kills and sleeps; man kills and watches", and as he listened to the developments to which it lent itself, Saniel said to himself that it was a real pity not to be able to respond to all this rhetoric with a simple fact of personal experience: never had he slept so well, so peacefully, as since, with the death of Caffié, he had rid himself of all the worries that in recent months had so tormented and shortened his sleep. Glady had particularly distinguished himself and, from the shock of this antithesis, he had conjured up images that had been warmly applauded or underlined by small cries of admiration from which Brigard gave the signal. Through the smoke, Saniel had looked for Nougarède, to see what effect his rival's success was having on him, but he hadn't found him.
In the end, Brigard summed up the discussion by observing that nothing better proved the power of human conscience than this difference between man and beast; then, after the jugs of beer had been emptied, everyone withdrew, Glady first, the fear of having to endure another assault from Saniel taking precedence over the joy of tasting his triumph and that of escaping a loan.
So Saniel, left alone with Brigard and Crozat, made his request.
- But it's the Caffié affair?
- Precisely.
And at length he explained his interest in Florentin, the son of one of his clients, and the situation of this client.
- Well, my dear, my advice to you is to entrust the case to Nougarède. You might say to me: Nougarède is this and that. Anything you say, if your objections go back two years; at the time, I agree, they were well-founded: a little hollow and empty, it's true. But since then he has come into his own; his words have lost none of their catchy charm, and his mind has grown stronger; he has gained in breadth as well as depth; in short, in my opinion, he is the man you need. I heard him in his last two cases at the assizes; with his southern loquaciousness, his seductive and cuddly manner, the sympathy he inspires at first sight, the warmth, the emotion, the tenderness he uses without abusing it, he won over the jury. No doubt he is not a master; but can your client afford a master, and would this master, busy with fifty important cases, give himself to yours as Nougarède will? Not to mention the fact that Nougarède will be influenced by you and me, and that he will try to get the case dismissed if possible, which will be better than an acquittal.
Crozat agreed, recommending that he go and see Nougarède the next day:
- In the morning, right? because after the Palace Nougarède is all tied up with his marriage, which, as you can see, has prevented him from coming this evening.
- What do you mean? Nougarède is getting married?" cried Saniel, surprised that his favourite disciple should give such a denial of his master's teachings and examples.
- My God, yes; you mustn't hold it against him too much. He suffers the fatalities of a special environment. Unbeknownst to us, Nougarède, it can now be said and even should be said, was the happy lover of a charming young woman, the daughter of one of our most graceful genre actresses and brought up in a fashionable convent; you can see the situation. From this affair was born a child, a delicious little boy. It seemed quite natural, didn't it, that they should live as husband and wife, since they loved each other, and not weaken by legal ties the strength of those that bound them to this child. But there was the mother, an actress as I told you, who in this capacity, because of her past and her background, wanted her daughter to receive all the sacraments that the law and the Church can confer. She manoeuvred so well that poor Nougarède gave in; he went to the town hall, he went to church, he legitimised the child, and even accepted a dowry of two hundred thousand francs. I pity him, the unfortunate man; but I confess that I have the weakness not to condemn him as he would have deserved if he had married in an honest environment.
Saniel had been a little surprised by these similarities with the charming young woman Caffié had proposed to him. The daughter of one of our most graceful genre comediennes, brought up in a fashionable convent, mother of a delicious little boy, endowed with two hundred thousand francs: it was a curious meeting, to say the least; but, if it was a question of one and the same woman, he was not angry to see that Nougarède had been less difficult than he had been.
II
On his way to Nougarède's, Saniel vaguely imagined that the lawyer would tell him that an acquittal was certain if Florentin went to trial, and even that a dismissal was likely. But his hopes had not been fulfilled.
- The adventure with the button wouldn't have happened to you or me in the same serious way as it did to this boy; we don't have any priors on which to base presumptions, but he does: the forty-five francs that constitute embezzlement by a hired man will certainly be the starting point for the prosecution; it starts with a weakness, ends with a crime. He begins with the portrait of the clerk of yesteryear, hard-working, exact, scrupulous, content with little and finding satisfaction in duty done; then, in contrast, he moves on to the clerk of today: as irregular in his work as in his conduct, consumed by needs, in a hurry to enjoy himself, dissatisfied with everything and everyone, with others as well as with himself. "What more striking example could there be than the one you have before you today, gentlemen of the jury? Here he is, the irregular of whom I spoke; with no special education, he has the unexpected good fortune to find an honourable position; but he has to work and work bothers him, above all because it prevents him from enjoying himself; what he wants are the pleasures of worldly life, that of the happy people of this world whom he envies. He leaves his situation for another that will give him the freedom to satisfy his appetites. What does he do with this freedom? He indulges in debauchery and immerses himself in the disorderly existence he wanted. Dangerous sirens lure him, charm him, fascinate him, and he is lost. They too are consumed by the need for luxury. Where would he find the money they demanded of him, and which his passion did not have the strength to refuse? In his boss's till. He starts by embezzling forty-five francs and ends up stealing thirty-five thousand after a murder." You can be sure that, if the case goes to trial, as I believe it will, you will hear these words, or at least this theme, and I assure you that we will have difficulty destroying the impression it will have made on the jurors; but we will get there... I hope.
We had had to give up on the dismissal and tell ourselves that the case would go to trial; but the fact that you are accused does not mean that you will be convicted, and Saniel had persisted in his belief that Florentin could not be convicted: Certainly preventive prison was hard on the poor boy, and appearing before the jury, with all the ignominy that goes with it, would be even harder; but in the end all that would disappear in the joy of acquittal: at that moment some ingenious idea, some sympathy, some effective support, would be found to pay him for what he had suffered. Things would certainly turn out that way, and the acquittal would be won hands down.
He had said it to himself over and over again, and from the day he had handed the case over to Nougarède, he had often gone to see Nougarède to hear it repeated.
- Isn't it true that he can't be convicted?
- You can always be convicted, even if you're innocent, just as you can always die, as you know, even if you're in excellent health.
Nougarède, clever as he was, exaggerated the possible danger in order to increase his importance and, ultimately, his success.
During one of these visits, he had met Madame Nougarède, who had been married for a few days, and, recognising in her the young virgin with the child whose portrait Caffié had shown him, he had been strengthened in his idea that conscience, as we understand it, was decidedly a singular weighing instrument, which could be made to say whatever we wanted: what was the point of all this hypocrisy and who did we think we were fooling?
Although he had always told Philis that the acquittal was certain, and had promised to look after Florentin, which he had in fact done, she had not completely entrusted either him or Nougarède with the task of defending her brother, and with them she had worked on this defence.
What had delayed the referral to the assizes, Nougarède believed, were the investigations into whether, during his stay in America, Florentin had not worked in some large meat factory, some sheepfold, some herdsman's shop, where he had learned to use the knife used by cattle cutters, which was the key point for the prosecution. To ward off this danger, Philis, for her part, had written to the various towns where Florentin had stayed, to prove that during his two-year stay his time had been used in such a way that he had not been able to complete this apprenticeship: it is true that he had worked in La Plata for six months as an accountant in the offices of a large sheepfold in Bahia-Blanca; but the fact that he had kept the books in a sheepfold did not mean that he had ever cut the sheep's throats.
When she received a letter, she took it straight to Saniel, and then to Nougarède; and at the same time, all over Paris, among those who had had dealings with her brother, she was looking for evidence to prove to the jury that he could not be the man the prosecution believed him to be. So it was that, on her own, with no means of action other than those she found in her fraternal tenderness and her valour, she had organised an investigation parallel to that of the courts, which, on the day of judgement, would carry some weight, it seemed, on the jury's conviction, by showing it what the real life of this irregular and debauched man, capable of anything to satisfy his appetite and his needs, had been.
Whenever she had obtained a favourable deposition, she would run to Saniel to tell him about it, and then in duet they would repeat to each other that a conviction was impossible.
- You think so, don't you?
- Haven't I always said so?
He had also said that Florentin could not be arrested on the basis of the accusation that the button had been ripped off; he had also said that the examining magistrate would certainly dismiss the case; but neither of them wanted to remember that.
Things had reached that point when, one Saturday evening, Saniel saw Philis arrive home radiant.
At the door, she exclaimed:
- He's safe!
- Dismissed?
- No, but now it doesn't matter; we can go to trial.
She heaved a sigh that showed just how great her fears were, despite the confidence she had in herself when she repeated that a conviction was impossible.
He had left the desk in front of which he was working and, coming over to her, had taken her in his arm to make her sit next to him on the sofa:
- You'll see that I don't let myself be taken away by illusion and that, as I'm telling you, he's saved, well saved. Did you know that an illustrated newspaper published his portrait?
- I don't read illustrated newspapers.
- You could have seen it on the newsstands where it's displayed; that's where I saw it yesterday morning, as I was leaving, and I was petrified, red with shame, distraught, not knowing where to hide: "Florentin Cormier, the murderer of the Rue Sainte-Anne. Isn't it infamous that an innocent man can be dishonoured in this way? That's what I said to myself as I lowered my eyes in front of the newsagents I came across on my way home, unaware of the joy the publication of this portrait would bring me. Where did the newspaper get the photograph from which the engraving was made? Someone had come to ask us for one, but you can imagine how I welcomed the person who presented himself, not imagining that anything good could come of what I considered a disgrace.
- And what did?
- Proof that it wasn't Florentin who was at Caffié's when the murder was committed. All day yesterday and all morning today, I had remained under the impression of shame that pursued me, when at three o'clock I received this little note from the concierge in the Rue Sainte-Anne.
She took out of her pocket a piece of paper folded into the shape of a letter, with a crudely written address, which she handed to Saniel.
"Mademoiselle,
"If you would like to come to Rue Sainte-Anne, I have something to tell you which I think will please you very much.
"I am your servant.
"Widow ANAÏS BOUCHU."
- You know that the old concierge with the ankylosed kidneys never wanted to admit that my brother could be guilty. Florentin had been polite and kind to her during his stay at Caffié's, and she remained grateful to him. She had often told me that she was certain the culprit would be found, that the cards said so, and that when that happened she would ask me to warn her. Instead of me having to bring her this good news, it was she, as you can see, who wrote to me to come and receive it from her. You can imagine how quickly I came down from Les Batignolles to the rue Sainte-Anne. However far my imagination went, it remained below reality: I was counting on some favourable clue, a discovery, a testimony, not on proof; and this proof we have. When I arrived, the old woman took both my hands and told me she was going to take me straight to a lady who had seen Caffié's killer.
- Seen!" exclaimed Saniel, struck by a blow that shook him from head to toe.
- Perfectly seen, as I see you. She added that this lady was the owner of the house and that she lived in the second part of the building, on the second floor overlooking the courtyard, just opposite Caffié's study. This lady, called Madame Dammauville, the widow of a solicitor, is paralysed and hasn't left her room for a year, I think. This is what the concierge explained to me as we crossed the courtyard and climbed the stairs, always repeating: "Your troubles are over, my poor lady", but without telling me how or why. When I pressed her, she replied: "Wait a little longer, Madame will explain it to you better than I can, and she doesn't like people chattering.
If she had been able to observe Saniel, she would have seen him turn so pale that his lips were as white as his cheeks; but she was absorbed in what she was saying.
- A maid took us to Madame Dammauville's house, where I found her lying on a small bed by a window, and the concierge told her who I was. She greeted me affably and, after making me sit opposite her, told me that, having heard from her concierge that I was busy gathering evidence in my brother's favour, she had one to give me which would show that Caffié's murderer was not the man the law had arrested and was holding in custody. On the evening of Caffié's murder, she was in that same room, lying on that same bed, in front of that same window, and, after reading all day, she was thinking and dreaming about her book, looking vaguely out into the courtyard at the evening that was descending and already blurring everything in its shadow. All of a sudden, she saw a tall man, whom she took to be an upholsterer, approach the window and try to move the curtains; he didn't succeed, so Caffié got up and, taking the lamp, shone it in such a way that the light fell right on the upholsterer's face. You understand, don't you?
- Yes," murmured Saniel.
- She saw him very clearly, enough not to forget him and mistake him for someone else: tall, long hair, curly blond beard; the clothes were those of a gentleman, not a poor devil. The curtains were drawn; it was now a quarter past five or twenty minutes past five; and it was at this very moment that Caffié's throat was slit by this false upholsterer who had obviously drawn the curtains only to kill Caffié in complete safety, without being seen, not imagining that, precisely, he had just been seen carrying out a deed that denounced him as the assassin as surely as if he had been caught with a knife in his hand. When she read Florentin's description in the newspapers when he was arrested, Madame Dammauville had thought that the law had found the culprit: tall, with long hair and a curly beard, there were indeed points of resemblance; but in the portrait published in the illustrated newspaper she received, she did not recognise the man who had operated the curtains, and she was certain that the law was mistaken! You can see that Florentin is saved.
III
As he made no reply to this cry of triumph, she looked at him in surprise.
She saw that he was pale, his face shaken, obviously the result of a violent emotion that she could not explain.
- What's the matter?" she asked worriedly.
- Nothing!" he replied brutally.
- You don't want to weaken my hope, do you?" she said, not imagining that he could not be thinking of that hope and of Florentin.
In his confusion, she was opening a path for him: by following it, he would have time to recognise himself.
- It's true," he said.
- Don't you think that what Madame Dammauville saw proves Florentin's innocence?
- What may be proof for Madame Dammauville, for you, for me, will it be proof for the law?
- However...
- I saw you so full of joy that I didn't dare interrupt you.
- So you think this testimony is worthless," she murmured, overwhelmed.
- I'm not saying that. You have to think things over, weigh up the pros and cons, consider the situation from different points of view - which is what I'm trying to do.
- Say that it crushes me; I had let myself be taken away so well.
- You mustn't be crushed, any more than you must be stolen away. Certainly, what this lady has just told you is a considerable fact...
- Is it not?
- Undoubtedly; but, if the testimony she has given can have far-reaching consequences, it is on condition that the witness is trustworthy.
- Do you think this lady could have invented such a story?
- I'm not saying that; but first of all you have to know who she is.
- A solicitor's widow.
- A solicitor's widow, a landowner: obviously, that constitutes a social condition that merits consideration for justice; but what is her moral condition? You say she's paralysed?
- For more than a year.
- What paralysis? That's a very vague word for the rest of us; there are paralyses that disturb vision, and there are those that disturb reason. Is this lady suffering from one of these paralyses? Or is it another which enabled her to really see, on the evening of the murder, what she is saying, and which now leaves her mental faculties in a healthy state? That, above all, is what it would be important to know.
Philis was devastated.
- I hadn't thought of all that," she murmured.
- It's quite natural that you didn't think about it, but I'm a doctor, and while you were talking, it was the doctor who was listening to you.
- It's true, it's true!" she repeated, overwhelmed; "I only saw Florentin.
- If I were in your place, I would have seen only my brother, as you did, and let myself be carried away by hope; but I'm not in your place: it's through your voice that this woman speaks, whom I don't know and against whom I must be on my guard by the very fact that it's a paralytic who's giving this account.
She couldn't hold back the tears that had welled up in her eyes, and silently let them flow, unable to think of anything to say in reply.
- I'm sorry to upset you," he said.
- All I could see was Florentin's freedom.
- I'm not saying that Madame Dammauville's testimony won't have an influence on the courts, and especially on the jury; but I must warn you that you would be exposing yourself to a terrible disappointment if you believed that, simply because she says that the portrait published in an illustrated newspaper is not that of the man she saw or thought she saw, at a quarter past five in Caffié's study, your brother will be set free. There are witnesses who, with the best faith in the world, believe they have seen the most extraordinary things that have never existed except in their imagination; and there are people who accuse themselves rather than have nothing to say.
He piled words on top of words, as if, by trying to convince Philis, he could hope to convince himself; but, when the noise of his words died down, he was forced to admit to himself that, whatever this woman's paralysis might have been, on this occasion it had produced neither a disturbance of vision nor a disturbance of reason: She had seen him, seen him well, the tall man with the long hair, the curly beard, dressed like a gentleman, who was not a Florentine; when she told the story of the lamp and the drawstrings, she knew what she was saying; all her explanations could therefore only have an effect on Philis, and they would stop with her. 
It's true that this was something, because in his first fit of confusion he had come very close to betraying himself. But everything had happened so quickly that he had never imagined that, precisely at the moment when Caffié raised the lamp to illuminate him, there was a woman opposite to look at him and see him so well that she would not have forgotten him. He had thought he had taken every precaution by closing the curtains, when on the contrary he would have done better to leave them open: No doubt the solicitor's widow would have witnessed part of the scene, but in the shadows she would not have been able to make out his features as she had been able to do when he was himself in a good position, against the window, under the light; and before she had recovered from her surprise, before she had called out, before they had reached her voice, before they had descended the two flights of stairs, he would have had time to reach the street. But this idea had not occurred to him and, in order to protect himself from an immediate danger, he had thrown himself into another which, although uncertain in its timing, was no less serious.
Little by little Philis had found herself again, and the hope that Madame Dammauville had put in her heart, crushed for a moment by Saniel's observations, had risen again.
- Is it not possible that Madame Dammauville really saw what she says she did?
- Without a doubt, and there's even a good chance that she did, since the man who drew the curtains wasn't your brother, we know that; unfortunately we're not the ones who need convincing, since our conviction is already in place; it's those who also have a conviction that they won't give up unless it's taken away from them by force.
- But did Madame Dammauville see correctly?
- What we need to know above all is whether she is in a fit state to see well; that's all I said.
- Surely a doctor would know by examining her?
- Without a doubt.
- What if you were that doctor?
- What if I were?
It was a cry rather than an exclamation: she wanted him to appear before this woman; but then she would recognise him!
Once again, at the risk of betraying his emotion, he had to go back on that first movement.
- But how do you expect me to examine this woman I don't know and who doesn't know me? You know very well that it's patients who choose their doctors, not doctors who choose their patients.
- If she called you!
- In what capacity?
- Without you seeing her, from what I would learn by making the concierge talk, couldn't you recognise her kind of paralysis?
- It would only be a very vague approximation: however, I urge you to make this enquiry and to extend it as far as you can; try to learn, not only everything about her illness, but also everything about her: what is her situation, what are her relations, this is important for a witness who imposes himself as much by what he is as by what he says: you understand that a deposition which destroys the whole system of the prosecution will be severely discussed, and that it will only be accepted if Madame Dammauville has, by her character and position, sufficient authority to break down resistance. 
- I'll also try to find out who her doctor is; perhaps you know him. What he would tell you would be better than anything I could tell you.
- We'd know right away about the paralysis, and we'd see how much credence we can give to this woman's words.
While listening to Philis and speaking himself, he had had time to consider the situation created by this thunderbolt: obviously, the first thing to do was to prevent suspicion from arising in Philis's mind, and that was what he had done by throwing himself into explanations of the various types of paralysis: he knew her well enough to see that he had succeeded. If at first she had been surprised by his excitement, she now found sufficient reason for him to worry about it later only in the event of direct accusations being made against him and causing her to backtrack. But he couldn't stop there; he needed more. What was she going to do now? How did she intend to use Madame Dammauville's statement? Had she spoken to anyone about it before? Was she going to tell Nougarède what she had just learned? All this had to be clarified. And as far as possible, she had to do nothing that he did not know about and approve of in advance. The circumstances were critical enough for him not to leave it to chance to direct them and lead them blindly.
- When did you see Madame Dammauville?
- Just now.
- And now, what do you want to do?
- I thought I should warn Mr Nougarède.
- Obviously, whatever the value of Madame Dammauville's statement, he must know it: it's up to him to judge; but as it's good to explain to him what could vitiate this statement, I'll go and see him if you like?
- I certainly want to and I thank you for it.
- In the meantime, go back to your mother and tell her what you've learned; but, so that she doesn't get her hopes up too much, tell her also that if there are chances, and big ones, in your brother's favour, on the other hand, there are some against. Tomorrow or this evening you will return to Rue Sainte-Anne and begin your enquiries with the concierge; if the old woman tells you nothing of interest, you will return to Madame Dammauville, whom you will ask to see on some pretext whatsoever: you will make her speak by listening carefully to the rhythm of her voice, you will note whether her ideas are connected, you will examine her face and her eyes; in short, you will leave no stone unturned. Having looked after your mother, you know the symptoms of myelitis almost as well as a doctor, and you'll be able to see straight away if there are similar symptoms in Madame Dammauville.
- If I dared!" she said timidly after a brief hesitation.
- What would you do?
- I'd ask you to come with me to the concierge's house straight away.
- Are you thinking about it?
Since the evening he had witnessed Caffié's death, he had never returned to the Rue Sainte-Anne, and it was not when the description given by Madame Dammauville was already circulating in the neighbourhood that he was going to commit the imprudence of showing himself.
But this exclamation had to be explained.
- How do you expect a doctor to carry out such an investigation? On your part, it's perfectly natural. From me, it would be inappropriate and ridiculous; add that it would be dangerous: you need to win the good graces of this Madame Dammauville; and it would really be going about it very badly to give her a pretext for believing that you're trying to find out whether she's sane or not.
- That's true," she said. I hadn't thought about that. I was thinking that, while I can only listen to what the concierge wants to tell me, you would know how to question her in a useful way to get her to say what it's in your interest to learn.
- In the meantime, go back to the concierge this evening, question her; if possible, go up to Madame Dammauville's, and don't return to your mother's until you've obtained some information about what we have so much interest in learning. I, for my part, am going to Nougarède's.
IV
It was not in order to falsify Philis' story that Saniel had insisted on seeing Nougarède: why bother? It would be a blunder that would sooner or later reveal itself and turn against him; he would have wanted, with the authority of a doctor, to explain that the testimony of a paralytic could have no more importance than that of a madwoman.
But at the first words of his explanation, Nougarède stopped him:
- What you are saying is very possible, my dear friend; but I would point out that it is not for us to raise objections of this kind. This is testimony that can save our client: let's accept it as it is and wherever it comes from. It is up to the prosecution to prove that our witness could not have seen what he says he saw, or that his mental state does not allow him to know what he is saying. And, rest assured, there will be no shortage of investigations. So let's not awaken this side ourselves; that would be naive.
This was certainly not what Saniel had wanted; he had simply thought it his duty, as a doctor, to point out the pitfalls that could be encountered.
- Our duty," continued the lawyer, "is to manoeuvre in such a way as to avoid them, and this is how I would understand the role of this truly providential witness, if it is still possible to make him take it. Since it has occurred to you, who wish to see this poor boy acquitted, that Madame Dammauville's testimony could be tainted by the very fact that it came from a sick woman, it is incontestable, is it not, that this same idea will occur to those who wish to see a conviction. This testimony would be irrefutable, it would impose itself in such a way that no reproach could be raised against it, that it would remove the acquittal at whatever moment it occurred: it was from a quarter past five to half past five that Caffié was murdered: at just a quarter past five, a woman respectable for her position, and whose intellectual as well as physical faculties make her worthy of all belief, would have seen in Caffié's study a man, whom it is materially impossible to confuse with Florentin Cormier, draw the curtains of the window and thus prepare for the crime; she would make her statement under these conditions and in these terms, and the case would be over: there would be no examining magistrate, after the confrontation, to send Florentin Cormier before the assizes, and if there were one, there would certainly not be two votes in the jury for conviction. But that's not how things are and won't be. Her husband was an esteemed solicitor, and one of his brothers was a notary in Paris.
- Did you ever have any dealings with her?
- Never; I'm telling you what is common knowledge in the business world. So morally she's beyond reproach. But physically, intellectually, is she the same? Not at all, unfortunately. She is a woman suffering from an illness that often leaves neither her mind nor her senses intact; her eyesight can be affected by aberrations, her mind by hallucinations. So we can argue about what she says, and if there is a doctor who affirms that her paralysis does not give rise to these aberrations or hallucinations, there will be another who will contest these assertions and come to a radically opposite conclusion. So much for the witness himself; now let's move on to the testimony. This testimony does not say that the man who drew the curtains at a quarter past five was made in such a way that it is materially impossible to confuse him with Florentin Cormier, because he was short, or hunchbacked, or bald, or dressed like a prowler at the gate, whereas Florentin is tall, straight, hairy, bearded and dressed like a gentleman; He simply says that the man who drew the curtains was tall, with long hair, a curly blond beard, and dressed like a gentleman. But this description is precisely that of Florentin Cormier, just as it is yours...
- Mine!" exclaimed Saniel.
- Yours, like that of many people. And that, unfortunately for us, is what deprives it of the irrefutability we need. How can she be sure that this tall man with long hair and a curly beard isn't Florentin Cormier, since that's what characterises him? And it was at night, twelve or fifteen metres away, through a window obscured by paper dust and fog, that this sick woman, whose eyes are cloudy and whose mind is weakened by suffering, was able, in a very short space of time, to see Florentin Cormier, when she had no interest in remembering what she saw, to grasp certain signs that she now remembers strongly enough to affirm that the man who drew the curtains was not Florentin Cormier, against whom so many charges have been brought on various sides, and who has only this testimony to give.... which any sensible person could not fail to find suspicious!
- But it's true," said Saniel, happy to be taken in by his own plea.
- What makes a thing true, my dear, is the way in which it is presented; change that way and we'll distort it. To arrive at the conclusion that made you say: "It's true", I started from the idea that tomorrow Madame Dammauville's account would be known to the courts, that the good lady would be heard in the investigation, and that there would be plenty of time to examine this testimony that you yourself find suspect. Now let's start from the opposite point. Madame Dammauville's account is not known to the courts, or if something transpires from it, we will arrange for that something to be so vague that the prosecution attaches little importance to it; and that is possible if we ourselves do not base a whole new defence on this testimony. We come to the trial, and when the prosecution has heard its witnesses who have overwhelmed us: the business agent Savoureux, the tailor Valérius, etc.; it's Madame Dammauville's turn: she simply tells what she saw, and affirms that the man in the dock is not the same as the one who drew the curtains at a quarter past five. Do you see the twist? The prosecution didn't anticipate it: they didn't investigate the health of the witness; they don't have a doctor there ready to allege impaired vision and reason; most likely, they didn't think about the obscured window, or the distance; in short, all the arguments that could be put against us if we had the time to put them in the right order are missing, and we win the acquittal hands down.
Things, arranged in this way, were too favourable for Saniel not to welcome with a feeling of relief this combination which led to Florentin's acquittal more surely, it seemed, than anything they had put together up to that point in his defence:
- Do we want to admit that Madame Dammauville kept silent about such a serious fact and waited until the hearing to reveal it?
- It's obvious that if she didn't say what she had seen, it's because she had reasons for keeping silent; it's likely that, being ill, she didn't want to expose herself to the trouble and fatigue of an interrogation, when her statement could, in her eyes, be of no great importance. What would she have revealed during the investigation? That the man who had committed the crime was tall, with a curly blond beard? The law had this man, or she had one, the description matched that, which for Madame Dammauville was the same thing; so she didn't have to call the police, the examining magistrate, to tell them anything... insignificant: for her peace of mind and also because she felt she had nothing interesting to say, she didn't speak. It was only when chance put a portrait of the accused before her eyes that she recognised that justice did not have the real culprit, and so she broke her silence. We don't have to specify the moment when chance put this portrait in front of her eyes; I'll take care of that. That's not where the difficulty lies.
- Where do you see it?
- In this: that Madame Dammauville may already have told her story to so many people that it has fallen into the public domain, where the investigation has picked it up; then there's no more drama; she's questioned, her statement is examined, everything we said earlier is set against her, and we're left with nothing but suspect testimony. The first thing to do now, therefore, is to find out how far this story has spread and, if there is still time, to prevent it from spreading further.
- This is not easy, I think.
- I think Mademoiselle Philis can do it. She is a brave girl, valiant, intelligent, determined, who is not deterred or discouraged by anything, and who is living proof that we are only as good as the strength and suppleness of our inner spring; besides, I don't have to praise her, since you know her better than I do, and what I am saying has no other purpose than to explain the confidence I have in her. Since I cannot intervene myself, I feel that no one is in a better position than she to influence Madame Dammauville, without worrying or hurting her, and to bring about the result we are looking for. I am sure that she has already won over Madame Dammauville and that she will be listened to sympathetically.
- Would you like me to write to her and ask her to come and see you tomorrow?
- No; the best thing would be for you to see her yourself this evening, if that's possible.
- I'll go to Batignolles when I leave you.
- She will play her part perfectly, I'm sure; and she will succeed, I hope; but all will not be said.
- It seems to me that your combination depends above all on the coup de théâtre of Madame Dammauville's failure to recognise Florentin: how will you get this paralytic to the hearing?
- I'm counting on you.
- And how will you do that?
- You will examine her.
- I should go to her house!
- Why shouldn't you?
- Because I'm not her doctor.
- You will be.
- I couldn't possibly.
- I don't find it impossible at all that you should be called in for consultation; I haven't forgotten that your thesis was on paralysis due to affection of the spinal cord, and it was remarkable enough for us to have dealt with it in our talk in the rue de Vaugirard; you are therefore an authority on the subject.
- It is not for having done a few works on the pathological anatomy of spinal cord lesions, and especially on alterations of the anterior roots of the spinal cord, that one acquires authority in such a vast and delicate question.
-- Don't be too modest, dear friend; I recently had to consult the article Paralysis in my Dictionary of Medicine, and I saw your work quoted on every page. What's more, the way you've just passed your competitive examination has put you in the spotlight; you're the talk of the town. So it's not impossible that Mademoiselle Philis, telling how her mother was cured of paralysis precisely by you, will lead Madame Dammauville to the idea of consulting you, and that her doctor will call you.
- You won't do that!
- And why shouldn't I?
They looked at each other for a moment in silence, and Saniel looked away.
- I hate nothing so much as appearing to put myself forward.
- In this case, it's not a question of what you hate or what you love: it's a question of saving this unfortunate young man whom you know to be innocent; and you can, to a large extent, help us. You examine Madame Dammauville: you see what paralysis she is suffering from and, consequently, what criticisms can be levelled at her testimony; at the same time you see if you can cure her, or at least put her in a position to come to the hearing.
- And if it is established that she will not be able to leave her bed?
- Then I make a change to my order of battle, and that's why it's vitally important - you know that's the word - that I be warned in advance.
- Are you having the examining magistrate take his statement?
- On no account; but I have her write a letter which I read at the appropriate time, and I quote her doctor so that he can explain why he didn't allow his client to come to the hearing: no doubt the effect would not be what I was looking for, but in the end, we would still have one.
V
After Philis, Nougarède wanted him to see Madame Dammauville, and this coincidence was not the least danger of the situation that was opening up.
If she saw him, the chances were that she would recognise him as the man who had drawn the curtains; for, although he had been able to tell Philis and Nougarède about disturbances of vision or reason, he did not believe in these disturbances, which were for him no more than escape routes.
When he arrived at Madame Cormier's, Philis had not yet returned, and he had to explain to the worried mother why her daughter was late.
It was a delirium of joy before which he felt embarrassed. How could he crush the hopes of this unfortunate mother?
He repeated what he had said to Philis and Nougarède: before seeing Florentin free, it was necessary to know the value of Madame Dammauville's testimony; and he explained how the value of this testimony could be compromised.
- But it is also possible for a paralytic to enjoy all his faculties! cried Madame Cormier with a decision that was neither in her habits nor in her character.
- It certainly is.
- Am I not an example?
- You certainly are.
- Then Florentin would be saved.
- That's what we must hope. I can only protect you from excessive joy by excessive caution; besides, it's likely that Mademoiselle Philis will be able to fix us up when we get back.
- Perhaps you would have done better to go to Rue Sainte-Anne: you would still have found her.
So it was a universal mania to want to send her to Rue Sainte-Anne!
They waited, but conversation was difficult and slow between them; it wasn't Philis or Florentin that Saniel was thinking about, it was him and his own fears; for her part, Madame Cormier was running to meet her daughter: there were long silences which Madame Cormier interrupted by going into the kitchen to check on her dinner, which had been ready for more than two hours and which she was keeping warm.
Not knowing what to say or do in the face of Saniel's sombre expression and his preoccupation, which she could not explain, she asked him if he had eaten.
- No, he hadn't.
I've got some broth from yesterday, Philis didn't think it was bad.
But he didn't accept, which upset Madame Cormier. Saniel had long been a kind of god to her, and since she had seen him so zealous in looking after Florentin, her worship of him had become even more fervent. How many times had she cried out to Philis about him, "How can we ever repay Mr Saniel?" and now, just when she hoped to please him, he refused her. But she didn't hold it against him: no doubt he had his reasons; nothing that came from him could be wrong.
But as the minutes passed and Philis did not arrive, at last her hurried footsteps were heard.
- You've come to tell Mummy?" she exclaimed, catching sight of Saniel.
Normally, Madame Cormier listened respectfully, but she cut him off.
- And Madame Dammauville?" she asked.
- Madame Dammauville has excellent eyes; she's a headstrong woman who manages her fortune without the help of any businessman.
Failing, Madame Cormier dropped into a chair.
- Oh, the poor child! she murmured.
Exclamations of joy escaped her that had no precise meaning.
Philis, radiant, was looking at Saniel, who was making an effort not to remain sombre, and to seem to join in this joy.
- That's what I thought," he said, "but it was imprudent to abandon ourselves today to hopes that tomorrow would have destroyed.
As he spoke, he at least escaped the embarrassment of his situation and Philis' scrutiny.
- What did Mr Nougarède say?" she asked.
- I'll explain it to you in a moment; start by telling us what you've learned about Madame Dammauville; it's her condition that will decide what we do, or at least what Nougarède advises us to do.
- When the concierge saw me coming back," began Philis, "she showed a certain surprise; but she's a good woman who's easily tamed, and I didn't have too much trouble getting her to tell us everything she knows about Madame Dammauville. Madame Dammauville has been widowed three years ago, without children; she is about forty years old; and it is since her widowhood that she has lived in her house in the rue Sainte-Anne. Until last year, she was not in poor health, although she went to the waters at Lamalou every year. A year ago, she was seized by pains that were thought to be rheumatic, and which led to the paralysis that keeps her in bed. Sometimes she suffers to the point of screaming, but these attacks do not last forever. She reads a lot, entertains a few friends, her sister-in-law, the widow of a solicitor, her nephews and nieces, and one of the parish vicars, because she is pious and above all very charitable. Her eyes are excellent. She has never had any delusions or hallucinations. She is very reserved, hates gossip, and seeks above all to live in peace; so the murder of Caffié exasperated her: she didn't want anyone to talk to her about it, and she herself didn't talk to anyone about it; she even said that, if she were in a position to leave her house, she would sell it so as not to hear the name Caffié mentioned again.
- How did she talk about the portrait and the man she saw in Caffié's study?" asked Saniel.
- That's precisely the question the concierge couldn't answer, so I decided to go and see Madame Dammauville again.
- Are you brave!" said Madame Cormier proudly.
- I can assure you that I was hardly brave when I went up the stairs: after what I had just learnt about her character, it was really audacious to come a second time, two hours apart, to disturb her peace and quiet; but it had to be done. She agreed to receive me. On the way up, I had been looking for a reason to justify, or at least to explain, my second visit, and I had only found an adventurous one, for which I must ask your indulgence.
She said this as she turned to Saniel, but with downcast eyes, without daring to look at him and with an emotion that was worrying for him.
- My indulgence?" he said.
- I acted without having the time to think things through and under the pressure of immediate necessity. As Madame Dammauville was surprised to see me again, I told her that what she had told me was so serious and could have such consequences for the life and honour of my brother, that I had thought of coming back the next day accompanied by someone who knew the business, before whom she would repeat her story, and that it was permission to come with this person that I was asking her for; this person was you.
- That person was you. Me!
- And that's why," she said weakly, without looking up at him, "I need your indulgence.
- But I told you..." he exclaimed with a violence that his displeasure at having been disposed of in this way was not enough to justify it.
- ... I had not forgotten that you could not come to Madame Dammauville's house as a doctor without her having sent for you; so it was not as a doctor that I wanted to ask you to come with me... but as a friend, if you will allow me to speak in this way, as the most devoted, firm and generous friend we have had the good fortune to meet in our distress.
- My daughter speaks for me as well as for herself," said Madame Cormier with moved gravity, "and I would like to add that it is a respectful friendship, a deep gratitude that we have dedicated to you.
Although Philis was trembling to see the effect she had had on Saniel, she continued firmly:
- So you accompanied me and, without doing anything ostentatious, without saying anything that could have been said by a doctor, you could examine her while she was talking. Madame Dammauville responded to my request with her consent, given with perfect good grace. I'll go back to her tomorrow and, if you think it's useful, if you think you should accept the role I've given you without consulting you, you can come with me.
He did not reply to these last words, which were both an invitation and a question.
- Didn't you examine her as I told you?" he asked after a moment's reflection.
- With all the attention I was capable of in my anguish: the eyes seemed to me to be straight and uncluttered; the voice was regular and well-paced; the words followed one another without hesitation, the ideas flowed and were expressed in an orderly fashion. There is no trace of suffering on this yellow face, which bears only the mark of resigned pain; she moves her arms freely; but her legs, as far as I could see under the blanket that hid them, are immobile; in several respects it seems to me that her paralysis is similar to Mama's; it is true that in others it differs; she must be extremely cold, for, although the weather is not cold today, the temperature in her room seemed to me to be very high.
- This is an examination," said Saniel, "that a doctor would not have conducted any better, unless he had questioned the patient, and if I had been with you on this visit we would not have known any more than what you have observed. It seems certain that Madame Dammauville is in full possession of the faculties that make her testimony unassailable.
Madame Cormier drew her daughter to her and kissed her passionately.
- So there's nothing for me to do at this lady's house," continued Saniel, with the haste of a man who has just escaped danger; "but your role, mademoiselle, is not over, and tomorrow you must return to her house to fulfil the role that Nougarède has entrusted to you.
He explained what Nougarède expected of her.
- Certainly," she said, "I'll do whatever I'm advised to do for Florentin: I'll go back to Madame Dammauville, I'll go everywhere; but will you allow me to be surprised if we don't take immediate advantage of this declaration to obtain my brother's release?
He repeated the reasons Nougarède had given him for not proceeding in this way.
- I wouldn't want to say anything that sounded like a reproach," replied Madame Cormier with more determination than she usually put into her words; "but perhaps M. Nougarède is bringing personal considerations into his advice. Our interest is that Florentin should be returned to our affection as soon as possible, and that he should be spared the suffering of prison. But I can understand why Mr Nougarède prefers the big day of the hearing, where he can make a fine plea, useful to his reputation, to an order to dismiss the case in which he does not appear.
- Whether or not he made that calculation," says Saniel, "that's just the way things are. I too would have preferred a dismissal, which had the great advantage of ending everything immediately. Nougarède doesn't think this is a good route to take: we have to follow the one he has outlined for us.
- We'll follow it," says Philis, "and I think it will bring the result Mr Nougarède is hoping for, because Madame Dammauville must have told very few people about the portrait. When I tried to get her to explain herself on this point, without asking her the question directly, she told me that she had only told her concierge about the lack of resemblance between the portrait and the man she saw draw the curtains, so that the concierge, who had spoken to her several times about Florentin and my actions, would warn me. So tomorrow I'll see how far her story has spread and I'll come and tell you about it at about five o'clock, unless you'd prefer me to go and see Mr Nougarède straight away.
- Start with me, and we'll go to his house together, if necessary. I will write to him.
- If I understand Mr Nougarède's plan correctly, it seems to me that it depends on Madame Dammauville appearing at the hearing. Will this appearance be possible? That's what I can't know; only a doctor could answer that question.
Saniel didn't want to show that he had understood this new appeal.
- I forgot to tell you," continued Philis, "that the man treating her is Dr Balzajette, who lives in the rue de l'Échelle.
- A solemn man who hides his worthlessness beneath the dignity of his manners.
She must have had an excellent doctor, Madame Dammauville, and one so highly esteemed by his colleagues that, if he didn't cure her, it was because she was incurable.
- So how can we expect him to cure her in time for her to appear at the hearing?
He didn't answer and got up to leave. Mrs Cormier timidly repeated her invitation, but he did not accept, despite the tender look Philis gave him.
VI
Could he resist the pressure that from all sides was pushing him towards the Rue Sainte-Anne? It seemed that nothing was easier than not to commit the folly of yielding to it, and yet such was the persistence of the efforts that were united against him, that he had to wonder if one day he would not be led in spite of himself to obey them: Philis, Nougarède, Madame Cormier; now where would the new attack come from?
Wasn't there something extraordinary here that he would even have described as providential or fatal, if he had believed in Providence or Fatality?
For several months he had achieved complete security, which had convinced him that all danger had been averted for ever; but now all of a sudden this danger was erupting in such conditions that he had to admit that there could no longer be any security for him: today Madame Dammauville was threatening him; tomorrow it would be someone else. Who? He didn't know. Anyone. And it was the anguish of his situation to be condemned to live from now on in fear and on the defensive, without rest, without forgetting.
But it wasn't tomorrow that he had to worry about at the moment, it was the present, in other words Madame Dammauville.
For her to have said so firmly, on the sight of a simple portrait, that the man who had closed the curtains was not a Florentine, she must have had an excellent memory for eyes, together with a resolution of mind and a decision in ideas which enabled her to affirm without hesitation what she believed to be the truth.
If they ever met, she would recognise him and, recognising him, she would speak.
Would she be believed?
That was the decisive question, and it seemed that given the character of this woman surrounded by consideration and respect, who was intelligent, circumspect, reserved, there should be little doubt that she would be, or at least that she would not raise presumptions against him which he would be obliged to defend himself against.
Denials would not suffice. He had not been at Caffié's at a quarter past five. Where was he at that time? What evidence could he adduce to justify the use of his time that evening? Wouldn't it be then that his presence in the café would be pointed out, and would rise up against him to crush him? Caffié's wound had been inflicted by a hand skilled in the art of killing, and it was precisely this skilled hand that was his, even more than that of a professional killer. His situation at the time of the crime was desperate, as everyone knew: desperate, harassed by his creditors, pursued by the bailiffs, threatened with being put out on the street; and it was at this moment that he had suddenly, miraculously, paid his debts. With what? Who could accept the story of Monte Carlo, good when there were no charges against him, detestable and accusatory on the contrary when those charges had been raised.
One word, one insinuation from Madame Dammauville and he was lost, defenceless, without a fight.
In truth, and fortunately, since she was paralysed and confined to her bed, he was not exposed to finding himself face to face with her on the corner of a street or in a third house, nor to hearing the cry of surprise that she would not fail to utter on recognising him; but that was not enough for him to fall asleep in an imprudent security, telling himself that this meeting was improbable. It was also implausible to admit that someone was standing precisely opposite Caffié's window when he had drawn the curtains, and even more implausible to believe that this insignificant fact in itself, this vision of a brief moment, would be engraved firmly enough in a woman's memory to remain vivid after several months ; And yet, out of all these improbabilities, he had formed a reality that held him so tightly that from one moment to the next it could suffocate him. 
In spite of the urgings of Philis, Madame Cormier, Nougarède and all the others, whoever they might be, he would not be foolish enough to face the danger of a reconnaissance in the room where this paralytic was confined -- at least that was probable, for after what had just happened, he was certain of nothing -- but this reconnaissance could very well take place elsewhere.
In Nougarède's plan, Madame Dammauville would come to the hearing to make her declaration; he himself was a witness: they would therefore, at some point, have to meet, and it was not impossible that it would be in the middle of the hearing that the recognition would break out in a more dramatic coup de théâtre than the one Nougarède had arranged.
Undoubtedly, there were many chances that Madame Dammauville would not be able to leave her bed and come to the hearing; but if there was only one chance that she would leave him, he had to foresee it and take precautions.
There was only one guarantee: to make himself unrecognisable, to cut off his beard, to cut off his hair, no longer to be the man with the long hair and the curling blond beard that she remembered: If he had been like everyone else, she would probably not have noticed him when he came to the window, and she would certainly have forgotten him, or at least confused him with others, whereas the singularity of his face and his bearing, his Gallic air, had left a characteristic imprint on her memory that time had not erased: you should only allow yourself to be original when you are sure in advance that you will never have anything to fear. 
Certainly, nothing was easier than to have his beard and hair cut: all he had to do was go to the first barber he came across: in a few minutes the transformation would be radical.
Among the indifferent, he didn't have to worry too much about the curiosity that this change might produce; more than one would not notice it, and those who would be surprised at first would soon think no more of it; besides, for them, there was an easy answer: on the eve of becoming a serious character, he abandoned the latest eccentricities of the old student, and crossed the bridges without a second thought to the left bank.
