But it was not only to the indifferent that he owed a debt of gratitude; he owed even more to those with whom he was in regular contact: to Philis, to Nougarède.
Hadn't the lawyer noticed them, the long hair and the curling beard, when he had noticed the similarities between the two descriptions, and, therefore, wasn't it imprudent to make him wonder why all of a sudden what constituted this similarity disappeared in one of the two?
Dangerous for the lawyer, this question became even more so for Philis: Nougarède could only show surprise; Philis could ask for explanations.
And he would have to answer her all the more clearly because, four or five times already, he had almost betrayed himself in relation to Madame Dammauville, and although she had let his exclamations or his moments of embarrassment, his hesitations or his refusals pass without questioning him frankly, she had certainly been astonished: If he appeared before her without hair or a beard, that would be a new astonishment added to the others, connections would be established, and logically, by the very force of things; in spite of herself, in spite of her love and her faith, she would arrive at conclusions from which she could not escape: -- Just like that, all of a sudden; why? -- As he complained one day about middle-class people who didn't want to come to him, she had gently explained to him that, in order to please these middle-class people and attract them, it might not be a very good idea to astonish those who were not shocked: that frock coats of shorter length, hats with a narrower brim, shorter hair, a less shaggy beard, in short, an ensemble that brought him closer to them, would perhaps be more agreeable to them; and at this point he got angry, telling her clearly that he was to be taken as he was or not to be taken, and that these concessions were not part of his character. He had resisted when he needed everyone and a client was a matter of life and death for him; he gave in when he no longer needed anyone and didn't care about clients. The contradiction would really be too strong and such that it could not fail to strike Philis, whose attention had already had too many opportunities to be aroused.
And yet, however delicate the decision to make herself unrecognisable was, it would have been madness on her part to put it off: The sooner the better; his fault had been not to foresee, the day after Caffié's death, that circumstances might arise one day or another that would force him to do it; at that time it would not have presented the same dangers as now; but starting from the idea that he had not been seen by anyone, that he could not have been seen, he had become complacent in the security that this conviction gave him and, quietly, he had become numb to it. 
Instead of taking refuge in miserable loopholes when Philis and Nougarède asked him to see Madame Dammauville, he would have boldly stood up to them and gone to see her as they wanted: now he would be saved and soon Florentin would be too.
Now he would be saved and soon Florentin would be too. And he had thought himself clever! And proudly he had imagined that he could combine things so well in advance that he would be safe from any surprise! The truth was, however, that this impression had faded fairly quickly; but now it was stronger than ever, and was sure to stay with him forever.
But there was no point in going back; it was the present and the future that he had to take a clear, firm look at, if he did not want to lose himself.
After careful consideration, he had to have his hair and beard cut; for, however adventurous this resolution might be, however unfortunate it might become by provoking curiosity and questions, it was the only way to prevent a possible recognition.
Machinically, out of habit, he headed for the rue Neuve-des-Petits-Champs, where his barber lived; but he had not taken a few steps when reflection stopped him: it would certainly be clumsy to provoke the chatter of this barber who knew him, and who, for the pleasure of chatting, would tell the locals that he had just cut Dr Saniel's hair and beard. -- The one who had such a long beard? -- Himself. -- That's funny. -- There had to be nothing funny about him, or about himself. So he returned to the boulevard where he was certainly not known.
But just as he was about to put his hand on the knob of the shop he had decided to enter, he changed his mind again: he had just found the explanation he needed for Philis, and as he wanted to avoid the surprise she would feel if she suddenly saw him without hair and beard, he would give her this explanation before having them cut, so that, right away and without looking for anything else, she would understand that this operation was essential.
And he went off to dinner, furious at himself and at the miserable expedients to which he had been reduced.
VII
The next day, at five o'clock, when Philis rang the bell, it was he who went to open the door; for Joseph, who had never come on Sundays, did not come now either.
As soon as she entered, she wanted to throw herself around his neck, as usual, with an impulse that showed how happy she was to see him; but he stopped her with his hand.
- What's the matter?" she asked, paralysed and full of fear.
- Nothing, or at least not much.
- Against me?
- Certainly not, my dear.
- Are you ill?
- Not ill, but I have precautions to take that prevent me from kissing you. I'll explain that to you. Don't worry, it's not serious.
- Quick," she exclaimed, examining him to try and anticipate his thoughts.
- Do you have something to tell me?
- Yes, and good ones. But, please, speak first; don't leave me in this state of anxiety.
- I assure you that you have nothing to worry about, and when I talk to you like this, you know that you have to believe me; you can see that I don't seem worried myself.
- You worry about other people, never about yourself.
- Do you know what alopecia is?
- No, I don't.
- It's a special disorder of the hair and beard due to the presence of a kind of fungus in the epidermis; well, I've probably got it.
- How serious is it?
- Annoying and embarrassing for a man, but disastrous for a woman, since before any treatment you have to start by cutting your hair. So you understand that if, as I believe, I have alopecia, I'm not going to expose you to it by kissing you, because it's transmitted extremely easily by contact, and then I'd have to do for you what I'll probably have to do for myself, i.e. cut your hair. For me, it's insignificant; but wouldn't it be murder to sacrifice those beautiful curly locks that give such charm to the face?
- You say: probably.
- It's just that I'm not quite sure I've got alopecia yet. A fortnight ago, I felt a slight itch on my head, and naturally I didn't pay any attention to it; I had other things to do and, besides, I wasn't going to think I had a parasitic disease because of an itch. But, after a while, my hair became dry in patches, dull, and it pulled easily, then fell out spontaneously. I said to myself that I would take care of it, but I didn't have the time and the days went by; besides, in the overwork that my competitions imposed on me, there were more than enough reasons to explain the loss of my hair. Finally today, shortly before your arrival, having a bit of freedom, I wanted to know what I was dealing with and I examined one of these diseased hairs under the microscope; if I hadn't been disturbed, I'd have known for sure by now.
- Resume your examination.
- I've got time; besides, the operation, to be complete, can't be done in a few minutes; after studying the hair, you have to look for the spores in the epidermal films. If it really is alopecia, as I have reason to believe, tomorrow you'll see me without hair or beard; I won't hesitate, despite the astonishment I'll provoke by showing myself shaven.
- What do you care?
- I can't tell everyone: I've had my hair and beard cut off because I'm suffering from a parasitic disease: we know that this disease is contagious, many people would run away from it.
- Once I've had my hair cut, what will happen to the disease?
- With energetic treatment, it will disappear quickly; before long you'll be able to kiss me if... you don't think I'm too ugly.
- Oh dear.
- Now it's your turn: have you just come from Madame Dammauville's?
He didn't need to insist: Philis had accepted his story well enough for him to be reassured on her side; she wouldn't be the one to worry; as for the others, the embarrassment of confessing a contagious disease would also be a sufficient explanation, if he were ever obliged to provide one.
- What did she say to you?
- To begin with, some kind and gracious words which show what an excellent woman she is. After visiting her twice yesterday, you can understand that I was not at ease asking her to see me again today. As I tried to apologise, she interrupted me: "I am delighted to see your devotion to your brother, and you will never have to apologise for asking for my help; you can count on it in everything I can do. Thus encouraged, I explained to her what we wanted from her; but, contrary to the promise she had just made me, she seemed reluctant to grant us this assistance. "What a strange procedure!" she repeated several times. Without being able to give her Mr Nougarède's reasons, I told her that we were obliged to follow the advice of those in charge of the case, and that I begged her to help us. Finally, she relented, but reluctantly and under protest. I'll do what you want," she said; "but I can't assure you that the people around me and the people in my service haven't talked; likewise, I can't promise you that I'll leave this bed to go to the court of assizes on the day of the hearing; I've been keeping the room for a year: they promise me it'll get better soon..." She's planning to get up soon?
- Is she going to get up soon?" interrupted Saniel.
- I'll repeat what she said, which I paid enough attention to remember: "I've been promised that I'll get better soon, but will it come true? I'm going to press my doctor for an answer, and when you come back to see me, I'll let you know. Taking advantage of the door she opened for me, I put the discussion on this doctor: it seems to me, but I'm not sure, that she has only relative confidence in him; He was her husband's classmate, as well as her brother-in-law the solicitor's, he is the relative or friend of everyone she sees, he marries the girls, breaks up the boys' compromising relationships, mends households that are going badly, confesses the wives, distracts the husbands, chooses the servants and, above all, when the opportunity arises, he treats those who are ill, cures them when necessary or lets them die at random; you can see what category of doctor he belongs to. 
- I told you I knew him.
- See if I was wrong, and add to what I'm telling you what you already know. Frightened to see in whose hands Mrs Dammauville was, I took all the roundabout ways I could and ended up knowing - without having asked her directly - that she had not seen another doctor for a year: When the paralysis set in, there was a consultation, and since then she has been content with Dr Balzajette, not so much out of indifference or incredulity, despair or apathy, but so as not to upset him: "He's such a good man! she said to me; why should I upset him? My illness has been established by consultation: he looks after it as well as anyone could.
Saniel took the opportunity to correct the blunder he had made in expressing his opinion of the solemn Balzajette:
- It's likely," he said.
- Is it certain? Do you think that in the last year nothing has arisen in Madame Dammauville's illness that would have required new treatment, which the solemn Balzajette was incapable of finding, on his own?
- He's not as bad as you think.
- You're the one who's talking nonsense.
- Diagnosing an illness and treating it are two different things; it was the consultation you mentioned that established Madame Dammauville's illness and instituted the treatment that Balzajette has only to apply, and his ability, I assure you, is sufficient for the task.
As she showed little reassurance, he felt he had to insist, for it would have been imprudent to leave Philis with the idea that if he treated Madame Dammauville he would surely cure her, even if it took a miracle:
- We've got some time to wait, since the court order for the trial has not yet been handed down; on the other hand, Madame Dammauville has promised to press her doctor to find out whether he hopes to get her out of bed soon; so let's wait and see.
- Wouldn't it be better to act than to wait?
- At least, let's wait for Balzajette's reply to his patient's request; either it will be satisfactory, and then we'll have nothing to do; or it won't be, and then I promise to see Balzajette. I know him well enough to be able to talk to him about your client, especially as I am allowed, by involving your brother, to take an open interest in her recovery.
- Oh, dear, dear," she murmured in a burst of emotional gratitude.
- You cannot doubt my devotion, first to you and then to your poor brother. You asked me to do something impossible which, to my great regret, I had to refuse you, precisely because it was impossible; but, as you well know, I am entirely yours and yours alone.
- Please forgive me.
- I have nothing to forgive you for; in your place I would think like you, but I think that in mine you would act like me.
- You can be sure that I have never had a thought in my heart of blaming you for your dignity; it is because you are tall and proud that I love you so passionately.
She rose to her feet.
- You're leaving," he said.
- I'd like to pass on Madame Dammauville's kind words to my mother: you can feel how anxious she is waiting for me.
- Let's go; I'll leave you at the boulevard to go up to Nougarède's.
The interview with the lawyer was short.
- You see, my dear friend, that my plan is a good one; bring me Madame Dammauville to the hearing and we'll spend a few pleasant moments together.
This time, Saniel didn't hesitate as he had the day before, and he went into the first barber's shop he found on his way.
- Does the gentleman want his hair curled?" asked the waiter as he sat down in an armchair.
- No, I want my hair clipped and shaved.
- That's a good example!
When Saniel returned home, he lit two candles and, placing them on his mantelpiece, looked at himself in the mirror.
Coquetry had never been his sin, and he had often spent weeks without stopping to look in the mirror: a washcloth with a rough cloth, a comb through his hair, a hard brushing of his beard, his tie tied up like the devil, and that was his whole grooming routine, for which mirrors were of no use to him. However, when he was a young boy, before he grew a beard, he had sometimes stood in front of the little mirror in his washbasin, studying himself with curiosity, wondering what he was and what he would become, just as, thinking about his future and probing his intelligence, he had wondered what he would be good at and what kind of man life would make of him; And from that time he remembered an energetic face with clearly defined features, an open and frank physiognomy which, without being what one would call pretty, was nevertheless not unpleasant. Since then, the beard had grown, hiding some of his features and changing his physiognomy; but now that it had fallen away, he told himself without thinking too much that he would undoubtedly find the young boy whose image he had kept in his memory.
What he found in front of the mirror was a transversely wrinkled forehead, oblique eyebrows, raised at the inner end, and a mouth with tight lips, lowered at the corners; furrows were dug into the cheeks, and the whole face, battered and ravaged, expressed harshness.
What had become of the young man of yesteryear? He was looking at the man life had made, whose face had been shaped by the violent contractions of his facial muscles.
- That really is the face of a murderer! he murmured.
Then, looking at his shaven head, he added with a sad smile:
- And perhaps that of a condemned man whose toilet has just been prepared for the guillotine.
VIII
To have made himself unrecognisable was no doubt a good precaution; but, having taken this route, he would not rest until he had exhausted them all, and in such a way that Madame Dammauville would never be able to find the man she had seen so clearly under Caffié's lamp.
Precisely because he was not coquettish and had no pretensions to beauty or seduction, he had escaped the photographic mania. Only once had he been photographed, and even then in spite of himself, after defending himself, simply so as not to refuse without reason an old friend who, abandoning medicine for photography, had wanted his head.
But now this one time was too much, because there could be a danger that his portrait, made three years earlier, and showing him with the hair and beard he had just removed, would be seen all over the world. Undoubtedly, there was little chance that he would ever pass a proof before Madame Dammauville's eyes; but, if there was only one against a hundred thousand, he had to make sure that he didn't have to fear it. One newspaper had published Florentin's portrait; at a given moment and in certain circumstances that could not be foreseen, but which were possible at the very least, another newspaper might also publish his. This was not to be, and for his own peace of mind he did not want to think that this danger could threaten him.
He had sent one to his mother, who was still alive at the time, one to the village priest and, later, he had given one to Philis, so he must have had nine left. He searched for them and, having found them in their box at the back of the drawer where he had put them when he had received them, he immediately burnt them.
Of the three that remained, only one could testify against him, that of Philis; but it would be easy for him to take it back by inventing a pretext; as for the other two, it was not admissible, really, that he would ever have anything to fear from them, his mother's having passed into the hands of an old aunt, who kept it hung by four nails on the smoke-filled wall of her cottage; his priest's being hidden deep in a remote village where no one would go and dig it up.
But where danger could arise, and where it was wise to wait for it, was with the photographer, who might have proofs in hand and who would surely keep the photograph.
As he entered the studio of his old comrade, he felt an unpleasant disappointment that troubled and worried him: he hadn't given his name, and relying on the changes that the cut of his hair and his beard brought to his physiognomy, he had told himself that this comrade, whom he hadn't seen for a long time, would certainly not recognise him.
When he had taken a few steps into the studio, hat in hand, like a stranger approaching a stranger, he saw the photographer coming towards him, hand outstretched, a friendly smile on his face:
- You, my dear! What good fortune has earned me the pleasure of your visit; is there anything I can do for you?
- Do you recognise me?
- What if I do? Are you asking me to get rid of your beard and hair? Obviously, it changes you and gives you a new look; but I would be unworthy of my profession if, because of a hair arrangement, I allowed myself to be so confused that I didn't recognise you. Besides, steely eyes like yours cannot be forgotten: they are a sign and a signature.
So this method in which he had put so much trust was just another careless move, just as the question "Do you recognise me?
- We're going to pose right away, aren't we? said the photographer. Very curious, that shaven head, and even more interesting, I think, than with the beard and hair: the character traits stand out better.
- It's not for a new portrait that I've come, it's for the old one; do you have any proofs?
- I don't think so; but we'll look for them; in any case, if you want some, it's easy to get away with it, since I have the plate.
- I don't have a single print of the ones you gave me, and when I looked at myself in the mirror this morning, I found such changes between my face today and the one I had three years ago that I'd like to study them; in this connection, ideas have occurred to me about facial expression, the accuracy of which I'd like to check with supporting documents.
The research carried out by a clerk produced no results: there were no proofs.
- A few days ago I was thinking of having some printed," said the photographer, "because now you've finally reached the day of glory when your portrait has its rightful place in showcases and collections: your competitions are the talk of the town. Although I have given up medicine with no intention of returning, I have not become indifferent to everything to do with it, and I have experienced your successes. Which portrait will we put into circulation? The old one or the new one?
- The new one.
- So let's get ready to pose.
- Not today; it was only yesterday that I had a shave, following a threat of alopecia, and the skin covered by the beard has retained a rawness of whiteness, which would accentuate the harshness of my physiognomy, which is really unnecessary; so we'll wait until the air has browned me a little; then I'll come back, I promise.
- How many proofs would you like of your old portrait?
- One is enough for me.
- I'll send you a dozen.
- Don't bother, I'll take them when I come to pose, but in the meantime couldn't you show me the plate?
- Nothing could be easier; it will be brought to you.
Saniel took the glass plate with his fingertips, delicately and carefully, at two opposite corners, so as not to erase it; then, as he was in the shadow of a blue curtain stretched under the window, he stepped out of it towards the fireplace, where the light was falling brightly, and began his examination.
- That's it," he said, "very curious!
- Only a photograph has this kind of documentary value.
To compare this document with reality, Saniel moved closer to the fireplace, above which was a mirror; when his feet touched the marble slab that formed the frame of the hearth, he stopped, looking alternately at the photograph, which he held preciously in his hands, and at his face reflected in the mirror.
Suddenly he let out an exclamation: he had just dropped the cliché, which, falling flat on the marble, had broken into small pieces that had jumped here and there.
- How clumsy of me!
He showed a spite that should have left no doubt in the photographer's mind: it was a lost piece of work, and anyone who has worked knows that you don't accept such a disappointment light-heartedly.
You'll have to get hold of one of the proofs you gave us," said the photographer, "because as far as I'm concerned I haven't got a single one; but that shouldn't be impossible.
- I'll look for one.
What he wanted to know when he left was whether or not he had succeeded in making himself unrecognisable, for he could not confine himself to this experience, which was flawed by the very fact that this old friend was a photographer: it was his trade to note the typical features that distinguish one physiognomy from another, and he had acquired through long practice a sure eye that Madame Dammauville could not possess.
Of all the people with whom he was acquainted, it seemed to him that the one in the best position to give certainty to the test was Madame Cormier. And he immediately went up to Les Batignolles: at that hour, he knew Philis was going out for a lesson; Madame Cormier would be alone, and, as she had certainly not been informed by her daughter that he was to have a shave, the experiment would be conducted in such a way as to give as exact a result as possible.
When the doorbell rang, it was Mrs Cormier who came to open the door and he saluted without her recognising him, but as the entrance was dark, this was of little significance. Hat in hand, he followed her into the dining room, without speaking so that his voice would not betray him.
Then, after she had stared at him for a moment, surprised at first, she began to smile:
- But it's Mr Saniel! she exclaimed. My God! how silly I am not to have recognised you; it's such a change to have had a shave! I beg your pardon.
- It's because I've had a shave that I've come to ask you a favour.
- To us, dear sir! Ah, speak quickly; we would be so happy to show you our gratitude.
- I'd like to ask Miss Philis to give me back, if she still has it, a photograph I gave her about a year ago.
As Philis wanted the freedom to show this photograph frankly, so as to keep it always in front of her, it was in the presence of her mother that she asked for it and in the presence of Madame Cormier that Saniel gave it to her.
- If she still has it!" exclaimed Madame Cormier; "my dear sir, you don't know the place that all your kindnesses and the services you have rendered us have won for you in our hearts.
Saniel took it out, explaining the study for which he needed it and, having promised to bring it back soon, he returned home.
Decidedly, everything had worked out well: the cliché destroyed, Philis's proof in his hands, he had nothing more to fear on that score; as for the experiment tried on Madame Cormier, it was decisive enough to inspire him with full confidence: If Mrs Cormier, who had seen him so often, for so long, and who thought of him every moment, had not recognised him, how could Mrs Dammauville, who had seen him only from a distance and for only a few seconds, recognise him after several months? 
He would never get used to the idea that his life could not have the tranquil monotony of a bourgeois existence, that it would have its bumps and storms, but that, if he knew how to always remain in control of his strength and will, he would have to bring it to a safe harbour!
The calm he had felt before the storm soon returned, and when the final rounds of his competitions, confirming the success of the first, had given him the two titles he had so ardently desired and pursued at the cost of so much pain, so much effort and so much hardship, he could safely enjoy his triumph.
At last, he had the present in his strong hands, and the future was his!
Now he could walk straight, boldly, with his head held high, shoving aside those who got in his way, at a pace that suited his temperament.
Although the last few months had been terribly agitated for him by everything to do with the Caffié and Florentin affair, and above all by the fatigue, emotions and fevers of his competitions, he had nevertheless not interrupted his individual work for a day or an hour, and his experiments, which he had been pursuing for so many years, had finally yielded important results, which only prudence had prevented him from publishing. In opposition to the official teaching of the school, these discoveries would have raised the hairs on old skulls where none had been seen for a long time, and it was not the moment, when he asked for the door, to attract the hostility of those venerable doorkeepers who would block the way to a revolutionary. But now that he was in place for at least ten years, he no longer had to be sparing with people or ideas, and he could speak out.
IX
Saniel had seen his colleague the solemn Balzajette, and, skilfully enough not to provoke either surprise or suspicion, he had been able to tell him about Madame Dammauville, in whom he was incidentally interested; without insisting, in passing and only to justify his question, he had explained the nature of this interest.
To be solemn, Balzajette was no less talkative, and it was even his solemnity that made him talk: he listened to himself talk, and when, with his legs slightly apart, he was sitting comfortably on a pavement that wasn't too narrow, puffing out his chest, resting his pink, clean-shaven chin on his white tie, and making noble, demonstrative gestures in the air with his ringed hand, if you had the patience to listen to him, you could get him to say anything you wanted: His patients could be waiting for him in pain or in anguish, but he would not hasten the majestic flow of his rhapsodic sentences with their choice adjectives, and unless he was going to an invitation to dinner, which happened to him at least five days a week, he would not let you go until he had shared with you the admiration he professed for himself. 
Madame Dammauville's paralysis was due to an affection of the spinal cord: consequently, it was perfectly curable; and even Balzajette was surprised that, with his treatment and care, this cure was slow in coming:
- But what can I tell you, young colleague? You know better than I do that with women anything is possible... especially the impossible!
And for half an hour he had complacently recounted the astonishment caused to his knowledge and experience by the women of the world whom he treated: certainly he had no intention of disputing the lessons that the doctor receives in hospital - God forbid that he should have such arrogance! -- But how much more varied, how much more complete, how much more profound were those given by a social clientele when one was fortunate enough to have created one for oneself.
- To sum up, what can I tell you, young colleague?
And what he had said over and over again, explained and re-explained in tangled digressions, was how he wanted to get his client back on her feet before long. Of course, he had no intention of disputing the recent work published on the pathological anatomy of spinal cord lesions - God forbid that he should be so arrogant! -- But experience is experience, and, without being boastful, he believed he could rely on the experience he had acquired over thirty years of practice with his social clientele.
- You don't treat a duchess like a trader of the four seasons, do you, my young colleague?
Although Balzajette did not read anything outside a boulevardier newspaper and never opened a book to keep up to date, Saniel's young reputation had reached him, - through his ears, - and precisely because it was young, he was keen to spare this colleague, who seemed destined to make a name for himself. Despite the high esteem he professed for his merits and his person, he was vaguely aware that the doctors of his generation, who had reached great heights, did not treat him with all the consideration he gave himself, and, to teach a lesson to his old comrades, he was quite happy to establish good relations with a young man on the move; he would speak of his young colleague Saniel: "You know, the one who has just been appointed agrégé", and he would tell of the advice Balzajette had given him.
Saniel doubted very much that Madame Dammauville would be back on her feet in time to come to the hearing, especially after Balzajette had explained her treatment; but, with the situation that this appearance would have put her in, he could only rejoice. Without doubt, it would be unfortunate for Florentin not to have this testimony and not to take advantage of the coup de théâtre prepared by Nougarède; but, for himself, he could only be happy. Despite all the precautions he had taken, it was better not to expose himself to an encounter with Madame Dammauville in the witness room or even at the hearing. They would stick to a letter supported by Balzajette's deposition, and Florentin would still be acquitted: only Nougarède would have to regret his stunt; but he had no need to worry about Nougarède's satisfaction or disappointment.
Of course, he had not told Philis the ideas that his conversation with Balzajette had suggested to him, merely summarising the conclusions of that conversation: before long Madame Dammauville would be back on her feet; Balzajette said so; if he was not an infallible master, he knew enough to be taken at his word: If he wasn't an infallible master, he knew enough for us to take him at his word: since he promised a quick improvement, we had to believe it: Florentin would be saved; all we had to do was let things go, they were on the right track, as right as if we had directed them ourselves. 
And Philis, Madame Cormier, Nougarède, Florentin himself, whom the Mazas cell had not, however, reconciled either with hope or with providential justice, were all complicit in this idea.
So when the indictment division referred Florentin to the assizes, Madame Cormier and Philis were not too upset: Madame Dammauville would be in a position to give her evidence, since she had been able to leave her bed the day before, and even though she had only been up for an hour, even though she had only been able to leave her room to go to her sitting room, that was enough. Nougarède said that the case would come up at the second session in April: by then, Madame Dammauville would be strong enough on her feet to appear before the jury and win her acquittal.
With Philis, Saniel had repeated that the cure was certain, and with her too he had rejoiced greatly; but at the same time he had been worried about this cure: this meeting, the very idea of which had frightened him to the point of making him lose his head, was therefore going to happen and under conditions that could not fail to move him. In truth, the precautions he had taken should have reassured him, but there was still a disturbing uncertainty. Who could know! He would have preferred it if she hadn't left her room, as Balzajette's treatment suggested she would, and if Nougarède had found a way of obtaining her statement without her bringing it herself; he would have felt more reassured, and it would have been with a calmer mind, and a more impassive face, that he would have gone to the hearing.
Was he really unrecognisable? This was the question that now obsessed him, and several times a day he would stand in front of a mirror, holding the photograph he had taken at Philis's house, and at length he would compare his present appearance with the one in the portrait. Sometimes he found that the dissimilarities were such that anyone who didn't know in advance that these two faces belonged to the same head would never imagine it. But at other times he was struck by the points of contact, and then he told himself that they could also strike Madame Dammauville. The beard and hair had fallen out, but the eyes - those steely eyes, as his old friend the photographer used to say - had remained, and nothing could change or hide them. There was one way: wear a blue lorgnon or glasses, injure himself in a chemistry experiment that would require a bandage; but this would be a disguise that would provoke curiosity and questions all the more dangerous because it would coincide with the removal of the beard and hair. -- Why did he try to change his appearance so completely? -- We shouldn't have asked ourselves that, because it would be opening up a trail that could lead far.
But these worries did not torment him for long, for Philis, who now passed every day in the rue Sainte-Anne to check on Madame Dammauville, arrived one evening in despair and told him that that day the patient had only been able to stay up for a few minutes and had had to return to bed.
So she would not be going to the hearing.
This apprehension of coming face to face with Madame Dammauville had finally exasperated him: he thought himself a coward to put up with it, and, since he didn't have the strength to shake it off, he was happy to find himself rid of it by the mere intervention of chance, which, after having been bad for him for so long, was becoming good for him; the wheel was turning.
- See Madame Dammauville every day," he said to Philis, "and take note of everything she feels; perhaps I'll find something to fix this snag that I can suggest to Balzajette without him suspecting anything. Besides, it's likely that the cold snap we're experiencing has a lot to do with her relapse, and it's probable that with a bit of spring warmth she'll get better.
In her anguish, which was increasing as the trial drew nearer, Philis could not rely on probabilities or on the uncertain influence of spring. She needed more and better; but, for fear of being refused, she was careful not to tell him what she hoped to obtain.
It was only when she had succeeded that she spoke.
Every evening, as she left Madame Dammauville's, she came to tell her what she had learned, and for three days her story had been the same:
- She can't leave her bed.
And he always gave her the same answer:
- It's the cold; certainly, the weather will change: at the end of March, this frost and this wind cannot continue.
He was saddened by her desolation and anguish, but what could he do? It wasn't his fault that this relapse had occurred just at the decisive moment; chance had been against him for long enough: he wasn't going to upset it at the very moment when it was on his side, by giving in to the desire that Philis didn't dare express, but which he guessed, and agreeing to see Madame Dammauville.
On the fourth day, when she entered his study, he knew at once from her demeanour that some happy change for Florentin had occurred.
- Has Madame Dammauville got up yet?
- No, she hasn't!
- I thought so from the vivacity and lightness of your entrance.
- Well, I'm very happy indeed: Madame Dammauville wants to consult you.
He grabbed both her hands violently and shook them:
- You did this!" he cried.
She looked at him in horror.
- You! You!" he repeated with growing fury.
- At least listen to me," she whispered, "you'll see that I haven't compromised you in any way.
Compromise! It was professional dignity he was really thinking of!
- I don't have to listen to you: I'm not going.
- Don't say that.
- All you needed was to do with me as you pleased.
- Victor?
Anger made him panic.
- So I belong to you; so I'm your thing; so you do what you want with me; you decide and all I have to do is obey! It's all too much in the end! You can leave, it's all over between us.
She listened to him shattered; but this last word, which struck at her love, gave her back her strength; in her turn she took both his hands, and although he wanted to free them, she held them in hers:
- You can throw all the words of anger in my face; you can reproach me as much as you think I deserve; I won't rebel. No doubt I am wrong towards you, and I feel their full extent when I see how deeply you are hurt; but to drive me away, to tell me that everything is over between us, no, Victor, you won't do that; you won't say that, because you know that no man has ever been loved like I love you, adored, respected: and voluntarily, of my own free will, even to save my brother, I would have compromised you!
He pushed her away:
- Go away," he said harshly.
She threw herself on her knees and, holding the hands he had taken from her, she kissed them desperately.
- But listen to me," she cried, "before you condemn me, let me say what I have to say for myself. Even if I were a hundred times more guilty than I really am, you cannot reject me with such merciless harshness.
- Go away!
- You're losing your mind; your anger is leading you astray. What's the matter with you? It can't be me who, through my clumsiness, through my fault, has put you in this state of mad exasperation. What's the matter, my beloved?
These few words did more than Philis's desperate submission and her bursts of love: she was right, he was losing his head; and however guilty she was towards him, she could not obviously admit that the fault she had committed had thrown him into this fit of furious madness; it was not natural, and in his words as in his actions, everything had to be natural, everything had to be explainable.
- Well, speak," he said, "I'm listening; besides, it's better to know. So speak.
X
- You must understand," she said a little more calmly, for, since he was allowing her to speak, she hoped to convince him, "that for the last four days I have done everything I could to bring Madame Dammauville to the idea of calling in a doctor to consult with Mr Balzajette?
- Who would be me.
- ... You or someone else; I didn't mention any names; you mustn't think me clumsy enough to rudely put yourself forward; it wouldn't have been a good way of getting an intelligent woman to accept you; and I care enough about your dignity not to play with it. I believed that a doctor other than Mr Balzajette would find a remedy, a means of some kind, a miracle, if you like, that would enable Madame Dammauville to go to court, and I said so; I said it in every tone, in every way, with as much persuasion as I could put into my words. Wasn't it my brother's life I was defending, our honour? At first I found Madame Dammauville very opposed to the idea. -- Another doctor, what's the point? Mr Balzajette had treated her well, since he had been able to get her out of bed. It's true that she had to take him back; but that was an accident that could not be blamed on him without injustice. How many reasons could explain this accident? Her long illness, her weakness, the bad weather. She would get better soon, she felt. Besides, even if she had to be taken to the courthouse, she wouldn't hesitate.
- She would do that!
- Of course she would. No one has a greater sense of what is right than she does: she would be guilty of not giving evidence for an innocent man; not to save him when she could would be to take responsibility for his loss. So it's certain that, if she can't come to the hearing on her own, she'll do her utmost to get there any way she can, on Mr Balzajette's arm, on a stretcher. So I was quite calm about that, but not about the stretcher. What would people think if they saw it in that state? What impression would this patient make on the jurors! Would her illness make her testimony worthless? That made me insist. I think I told you that Madame Dammauville now shows me affectionate sympathy, which increases every day: she makes me stay near her longer; she listens to me with kindness; finally she shows me true friendship; as if I had known her for a long time and had been able to do her a favour. I took advantage of this kindness to return to the question of the consultation, but, I repeat, without mentioning your name and without ever putting yourself forward. Let that be clearly understood and, please, believe me when I say it. I told her that, since Mr Balzajette could say to himself, with all the appearances of reason, that he had cured her, he should not be angry that she wished to seek to consolidate this cure; that, moreover, she had compelling reasons which obliged her not to wait, for it would cost her a great deal to appear at the court of assizes in a theatrical device which was not at all in her character or in her habits. It didn't take much shrewdness for me to guess that the main obstacle to this consultation was the concern to upset this old friend of her husband's, whom she is too intelligent not to know. It was then that your name was mentioned.
- So you admit it!
- You'll see how and you'll say whether you should be angry about it. I didn't spend so much time with Madame Dammauville without telling her about Mama, and consequently without telling her how you cured her of a paralysis which, in more ways than one, resembled hers. It wasn't wrong, was it, to say what you had done for us, and, without giving anything away about my love, I could undoubtedly praise you out of sheer gratitude. You know patients too well not to guess that Madame Dammauville herself had first asked me many times about the similarities between her paralysis and Mama's, about the treatment you had ordered and the effects it had produced, and naturally, as always when I speak of you, when I have the joy of pronouncing your name, I answered her at length and in detail.
She waited a moment, looking at him; without softening the hardness of his gaze, he beckoned her to continue.
- When I insisted on the consultation, Madame Dammauville remembered what I'd told her and was the first -- you mean the first -- to mention your name. I had no reason, it seems to me, to confine myself to a reserve that would have been inexplicable and incomprehensible; so I told her everything a woman could say about a man in your position. Since you had treated and cured my mother, I had every right to praise you; with a nature like hers, she wouldn't have understood if I hadn't, and she would certainly have thought I was being ungrateful. I quoted your work on diseases of the medulla oblongata, and that too was quite natural: since it was from a disease of the medulla that my mother had been cured, it was permissible for me, however ignorant I was of medicine, to have read and studied it before the cure. As she expressed a desire to know it, I offered to lend it to her...
- Is that natural?
- With anyone other than Madame Dammauville, no, no doubt; but she is not a frivolous person, her reading is serious; she knows a lot; in the end, I thought I could lend it to her without doing any harm and without incurring your reproach. I took it to her two days ago, and just now she told me that she had decided to call you after reading it.
- I'm certainly not going: she has her doctor.
- Don't imagine that I've been asked to ask you to visit her; everything is agreed with Mr Balzajette, who is supposed to write to you or see you, I don't know exactly.
- That would be quite extraordinary coming from Balzajette!
- Perhaps you're misjudging him. When Madame Dammauville spoke to him about you, he didn't raise the slightest objection; on the contrary, he praised you; he says that you are one of the rare young people who can be trusted; these are his own words that Madame Dammauville told me.
- I don't care what that old beast thinks!
- I'll explain how you came to be seen, not because I mentioned you, but because Mr Balzajette trusts you. No matter how stupid he is, he does you justice and knows what you're worth.
So the moment had arrived for this meeting, which he had not wanted to believe possible at first, but which, nevertheless, presented itself in such conditions that he could not see how to avoid it. He could refuse Philis, but Balzajette? How? On what pretext? A colleague was calling him in for a consultation, why wouldn't he go? He would have left Paris until the trial, but he was caught unawares. What could he say to justify an absence he had not announced? He had no mother, no brothers who could call him and with whom he was obliged to stay. Besides, he wanted to go to the hearing, and since his testimony would carry considerable weight with the jury, it was his duty to bring it to Florentin; it would have been a contemptible cowardice to fail in this duty, and, moreover, it would have been imprudent: in the eyes of everyone, he had to appear to have nothing to fear, and this assurance, this self-confidence, was one of the conditions of his salvation. Now, if he came to the hearing, and from every point of view it was impossible that he wouldn't, he would meet Madame Dammauville there, since she wanted to be taken there in case she couldn't go freely. Either at her home or at the Palace of Justice, the meeting was therefore fatal, and, whatever he might have done, circumstances stronger than his will had prepared and brought it about: whatever he tried would not prevent it.
The only question worthy of serious consideration at this time was where this meeting would be less dangerous for him -- at Madame Dammauville's, or at the Palais? Everything else was above him and beyond his control.
He was thinking silently, not paying any more attention to Philis than if she were not near him, not looking at her, his eyes lost in vagueness, his forehead contracted, his lips tightened, when the doorbell rang: as Joseph was at his post, Saniel did not move.
- If he's ill," said Philis, who didn't want to leave yet, "I'll wait in the dining room.
And she got up.
Before she had left, Joseph entered:
- Monsieur le docteur Balzajette," he said.
- You see," cried Philis.
Without answering, Saniel motioned to Joseph to bring in Dr Balzajette, and as she quietly disappeared, he headed for the living room.
Balzajette came to him with both hands outstretched:
- Hello, my young colleague! Pleased to meet you.
The welcome was kind, friendly and also protective; Saniel responded as best he could.
- Ever since we met," continued Balzajette, "I've been thinking about you. The first time you came to visit me, I liked you straight away; I guessed you and said to myself: "There's a big boy who'll make his mark". Do you remember?
He certainly did, and of all the visits he had made to the doctors and pharmacists in his neighbourhood at that time, the one to Balzajette had been the hardest; it was impossible to show more arrogance, more haughtiness, more disdain, than this solemn man had shown in his welcome. But then the young confrere was lost in the crowd of poor devils and in all likelihood he would stay there; whereas, now that he had left it, we didn't know where he would go.
- I told you I'd been thinking about you," continued Balzajette, "about that client you told me about, you know?
- Madame Dammauville?
- Precisely. I got her back on her feet, as I was sure I would and as I told you; but since then, this bad temperature has put her back to bed. It's only a matter of days, no doubt; only, in the meantime, the poor woman is getting irritated and impatient; you know, young colleague, women! So what can I tell you? To calm this impatience, I spontaneously offered her a consultation, and naturally I mentioned your name, which had been indicated by your fine work on spinal cord lesions; I supported him appropriately, with the esteem he had earned for himself, and I had the satisfaction of seeing him accept.
Saniel thanked him as if he believed in the sincerity of this spontaneous proposal.
- I like young people, and I want you to know that whenever the opportunity arises, I'll be happy to introduce you to my clientele. For Madame Dammauville, what day would you like us to take?
As Saniel seemed hesitant, Balzajette, misunderstanding the reason for his silence, insisted:
- She's impatient," he said; "so let's take the earliest possible day.
An answer had to be given, and under the circumstances a refusal could not be explained.
- Do you want tomorrow?
- Tomorrow, of course. What time?
Before answering, Saniel went to his desk and consulted an almanac, which seemed perfectly ridiculous to Balzajette:
- Does the young colleague imagine that I'm going to think his time is so tightly taken that he needs some combination to give me an hour? But it was nothing of the sort that Saniel was looking for: to pose in front of this old fool, to dazzle him, he had a mind for that! His almanac gave sunrise and sunset, and it was the precise time of sunset that he wanted: 26th March, 6.20 a.m. At that moment, it would not yet be dark enough for the lamps to be lit in Madame Dammauville's house, and yet the day would already be dark enough for her to see it badly in the uncertainty of the evening.
- Would you like a quarter past six? I'll pick you up at six.
- Gladly; only I would ask you to be very exact; I have a dinner for seven o'clock in the Rue Royale.
And Saniel promised to be exact; this dinner was a favourable circumstance which promised him to leave Madame Dammauville's before the lamps were brought in.
When Balzajette had left, he went to join Philis in the dining room, where she was waiting anxiously:
- An appointment has been made for tomorrow, at a quarter past six, at Madame Dammauville's house.
She threw herself around his neck:
- I knew you would forgive me.
XI
It was not without emotion that Saniel watched the afternoon pass the next day and, although he had set to work to make the most of his time, he stopped every moment to look at the clock.
Sometimes he found that the clock was ticking fast, then immediately that it was not ticking at all.
This restlessness exasperated him, because calm had never been more necessary than in these circumstances; if danger presented itself, it was only by remaining in control that he could save himself: he needed the composure of a surgeon in an operation, the vision of a general in battle, and composure and vision are neither found in the nervous or the restless.
Would this danger arise?
This was the question that kept coming back, that imposed itself, whatever he did to avoid it, and threw him into a confusion that was all the more irritating because he was perfectly aware of the futility of such an examination. What was the point of studying the pros and cons? It was all up in the air, in the hands of chance, and therefore beyond his control.
Either he had succeeded in making himself unrecognisable, or he had not; that was a fact he could do nothing about now.
What was humanly possible in this respect he had foreseen by choosing an hour when the darkness of the evening put the odds in his favour; for the rest, he had to rely on Fortune.
And going to his fireplace, he stood in front of the mirror, comparing his face with the photograph Mrs Cormier had given him, and muscle after muscle, he studied himself. Ah! if he hadn't had those pale blue eyes, those steely eyes, he would have felt calmer; but in the darkness, would Madame Dammauville see his gaze?
All day long he had studied the sky, because for the success of his plan it was important that it was neither too light nor too dark: too light, because Madame Dammauville would be able to get a good look at him; too dark, because the lamps would be lit. He had to remember that it was precisely under the light of a lamp that she had seen him. Until the evening, the weather was uncertain, with a sometimes sunny, sometimes cloudy sky; but then the clouds were blown away by a northerly wind, and the weather turned decidedly cold, with the pale pink light of late March when it is still freezing.
On examining himself carefully, he had the satisfaction of noting that he was calmer than he had been in the morning, and that as the moment of the assault drew nearer his agitation subsided: decision, firmness and composure had returned to him; he felt master of his will and capable of obeying it alone.
At six o'clock on the dot, he rang Balzajette's doorbell and they left immediately for the Rue Sainte-Anne. Happy to have a listener with willing ears, Balzajette did all the talking, without Saniel having to answer anything other than yes or no from time to time, and, of course, it wasn't Madame Dammauville he was talking about, but worldly stories: of the first performance of the previous day at the Opéra-Comique, which he had attended; of politics; of the forthcoming Salon, where several important paintings would be shown, for which the painters had asked his opinion, certain in advance that it would be that of public opinion. 
At just a quarter past six, they arrived at the house in the rue Sainte-Anne, where Saniel had not been since Caffié's death. As they passed the dressing room of the old concierge, who greeted Balzajette respectfully, he was pleased with himself: his heart wasn't beating too fast, his ideas were firm and clear, all in the present moment, neither here nor there: if any danger arose he felt sure he could face it without panic or brutality.
When Balzajette rang the bell, the door was immediately opened by a chambermaid who had obviously been posted in the vestibule to await their arrival.
Balzajette went first and Saniel followed, giving a quick glance at the rooms they were passing through: a dining room furnished in mahogany, and a drawing room with faded tapestries; they had reached a door on which Balzajette knocked twice.
- Come in," answered a firm-sounding woman's voice.
It was the decisive moment: the day was just right, neither too bright nor too dark. What would Madame Dammauville's first glance tell her?
- My colleague Dr Saniel," announced Balzajette as he went to shake hands with Madame Dammauville.
She was lying on the little bed Philis had mentioned, but not against the windows, but rather in the middle of the room, obviously placed there according to the experience of a patient who knows that people will have to turn around her to examine her.
Taking advantage of this arrangement, Saniel passed immediately between the bed and the windows, so that the light would hit him from behind and leave his face in shadow; this was done naturally, without any affectation, and it seemed that he had only taken this side of the bed because Balzajette had taken the other.
The examination began, conducted by Saniel with a neatness and precision that pleased Balzajette: He went straight to the point, asking and seeking only what was essential, and, as Madame Dammauville's answers were as precise as Saniel's questions, while listening and saying a brief word from time to time, he told himself that his dinner would not be delayed, which was the main point of his concern. He was a boy to protect, to launch: with his heavy bearing, his rough demeanour, his careless dress, there would be no rivalry to fear; before he had acquired the manners and correctness of a man of the world, if he ever did, time would pass.
However, when Madame Dammauville complained about the cold she was feeling, Balzajette found that Saniel let her lose herself in rather minute details.
- Have you always been cold?
- Yes, and with an annoying tendency to catch a cold if the temperature drops a degree or two.
- Did you exercise outdoors?
- Very little.
- Didn't anyone ever advise you to take cold water baths?
- I couldn't stand them.
- I must tell you," interrupted Balzajette, "that before living in this house, this old house which belongs to her, Madame Dammauville occupied a more modern flat, where she was heated by the calorifère, and where, consequently, it was easy for her to maintain a mild and uniform temperature to which she had become accustomed.
- When I came to live in this house, where it was not possible to install a calorifier," continued Madame Dammauville, "I did everything I could to protect myself from the cold which, I am sure, is my great enemy: you can see that I had double stuffing put in the doors as well as the windows.
Despite this invitation and the gesture that accompanied it, Saniel was careful not to turn his head towards the window; he kept his face in the shade, content to look at the door that faced him.
- At the same time," continued Madame Dammauville, "I've put wall hangings on the walls, carpets on the floor, thick curtains on the windows and doors, and despite the big fires I keep in my fireplace, I often can't keep warm.
- Do you light this stove too?" asked Saniel, pointing to a small mobile stove in the corner of the fireplace.
- Only at night, so that my servants don't have to get up hour after hour to keep the fire going: we load it in the evening before I fall asleep, we put the pipe in the chimney, and until morning it keeps up a more or less adequate heat.
- I think it would be a good idea to do away with this method of heating, which can have serious disadvantages," says Saniel, "and my colleague and I will shortly be examining the question of whether it would not be possible to give you the heat you need, with this chimney alone, without tiring your servants and without waking you up too often to load the fire. But let's continue.
When he had come to the end of his questions, he got up to examine the patient on her bed, but without turning around her, and so as to remain against the light.
As the reverberation of the setting sun in the sky gradually faded, Balzajette suggested asking for some lamps; without being too hasty in his reply, Saniel refused: there was no need, daylight was enough.
They went into the living room, where they agreed all the more quickly, as Balzajette replied to everything Saniel said:
- I'm glad to see that you share my point of view; that's it, that's it!
They both had their reasons for hurrying: Saniel, fear of the lamps; Balzajette, concern for her dinner. Saniel proposed the treatment, Balzajette approved.
- That's it, that's it!
The question of doing away with the mobile stove was also decided in two words: for the night, we would install a grate in the chimney, fill it with coal and cover it with wet dust, and with this system, used in many small railway stations, we would have fire until the morning.
- Let's go home," said Balzajette, who had initiative and decisiveness in all material matters.
Saniel, who had kept his eyes on the windows, was calm: it was still light enough for lamps not to be needed; moreover, during their tête-à-tête, no servant had crossed the salon to enter Madame Dammauville's house.
But when Balzajette opened the bedroom door to return to the sick woman, a flood of light filled the living room and enveloped them: a lamp with a shade was placed on a small table beside the bed; two other lamps with globes were lit on the mantelpiece, reflecting their light in the mirror.
How could he not have foreseen that there was another door to Madame Dammauville's room other than the one in the living room? But if he had foreseen it, if he had seen the door hidden by the curtain, it would have done nothing to lessen the danger of his situation. He would have found the time to prepare himself, that's all. But what was there to prepare for? Either enter the room and face up to the danger, or flee. He entered.
- This is what we've decided," said Balzajette, who never lost an opportunity to speak up.
While he was speaking, Madame Dammauville did not seem to be listening to him; she had fixed her eyes on Saniel, placed between her and the fireplace so as to turn her back on the lamps, and she was looking at him with a characteristic stare.
Balzajette, who was listening to herself speak, noticed nothing; but Saniel, who knew what was behind that look, could not help but be struck by it; fortunately for him all he had to do was let Balzajette go, which meant that he could not betray himself by the quivering of his voice.
However, Balzajette seemed ready to get to the end of his explanations: all of a sudden Saniel saw Madame Dammauville reach for the lamp on the table and lift the shade, lowering it towards her so as to form a reflector that projected the light onto him; at the same time he received a ray of light in the face.
Madame Dammauville gave a muffled cry.
Balzajette stopped, then his astonished eyes went from Madame Dammauville to Saniel, and from Saniel to Madame Dammauville.
- You're not ill? he said.
- I'm not ill at all.
Then what was the matter? But he rarely asked for an explanation of something that surprised him, preferring to guess and explain it himself.
- Ah, I've got it," he said with a satisfied smile, "you're surprised at the youth of my young colleague. It's his fault; why on earth did he have his long hair and curly beard cut?
If Madame Dammauville hadn't dropped the lampshade, she would have seen Saniel's face discolour and his lips quiver.
- But that's it!" continued Balzajette; "he has made this sacrifice to his new duties: the student has disappeared in front of the teacher.
He could have gone on for a long time: neither Madame Dammauville nor Saniel were listening to him; but, thinking of his dinner, he was not going to launch into a speech that at any other time he would not have failed to make; he rose to withdraw.
As Saniel waved, Madame Dammauville stopped him with a wave of her hand.
- Didn't you know the unfortunate man who was murdered opposite?
However serious an admission this was, Saniel could not respond with a denial.
- I was called in to confirm his death," he said.
And he took a few steps towards the door, but she stopped him again:
- Were you in touch with him?
- I had seen him sometimes.
Balzajette cut this idle conversation short:
- Good evening, dear lady. I'll see you tomorrow, but not in the morning, as I'm leaving for the country at six and won't be back until midday.
XII
- Did you notice how I cut him off?" said Balzajette on the stairs. If you listened to women, they'd never let you go. Damned if I know why she told you about this murdered man! Do you have any idea?
- No, I don't doubt it.
- I think the murder has to some extent scrambled her brains; in any case, it's made her hate this house.
He went on like this without Saniel listening to what he was saying; as he reached the rue Neuve-des-Petits-Champs, Balzajette hailed a carriage that was passing empty.
- You've been kind enough not to make me late," he said, shaking hands with his young colleague; "but I can see that I'll have to walk fast to get to my dinner, and I don't like to sit at a good table without feeling at my best. Goodbye, then!
What a mess! All this chatter was making Saniel dizzy.
He had to recover, recognise himself, consider the situation and what could, what should, come of it.
It was a simple situation; Madame Dammauville's cry had revealed it: when the light from the lamp had struck him in the face, she had found in him the man she had seen pulling back Caffié's curtains. If, in her astonishment, she had at first refused to believe it, her questions about Caffié, and Balzajette's explanations about the young colleague's long hair and curly beard had destroyed her hesitations and replaced doubt with the horror of certainty: she knew it, she had seen it; and, as she had just revealed herself to him, it did not seem that she would be a woman to deny the testimony of her eyes and to allow the solidity of her memories, supported by the words of Balzajette, to be shaken by simple denials. 
But what he could not see was how she was going to push him into this vertiginous abyss, in other words to whom she would reveal the discovery she had just made: to Philis, to Balzajette, to the law.
It was almost a relief to him to think that for this evening at least it couldn't be Philis, since at this very moment she was supposed to be at his house, anxiously awaiting his return; there was a pain and a revolt in him at the thought that she might be the first to learn the truth. He didn't want that to happen, and he would prevent it.
This concern gave him a purpose; he returned to Rue Louis-le-Grand, thinking more of Philis than of himself. What a disaster when she knew everything! How would she bear this blow and what feelings would it inspire in her, what judgement would she make of the one she loved? The poor girl! He felt sorry for her. He was lost, and it was his fault; he bore the sorrow of his clumsiness; but she would bear the sorrow of her love: what a wound for such a sensitive heart, for such a high and proud soul!
Perhaps he was going to see her for the last time; this hour and nothing more.
So he wanted her to be sweet to him, and to leave him a memory that would later ease his pain, a clear, warm ray in his mourning. He had been hard lately, whimsical, brutal, inexplicable, and, with that serenity of mood that was his very nature, she had not held it against him; when he jostled her with his heavy hand, she had kissed that hand, fixing her beautiful, tender eyes on him, full of passionate caresses. She had to forget that, and take away from their last meeting a tender impression that would sustain her.
What would he do for her? He remembered how happy she had been at their impromptu dinners by the fire six months before and he wanted to give her the same pleasure: he would see her happy again and, next to her, under her gaze, perhaps he would forget the next day.
He went into the restaurant that provided his lunches and ordered two dinners to be brought to his house immediately.
He didn't have to put the key in the lock: Philis was behind the door, listening. When she recognised his footsteps on the landing, she opened the door.
- And what happened?
- Your brother is safe.
- Is Madame Dammauville going to the hearing?
- I promise you he's safe.
- From you?
- Yes, by me... precisely.
In her confusion of joy, she didn't notice the accent of these last words.
- So you forgive me?
He took her in his arms and kissed her with deep emotion:
- With all my heart, I swear!
- You can see that it was written that you would go to Madame Dammauville's, in spite of yourself, in spite of everything; it was providential.
- It's certain that your friend Providence couldn't have intervened in my affairs at a better time.
This time she could not help being struck by the tone he had used in his reply; but she imagined that it was only this allusion to a higher intervention that had upset him.
- I was thinking of us," she said, "not you.
- I understand that. But let's not talk about that; you're happy, I don't want to spoil your joy; on the contrary, I thought I'd join in by surprising you: we're going to have dinner together.
- Oh, dear!" she cried, shivering, "how kind you are!
But then she stopped:
- And Mum waiting anxiously! I can't let her devour herself.
- I'll give your letter to Colliot's boy who's going to bring our dinner up, and he'll send it by messenger.
The letter was quickly delivered.
- Now," said Philis cheerfully, "I'm going to set the table; you light the fire, because we need a nice blaze that will sparkle in the fireplace, cheer us up and keep our dinner warm. What did you order?
- I don't know: two dinners.
- So much the better! We'll have some surprises; we'll leave the dishes covered in front of the fire, and we'll take them at random; maybe we'll eat the roast before the starter; but that'll just make it more fun.
Light, lively and busy, she came and went around the table, graceful and charming.
When the waiter rang the bell, the table was set; Philis gave him her letter and sent him away as quickly as possible, for she was anxious to be face to face with Saniel.
So they sat down at the table in front of the fire, facing each other.
- What a joy to be alone," she said, "to be able to talk and look at each other freely!
He was looking at her with a tenderness she had never seen in his eyes, a depth of emotional contemplation that overwhelmed and devastated her. From time to time, little cries of happiness escaped her:
- Oh, dear, dear," she murmured.
However, she knew him too well not to see that often a cloud of sadness veiled those eyes, so full of love, and that often too they were expressionless, as if they were looking inwards. At first she said nothing, but as time went on, she could not always silence her concern: why this melancholy at a time like this?
- What a difference between this dinner," she said, "and those at the end of October! At that time, you were crushed by the most difficult difficulties, battling with creditors, threatened on all sides, with no tomorrow; and now everything is smoothed out: no more creditors, no more battles, the troubles I imposed on you have come to an end, life opens up easy and glorious, the goal you were pursuing has been achieved, you only have to walk straight ahead, proud and superb. And yet there is a sadness in your face that torments me. What's the matter with you? Please speak. To whom will you confess, if not to the one who adores you?
He stared at her for a long time without answering, wondering whether, for the repose of his heart, this confession would not be better than silence, but his courage failed him, pride closed his lips.
- What do you want from me? If my face is sad, it's because it doesn't accurately reflect what I feel; because what I feel at the moment is an ineffable feeling of tenderness for you, an inexpressible gratitude for your love and for the happiness you have given me. If I've been happy in my rough and tumble life, it's been because of you; whatever good I've had - joy, confidence, hope, memories - I owe to you; and, if we hadn't met, I'd have the right to say that I've been the most miserable of unfortunates. Whatever happens to us, keep these words, my sweet, and take them down to the depths of your heart, where you will find them one day when you want to judge me.
- Judge you, me!
- You love me, you don't know me, but there will come a time when you will want to know the one you have loved: so remember this evening.
- It is too radiant for me to forget.
- Whatever it was, remember it: life is such a fragile and ephemeral thing, and it is beautiful to be able to concentrate it, to sum it up in memory, in an hour that marks it and gives it its significance: that hour is the one that is passing at this moment when I am speaking to you with this moved sincerity.
Philis was not accustomed to such outbursts, for in the rare outpourings he had sometimes indulged in, Saniel had always maintained a certain reserve, as if he feared giving himself away and letting the depths of his nature be read. How often had he mocked her when she sentimentalised, as he put it, and "sang her romance"; and now he was singing it himself, this romance of love.
No matter how happy she was to hear him, she could not help but feel an uneasy astonishment and wonder what melancholy impression he was under at the moment.
He knew how to read her too well not to guess this concern; so, not wanting to betray himself, he brought a smile to her eyes:
- You don't recognise me, do you?" he said, "and I'm sure you're wondering whether I'm ill.
- Oh, dear, don't scoff and stiffen against the feeling that puts such sweet music on your lips: happy, so happy to hear you speak like this that I would like to see your happiness equal to mine, to dispel the dark cloud with which your eyes are veiled. Will you never give up? Especially at this hour when everything is singing and laughing within us and around us! That you were sad six months ago, nothing was more legitimate: it was despair that you could have faced tomorrow; but today what do you lack to be happy?
- Nothing, it's true.
- Isn't this present the radiant morning of a superb future?
- What do you want! There are sad faces, just as there are happy ones: mine is not yours, but let's not talk about that any more, or about the past or the future: let's be in the present.
He got up and, taking her in his arms, sat her down next to him on the sofa.
The ringing of the doorbell startled Saniel as if he'd had a strong electric shock.
- Aren't you going to open the door? I don't want them to take away our evening.
But soon a new, firmer ring put him on his feet.
- The best thing is to know," he said, and went to open the door, leaving Philis in her study.
A chambermaid handed him a letter:
- From Madame Dammauville," she said; "there's a reply.
He left her in the hallway, lit by the open door on the landing, and went back into his study to read the letter: the dream had not lasted long, reality was taking hold of him again with merciless hands; this letter was certain to herald the blow that threatened him.
"If Dr Saniel is free, I beg him to come and see me this evening on urgent business; I shall wait for him until ten o'clock; if not, I expect him tomorrow morning from nine o'clock.
" A. DAMMAUVILLE.
He returned to the hall:
- Tell Madame Dammauville that I shall be with her in a quarter of an hour.
When he returned to his study, he found Philis standing in front of the mirror, putting on her hat.
- I heard," she said. How sad! But I can't blame you, since you're leaving me for Florentin.
As she moved towards the door, he stopped her:
- Kiss me once more.
- He had never held her in such a long, passionate embrace.
XIII
Saniel hadn't had a second's doubt; it wasn't to talk about medicine that Madame Dammauville was calling him, it was to tell him about Caffié, and, in the crisis that had just erupted, he had to tell himself that it was perhaps a good thing that it was so: at least he would be the first to know what she had decided, and then he could defend himself; nothing is hopeless as long as the struggle is possible.
When he rang the bell firmly, the servant who had brought the letter opened the door and, with a small lamp in her hand, led him through the dining room and the sitting room into Madame Dammauville's bedroom.
As soon as he reached the threshold, a glance showed him that the layout of the room had changed: the little bed, on which he had seen Madame Dammauville, was tucked away between the two windows, and she was lying in a large four-poster bed with dark curtains; beside her was a fairly large table on which was placed a lamp with a lampshade, books, a blotter, a teapot and a cup; The fire in the fireplace was out, but the mobile stove radiated a heat that told her it had been charged for the night. 
This warmth seized Saniel, who mechanically unbuttoned his overcoat.
- If the heat bothers you, please get rid of your overcoat," said Madame Dammauville.
As he placed it and his hat on an armchair beside the fireplace, he heard Madame Dammauville say to her chambermaid:
- Stay in the sitting room and warn the cook not to go to bed.
What did this recommendation mean? Was she afraid he would cut her neck?
- Would you like to come closer to my bed," she said, "so that we can talk without raising our voices, which is important.
He took a chair and sat down at a certain distance from the bed, so as not to be in the circle of light from the lamp; then he waited.
A moment of silence, which he found terribly long, passed before she spoke.
- You know," she said at last, "how chance made me see from this place -- she pointed to one of the windows in her room -- the face of the murderer of my unfortunate tenant, M. Caffié.
- Mademoiselle Cormier told me," he replied in his usual conversational tone.
- You may be surprised that at such a distance I could see that face well enough to see it again after five months as if I were still looking at it.
- This is indeed extraordinary.
- Not for those who have a memory for faces and attitudes, but in my case this memory has always been very well developed. I still remember my childhood friends, and I see them as they were when I was six or ten, without any confusion in my mind.
- Childhood impressions are generally vivid and persistent.
- This persistence does not only apply to my youthful impressions: today, I do not forget or confuse a physiognomy any more than I did in the past. Perhaps, if I had had many acquaintances and had seen a large number of people every day, this confusion would have become established, but this is not my case, the fragility of my health has forced me to lead a secluded life, and there is no one with whom I have had relations, even fleeting ones, that my eyes do not remember. When I think of someone, it's not primarily their name that comes to mind, but their physiognomy. Whenever I have been in the Senate or the House, I have not had to ask the names of the deputies or senators who were speaking; when I had seen their portraits, I certainly recognised them. If I go into these details, it is because they are of great importance, as you will see.
He didn't need her to point out this importance, he understood it only too well.
- Well, that's just the way I am," she continued, "so it's not surprising that the face and appearance of the man who drew the curtains in M. Caffié's study have not escaped my memory; you admit that, don't you?
- Since you are consulting me, I must tell you that the operations of memory are not as simple as we imagine them to be in the world; they comprise three things: the conservation of certain states, their reproduction and their localisation in the past, which must be brought together to constitute perfect memory. But this combination does not always take place, and often the third is missing.
- I don't quite understand; what is this third thing?
- Recognition.
- Well, I can assure you that in the case before me, it is not lacking.
