Since the action had begun in this way, it was of the utmost importance for Saniel to cast doubts in Madame Dammauville's mind and lead her to believe that the memory she was so sure of was perhaps not as solid or as perfect as she imagined.
- It is precisely that," he said, "this third thing is the most delicate and the most complex of the three, since it presupposes, in addition to the principal state of consciousness, secondary states varying in number and degree which, grouped around it, determine it.
Madame Dammauville remained silent for a moment and Saniel saw that she was making an effort to focus her mind on these obscure words:
- I don't understand," she said at last.
This was precisely what he wanted; however, as it would not have been clever to suggest that he was trying to deceive or stupefy her, he thought he could be a little more precise:
- I want to ask you," he continued, "if you are sure that there has been no confusion between the mechanism of vision and that of recognition, which is a vision in time.
She drew a breath, obviously satisfied to be out of these subtleties that were troubling her.
- It's precisely because I admit the possibility of this confusion, at least in part, that I called you," she said, "so that you could establish it.
Saniel didn't seem to understand:
- Me, madam.
- Yourself. A few hours ago, when you came with Mr Balzajette, you couldn't have failed to notice that I was examining you in an unnatural way. Before the lamps were lit, and when your back was to daylight, I was looking for where I had seen you before, but I couldn't find it. I was sure that you bore some resemblance to a face I knew, but the name to put on that face eluded me. When you returned and I saw you better in the light of the lamps, my memories became clearer; I lifted the lampshade, the light hit you full on and then your eyes, so characteristic, at the same time as a violent contraction of your face, shouted this name to me: this physiognomy, these eyes, this face belonged to the man that, from this place, -- she pointed to the window, -- I saw draw the curtains of M. Caffié.
Saniel did not flinch.
- That's a resemblance that would be unfortunate for me," he said, "if your memory was accurate.
- I said to myself that it might not be, and after an initial gasp of surprise, I confirmed myself in this thought by noticing that you were not wearing the big hair and the long blond beard that the man who had drawn the curtains had; but at that very moment Mr Balzajette spoke of this beard and this hair that you had had cut; I was crushed; nevertheless I had the strength to ask you if you had been in contact with Mr Caffié. Do you remember your answer?
- Yes, I do.
- After you left, I was left in a state of cruel anguish: it was you that I had seen draw the curtains, and it couldn't have been you. I tried to think of what I should do: notify the police, ask you for an interview. For a long time I wavered over my decision. Finally, I decided to talk to you. I wrote to you.
- I answered your call; but I confess that I don't know how to respond to this strange communication; you think you recognise in me the man who drew the curtains.
- I do recognise you.
- So what do you want me to say: you're not asking me for a consultation?
She thought she understood the meaning of this reply and guessed its purpose:
- It's not about me," she replied, "it's not about my moral state or my mental state, it's about you: my eyes, my memory, my conscience are making a terrible accusation against you: I can't believe my eyes or my memory, I reject my conscience, and I'm asking you to make this accusation null and void.
- And how, madam?
- Oh, not by protest.
- ... How can you expect a man in my position to stoop to discussing accusations based on a hallucination?
- Do you think I'm hallucinating? If so, call in one of your colleagues for a consultation tomorrow; if he believes as you do, I'll submit; if not, I'll remain convinced that I saw, and saw well, and I'll act accordingly.
- If you have seen correctly, madam, and I am prepared to concede this to you, it is because there is someone in the world who is my double; these extraordinary resemblances exist, you know that.
- I told myself so, and it was precisely this idea that made me write to you; I wanted to give you the opportunity to prove that you could not be that man.
- You will agree that I find it difficult to discuss such an accusation.
- One can find oneself accused by a combination of fatal circumstances and still be innocent, like this unfortunate boy who has been imprisoned for five months for a crime of which he is not guilty; and I start from your innocence as well as his to ask you to show that the charges brought against you are false.
- There are no charges against me.
- There may not be; that depends on you. For example, your hair and beard may already have been cut at the time of the murder: then it is quite certain that the man I saw was not you, and that I am a hallucinating woman. Were they or weren't they?
- They weren't; I had them cut off a few days ago to recover from a contagious disease.
- It may be," she continued, without seeming touched by this explanation, "that on the day of the murder, at the time I saw you, you were somewhere busy, so as to prove that you could not have been in the Rue Sainte-Anne at the same time, and that I was the victim of an hallucination; Finally, it is still possible that at that moment your situation was not that of a desperate man, fatally driven to crime by poverty or ambition, and that, consequently, it was not in your interest to commit this desperate crime. What do I know? Twenty other means of defence may be in your hands.
- You cited the example of this poor boy who is imprisoned even though he is innocent. Would it not apply to me if you did not recognise the error of your eyes or of your memory: would he not be condemned without your testimony; would I not be condemned if I did not find one that would nullify your accusation; and I do not know who to ask for this testimony. Have you thought of the infamy with which such an accusation will cover me? If I reject it, and I will reject it, will it not have disgraced me, lost me forever?
- It is precisely because I thought of this that I called you, so that by an explanation that you should give me, it seemed to me, you would prevent me from communicating this accusation to the law. You won't give me this explanation, so all I have to do now is think about the man whose innocence has been proven to me, and defend him against the man whose guilt has been proven to me no less.
- You won't do that.
- My duty obliges me to do so, and whatever may happen, I have always obeyed my duty: for me, in this horrible affair, there is an innocent and a guilty party involved; I side with the innocent.
- I can prove to you that it was an aberration of vision...
- You can prove it to the courts: they will decide.
He stood up violently; she put her hand on the doorbell; they looked at each other for a moment and what their lips did not express, their eyes said: -- I do not fear you, my precautions are taken. -- This bell will not save you.
Finally, he spoke in a hoarse, trembling voice:
- You are responsible for what is going to happen, madam.
- I accept it before God," she said with calm firmness. Defend yourself.
He went to the armchair on which he had laid his overcoat, and stooping to pick it up, quietly turned the key to the stove.
At the same time Madame Dammauville pulled the doorbell; the chambermaid opened the sitting room door.
- Show Dr Saniel out.
XIV
On his way home, Saniel fell back on his sofa in a daze, and without even lighting a candle, he remained there, devastated.
What was appalling was the speed with which he had condemned this poor woman to death and, without hesitation, executed her: for him to be saved, she had to die: she would die. This time, the idea had not twisted and turned as it had for Caffié, going from one contradiction to another, taking him through successive stages that had made him accustomed to it; was it only the first crime that costs, and, on the road he had entered, would he go all the way, sowing corpses behind him?
He shuddered from head to toe at the thought that this victim might not be the last that his salvation would require. When she had threatened to call the law on him, he had only believed her threat: if she spoke, he would be lost; he had kept his mouth shut. But hadn't that mouth already opened before he closed it, hadn't it already spoken? Before deciding to have this conversation, she could have told everything to the people around her who, between the time he left with Balzajette and the time he returned, had visited her or whom she had asked to consult her. So those were also condemned to death?
A pointless crime, or a series of crimes.
The horror that seized him was so strong that he thought of running to Rue Sainte-Anne; he would wake up the sleeping house, have the doors opened, break the window panes, save it ; But since he had gone out until that moment, the carbonic acid and carbon monoxide had had time to produce asphyxiation, and he would certainly arrive after death, or else, if he found her still alive, it would be because they had noticed that the stove key had been turned, and by appearing in this way for no reason, he was betraying himself as surely as by a confession. 
After all, it was possible that they would have seen that the key had been turned, and even the odds were in their favour; then she would have been saved and he lost: chance would have decided between them.
It was a solution like any other, and the good thing about it was that it eluded him: come what may. There are times when the shipwrecked man, tired of swimming, not knowing which way to turn, without a lighthouse, without direction, at the end of his strength and hope, makes a plank and lets himself be tossed about, the plaything of the waves, to rest and wait for a break in the weather - in his case, all he had to do was wait; everything else would be sterile and dangerous agitation.
He was not going to repeat the folly of trying to foresee and know as he had done with Caffié; he would always know soon enough, whatever the result, too soon.
Rising to his feet, he lit a candle and began to walk through his flat, turning on himself, coming and going like a beast in a menagerie; then the idea came to him that his footsteps should be heard below him; No doubt people would notice his restless walking, be surprised, and look for explanations, and in his situation he had to be careful not to give in to any remarks that were not naturally self-explanatory; and it's not natural for people to walk around at night with jerky steps instead of sleeping soundly in their beds. He took off his boots and, as he couldn't sit still, he resumed his whirling, his neck tucked into his shoulders, his back tense, his arms flailing.
But why had she told him about the double beading on the doors and windows, the drapes on the walls, the thick curtains? It was she who had suggested the idea of the stove key, which would not have occurred to him spontaneously. If he admitted the influence of chance, there was certainly a very characteristic one, which, to a certain extent, freed him.
Sometimes he felt that the time was not right, that morning would never come, sometimes, on the contrary, he felt that it was slipping away from him with astonishing rapidity; at times, sweat fell from his forehead onto his hands; at others, he felt frozen.
When dawn began to whiten the windows, he returned to the sofa, sitting down, shivering and overwhelmed. He leaned back against a cushion and caught the scent of Philis; then, burying his head in it, he lay there motionless and fell asleep.
A ring of the doorbell woke him, and he found himself on his feet, frightened, not knowing where he was. It was daylight; cars were driving down the street. The doorbell rang a second time, louder and more hurriedly. He went to open the door, shivering, and recognised the chambermaid who had brought him Madame Dammauville's letter the day before. He didn't need to question her: chance had sided with him. His eyes blurred; it was without seeing her that he heard the chambermaid explain what had brought her.
- She had been at Mr Balzajette's, but he had just left for the country, so she had come running: her mistress was almost cold in bed, not speaking, not breathing, but with a rosy face.
- I'll go with you.
He didn't need to know any more: this pinkish colouring, which is noticeable in asphyxiation by carbon monoxide, had fixed him. However, on the way, walking quickly beside her in the still deserted streets, he asked her what had happened after she left:
- Nothing had happened; she had talked in the kitchen with the cook who, around midnight, had gone up to her room on the fifth floor, and she had then gone to bed in a study adjoining her mistress's room. During the night, she had heard nothing; in the morning, at daybreak, she had found her mistress in the state she had just described, and she had immediately run to Mr Balzajette.
Continuing his interrogation, he wanted to know what Madame Dammauville had done since her consultation with Mr Balzajette.
- She had eaten dinner as usual, but less than usual, eating almost nothing; then she had received a visit from one of her friends who had stayed only a few minutes, leaving on a trip.
This was exactly what he had feared: Madame Dammauville had opened up to this friend. If that was the case, then his crime would be pointless; how far would it take him?
After a few moments, and in a tone that he tried to make indifferent, he asked who this friend was.
- A childhood friend, Madame Thézard, living at No. 9 rue des Capucines, the wife of a consul.
All the way to the house in the rue Sainte-Anne, he kept repeating the name and address to himself, a name he couldn't write down, but which he couldn't let his troubled head forget, because that was where the danger would come from if Madame Dammauville had spoken.
He had long been accustomed to the spectacle of death, but when he found himself face to face with this woman lying on her bed as if asleep, a contraction shook him.
- Give me a mirror and a candle," he said to the chambermaid and the cook, who were standing at the door of the living room without daring to enter.
While they searched, one for the mirror, the other for the candle, he went to the stove: the key remained as he had turned it the day before; he opened it and returned to bed.
It didn't take him long to examine her: she had succumbed to asphyxiation caused by the coal fumes. Was the poor draught due to the construction of the stove or a defect in the chimney? That would be for the inquest to decide; as far as he was concerned, all he could do was confirm death.
When Philis left him the day before, she had told him she would come early the next morning to find out what Madame Dammauville wanted. When he told her that she was dead, she went into a desperate prostration: Florentin was lost.
He tried to reassure her, but without success; she had put too much hope in his testimony to accept the catastrophe that was taking it away from them.
Nougarède didn't accept it either, and his disappointment was compounded by regret: if he had done things differently and followed the normal procedure, this testimony would now be in Florentin's favour.
However, he tried to reassure Philis: in short, the prosecution was only relying on the button and the struggle that had pulled it off. Saniel would destroy this hypothesis: he had to be counted on.
Saniel thus once again became, as he had been until Madame Dammauville's intervention, the supreme hope of Philis and Madame Cormier:
- When I have proved that there was no struggle, the hypothesis of the torn button will collapse on its own.
- And if it still stands, how and with what will we bring it down?
If he had shown himself as he was before, she would have shared the confidence he tried to inspire in her; but since Madame Dammauville's death, such great changes had taken place in him that she could not help worrying. Obviously it was the disappearance of Madame Dammauville that had made his mood so sombre and his temper so irritable that no one could object to him: he saw the dangers of the situation that this disappearance had created for Florentin, and, with his usual generosity, he reproached himself for not having agreed to look after Madame Dammauville sooner; he would certainly have saved her, since he had first asked for the removal of this stove, and Florentin would have been saved too.
Finally the day of the hearing arrived, without Saniel having heard anything about Madame Thézard, which proved that Madame Dammauville had said nothing to her friend. In the six months since the murder, the case had lost some of its interest for the Parisian public; in the provinces it was still talked about, but in Paris it had already been discounted for a long time: a clerk who cuts off his boss's neck to rob him lacks romance; no woman, no mystery. So the president, who loved beautiful rooms, had been annoyed not to see himself swamped with requests: his room was full, but not too full.
Saniel would have liked Philis to stay with Madame Cormier; but she had insisted, in spite of his observations and prayers, on coming to the hearing: she had to be there, Florentin had to see her, take courage from her eyes; he would defend himself better if he felt supported.
He defended himself badly, or at least weakly, without conviction, like a man who gives in because he knows in advance that everything he says will be useless.
Until Saniel's deposition, the witnesses who came forward were rather insignificant and revealed nothing that was not known; only Valerius, with his claims to professional secrecy, which he developed at length, amused the audience. But then Nougarède rose and asked the president to ask the witness to explain the struggle that had taken place between the victim and his assassin; and the president, who had begun by arguing, had to decide to ask this question in the face of the insistence of the defence. Saniel then explained at length how, from the position of the corpse in the chair and its condition, there was scientific proof that the struggle had not occurred.
- That's an opinion," said the president curtly; "the jury will decide.
- Perfectly," replied Nougarède, "and I reserve the right to make the jurors feel the weight it lends to the authority of the person who formulated it.
This effective sentence was intended to negate in advance the contradictions that the prosecution would, he believed, raise against this testimony; but these contradictions did not arise, and Saniel was able to take his place next to Philis without being called back to the stand to support his opinion against a doctor whose scientific authority was opposed to his own.
In the absence of Madame Dammauville, Nougarède had summoned the concierge of the rue Sainte-Anne, as well as the chambermaid and the cook, who had heard their mistress say that the man who had drawn Caffié's curtains did not resemble Florentin's portrait; but these were only rumours repeated by unimportant people, who could not produce the effect of the coup de théâtre on which he had based his defence.
When the public prosecutor delivered his indictment, it became clear why Saniel's opinion that there had been no struggle had not been contradicted; although the prosecution believed that there had been a struggle, it was willing to abandon it for a moment, since it did not need this hypothesis to prove that the button had not been torn off by falling from a ladder: it had been torn off in the very act of murder, in the effort to cut the neck of the victim who had violently stretched out his right arm and, by the jolt to the shoulder strap, torn off the button. The effect of Saniel's testimony was therefore destroyed, as was the much less powerful effect produced by the testimony of Madame Dammauville's servants, when the public prosecutor demonstrated that these rumours turned against the accused: it was said that she had seen a man with long hair and a curly beard draw the curtains; well, didn't this description apply to the accused? In truth, it was reported that she had not found him in a portrait published by an illustrated newspaper. Well, the portrait was not a likeness. Besides, was it plausible to admit that a woman of Madame Dammauville's character would not have warned the law if she had thought her testimony important and decisive? The proof that she herself had recognised its insignificance lay in the fact that she had kept it quiet.
Nougarède's plea, however eloquent, had not destroyed these two arguments, nor had it erased the impression made by the businessman's testimony about the theft of the forty-five francs, and the jury had returned an affirmative verdict, ruling out premeditation and admitting extenuating circumstances.
On hearing the verdict condemning Florentin to twenty years' hard labour, Philis, suffocated, clung to Saniel's arm; but he could not look after her as he would have liked, for Brigard, who had come to the hearing to witness the triumph of his disciple, had just approached him:
- Please accept my congratulations on your deposition, my dear; it is an act of courage that does you great credit. If this poor boy could have been saved, it was by you: you can say what you like, you are the man of conscience.
Part Three
I
During his first years in Paris, Saniel had published an article on Shakespeare's Pharmacy in a youth magazine in the Latin Quarter: The poison in Hamlet, the poison in Romeo and Juliet; and although, since his choice of medicine, he had read little more than science books, he had at that time had to study the theatre of his author; from this study there had remained in his memory a phrase which, for ten years, had never returned to his lips, and which suddenly imposed itself on his memory with the exasperating persistence of a tune once heard and repeated, -- that of Macbeth: "Macbeth has killed sleep, sleep, innocent, death of every day's life, bath of painful labour, balm of wounded souls. "
This sentence was the translation of a particular state he was going through: he too had lost "this innocent sleep, the bath of painful work, the balm of wounded souls". He had never been a great sleeper, at least in that he had imposed on himself the habit, hard at the beginning, less painful as he continued, of spending only a few hours in bed; but he used these few hours well, sleeping like the tired, fists closed, without dreams or waking up: The side on which he lay down was the side on which he opened his eyes, and the thought that occupied his mind in the evening was the first thought he had in the morning, not having been chased away by other thoughts or dreams. 
After Caffié's death, this tranquil and restorative sleep had continued just as calmly and just as solidly; but suddenly, after Madame Dammauville's death, it had been interrupted.
At first, he didn't mind: he wasn't asleep, so much the better! he would work more. But you can't always work any more than you can always go without eating. Saniel knew better than anyone that the life of any organ is made up of alternating periods of rest and activity: one during which the organ renews its supply of nutritive materials, the other during which it consumes these materials in the development of living forces, and he did not imagine that he could work indefinitely without sleeping: only he believed that after twenty-hour days of work, crushed with fatigue, he would nevertheless have four hours of complete sleep, the sleep that Shakespeare called the bath of painful work.
He didn't get those four hours, and the law that says that any state of prolonged excitement leads to exhaustion, which must be remedied by functional rest, was wrong for him. When, after his long days of work, at about one or two in the morning, when all the sounds of life had ceased in the house and in the street, and weariness was reflected in the inertia of the muscles that allowed his head to droop on his chest and his eyelids to droop over his blurred eyes, he went to bed, walking carefully, undressing slowly and smoothly, and fell asleep without delay in his sheets. But almost immediately, and very often, he would wake up with a start, suffocated, covered in sweat, in a state of extreme anxiety, his mind agitated by hallucinations that he could not immediately shake off. Then his sleep was over for a long time, despite his general need for it; or if, after tossing and turning on his bed, he managed to fall back asleep, it was never the complete and deep sleep he used to have; Some parts of his brain were still awake and, if their excitation did not cause him to wake up suddenly, they produced dreams that were more painful for him, because he was aware of them, he followed them, examined them without being able to detach himself from them, get rid of them, wake up completely, their plaything, their victim : and they were terrible, these dreams, always the same, leaving Madame Dammauville only to take him back to Caffié. Wasn't it strange that Caffié, who until then had been completely erased from her memory, dead as he really was, had found himself resurrected by Madame Dammauville, and only at night, the spectre of the shadow that the day dissipated?
Believing that one of the causes of this excitability of the brain could well be the mental work he was imposing on it in excessive doses, at precisely the time when this excitability should not have been prolonged but, on the contrary, numbed, he had decided to change a system that had worked so badly for him: instead of intellectual work, he would do material work, which, by exhausting his muscular functions, would give him the sleep of the poor people who toiled all day, labourers or diggers, carters or lumberjacks, and as he couldn't roll the wheelbarrow or split the wood, he had started, every evening after dinner, to walk straight ahead at an accelerated pace, doing his seven or eight leagues before going home: he would certainly get the better of this reactive brain. 
The physical work had been no more successful than the spiritual work; he had been able to give himself the fatigue of the diggers and lumberjacks, but not their sleep. When he went to bed with his legs broken, his feet aching, slumped by his long, hurried walk, he had the same startled awakenings, the same painful dreams that panicked and exhausted him. Decidedly, the fatigue of the body was no better than that of the brain. And even less so. At his desk, immersed in his books, or in his laboratory, bent over his microscope, he absorbed himself in the task in hand and, by the strength of a will that had been exercised for a long time and submitted to obedience, he managed to keep his mind focused on the subject in hand, without distraction or daydreams. But when he walked through the streets of Paris, on the deserted roads of the suburbs, across the fields or through the woods, along the Seine or the Marne, his free thought ran where it wished; he did not command it; it was the mistress, and it always led him back to Madame Dammauville, to Caffié, to Florentin : It seemed as if the heat of walking put pressure on his brain like the boiler of a machine, which then carried him along, without any possible brake, without any direction, vertiginously. When he returned in this state, after several hours of cerebral excitability, how would he have found the peaceful and restorative sleep, complete and deep, of poor people who have only worked their muscles?
Having never been ill, he had never had to examine or treat himself: medicine was good for others, useless for him! With an organised machine like his, all he had to fear were accidents, and so far he had been spared them; like the true son of a farmer that he was, he had victoriously resisted both the life of Paris and the overwork of the mind. But the time had come to give himself up to this examination, and to try a treatment that would give him rest: he was not a sceptical doctor, and he believed that the remedies he had prescribed for others were good for him.
The unfortunate thing was that he had none of the causes of insomnia: he had neither meningitis nor encephalitis, nor anything to suggest a brain tumour; he was not anaemic; he ate; he suffered from no neuralgia or any of the acute or chronic ailments that generally accompany the absence of sleep; he drank neither coffee nor alcohol, and, without this state of overexcitability of the encephalic centres, he could have said that he was in good health, only a little emaciated, but that was all. 
It was this excitation that he had to cure, and as there are classic remedies for insomnia, he had used the one that seemed to suit his condition; but potassium bromide, despite its hypnotic properties, had produced no more effect than intellectual or physical overwork. Recognising its ineffectiveness, he had replaced it with chloral; but chloral, which after a few days' use should have created a particular disposition to sleep, had failed just like potassium bromide. So he tried morphine injections.
It was not without some trepidation that he came to this third attempt, the first two having been so unsuccessful, and, since it is known that chloral produces a calmer sleep than morphine, it seemed that it would fail again; nevertheless he had slept without being tormented by the awakenings and dreams that were the terror of his short nights, and the next day he had slept again.
But he knew too well the effects produced by the prolonged use of these injections to continue them beyond what was strictly necessary; he had therefore wanted to interrupt them: sleep had again abandoned him. He resumed them; then, as the primitive doses lost their effectiveness, he gradually increased them. After a while, what he should have feared and what he did fear had come true: his weight had increased; he had lost his appetite, his muscular strength and his moral energy; his pale face had begun to take on the expression so characteristic of morphine addicts.
Then he stopped in horror.
If he continued, he would indeed become a morphine addict within a given time, and the apathy into which he fell would prevent him from resisting the need to absorb new doses of poison, a need as imperious and irresistible in morphine addiction as that of alcohol is for the alcoholic, and more terrible in its effects: the perversion of the intellectual faculties, the loss of willpower, memory and judgement, paralysis or mania leading to suicide.
If he did not continue, those sleepless nights or restless sleeps that had distressed him would return, followed by a return of that over-excitability of the brain which, by disturbing the nutrition of the encephalic mass, could be the prelude to some serious cerebral disorder.
On the one hand, mania due to morphine; on the other, dementia due to constant and disordered excitability of the brain: that was what awaited him.
Between a result that was inevitably certain and one that was only possible, he had no choice: he had to give up morphine; and this choice was all the more compelling because, while morphine ensured that he slept at night, it did nothing to give him peace of mind during the day -- quite the contrary.
When he had begun to use this remedy, it was only during the night that he fell under the influence of certain ideas; during the day, by applying himself to his work and maintaining his tense application by an effort of will, he escaped these ideas: he was the man he had always been, master of his strength and his thoughts. But the action of the morphine had soon weakened his hitherto omnipotent will, so that when these ideas had crossed his mind during the day, he no longer had the energy to chase them away. He tried to shake them off, but to no avail; they would not let themselves be detached from his brain, to which they clung and which they invaded with a profuse expansion.
In truth, these two corpses were horribly bothering him. Wasn't it exasperating, for a man who had seen so much and butchered so many, that there were only two of them always before his eyes, even when they were closed -- that of this old rascal and that of this unfortunate woman? So as not to compound this impression with another that humiliated him, he had got rid of the bundles of banknotes taken from Caffié's house by sending them 'as restitution' to the director of Public Assistance; but this had had no appreciable effect.
He was also obsessed by the thought of Florentin, and while he could see Caffié slumped in his blood-soaked armchair and Madame Dammauville motionless and pink on her bed, it was no less cruel for him to see Florentin in the steerage of the transport that would soon take him to New Caledonia.
The ideas he had expressed to Crozat about conscience, and those he had explained to Philis about remorse, were still his own; but he was no less certain that these two dead men and this condemned man weighed terribly heavily on him, frightening and suffocating like the epialte of a nightmare: it was neither his upbringing nor his milieu to have these corpses behind you and this victim in front of you.
But where his old ideas had been turned upside down, since these deaths had seized his life, was in his confidence in his own strength.
The strong man he had believed himself to be, the one who walks straight to his goal, without concern for anything or anyone, looking only ahead and never behind, master of his mind as well as his heart and his arm, was not at all the man reality had revealed.
On the contrary, he had been weak in action, and even weaker afterwards.
And it was not only a humiliation in the present that he felt at recognising this weakness, it was also a worry for the future: for, if he did not have this strength that he had attributed to himself before having experienced its power, he would, if his beliefs were true, one day succumb with the weak.
Obviously, if he had been completely strong, he would not have complicated his life with a love: the strong go it alone, because they don't need anyone; and he needed a woman, so much that it was through her alone, close to her, when he looked at her, when he listened to her, that he felt a little calm.
Was he therefore weak and a coward? No, perhaps, but simply human.
II
Precisely because he felt calm with Philis, Saniel wanted her not to leave him.
But however happy she was, in her grief, to see that instead of moving away from her - which a less generous man might have done - he was trying to get closer to her every day, she could not give up her lessons and her work, which were their very life, to give all her time to her love, any more than she could always leave alone her grief-stricken mother, who had never needed to be lifted up and supported as much as she did now.
She didn't spend a day without coming to see him; but, as much as she wanted to, she couldn't stay with him for as long as she would have liked and as he himself asked. When she got up to leave and he stopped her, she didn't leave, but they were never more than a few minutes saved: they were short, and soon came the time when, after going over it ten times, she had to leave him.
These separations had always been despairs for her, the apprehension of which from the moment of arrival paralysed her; but now they were even crueller. In the past, when she left him, she often saw him already immersed in his work before she had drawn the door; now, on the contrary, he led her into the hallway, held her back, and only let her go down the stairs when she had torn herself away from his embrace, after she had promised and repeated to come early the next day and stay longer with him. In the past, too, she had been at peace when she went away, not having to worry about his health or how she would find him again: solid and valiant as usual, as sound in body as in mind, that was certain. On the contrary, now she had the worry of trying to predict, each time she arrived, how he would be: would the sadness, the melancholy, the despondency persist that day; would his weight loss and his paleness increase? It was his worry, his anguish, to try to guess the causes of the changes that had taken place in him, and which were so evident in everything, in his feelings as well as in his person. Wasn't it really extraordinary that he should be more gloomy or more worried, now that his life was assured, than at such a hard time when it was threatened without him ever knowing what his tomorrow would be? His experiments had produced better results than he could have hoped for, he himself agreed; the studies he had just published on these experiments were hotly debated, praised by some, contested by others; it seemed to him that he had achieved his goal, and he was chagrined, discontented, unhappy, more tormented than when he was exhausting himself in his efforts, with no other support than his will. Finally when, frightened to see him like this, she asked him what he was feeling, he got angry and replied brutally:
- Sick? Why do you want me to be ill? Aren't I in a better position than anyone to know what's wrong with me? I overworked myself, that's all; and, as my life of deprivation didn't allow me to repair my strength, I ended up anaemic. It's really strange that you should go looking for extraordinary explanations for what is natural and, in a way, obligatory: count the teeth of polytechnicians and look at their hair after their exams, and tell me what you think. Why should I be any different? You can't spend your money with impunity, that would be too good; everything in this world has to be paid for. Only the bourgeois earn their fortune by playing cards or dominoes on café tables and rolling billiard balls, which allows them to be amiable and well-groomed.
She had to believe that he was right and could see through her condition, but she couldn't help tormenting herself. She didn't know anything about medicine, she didn't know what overwork and the anaemia that resulted from it were, but she felt that anaemia wasn't enough to explain everything, any more than his sudden mood swings and fits of anger about nothing, than his bursts of tenderness, his breakdowns and his despondency, his preoccupations and his absences.
By the very fact that she was observing him closely, she had noticed very well the effect she was having on him, and how, by her very presence, she was brightening up his gloomy mood and relieving his despondency, on the sole condition that she did not ask him awkward questions on certain subjects which she had not yet managed to determine, but which she hoped to avoid. So she wished she could never leave his side, and did her utmost to create opportunities for him to come and see her up to twice a day, in the morning on her way to lessons, and in the afternoon or evening on some pretext: he was so happy when she surprised him!
One evening, late, she rang his doorbell with a hand that was nervous with joy.
- I've come to stay until tomorrow," she said in a triumphant voice.
She expected him to respond to her joy with an embrace of happiness, but he did not.
- Do you have to go out?
- Not at all; it's not me I'm thinking about, it's your mother.
- Do you think I would have left her alone in her present state of nervous and moral weakness, afraid of everything? A cousin arrived from the provinces who is going to sleep in my bed, and I quickly took advantage of the situation to say that I would be staying at the boarding house. And here I am.
Despite the desire he had had more than once, he had never dared ask her to give him the night off; during the day, he only betrayed himself by his chagrined or whimsical mood; but at night, with the hallucinated sleep that was his, wouldn't he betray himself by some serious word that escaped him?
However, since she had come, it was impossible to send her away; he could do it neither for her nor for himself. What pretext could he find to say: "Go away; I don't want you"? He wanted her: he wanted to look at her, listen to her, hear her voice, which lulled and numbed his anguish, feel her close to him, just to have her there and not be face to face with his thoughts.
On the sly, she examined him, wondering about the reason for this strange welcome, standing in the study where she had entered after him, not daring to get rid of her hat. How could her arrival have produced such a different effect from the one she had expected when she had come in so happy and light?
- Aren't you taking off your hat?
- I was wondering if you didn't have to work.
- Why did you wonder that?
- For fear of disturbing you.
- What rage is it that you always ask yourself something? What are you looking for in me? Who surprises you? Why are you bothering me? Why? Come on, speak up once and for all: explain yourself.
It was a long time ago that these explosions stunned her, but they still distressed her, and each time they broke out, even more: how irritable and worried he was! But she no longer let her pain and surprise show.
- I've been clumsy explaining myself again," she said. What do you want? I'm like that; forgive me.
This single word, "Forgive me", was crueler to him than any reproach, for he knew that he had nothing to forgive her for, since she was the victim and he was the guilty party. Would he never be able to control such reckless and unjust outbursts?
He took her in his arms and made her sit next to him:
- It's up to you to forgive.
He was mad to imagine that she might be suspicious, and the surest way to arouse suspicion was precisely to show fear that she might be; betraying himself by these clumsinesses was as serious as letting a complaint or a confession slip out while he was asleep.
Besides, for this night he had found a way, which in reality was not very difficult, of not exposing himself to talking in his sleep: it was not to sleep; when sleep overtook him, he would make sure he stayed awake. After spending so many nights without closing his eyes, he would no doubt keep them open all night.
But he was mistaken; when he heard Philis's calm, regular breathing, and felt the gentle warmth of her shoulder, where she had rested her head, penetrate him, in the immobility he had imposed on himself without noticing it, believing himself to be far from sleep and convinced that he had no effort to make to avoid succumbing to it, he suddenly fell asleep.
When he awoke, a pale ray of sunlight filled one corner of the room; leaning on the bolster, Philis was watching him.
He made a sudden movement and threw himself back.
- What is it?" he cried. What did I say?
Instantly his face paled, his lips quivered; he felt his heart beat tumultuously and his throat tighten with a painful constriction.
- But there's nothing there," she replied, looking at him tenderly, "you didn't say anything.
In fact, why would he have said anything? There was no reason for it, apart from his very fear of betraying himself: in his troubled sleep, under the fright of his hallucinations, he might have moaned, shouted, spoken, but he didn't know if he had really ever shouted or spoken, as no one had ever been near him to observe and hear him. Besides, he had not just awoken from one of his restless slumbers; at least there was nothing in him to indicate that he had been agitated. In spite of his confusion and fright, these reflections had formed instantly in his mind, and his face had relaxed.
- What time is it now?
- Nearly six.
- Six o'clock!
- Can't you hear the cars in the street? The pierrots are chirping.
It must have been about one o'clock when his eyes closed; so he had slept for five hours, and a deep, complete sleep, the kind he had sought for so long without obtaining it, "the balm of afflicted souls, the bath of painful labour", and he emerged from this bath, calm, rested, rejuvenated, his body at the ready, his mind at ease, in all the man he had once been in his years of happy youth, and not the man of these last frightfully hard times.
A sigh swelled in his free chest.
- Ah, if I still had you," he murmured, addressing these words to himself as much as to her.
And he fixed a long gaze on her, in which shone an emotional smile; then, wrapping an arm around her shoulders, he drew her against him.
- Dear little woman!
Never before had she felt such deep, vibrant tenderness in his voice; never before had she been able, as she did when she heard those three words, to measure the greatness of the love she inspired in him, and it even seemed to be the declaration of a new love.
Hugging her passionately, he repeated:
- Dear little woman!
Distraught, she answered nothing, shattered by her happiness.
Suddenly, he gently pushed her aside and looked at her with the same smile:
- Doesn't that word mean anything to you?
- It tells me that you love me.
- Is that all?
- What more could I want? You say it, I feel it, you give me the greatest joy I could ever dream of.
- Is that enough for you?
- It would be enough for me if it didn't have to be interrupted: but it is the misfortune of our lives that we have to separate when the ties that bind us are most strained.
- Why separate?
- Alas! What about Mum? What about life?
- If you didn't leave your mother; if you didn't have to worry about your life.
She looked at him, not daring to question him, betraying her thoughts only by a shudder that she could not suppress despite her efforts.
- I mean, if you were to become my wife.
- Oh, my beloved!
- Don't you want that?
She threw herself into his arms, fainting, but after a short moment she straightened up.
- Alas," she murmured, "it's impossible.
- Why impossible?
- Don't ask me, don't make me say it.
- On the contrary, I want you to say it.
She turned her head away and, in a voice that was barely perceptible, with a stifled breath:
- My brother...
- It's because of your brother that I want this marriage.
Then, immediately picking himself up:
- Do you think I'm a man to put up with the foolish prejudices of the world?
III
Saniel had not waited for this day to recognise the salutary influence that Philis, by her very presence, exerted on him. However, the idea of taking her as his wife had never occurred to him: he was so little suited, he thought, to marriage; he felt so little like a husband; until recently, he had had so little need of a home!
It was all of a sudden that this idea came to him and struck him strongly, at least as much by the calm he felt inside him as by the charm that emanated from her, the health, the happiness, the cheerfulness and the life.
- Ah, if I still had you!
This word, which had escaped him, characterised the situation -- what he lacked and what he hoped for.
It wasn't just the bodily calm that she gave him by some mysterious affinity that his medicine didn't understand, but which he felt was no less powerful; it was also the moral calm.
He had duties towards her, and terribly heavy ones, towards his mother, towards Florentin.
For this one, he had done what he could, and even more than he could, suddenly becoming a solicitor, besieging people, importuning them, daring anything to soften his fate and prevent his embarkation, which until now he had succeeded in doing, while waiting for something better.
But that was really not all he owed them: Florentin was no less imprisoned with wretched people; Madame Cormier, fallen into dreary despair, grew weaker every day, and Philis, despite her resilience and valour, was crushed under the weight of unjust fate.
How much the situation would change if he married her -- for them, and for him!
What a relief! Hence his cry: "It is for your brother's sake that I want this marriage"; at least he would have done what he could to redeem, within human means, what he had been powerless to prevent.
When Philis had recovered a little from her confusion, she asked him:
- When did he decide to get married?
He didn't want to lie and replied that it was at that very moment that the thought had occurred to him, precise enough and strong enough to give substance to the ideas that had been floating around vaguely for several months.
- Did you at least think it through?" she asked fearfully, "did you not give in to a love drive?
- Was it better to give in to a long-reasoned calculation? I'm marrying you because I love you, and also because I'm certain that I can't be happy without you: frankly, I recognise that I need you, your tenderness, your love, your strength of character, your even temperament, your invincible faith in hope, which for me, as I am organised, are worth the finest dowry.
- It's just that I don't have the slightest dowry to bring you. I could well, when you were desperate and crushed, ask to become the wife of the poor village doctor you were going to be; but today, in your position, especially in the one you will soon occupy, is poor little Philis worthy of you? Today you are giving me the greatest joy I can taste, the one I only dreamt of when I told myself it would be madness to hope for its realisation; but it is precisely this that gives me the strength to ask you to reflect, and to see if you will ever regret this moment of training that makes me so happy.
- I have thought about it, and what you are saying to me right now proves, better than anything, that I was not mistaken; I want a woman who loves me, and you are that woman.
- More than I can say at the moment, dizzy with happiness, but no more than I can prove to you in the continuity of our love.
- Besides, my dear girl, don't delude yourself about the splendours of this position you speak of; it is more than likely that they will never come true, because I am not a man of money and will do nothing to earn any; unless it comes on its own...
- It will come.
- That's not the goal I'm pursuing: I've largely achieved what I wanted; if I were to earn money now and build up a rich clientele, the jealousy of my colleagues would make me miss out or wait too long for what I still want and what my ambition prefers to fortune. For the time being, therefore, my position will be modest: my four thousand franc salary as an agrégé, what I will earn at the central office until I become a hospital doctor, plus five hundred francs a month that my publisher is offering me for work on a bacteriology journal, will give us about a dozen thousand francs, and we will have to make do with that for quite a long time.
- For me, it's a fortune.
- For me too; but I didn't want to warn you any less.
- And when do you want us to get married?
- Immediately, as soon as the time required by law has elapsed, and also as soon as I have moved into a new flat, because you can't go in with my wife in this one, where we have seen you come so often: it would hurt you to pass in front of the concierge and would embarrass me to go in with you. I hope I'll find a place easily, and I think you'll want to be no more demanding than I am.
- Oh dear!
- Besides, this time we won't do the folly of putting ourselves at the discretion of the upholsterers: the first one was expensive enough.
He said these last words with fierce energy, but immediately continued:
- What else do we need? A sitting room for the clients, if any come; a study for me, a room I can use as a laboratory; a bedroom for us, one for your mother...
- You name it...
- But of course! Did you really think I'd ask you to part with her?
She took his hand and kissed it with a passionate impulse:
- Oh, the dearest and most generous of men.
- Let's not talk about that," he said with obvious embarrassment. In your mother's state of moral prostration, it would kill her to leave her alone; the doctor wouldn't allow it: she needs you, the poor woman, and I promise to help you ease her pain. Precisely because she doesn't have your strength, we'll have to take a lot of care of her. We will arrange a home for her where she will not be sad; and, although I am not a very tender person, I will try to replace the one from whom she is separated: she will be happy if she sees you happy.
He spoke at length about what he wanted, feeling a sense of satisfaction in talking about what he would do for Madame Cormier, in whom at the moment he saw much more Florentin's mother than Philis'.
- Do you think we'll make her forget?" he said from time to time.
- Neither she nor I will ever forget, but it is certain that our sorrow will be drowned in our happiness, and we will owe that happiness to you. Oh, how you will be adored, respected and blessed.
Adored, respected! He repeated these words softly to himself. So it was possible to be happy by making people happy. Until now he had had so little opportunity to care for others that this was a kind of revelation of a feeling that surprised him, but which, because it was new, was all the sweeter for him.
He wanted to give himself the satisfaction of tasting all the sweetness.
- Where are you going this morning?
- I'm going back to the boarding school to work on my pupils, who are doing their compositions for the prizes; it's this circumstance that has given me an excuse to sleep there: I need some nice coasters to take home to my parents for the holidays.
- Well, while you're at boarding school this very morning, I'll go and see your mother. The method of proposing marriage that we have just used may be original and in accordance with the laws of nature, -- if nature admits of marriage, which I don't know, but it is certainly not in accordance with the laws of the world, and now I must address this proposal to your mother.
- What joy you will give her!
- I certainly hope so.
- All the same, I'd like to be there to enjoy her happiness. Imagine that Mum has a mania for marriage; she spends all her time marrying people she knows or doesn't know; she only reads novels about the wedding at the end, happy if it takes place, desperate if it doesn't; and she was convinced, poor woman, like me, that I would die in the yellowed skin of an old maid. Finally, this evening she will have the pleasure of telling me about your visit and your request. As for this visit, don't make it until after midday, because by then our cousin will have left.
Saniel spent the morning looking for the flat he wanted and, as he had no requirements other than those of a distribution appropriate to his needs, he found one in a deserted street in the Invalides district, which he stopped at. Perhaps this district was not within easy reach of customers, but would he ever have customers? In any case, if he did, it wouldn't be the Auvergne, and they could come and get him at Les Invalides!
It was around one o'clock that he went up to Les Batignolles, where he found Madame Cormier putting her little flat in order after the cousin had left. As always, when he came in, she gave him a look of anxious curiosity, the meaning of which he knew only too well: what had Florentin got? would he not leave?
- It's not him I have to talk to you about today," he said, without mentioning his name, which was unnecessary.
Madame Cormier's face expressed painful disappointment.
- It's about Mademoiselle Philis...
- Do you think she's ill?" cried Madame Cormier, who could admit nothing but misfortune.
- Not at all; it's from her and me. Don't worry: I hope that what I have to tell you will not cause you any grief.
- You must forgive me if I see cause for fear everywhere; we have been so terribly tested, so unjustly!
He cut her off, for her complaints were not to his liking:
- For a long time," he said briskly, "Mademoiselle Philis has inspired in me a deep feeling of esteem and tenderness: I could not have seen her so brave, so valiant in adversity, so determined in life, so good to you, so charming in everything, without loving her, and I have come to ask you to give her to me as a wife.
At Saniel's first words, Madame Cormier's hands had been shaking with an increasing tremor:
- Is it possible?" she murmured, bursting into tears. Such happiness for my daughter! Such honour for us, for us, for us!
- I love her.
- Forgive me if I am carried away by emotion beyond the bounds of propriety, but I am losing my head. We are so unhappy that our soul is weak against joy. Perhaps I should not speak like this; but, on the other hand, it seems to me that I would be unworthy of the happiness you bring us if I did not answer you frankly. Perhaps I should also conceal my daughter's feelings; but, for the same reason, I cannot fail to tell you that this esteem, this tenderness of which you speak, she shares; I have known it for a long time, although she has never admitted it to me; your request can therefore only be welcomed with happiness by both mother and daughter.
This had been said in broken tones, obviously from an overflowing heart, but suddenly her face saddened:
- I have just spoken to you," she continued, "in the sincerity of my soul; carried away by a surge of joy that I did not believe I could yet experience. But reflection must take us back. You are young, I am no longer young, and my age makes it my duty not to give in to temptation. We are unfortunate people, you know that better than anyone; crushed outcasts. You are a happy man of this world; soon you will be a rich man, a glorious man; is it wise for you to burden your life with a woman who is in my daughter's position?
That was Philis' answer, except for a few words, and he gave the same answer to the mother as he had given to the daughter.
- I'm not speaking for you," continued Madame Cormier, "I wouldn't presume to give you advice; I'm only speaking from my daughter's point of view, from my own, her mother's point of view, who, with the experience of my age, must look after her future. Is it certain that in the struggles of life you will never have to suffer from this marriage, not because my daughter will not make you happy - on that point I am at ease - but because the situation that fate has created for us will weigh you down and hamper you? I know my daughter, her delicacy, her worried susceptibility, - that of the unfortunate, - her pride: that of the blameless; this would be a wound for her that would make unhappiness succeed happiness, because she would not bear contempt.
- If that is human nature, it is not mine; I give you my word on that.
- You can be sure that I only want to believe you; I only said what I did because I had to.
It was true, then, that there were men in this world who were clairvoyant enough to recognise the qualities of a poor girl and to ask only those qualities of her?
He explained how he intended to organise their lives and, when she realised that she had a place between them, she cried out, clasping her hands together:
- Oh God, who took my son from me, how good of you to give me one back!
IV
He wanted nothing better than to be a son to this poor woman; in reality he would be better than this unfortunate boy, soft and incapable. What did this mother-hungry woman need? A son to love. She would find him in her son-in-law. Seeing her daughter happy, how could she not be happy herself?
He could tell himself that no one was less inclined to infatuation than he was; so it was only fair to acknowledge that he was repairing as far as possible the fate of which they had been victims.
Obviously they would be happy, the mother as well as the sister, and whatever Philis thought, still suffering from grief, they would forget. They owed him this consolation. For him, it was something, even a lot.
It had been a long time since he had worked with the serenity that sustained him that day, and when he went to bed in the evening, worried as always about his night, he fell asleep as peacefully as if Philis had leaned her charming head on his shoulder and breathed in her perfume.
Decidedly, making people happy was still the best thing in the world, and when you could give yourself this satisfaction, there was no need to worry about being unhappy yourself: when you create an atmosphere of happiness for others, you benefit from it at the same time as they do.
He waited impatiently for Philis, for she was sure to bring him an echo of her mother's joy, and this was a reward that was owed to her.
No doubt she arrived happy, smiling, full of tenderness; but he watched her too closely not to see that she had some ulterior motive, something that embarrassed her and that she didn't say.
He was not prepared to admit that she could hide from him and not tell him everything.
He immediately asked her:
- What are you hiding from me?
- How can you think I'd hide anything from you?
- What is it? You understand, don't you, that nothing can happen inside you that I can't read in your eyes? Well, your eyes speak when your lips are silent.
- It's because I have a request to make of you, a prayer.
- Why don't you say it?
- Because I don't dare.
- But it seems to me that I'm not so inclined as to make you think I'm refusing you anything.
- That's precisely where my embarrassment and reserve come from: I'm afraid of upsetting you at a time when I want to show you all the gratitude and love in my heart.
- If you must upset me, the best thing is not to keep me waiting.
She hesitated; then, faced with an impatient gesture, she made up her mind.
- I wanted to ask you how you intend our marriage to take place?
He looked at her surprised.
- But like all weddings!
- All marriages?
- Are there any that are different from the others?
- Yes, they do.
- You know that I don't understand this way of asking questions in riddles; if you want to allude to a social custom that I don't know, say it frankly: it's not to hurt my feelings, since I'm the first to admit that I don't know any. What is it you want?
She could feel her irritation growing, and yet she couldn't make up her mind.
- I should have told you first of all that you will always find in me a woman who respects your ideas and your beliefs, who will never allow herself to judge them, let alone try to combat or modify them.
- Concluded," he said impatiently.
- So I think," she said with embarrassed and fearful hesitation, "that you will not admit that I am disrespecting your ideas by asking you to marry in church.
- But that was my intention.
- Oh dear, and I was so afraid of hurting your feelings!
- Why do you want to hurt my feelings?
- Do you agree to go to confession?
Instantly the smile that had been in his eyes and on his lips was replaced by a flash of fury.
- And why shouldn't I go to confession?
- he cried.
- You think I'd be afraid to go to confession! Why do you suppose that? Say why.
He looked at her with eyes that pierced her to the core, as if they wanted to search inside her.
Stunned by this outburst of fury, which had come out of nowhere, since he had just smiled in response to her request for a religious marriage, which she had thought so dangerous, she could think of nothing to say, not understanding how the simple word "confession" could have infuriated him so. And yet she could make no mistake, it was this one and not another who had put her in this state.
He continued to examine her, so she tried to explain:
- I only assumed," she said, "that I could hurt you by asking you to do something that contradicted your beliefs.
The mad anger that had so clumsily carried him away was beginning to lose its initial violence: a word added to what had escaped him would be a confession. Would he never be able to rid himself, even when his mind was in the best of conditions, of the fixed idea that obsessed him? A man like him might be reluctant to confess, but he could not accept the idea that it was supposed that he was afraid of this confession: why afraid?
- Let's not talk about it any more," he said; "above all, let's not think about it any more.
- Allow me just one word," she replied. I would have been in the same situation as everyone else, and I wouldn't have asked you anything; any ideas I might have in my heart would have bowed to yours, I love you enough for that; but for your own sake, for your future, for your honour, you mustn't appear to be marrying an outcast in secret, in shame.
- Don't worry; I feel, as you do, more than you do, that we need consecrated ceremonies.
She soon realised that he was going much further down this road than she was.
So as not to be left with the unpleasant impression of the unfortunate word that had caused the explosion, he offered to show her the flat he had chosen the day before, and they immediately went to the Rue d'Estrée.
For the first time, they walked side by side, head held high, through the streets of Paris, without fear of meeting anyone: what a source of pride for her! Her husband! It was on his arm that she leaned! When they crossed the Tuileries, she was almost surprised that no one turned round to see them go by; she would gladly have shouted to those indifferent, ignorant people: "It's him! Mothers who gaze so tenderly at your children, it is he who will heal them; children who embrace their mothers, it is he who will keep them in your affection for a long time.
In the mood she was in, she couldn't help but find his choice of location admirable: the street, the house and the flat.
As it comprised three bedrooms opening onto a terrace where he would house the animals destined for his experiments, Saniel wanted her to decide which of these bedrooms she would choose; as she was to share it with him, she wanted to take the most beautiful, but he did not accept this arrangement.
- I was asking you to choose between the two small ones," he said. "The large and beautiful one should be reserved for your mother, who, not being able to go out, needs space, air and light more than we do.
How could she not have been overwhelmed with gratitude when she found him so perfectly kind, thoughtful, delicate and generous in all things, small and great alike? She would never have loved him enough to rise to him.
By a stroke of luck, the main rooms, the salon and the study, were more or less the same size as those in the rue Louis-le-Grand, so there would be little or nothing to change in the furnishings. As for the other rooms, it didn't matter; they'd make do with what they had, supplementing it with the furniture from the rue des Moines: it wasn't the present they had to worry about, it was the future; later, we'd see.
This feminine chatter, interspersed with effusions and passionate outbursts, charmed Saniel, who had forgotten the incident of the confession, his anger as well as his obsession, thinking only of Philis, seeing only her, delighted by her gaiety, her vivacity, stirred to the core of his being by the tender caresses of her beautiful dark eyes.
How could he not be happy with this delightful woman, who had taken such control over him, and who loved him so ardently? An unconscious inspiration would not have led him to marriage, but reason and calculation should have. For him, there was only one danger from now on -- solitude -- she would protect him from it; with her zest, her good humour, her courage, her love, she would not let him go back to his thoughts; work would do the rest.
After the question of furnishings, they settled that of the wedding itself, that is to say the ceremony; and it was then that she was astonished to see in him ideas and demands that she had not suspected, and which were even the negation of what she had believed until that day.
The dress had been decided, a dress as simple as possible, which she would make herself like all her other dresses, and they had arrived at the witnesses.
- We no longer have any relations," said Philis.
- You used to have them; your father had friends, comrades.
- I'm no longer my father's daughter, I'm my brother's sister; I wouldn't dare ask them to witness my wedding.
- It's precisely because you're your brother's sister that they can't refuse you: that would be cruelty coupled with rudeness! Cruelty is fine, but rudeness! Who was your father's best friend among talented people?
- Cintrat.
- Isn't he a bohemian, a drunkard?
- My father considered him to be the greatest painter of our time, the most original...
- It's not a question of talent, but of name; I'm sure he's not just decorated. Your father had many other friends, more unquestionably arrived, more bourgeois, if you like?
- Glorious.
- The member of the Institute, perfect?
- Casparis, the statuary.
- An Academician too. That's what we need, and both highly decorated. There's no need to look any further; you just go and invite them, tell them who I am: associate professor at the School of Medicine, hospital doctor; I promise you they'll accept. As far as I'm concerned, I'll take my old master, Carbonneau, currently President of the Academy of Medicine, and Claudet, the former minister, who, as a member of parliament for my department, won't be able to shirk any more than the others; and that will give us some decorative witnesses who will look good in the papers.
It wasn't just in the newspapers that they did well, it was also in the church of Sainte-Marie des Batignolles when they were seen at the head of the procession on the carpet which, in the slightly dark nave, led up from the street to the altar.
- Glorient! Casparis! Carbonneau! Claudet! The arts, science and politics. Unless you had diplomats, you couldn't have hoped for more flowery boutonnieres.
It took the beauty and charm of the bride not to be overshadowed by these glorious witnesses; but when she was seen to pass on the arm of Glorient, so free in her modesty, so radiant with grace, exclamations of admiration and sympathy accompanied her to the sanctuary.
While the priest was celebrating mass at the altar, outside, in front of the gate, a man dressed in a brown velvet suit and wearing a battered felt hat, was strolling along smoking a bouffarde: it was the Count de Brigard, whose principles forbade him to enter churches for weddings as well as funerals, and who was strolling along the pavement with his disciples, waiting for the bridegroom to leave, to congratulate him. When the wedding took place, he cut the procession short and took Saniel's hand, shaking it warmly as he separated from his wife:
- It's good, it's noble," he said; "it's the situation that has made this marriage useless without her. I understand; for that I forgive her; I do more than that, I applaud her. My dear, you are a man.
And as it was a Wednesday evening, during a chat at Crozat's, he publicly revisited this approval which, under the conditions in which it had been given, was not sufficient for his conscience.
- Gentlemen, today we have witnessed a great act of reparation, the marriage of our friend Saniel to the sister of this poor boy, the victim of an injustice that cries out for vengeance. One evening, in this very room, I spoke lightly of Saniel, some of you may remember, despite the time that has passed; I would like to make amends to him publicly, now that he has asserted himself as a man of duty and conscience, bravely putting himself above social weaknesses.
- Isn't it a social weakness," says Glady, "to have chosen as witnesses to this act of reparation people who seem to have been chosen only for the decorative aspect of their official positions?
- Profound irony, on the contrary!" said Brigard, securing his felt hat, "a powerful and fruitful lesson, that which makes the very people who are its professional defenders work together to demolish prejudice! Saniel is a man.
V
On the Sunday after her wedding, Philis was surprised by something she thought about for a long time, without finding a satisfactory explanation.
As she was getting dressed, Saniel came into her room:
- What are you going to do today?
- What I do every day.
- Aren't you going to Mass?
She looked at him astonished, not being in control of her first reaction, and, as always when she seemed to want to read his mind, he became ill-tempered.
- Why is my question so extraordinary?
- Mass isn't exactly the usual subject of your preoccupations, it seems to me.
- It can become so exceptionally when I think of others, and that's the case: didn't you go to Mass sometimes?
- When I could.
- Well, you can today if you like; that's what I had to tell you: and I thought it should be said. I haven't forgotten the promise you made to respect my ideas and beliefs: I want to return the favour, that's very simple.
- Everything that is good and generous seems simple to you.
- So, what do you say?
- I'll go straight away.
- What do you mean, straight away? It's not eight o'clock. You'd better go to High Mass, it's more appropriate.
Proper! What a strange word from him! It wasn't out of respect for propriety that she sometimes went to mass, and more often these days than in the past, but because she had some vague religious and pious feelings that Florentin's misfortunes had intensified.
- I'll go to High Mass," she said, without revealing anything that this word had suggested to her, and continuing to dress.
- Is that the dress you're going to wear?" he asked, pointing to the one lying on a chair.
- Yes, unless you don't like it.
- I think it's a bit plain.
In fact, it was extremely simple, made from a cheap fabric that was only worth the originality Philis had given it by cutting it herself.
- Don't forget," he continued, "that Saint-François-Xavier isn't a church for drudgery; when you're as charming as you are, you get noticed everywhere: people will want to know who you are.
- You're right; I'll take my prize-giving dress.
- That's it, and your closed hat, isn't it? rather than a round hat; the first impression you make should be the right one.
Wasn't this mixture of religious and worldly preoccupation quite surprising in him? She hadn't known much about him until now, had she? After all, perhaps it was only an exception: at the beginning, he had wanted to give her advice that he considered wise.
But these demands on her grooming were repeated.
Although before the wedding she had only passed through Saniel's life, she knew her well enough to know that she was rigorously devoted to work, giving nothing of her time to distractions or even simply to worldly relations; and she had believed that things would continue like that; married, he would work as before he was married. She had been right about work, wrong about distractions, or rather relationships. Shortly after their marriage, one of their witnesses, the former minister Claudet, had caught up with a good portfolio and, Saniel having cured him of a facial neuralgia just in time for him to do the shopping and conduct the negotiations that had led to his appointment, he had taken a great liking to this young doctor to whom he owed his ministry: he was a good man to have on hand, the one who worked miracles and allowed you to go or not to go to the House according to circumstances; not to mention that he took away from you a pain that only those who have experienced it can talk about. Given Saniel's character and his habits, it seemed that this friendship should have had little influence on him: he was a doctor, not a courtier; but it turned out that the doctor and the courtier had become one and the same person, and Saniel had become the commensal of the ministry; there were no meetings, no parties without his invitation, and he accepted them all, for himself as well as for his wife.
What astonishment when she saw him leave everything to sit at the minister's table or appear in his salons, and also when the observations about the dress for mass had been repeated for those for dinners and parties!
First of all, the wedding dress had been adapted to these requirements by skilful decolletage; but it couldn't always fit: it had to be embellished, modified, made into three or four dresses, which wasn't easy; no matter how ingenious she was at these arrangements, a few metres of tulle and gauze didn't provide her with indefinite combinations.
Moreover, they were not enough for him; he found them too simple and wanted lace, jet, flowers, shine, glitter, all the things he saw in other women.
How could she satisfy him with the meagre resources at her disposal? She had brought a miser's thrift into her household; Joseph, who had been dismissed, had been replaced by a maid who did everything, the flat, the cooking and even a bit of laundry; this cooking was of the simplicity of poor people; but these small savings, earned on the one hand, quickly melted away on the other, in the toilets, in the cars that had to be taken, willingly, unwillingly, too often.
So she wanted to get back to work, not on lessons, which was no longer possible, but on menus, which would give her a hundred francs a month quite easily. He didn't agree and, as she gently insisted, he got angry:
- That wouldn't be worthy of you; I don't want it said that my wife does these jobs.
He had only allowed her to paint; since she had painted in her father's studio for fun in the past, and had only given up painting when she had to earn a living because she didn't have enough time to work honestly ; She could return to it now that she was no longer driven by the daily grind; if the profession was shameful, art could be honourable; if she had talent, it would be happy, even glorious; if she sold her paintings, it would be an originality that would make her known in the world. 
Part of the salon had been transformed into a studio and she had tried her hand at a few small paintings which, although they had no pretensions to great art, were nevertheless pleasant, easy, and done with a brilliant chic that appealed. Glorient, to whom she had shown them, had found them "as nice as anything", and he had had two of them bought by his dealer, who had ordered others, at a low price, it is true, very low even, but in the end, for her, much higher than she had expected.
With the courage and constancy that women bring to what pleases them, she would gladly have worked from morning to night; but the relations that Saniel had created for himself did not allow her the freedom to do so. By the very fact that he was a regular at Claudet's, he had been invited elsewhere, and since instead of shunning these invitations he had sought them out, the result had been worldly obligations which devoured her time. Every day she had one or more visits to make: she had to go to funerals, weddings, show up at charity sales; she herself had her day, and for three hours she had to listen to feminine chatter of no interest to her.
And what pleasure could he take in putting on a suit when he was tired after a day well spent, to go to a salon, he son of a peasant, who remained a peasant in so many ways, he who once understood nothing of worldly life and had nothing but contempt for it, finding it as boring as it was ridiculous.
She had tried to guess the cause of this change, and when she had deftly, lightly and in a roundabout way got him to explain himself, she had only come up with one answer, which for her was no answer at all:
- You have to be part of the world.
Was it for her sake, because she was the sister of a convict, that he wanted to impose himself everywhere and have her admitted with her head held high? She would have understood this to a certain extent, even though the role he was making her play was the cruellest one she could have been given, and precisely the opposite of the one she would have taken if she had been free.
But there was more to her need to be part of the world than that.
