He, for having married her, was not the brother of a convict, and yet, observing him closely, one could believe that what he asked of these relations and of the people in high positions that he sought was a share in their importance, their consideration, their honour, as if he wanted to cover himself with it. But he didn't need that importance, consideration or honour, and he had nothing to take from them by rubbing shoulders with them. He was someone in his own right. The place he had made for himself was worthy of his merit. His name was honoured. His future was envied.
And yet, as if he didn't feel this, he sought small satisfactions unworthy of a serious ambition and an undisputed worth; hadn't she been surprised, one evening when they were walking home, to hear that he had just been offered the decoration of a Spanish republic. Although she had learned to watch her words, an exclamation escaped her:
- What would you do with that?
- I couldn't refuse it.
Not only had he not refused that one, but he had also accepted others: blue, green, yellow and tricoloured ones too; he had worn them in his lapel, around his neck and on his suit. What good could these decorations, which diminished him, do him? And how could a man of his merit be in such a hurry to obtain the Legion of Honour before it fell naturally to him when it was ripe for him?
There were astonishments, obscurities and nonsense that kept her mind busy when she was sitting alone at her easel, painting, while next to her, in his laboratory, he was carrying out his experiments or writing an article for his Revue.
But it was not without resistance that she allowed herself to judge him in this way: we don't judge those we love, and she loved him. Wasn't it disrespectful of her love not to admire him in everything? When these ideas tormented her, she left her easel and, getting up, went to find him where he was: close to him, they dissipated. The first few times, so as not to disturb him, she tiptoed in, walking with muffled steps, and leaned over his shoulder, kissing him before he had seen or heard her; but then he betrayed such fright, such fear, that she gave up this way of approaching him.
- Why are you coming at me like that? What are you after? What do you want?
Instead of surprising him, she announced her arrival, slamming the bolt on the door and dragging her feet; and instead of greeting him with concern, he received her with open joy.
- You're not working any more?
- I've come to see you for a bit.
- Well, stay here, don't go back straight away; I'm never so happy, I never work so well as when I have you near me.
It was true, she could see and feel it; by the very fact that she was near him, whether she spoke or not, he was happy just by her presence.
But it was important that she didn't seem to be looking at him too attentively, with the obvious intention of observing him, because when this had happened in the early days of their marriage, he had become angry and upset, as when she had had the clumsiness to fall on his back unexpectedly:
- Why are you examining me like this? What are you looking for in me?
She had taken it for granted and, when she stayed close to him, she observed herself to keep a discreet attitude that wouldn't upset him: no curious looks, no questions, he was happy. However, as this attitude was not always convenient, she would ask him to help her, and after him she would go over the proofs in seconds, or she would clean up the rather crude drawings that he had made himself for his microscopic research. If he had wanted to stay like this and, in this sweet intimacy, let the evening pass by without mentioning going out, how happy she would have been! But he never forgot the time:
- Come on," he would say, interrupting himself, "it's time to go out.
She had never dared to ask the real reason for this "we must".
VI
If she didn't dare ask him frankly, "Why do you have to go out?" no more so than the others: "Why is it appropriate for me to be seen at mass? -- Why do I have to wear clothes that ruin us? -- Why do you accept decorations that are of no value to you? -- Why do you seek the company of people who have no merit other than that which they derive from their official position or their fortune? -- Why do we impose on ourselves worldly duties that bore us as much as each other, instead of remaining face to face in a tender and intelligent intimacy that is as sweet to us as to each other?
Had they all belonged to this order of ideas, she would undoubtedly have found an explanation for them: a disposition of character; the demands of an ambition in a hurry to fulfil its desires; susceptibility or shady pride; but there were others that were based on observations or memories that had nothing to do with these, or so it seemed to her.
Had she begun to know her husband the day after her marriage, she might have believed that he had always been as he revealed himself to her; but that was not her case, and the man she had loved resembled so little the man whose wife she had become, that one might have thought they were two.
In truth, it was not marriage that had brought about the changes in her mood that struck her, but they were no less characteristic in that they dated back to a time before her marriage.
How they had begun, she recalled with a clarity that left no room for doubt or hesitation: it was at the moment when the proceedings of his creditors had brought him into contact with Caffié. For the first time, he, always so firm that she believed him to be above weakness, had had a moment's despondency when he announced that he might be obliged to leave Paris; but this despondency had none of the anger or faint-heartedness that she had found in him later: it was the just sorrow of a man who sees his future shattered, nothing more. The only surprise she had felt at the time had been caused by the idea of strangling Caffié and taking from his till the money he needed to get out of trouble, and also because he had told her -- as a consequence of this act -- of the remorse of the intelligent man, who never has to bear the reproaches of his conscience, since for him conscience does not exist. But this was obviously a mere philosophical theory, not a character trait, a word of jest or an argument for discussion.
Rid of his creditors with the money he had won in Monaco, he had regained his composure, working harder than ever, passing his competitions, and, when he was in the best conditions to show himself nervous, violent, unjust, brutal, remaining, on the contrary, the man he had always been since she had known him. Then, all of a sudden, shortly before Florentin's trial, these strange moods, these angers, these worries, inexplicable to her, had erupted at precisely the moment when, thanks to Madame Dammauville's intervention, there was hope that Florentin would be saved. She had not forgotten the furious anger with which he had rejected her request to see Madame Dammauville, without anything to explain or justify this outburst: He had chased her off harshly, he wanted to break off the engagement, and until she had witnessed it she could not imagine that such violence could be put into exasperation; This scene had been followed by another, quite the opposite, which had struck her no less, when, at their dinner by the fire, he had shown such deep desolation by advising her to keep the memory of that evening for the day when she wanted to judge him, and by announcing to her in a sort of prophetic way that an hour would come when she would want to know the man she loved. 
And now that hour, the thought of which she had dismissed long ago, had come; now she was trying to put together the elements of this judgment which, at the time, seemed criminal to her, and now imposed itself on her preoccupations no matter what she did to put it off.
How many times had this memory come back to her, to the point where it could be said that it had never left her, sweet and painful at the same time, and less sweet and more painful as new subjects of concern were added one after the other, emphasising the mysterious and troubling impression it had left on her!
Judging him! Why did he want her to judge him? And on what grounds?
And yet, for him, these were not insignificant words, but the observation of a particular state of his consciousness, which had been confirmed several times since. Indeed, was it not to this order of ideas that belonged the cry that had escaped him in the night when, waking up suddenly, he had asked with emotion, with fear: "What did I say? And to the same man belonged the anger that had overtaken him when, talking about their religious wedding, she had mentioned confession: "Why do you admit that I might be afraid to go to confession?
How could he imagine that she could admit to him the idea of this fear? It had never occurred to him until that moment, and if now the memory of his astonishment came back to him, it was because other little facts, adding to each other with the passage of time, evoked it.
How numerous and significant these facts were: his constant concern to see himself observed by her; his irritation when he could suppose that she was thinking of questioning him; his fits of anger when, through inadvertence or clumsiness, through forgetfulness, or simply by chance, she asked him a question on certain subjects, and immediately the returns of tenderness that followed, so sudden that they seemed rather intended for a specific purpose than natural and spontaneous.
For a long time she had been willing to admit that the sweet words that made her so happy were calculated; but in the end she had to open her eyes to the evidence and see that they were the consequence of the same constant preoccupation -- that of not giving herself away.
It was only a short step from wondering what he didn't want to give up.
However short it was, she had long resisted the curiosity that drove her: it was her duty as a loving and devoted woman not to look beyond what was shown to her, and this duty was in perfect accord with the dispositions of her love; but the very force of the things seen had prevailed over will and reason: she could not apply her mind to looking for what distressed her, she could not close her eyes and ears to what struck them.
And what struck them were the same observations, always turning in the same circle, applying to the same subjects and the same people:
The name Caffié irritated him;
Madame Dammauville's name angered or disturbed him;
Florentin's name made him positively miserable.
As for Caffié's and Madame Dammauville's, she had been able to prevent them from being uttered, when she had seen the effect they infallibly produced.
How could she have told her mother never to pronounce the name of the man who constantly occupied their thoughts; how could she herself have stopped it from her lips?
Despite Saniel's representations and solicitations, backed up by those of Nougarède, Florentin had been shipped off to New Caledonia, from where he wrote as often as he could: His letters had recounted his tortures in the penal colony where he had been imprisoned during his crossing, and since then they had been one long complaint which continued from one to the next like an endless tale, always on the same subject: his material sufferings, his humiliation, his disgust among the wretched people with whom he was a companion, his despondency. 
When these letters arrived, it was a desolation for the mother and sister that filled the house with tears for several days; and then he would get angry at this pain that neither of them could conceal.
- What would you do if he were dead?" he would say to Philis.
- Wouldn't he be less to be pitied?
- He'll be back at last!
- But in what condition?
- Are we masters of fate?
- We weep; we don't complain.
But he complained about the weeping faces around him, the tears that were hidden from him, the sighs that were stifled. Usually, he was gentle and affectionate with his mother-in-law, with a thoughtfulness and deference that, in some ways, even had something affected about it, as if it were by will rather than by natural feeling that he was like that; but then he forgot this gentleness and treated her harshly, so unfairly that more than once Madame Cormier could not help complaining to her daughter:
- How can your husband, who is so good to me, be so ruthless when it comes to Florentin? Our grief seems to have the same effect on him as a reproach.
One day, when things had gone further than usual, she had the courage to explain herself to him.
- Forgive us for imposing on you the tedium of our grief," she said to him: "when I complain about everything, men and things, you must think that you are excepted, you who have done everything to save him.
But these few words, which she thought would calm her son-in-law's irritation, had, on the contrary, exasperated him, and he left furious.
- I don't understand anything about your husband," she told her daughter. Won't you explain to me what's wrong with him?
How could she have given her mother an explanation she couldn't give herself? Faced with an unfathomable abyss, she didn't even dare lean over it to look into the depths, and instead of going forward along the path she had unwillingly embarked upon, she made an effort to turn back, or at least to stop.
That was the way it was. Well, there was nothing she could do about it. What was the point of finding out why he was like that, and what was underneath what he was so careful to hide? It could only be a guilty curiosity for which she would one day be punished.
By continually turning over and over these thoughts, she had lost her zest, her strength to resist the blows of fate, as well as the annoyances of life, which had once made her so valiant; the spring so vigorous in her had sagged under the too heavy weight that loaded it, and her smiling eyes now more often expressed anxiety than happiness and confidence.
No matter how carefully she observed herself, she had not been able to hide these changes from Saniel, for they were evident in everything: in her formerly open face, which now bore the imprint of locked-in pain, in her concentrated attitude, in her silences and distractions.
What was wrong with her? He had questioned her: she had answered nothing that would have been enlightening for him, other than the very caution she put into her answers. He had examined her as a doctor, and had found nothing to indicate a state of illness, and therefore nothing to justify her changes.
If she didn't want to answer his questions - and he had proof that she didn't; if, on the other hand, she wasn't ill, and he was convinced that she wasn't - something serious must have happened to her, for the woman in whom he used to read so easily had become the troubling enigma that worried him.
And what was it, if not the one whose crushing weight he himself bore on his sagging shoulders? She had guessed; she had understood, if not everything, at least part of the truth!
What an extraordinary situation he was in, and one that was perfectly suited to baffle his reason.
Nothing to fear from others, everything from himself: justice, the law, the world, on all sides he was left alone; nothing was asked of him: what was owed had been paid; but he, by some sick aberration, was going to wake the dead who were sleeping in their graves from which no one thought of pulling them, and turn them into spectres that only he could see, only he could hear.
And he thought he was strong. What a fool he was, and even more ignorant, to have taken on such a burden when, by exercising his will, he had not put himself in a position to carry it! To want! But he had not learned to want, nor to use the brake that the brain puts on, so that he was like the lower animals whose reflex movements are accentuated by the removal of the brain.
VII
The relative calm that Saniel had experienced since his marriage was due to Philis, to the strength, confidence and peace that he drew from her. Philis without strength, without confidence, without inner peace, as he saw her now, could not give him what she no longer had, and he returned to the troubled times that had preceded his marriage, with the same disordered and sterile agitation, the same anguish, the same panic. Good relations, worldly esteem, success, decorations and honours were good for others; but for his own rest, he needed his wife's tranquillity and serenity, her good moral health which passed through him when she slept on his shoulder; then, no sudden awakenings, no insomnia: at the sound of her gentle breathing, he was reassured and the ghosts remained in their graves.
But when that breathing became agitated, when he could no longer feel that tranquillity and serenity in her, when he saw her weak and worried, it was no longer like that: it was his fever that she was giving him, not his sleep.
- Aren't you asleep? Why aren't you asleep?
- Why aren't you?
He had to know.
He asked her more questions, but she always defended herself, or rather evaded him, without him being able to get anything out of her, stopped as he was by the fear of giving herself away, which seemed easy at the point where one would think she had arrived; a clumsy word, too much insistence would make her light up.
So he pretended to speak only as a doctor when he questioned her, and to seek in her only medical explanations for her condition: If you don't sleep, it's because you're suffering; what is this suffering? Where does it come from?
Having no reason to justify it, since she didn't even dare talk about her brother, she stubbornly denied it.
- But I've got nothing," she repeated; "I assure you I've got nothing. What do you want me to have?
- That's what I'm asking you.
- So I'm asking you: "What do you want me to hide from you?
He couldn't admit that he suspected she was trying to hide something from him.
- You're looking at yourself wrong.
- There's nothing I can do about that.
- I'll make you observe yourself better and talk.
- How will you do that?
- By putting you to sleep.
The threat was so terrible that it threw her off her feet.
- Don't do that!" she cried.
- Why shouldn't I?
They stared at each other in silence for a few moments, both equally appalled: she at the threat, he at the confession he had just extracted; but to show this apprehension was, for her part, to drop another even more serious one.
- Why shouldn't I try, by every means possible, to discover in you the causes of these illnesses that elude my scrutiny as well as yours; for this, induced somnambulism offers us an excellent method.
- But since I'm not ill," she tried, "what can I tell you that's more sleepy than what I tell you that's awake?
- We shall see.
- It's an experiment I'm asking you not to try: would you try a poison on me?
- Sleepwalking is not a poison.
- Who knows?
- Those who have used it.
- You are not one of them.
- I still know enough so that you are in no danger from me.
She thought he was opening a door for her to escape.
- If you ever want me to talk in a state of induced somnambulism, ask one of your colleagues whom you trust to put me to sleep.
In front of a colleague, she was sure he wouldn't ask her any dangerous questions.
He understood that she was still trying to evade him.
- Afraid of what? Afraid that I'll ask you about the past, about what your life was like before we met, and ask you for a confession that would be a wound to my love.
- Oh, Victor," she cried distraught, "what more cruel wound could you have inflicted on me than that of these words: my confession! But it consists of two words: I love you, I have only ever loved you, I will only ever love you; I have no past: my life began with my love.
He could not press her any further without showing the importance he attached to this interrogation:
- I won't insist," he said; "it was a way like any other, better than any other; you don't want it, let's say no more about it.
But he had given in too quickly for her to expect him to give up his plan, and she was left with a stupefying fright. What would she say if he made her talk? Wasn't everything possible, when she didn't even know what thoughts were lurking in the back of her mind and she didn't even know what the induced sleepwalking she was threatened with was.
At that time, the work of the Nancy School on sleep, hypnotism and suggestion had not yet been published, or at least the book that served as its starting point was not known, and she knew nothing of the procedures that can be used to induce hypnotic sleep, having been left with what she had read, without paying much attention, about Cagliostro. As soon as her husband had left, she searched the library for books that might enlighten her; but the dictionary she found only provided her curiosity with obscure or confused information, in the midst of which she drowned; the only precise point that struck her was the formula to be used to induce sleep; make the subject you want to put to sleep look at a shiny object placed 15 or 20 centimetres above his eyes; if this were true, she need have no fear of ever being put to sleep.
However, she did not allow herself to be reassured, and when a few days later she found herself at a dinner party next to one of her husband's colleagues, who, she knew, was interested in sleepwalking, she had the courage to overcome her usual shyness about anything to do with medicine and ask him:
- Is it only people who are ill with certain diseases who can sleepwalk?
- In the past, the public and many doctors believed that somnambulism could only be induced in people suffering from hysteria or nervosism, but this was a mistake: artificial somnambulism can be obtained in a large number of perfectly healthy subjects.
- Does one retain one's will during sleep?
- The subject retains only as much spontaneity and willpower as his hypnotist wants to leave him, who can make him sad, happy, angry, tender and play his soul like an instrument.
- But it's appalling.
- Curious at least; it is certain that there is a local paralysis of this or that cell, the study of which will become the starting point for interesting discoveries.
- Once awake, does the subject remember what he said during his sleep?
- There is some disagreement on this: some say yes, others say no. As for me, I believe that memory depends to a large extent on the degree of sleep of the subject: in light sleep, there is memory; in deep sleep, the subject remembers neither what he said, nor what he heard, nor what he did.
She would have liked to continue, and her interlocutor, happy to talk about what was occupying him, would have gladly followed her, but she saw her husband standing at the other end of the table looking at them several times, and lest he should guess the subject of their conversation, she left it at that.
But as long as she did not allow herself to be hypnotised, she had nothing to fear; and sticking to what she had read, she promised herself never to allow him to place her in conditions where he could put her to sleep: it was during sleep that the hypnotist's will took the place of the subject's, not during wakefulness.
Relying on this belief and also on the fact that he had never spoken to her again about putting her to sleep, she reassured herself: wasn't this a sign that he accepted the resistance she had put up to him and gave up his idea of induced somnambulism?
But she was wrong.
One night when she had gone to bed at her usual time, while he was still working, she woke up suddenly to see him standing beside her, staring at her with eyes that frightened her.
- What is it?" she asked, "what do you want?
- Nothing, I don't want anything; I'm going to bed.
Despite the strangeness of the look that had struck her, she didn't insist: questions wouldn't have taught her anything; and besides, now that he wasn't going to bed at the same time as her, there was nothing extraordinary about his attitude.
But a few days later she woke up again in the middle of the night with a feeling of embarrassment, and she saw him leaning over her, as if he wanted to wrap his arms around her.
This time, however frightened she was, she had the strength not to say anything, but her anguish was all the more intense: was he trying to hypnotise her while she slept? Was that possible? So the dictionary had fooled her?
The dictionary had been accurate at the time of publication, but it was no longer accurate in terms of the procedures to be used to induce sleep; it was, in fact, while she was asleep that Saniel was trying to transform her natural sleep into an artificial one by means of passes. Would he succeed? He didn't know, since the experiment was new; but he risked it.
The first time, instead of putting her into a state of somnambulism, he had woken her up; the second time, he had no better success; the third time, when he saw that after a while she did not open her eyes, he assumed that she was asleep. To be sure, he raised one of her arms, which remained in the air until he lowered it onto the bed. Then, taking both her hands, he turned them round, and withdrawing his own, the impulse he had given her continued until he stopped her: her face had an expression of calm and tranquillity that had not been seen in her for a long time: she was the pretty Philis of yesteryear, with a cheerful face.
- Tomorrow I'll put you to sleep at the same time," he said, "and you can talk.
The next day, in fact, he put her to sleep, and even more easily; but when he questioned her, she resisted.
- No," she said, "I won't talk, it's horrible, I don't want to, I can't!
He insisted, she defended herself.
- Well, so be it," he said, "not today, tomorrow, but tomorrow I want you to speak and not resist me; I want to!
If he hadn't insisted, it was not only because he knew that it would take a certain amount of habituation to make her submit to his will without her being able to defend herself, but also because he didn't know whether or not she would keep the memory of what had happened in her sleep when she was awake - which was a crucial point.
The next day she was as she had been the day before, and there was no indication that she was aware of her induced sleep, or of what she had said in that sleep.
This time she fell asleep even more quickly and easily, and her face once again took on the expression of relaxed tranquillity he had seen the day before. Would she answer? And, if she did, would she speak sincerely, without trying to soften or distort the truth? Emotion made his voice tremble when he asked her his first question; it was her life, her rest, their happiness that was being decided.
- Where are you suffering?
- I'm not in pain.
- But you're restless, sometimes gloomy or worried; you don't sleep well. Who's tormenting you?
- I'm afraid.
- Afraid of what? Of whom?
- Of you!
He shivered.
- Scared of me! Do you think I could hurt you?
- No, I couldn't!
His tightly wound heart relaxed:
- Then why are you afraid?
- Because there are things going on inside you that frighten me.
- What things? We need to be more specific.
- The changes in your mood, your character, your habits.
- Why should these changes worry you?
- Because they indicate a serious situation.
- What situation?
- I don't know; I've never said.
- Why didn't you say?
- Because I was afraid, so I closed my eyes to avoid seeing.
- See what?
- The explanation for all the mystery in your life.
- When did you notice any mystery in my life?
- At the time of Caffié's death; and before that, when you told me you'd kill him without any remorse.
- Do you know who killed Caffié?
- No, I don't know.
He was so relieved that, for a few moments, he forgot to continue his questioning; then he resumed:
- And then?
- A little before Madame Dammauville's death, when you became irritable and furious about nothing; when you chased me away because you didn't want to see Madame Dammauville; when, on the evening before her death, you were so tender and asked me not to judge you without remembering that hour.
- But you judged me.
- I never did. When worry drove me, my love stopped me.
- Who was causing this anxiety apart from these facts?
- The way you've been since we got married: your fits of anger and tenderness, your fear of being watched, your restlessness at night, your complaints...
- Have I spoken?" he cried.
- Never clearly, but you often moan, you complain, you say words that are interrupted and unintelligible...
The anguish had been violent; when he had recovered, he continued:
- What is it about this way of being that still worries you?
- Your constant concern not to give yourself away...
- Give what away?
- I don't know what.
- What else?
- The anger you feel, or the embarrassment, when people mention the name Caffié, Madame Dammauville, Florentin...
- And you conclude from my anger at hearing these three names?
- Nothing... I'm afraid!...
VIII
This confession shocked him, for although it did not go beyond what he had feared, it nevertheless revealed a terrible situation.
If she didn't know everything, she had only one more step to take to arrive at the truth, and if she didn't take it, it was because her love had held her back: if this love had been less solid, less great, she would certainly not have resisted the evidence that was pressing on her from all sides.
But the fact that this resistance had been maintained to this day did not mean that the struggle would continue and that a more violent blow, a stronger proof than the others, would not open her eyes in spite of herself.
All it would take was one careless, clumsy move on her part, and, unfortunately, he had run out of them.
Instructed by what he had just learned, it was easy for him, it is true, by observing himself strictly, to avoid dangerous subjects, those she had just pointed out to him; but if during the day he could watch her words and her looks, say nothing or let nothing appear that was an accusation, not confirm the suspicions against which she was struggling, he could do nothing at night.
He had not spoken, and it was a terribly heavy weight that she had lifted from his heart by answering his question in the negative, but he had moaned, he had complained, he had uttered words that were interspersed, uninterrupted, unintelligible, and there lay the danger.
What did it take for these sighs and groans, these interspersed, unintelligible words to become distinct and take on meaning? Nothing, by chance, since his current cerebral disposition put him in a state of somnambulism to a certain extent. Were these abilities congenital or acquired? He didn't know. And before the restless nights that had followed the death of Madame Dammauville and the conviction of Florentin, he had never had the idea that he could speak in his sleep; but now he had proof that the vague fears that tormented him on this subject were only too well founded: he spoke, and if the words that escaped him were not at the moment comprehensible, they might become so.
Without having made any particular study of sleep, whether spontaneous or induced, he knew that in natural somnambulists hypnotic sleep is easy to produce, and that by talking to a subject who talks in his sleep, it is easy to hypnotise him. No doubt he did not have that to fear from Philis; but what was possible was that one night when he let slip some incoherent words, she would not be able to resist the temptation to engage him in conversation and get him to confess what she wanted to know, what the love she felt for her brother made her want to learn. If this happened, which would win out, love for her brother or love for her husband? If she questioned him, what would he not say?
For the first time he asked himself if he had been right to marry, and if, on the contrary, he had not been foolish to introduce a woman into a life as tormented as his own. He had asked this woman for calm, and now she was bringing him terror.
To tell the truth, she was only dangerous at night, and if he could find a way of sleeping in separate rooms, he would have nothing to fear from her during the day, provided he remained rigorously defensive. Loving her as she loved him, she would resist the curiosity that was driving him.Little by little, this anxiety and curiosity, no longer overexcited, would subside, and they would be able to revisit the sweet days that had followed their marriage.
But in the present circumstances it was difficult to find such a way, for to suggest to Philis that they make two rooms was to admit that he was afraid of her, and consequently to give her a new mystery to study. He searched, and starting from the idea that the proposal for the two rooms had to come from Philis herself, he arrived at a combination which, it seemed, could achieve what he wanted.
Unaware that she had been hypnotised and not remembering that she had spoken, Philis was no doubt still afraid of being put to sleep; if he threatened her again, she would certainly try to defend herself by escaping from him.
This is what happened: when, the very next day, he told her that he definitely wanted to put her to sleep to find out what was going on inside her, she showed the same fear as the first time.
- Everything you've asked of me, everything you've wanted," she said, trying to contain herself, "I've wanted it like you and with you, but I'll never accept that.
- As your resistance is crazy, I won't stop there.
- You won't put me to sleep against my will.
- I won't.
- It's not possible.
Without answering, he went and got a book from his bookshelf and, leafing through it, read:
"Is it possible to change a sleeping person from natural sleep to hypnotic sleep without waking them up? It is possible, at least for certain subjects.
Then he handed him the book:
- You see, all I have to do to put you to sleep artificially is to take advantage of the moment when you are sleeping naturally; it's very simple.
- That would be odious.
- Words.
He had thrown her into a fright that kept her awake all night, feverish, and as he himself did not sleep for fear of speaking, he felt that she would do anything not to be asleep. But hadn't he gone too far, and by this threat wasn't he going to push her into some desperate act: if she ran away, if she abandoned him? What would become of him without her? Wasn't she his whole life? But he reassured himself that she loved him too much for a separation to ever be possible. After searching, she would undoubtedly come to the idea that he wanted her to have.
Indeed, when he came home in the evening, she told him that her mother was not in good health and asked him to examine her. The result of the examination was that Mrs Cormier was in her usual state; however, she complained of choking; during the day she had feared syncope.
- If you wanted," she said, "I'd sleep next to my mother; I'm afraid I won't be able to hear her tonight if she's unwell.
At first he refused, but then he agreed to this arrangement and, to thank him, she stayed with him in his study, affectionate, full of tenderness and caresses, until the moment he went to his room.
He was therefore free to sleep or not to sleep; whether he moaned or spoke, she would not hear him, since there was no communication door between his room and that of his mother-in-law; his voice, for sure, would not pass through the partition.
Who could have told him, the night he had decided to marry, that he would end up like this: afraid, hiding from the woman who had given him back the peace of sleep; and all through his own fault, through a chain of imprudence and blunders, as if it were written that his suffering would be his alone, and that if he ever succumbed to the whirlwind that was dragging him down, it would be his own doing, his own hand. In the meantime, he had ensured the tranquillity of his nights, and as a further precaution, although he had no reason to fear that Philis would enter his room while he was asleep to surprise him, she who did not dare look in the face what suspicion showed her, he locked his door. No doubt Philis wouldn't always be able to sleep next to his mother, but in the meantime he would look for a way of frankly sleeping apart, and he was sure to find one in the arsenal of medicine.
As the night was hot, he thought a bit of fresh air would calm him down and he opened his window; if the fresh air didn't calm him down, at least it put him to sleep.
Forced to improvise a bed in her mother's room, Philis had placed it against the partition separating her from her husband, without any preconceived intention, simply by chance, because it was the only place where she could put it. In the middle of the night an unusual noise woke her up: she sat up to listen and recognise herself; it seemed to be coming from her husband's room; worried, she put her ear to the partition; she had not been mistaken, they were muffled moans, complaints repeated at fairly short intervals.
Cautiously, but nevertheless briskly, she got out of bed, and as the dawn had already whitened the windows, she was able to leave deftly and quietly. When she reached the door of her husband's room, she listened; she had not been mistaken: they were indeed complaints, but louder and more painful than those she had heard so often during the night. She tried to enter, but the door resisted, obviously locked from the inside by the bolt or the latch. A vague fear froze her. What had happened? She had to find out, run to him, help him. She thought of knocking, of shaking the door, but since he hadn't answered when she tried to open it, it must be that he didn't hear or didn't want to hear. So she had the idea of going out onto the terrace, where she could see what was going on and, if necessary, break a pane to get in.
She found the window open and saw him on the bed, his head turned towards her, asleep; she stopped, wondering if she should cross the threshold and wake him.
At that moment he uttered, with closed lips, a few words more distinct than those that had escaped him so many times:
- Philis... forgive.
He was dreaming of her; poor friend, what did he want her to forgive him for? for threatening to put her to sleep, no doubt.
In the crack of the window, she moved her head forward without entering the room, so touched by this sign of love, to give him a look before going back to her mother; but when she saw his face, which the white light of the morning was striking, she was frightened: it expressed the most violent pain, that face with its convulsed features, anguish as well as horror. He was certainly ill. She had to wake him up. As she took a step towards him, he began to speak again:
- Your brother... or me.
She stopped dead in her tracks, then instinctively stepped back and clung to the hall window so as not to fall, repeating to herself the two words she had just heard, not understanding, not wanting to understand.
Instead of returning to her mother's side, she staggered into the living room, holding on to the wall, and fell into an armchair, crushed.
- Your brother or me!
It was the truth, the terrible truth, that she had never wanted to see.
She stayed there until the noise of the morning warned her that she was about to be surprised, then she returned to her mother, who woke up.
- I'm going out," she said, "I'll be back at half past eight or nine.
- But your husband won't see you until he leaves for the hospital.
- Tell him I've gone out.
She returned at half past nine. Mrs Cormier had finished dressing.
- There you are at last," she said.
But she could see from her daughter's face that something threatening was going on:
- What's the matter?" she asked, trembling.
- Something serious, very serious, but irreparable by misfortune: we're going to leave here and never come back.
- Your husband...
- You must never speak to me about him; that's my prayer to you.
- Alas, I understand. What I had foreseen, what I had told him, is coming true: you cannot bear the contempt he is bringing down on us because of your brother.
- We have to be strangers to each other from now on, and that's why we're leaving this house.
- My God, at my age, dragging my bones...
- I've rented a flat at Les Ternes; a removal van is coming to take the furniture that belongs to us, just the ones we brought here. As far as the concierge is concerned, we're going to the country. As for Joséphine, you need have no fear of indiscreet questions, I've just given her the day off.
- But what about money?
- I still have two hundred francs from the sale of my last painting, that's enough for now; before they're used up, I'll have done and sold another; don't worry, we won't be short of anything.
All this was said in a jerky but resolute tone.
They were interrupted by a ringing bell. It was the removal men.
- Make sure they only take what belongs to us," said Philis; "while they're loading their car, I'll write in the living room.
After an hour, the carriage was loaded. Madame Cormier went into the living room to tell her daughter.
- I've finished," said Philis.
Having enclosed her letter in an envelope, she placed it in full view on Saniel's desk.
- Let's go now," she said.
And as her mother sighed, walking with difficulty:
- Lean on me, poor Mum, you know I'm strong.
IX
Saniel was not due back until fairly late in the afternoon. When he came in, opening the door with his key as usual, he was surprised not to see his wife running up to kiss him.
- She's working," he said to himself, "she didn't hear me.
He went into the living room, convinced that he would find her in front of her easel: but he didn't see her, and the easel itself was no longer in its usual place, either there or anywhere else.
He knocked on the door of Madame Cormier's room, but there was no answer; he knocked harder and waited a moment before going in; the room was empty: no bed, no furniture, no-one.
He looked round in astonishment, then, hurrying back into the hall, called out:
- Philis! Philis!
There was no answer: he ran to the kitchen, there was no-one there; he went to his study, there was no-one there either, but as he looked around, Philis's letter on his desk jumped out at him:
"I have left never to return. My despair and my disgust with life are such that if it hadn't been for my mother and that poor creature over there, I would have killed myself; but despite the horror of my situation, I had to think it over, and I didn't want to add to the harm that has been done around me by a weakness. My mother is no longer young, she is ill and has suffered cruelly; not only do I owe it to her to ease her old age by my presence, by the material and moral support I can give her, but she must have the confidence that I am there to replace her and open my arms to her son, to my brother. It's the least I can do for them to wait courageously; and however heavy, terrible and appalling my life may be from now on, I'll bear it so that the unfortunate outcast that implacable fate has struck down will find a home, a house, a friend when he returns. This will be my sole aim, my raison d'être, and in order to save myself from cowardice and weariness, my thoughts will always be focused on the hour when the one I want to make my child, and whom my love must save and heal, will be returned to me. I know that long years separate me from that day, and that my broken heart will never have a moment's rest before it rises ; But I will use that time to work for him, for the brother, for the child, for the loved one who will come to me old and in despair, and I want him to still believe in something good, not to imagine that everything is unjust and vile in this world, because he will come back to me weighed down by twenty years of shame, degrading and undeserved shame. How will he have endured these twenty years? What efforts will I have to make to prove to him that he must not give in to despair, and that life sometimes offers a remedy, compassion for the deepest, most unjust human suffering! How can we make him believe that? How do you get his poor closed heart to trust, to open up, to weep, which is the only thing that can relieve him? Finally, God, who has tested me so much, will no doubt come to my aid and inspire me with consoling words, show me the path to follow and give me the strength to persevere; don't I already have to bless Him for being alone in the world apart from my mother and my brother, those who will not betray me? I have no children of my own, and I am saved from the terror of seeing a soul grow up for evil, an intelligence escape me and go towards infamy or dishonour. So I am leaving as I came: poor girl I was, poor woman I am leaving. I have taken back the clothes and personal belongings I brought into the common dwelling, none of those acquired with the common money, and I forbid you to change anything about my will in this material matter, any more than about my resolution to flee from you. Nothing can ever bring us together again; nothing will, no consideration, no necessity. I reject the past, this guilty past whose responsibility weighs so heavily on my conscience, and I would like to lose the memory of a hated time. It would be impossible for me to accept the struggle, nor the pleas if it were convenient for you to make them. I have severed our ties, and from now on we will be as far apart as if one of us had died, farther still. So don't have any qualms about leaving me alone to face a new life, a new beginning that may seem difficult and painful to anyone who is not in my shoes. The hardships of the past will have been good for me, because they have hardened me to the difficulties of work; the desolation of today will sustain me, in the sense that, having suffered all that can be suffered, I no longer have to fear some discouraging catastrophe that would stop me in my tracks. In order not to compromise you and to become myself again, I will take back my family name, a dishonoured name, but one that I will bear without shame. I will live in obscurity, absorbed in my work and endeavouring to forget even your existence: do the same. You may find me harsh, if you think of the past; however, this departure is not a selfish desertion; I am no longer good for you, and the rest you need would henceforth flee from you near me. On the contrary, seek oblivion as I will seek it myself. If you can erase from your life the time I spent there, you may be able to put the rest of it behind you and regain a little of the calm you once had. I can no longer remember that I loved you, because my situation is so peculiar that I do not even keep the refuge of memory; at my age, I have to remain without a past as well as a future; I lack everything that makes up the consolation of the unhappy. I can't get out of my despondency to try to find an hour when life has been kind to me; on the contrary, those hours make me shudder and I reproach them to myself as if they were a crime. So, wherever I turn, all I find is pain and poignant regret; everything is withered and dishonoured for me.
He had read this letter written in one go, in one go, standing in the middle of his study; when he reached the end, he looked around vaguely; his armchair was slightly away from his desk: he dropped into it and stood there, devastated, holding the letter in his clenched hand:
- Alone!
It was a dark and muddy October afternoon; in the rue des Saints-Pères, along the houses that hide the Hôpital de la Charité, coupés were lined up, waiting, and their line extended as far as the boulevard Saint-Germain, where the coachmen, having got out of their seats, were chatting like people who are used to meeting each other. From the porch with its pretentious, heavy columns, which forms the corner of the street and the boulevard, at about half-past four, in the darkness that was beginning to fall, we saw a number of serious-looking men in dark clothes emerge -- the members of the Academy of Medicine -- who, the Tuesday session having ended, were returning to their carriages: Some of them were alone, hurrying off at full speed; others were accompanied, taking their time, stopping to speak kindly to a journalist and recommending the day's paper, or continuing with a fellow non-academician the discussion begun in the Salle des pas-perdus; it was the end of the Bourse aux consultations, the most amusing feature of this place for those who know how to watch or are aware of the little electoral intrigues that are played out there. Not all the members of the Academy have a long list of patients to visit; but they all have a voice to give, and it is these that the candidates surround themselves with, trying to win them over... You already have a rich clientele, but you're not yet a member of the Academy; so you manoeuvre to get there: with the surgeon, you arrange, at the expense of clients who have faith, a consultation to see a superficial panic attack; with the doctor, you arrange one to visit a migraine sufferer. One hopes that, when the day comes to vote, the academician will not refuse his vote to a colleague who, through consultations of this importance, earns you a thousand or fifteen hundred francs a year, sends you game from his hunt, his box at the Opéra, his coupon from the French; and that, gallant man that he is, he will have the gratitude of his stomach or his purse.
One of the academicians, who appeared last at the top of the steps, was a tall man, but stooped, with a hollow, pallid face, illuminated by two pale blue eyes with a strange expression, hard and desolate at the same time; he advanced alone, and at his heavy gait, at his shuffling step, one could wonder if he was sixty years old, while in other respects he still retained a certain youthfulness, -- Saniel, aged twenty years.
Without anyone exchanging a nod or a handshake with him, he went down to the pavement, and, going up it, he came as far as the boulevard, where he opened the door of a coupé whose interior showed the complete installation of a travelling library: writing table, with paper, inkstand and lamp, pockets, bellows all full of books and pamphlets.
Just as he was about to get in, a voice stopped him:
- Dear master!
He turned round; it was one of his former interns, a doctor who had recently moved to the suburbs near Gentilly, who had come running.
- What is it?" asked Saniel.
- I'd like to ask you to come and help me with a very curious case of eclampsia, where your intervention could be decisive.
- And where might that be?
- At the White House, a poor woman... What day can you give me?
- Is it urgent?
- Yes, it is.
- Immediately then; come upstairs with me, after explaining to my coachman.
But just then, a white-haired man, dressed in brown velvet, wearing a battered felt hat and clogs, came towards them accompanied by two young men with whom he was talking loudly and gesticulating: as they passed, people turned to look at them, so original was the outfit of old Brigard, who had remained from head to foot the man of yesteryear.
He came to Saniel with both hands outstretched, and Saniel, hat in hand, greeted him with every mark of respect.
- I'm delighted to meet you," said Brigard, "because yesterday I went to your consultation without seeing you.
- Why didn't you let me know? If you need me, I'm all yours.
- Thank you, fortunately I don't need your advice, either for myself, as you can see, or for my family; I just wanted to see you. I arrived at your house before the hour, waited in your living room, and then several people came in behind me: a young woman who seemed to be in great pain, an old woman who gave every sign of anxiety, and a man who was agitated by disorderly movements and couldn't stay in place. And as I looked at them, I said to myself that, since I only had a friendly visit to make to you, I was going to prolong the wait of these unfortunate people who were counting the minutes; so I left.
- May I ask why I was honoured with this visit?
The two young men accompanying Brigard and Saniel's former intern had discreetly taken a few steps away.
- I wanted to offer you my congratulations. When I learned of your candidacy for the Académie de Médecine, I said to myself: "Here's someone who doesn't stand a chance; he's original, our friend Saniel, he's strong, he's succeeded brilliantly, and these qualities are not exactly academic". I was wrong: you broke down the doors, which is the only way to get into these places that I understand; that's why I congratulate you. Besides, I was wrong to you once...
- Wrongs, you?
- I accused you of thinking you were stronger than life: you were indeed stronger than life; my compliments! It's a comforting sight for me to follow you.
After shaking Saniel's hands warmly, he walked away, accompanied by his two disciples, preaching.
The young doctor approached Saniel:
- Here's an original," he said.
- A happy man!
END
